CASE 01 — Acute Follicular Conjunctivitis
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 42-year-old woman with 2-year history of intermittent bilateral ocular itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and morning eyelid crusting, recently worsened; symptoms unrelated to systemic illness or allergies and no ocular medications.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old woman with no past ocular history presents with complaints of bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, and crusting eyelids in the morning. Although she has experienced these symptoms intermittently over the last 2 years, her symptoms have recently become worse. She denies taking any ocular medications. Her symptoms are unrelated to any systemic illness including allergy, rosacea, or flulike symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Over the last 2 years, recently worsened
Symptom Duration: intermittently over the last 2 years
Progression: Worsening over recent period (symptoms recently became worse)
Characteristics: acute, bilateral itching/burning/foreign body sensation, crusting eyelids, mild eyelid margin erythema with scaling, trichiasis/madarosis/poliosis, mild conjunctival hyperemia with inferior papillary reaction, diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions, mild aqueous tear deficiency


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation and symptomatic treatment with cool compresses and unpreserved lubricants
Associated Symptoms: bilateral itching, burning, foreign body sensation, crusting eyelids in the morning, intermittent symptoms over 2 years, mild eyelid margin erythema, scaling, crusting at eyelashes, trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, mild conjunctival hyperemia, mild papillary reaction, diminished tear lake, inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions, mild aqueous tear deficiency
Previous Episodes: Intermittent symptoms over the last 2 years
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — symptomatic discomfort (itching, burning, foreign body sensation, morning crusting) with preserved 20/20 vision; may cause minor disruption (hygiene measures, occasional visual irritation) but not significant functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic illness including allergy, rosacea, or flulike symptoms
Risk Factors: Contagious exposure to adenovirus (contact with infected individuals or fomites), poor eyelid hygiene (crusting/blepharitis), recurrent ocular surface disease/tear deficiency (dry eye), and history of recurrent conjunctivitis (intermittent symptoms over 2 years)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen unaided
Intraocular Pressure – Right: Normal
Intraocular Pressure – Left: Normal
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Mild erythema of the eyelid margin with scaling and crusting at lash bases; trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, and no eyelid ulceration noted
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens, no cataract changes detected


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL, no APD
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild erythema of the eyelid margin with scaling and crusting at the base of the eyelashes; trichiasis, madarosis, poliosis, and eyelid ulceration noted; mild conjunctival hyperemia with mild inferior tarsal papillary reaction; diminished tear lake bilaterally; inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions on cornea (rose bengal positive)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral; bilateral mild blepharitis with meibomian gland dysfunction and mild aqueous tear deficiency causing inferior superficial punctate epithelial erosions. Observation and symptomatic treatment recommended; counsel on contagiousness and follow-up in 4–6 weeks.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute follicular conjunctivitis, likely adenoviral
Secondary Diagnoses: Blepharitis; Aqueous tear deficiency (mild dry eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Adenoviral (viral) conjunctivitis; Bacterial conjunctivitis; Adult chlamydial (inclusion) conjunctivitis; Allergic conjunctivitis
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: contagion/transmission to others; conjunctival membranes or pseudomembranes with risk of symblepharon formation; persistent subepithelial corneal infiltrates leading to corneal scarring and potential prolonged decreased vision


Plan
All Medications: cool compresses; unpreserved lubricants; loteprednol; 0.6% povidone iodine/0.1% dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension (FST-100); trifluridine; vidarabine; ganciclovir (ophthalmic gel); topical cidofovir; 2′3′-dideoxycytidine; interferon β; interferon γ; immunoglobulin; N-chlorotaurine; topical trifluridine (for herpes); oral acyclovir 400 mg five times daily; acyclovir; valacyclovir; tetracycline 250 mg orally four times daily; doxycycline 100 mg twice daily; erythromycin 500 mg four times daily; sulfisoxazole 0.5–1 g four times daily; azithromycin 1 g single dose; mitomycin C
All Procedures: - Removal of conjunctival membranes or pseudomembranes with forceps or cotton swab - Placement of a self-retained amniotic membrane - Schirmer test - Rose bengal dye examination (corneal staining) - Fluorescein, lissamine green, or rose bengal corneal staining - Tear breakup time measurement - Tear osmolarity testing - Metalloproteinase-9 testing (point-of-care) - Meibomian gland imaging (e.g., LipiView) - Eyelid cultures - Excimer laser phototherapeutic keratectomy with low-dose mitomycin C
Follow-Up Instructions: Return for re‑examination in 4–6 weeks to ensure symptom improvement, follicle regression, and corneal opacity resolution; sooner/more frequent visits if visually threatening corneal opacities or conjunctival membranes/pseudomembranes develop.
Patient Education: Observation and symptomatic care: use cool compresses and unpreserved lubricating drops. The condition may be contagious for 1–2 weeks (or longer); practice strict hygiene—frequent hand washing, keep used tissues, washcloths, and pillowcases separate from others. Return earlier if membranes/pseudomembranes develop (may need removal). Follow-up in 4–6 weeks to confirm improvement; seek sooner care if vision worsens or severe symptoms occur.
Work Restrictions: Avoid work that risks spreading infection; maintain strict personal hygiene (frequent hand washing, avoid sharing tissues/washcloths/pillowcases) and stay off work if contagious until symptoms improve (approximately 1–2 weeks).


CASE 02 — Chronic Follicular Conjunctivitis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photophobia, right eye discomfort, and headache centered around the right eye for 2 weeks.
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I specifically deny any sexual contact."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 37-year-old female with 2 weeks of right eye photophobia, discomfort, and headache; 1 week after symptoms began she developed sudden onset redness and watering of the right eye on returning to work after a Hawaii vacation, with associated swelling in front of the right ear and a mildly tender preauricular node. Treated by prior provider with 2 weeks topical tobramycin–dexamethasone; follicles persisted and 2 days after stopping drops she developed corneal epithelial infiltrates. Visual acuity 20/25 OD, 20/20 OS; unilateral ptosis ~1–2 mm OD, mild bulbar injection, moderate pretarsal follicles and papillae OD, few follicles OS; right corneal midperipheral and some central epithelial infiltrates without stromal involvement. Denies sexual contact and systemic/genital symptoms.
History Narrative: A 37-year-old female office worker with a 2-week history of photophobia, ocular discomfort, and right-sided headache. One week prior she developed sudden onset redness and watering of the right eye on the first day back at work after a vacation in Hawaii. She noted swelling in front of the right ear. She saw her eye-care practitioner, who found unilateral follicular conjunctivitis with a mildly tender preauricular node and treated her with a 2-week course of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone suspension. The node became slightly smaller, but 2 days after stopping the drops she developed epithelial corneal infiltrates and persistent follicles. She denies systemic or genital symptoms and reports no known bird or cat exposure; she stayed in hotel rooms and used swimming pool facilities while traveling. Visual acuity 20/25 OD and 20/20 OS. On exam there is a right-sided nontender preauricular node, 1–2 mm right upper lid ptosis, mild bulbar conjunctival injection OD, moderate pretarsal follicles and papillae OD (fewer follicles inferiorly OS), and diffuse midperipheral and a few central corneal epithelial infiltrates OD without stromal involvement; left cornea and anterior segment are normal. Prior topical corticosteroid use likely altered the clinical course. Tests for adenovirus, HSV, and chlamydia were negative. Medication history notable for brimonidine use, which was discontinued as the presumed offending agent. Symptoms and follicles improved rapidly after stopping brimonidine and resolved by 2 months.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks prior
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Improved after discontinuation of offending agent; follicles resolving by 3 weeks and completely resolved by 2 months.
Characteristics: Unilateral chronic follicular conjunctivitis with moderate pretarsal follicles and papillae, mild bulbar injection, small epithelial corneal infiltrates (midperipheral and some central), 1–2 mm right upper eyelid ptosis (pseudoptosis), and preauricular lymphadenopathy.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of topical tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension drops (topical corticosteroid) and continued use of brimonidine (topical glaucoma medication)
Alleviating Factors: Discontinuation of the offending topical agent (brimonidine) and use of preservative-free lubrication/cold compresses
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, discomfort, headache, redness, watering (tearing), swelling in front of right ear, mild eyelid ptosis, irritation, erythema
Prior Treatments: Topical tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension drops; brimonidine (discontinued)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — symptoms (photophobia, discomfort, headache) caused some irritation but vision largely preserved (20/25 OD, 20/20 OS) and improved after stopping brimonidine.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms
Recent Travel: Hawaii
Risk Factors: Recent use of topical ophthalmic medications (tobramycin–dexamethasone drops and brimonidine), recent swimming pool/hotel exposure (fomite exposure), recent travel (vacation to Hawaii)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N6 (R), N6 (L)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 1–2 mm ptosis of the right upper lid
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild injection of the bulbar conjunctiva on the right; left conjunctiva quiet.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Right cornea: diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates, no stromal involvement; left cornea normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria or abnormal shape noted)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Nontender preauricular node (right)
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate number of medium-sized pretarsal follicles and papillae in the right eye; right cornea with diffuse midperipheral and a few central epithelial infiltrates, no stromal involvement; mild injection of the right bulbar conjunctiva; left eye quiet with substantially fewer inferior fornix follicles; right-sided 1–2 mm upper lid ptosis; right corneal epithelial disease only, left cornea and anterior segment normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis most consistent with medication-induced (brimonidine) toxic conjunctivitis after exclusion of adenovirus, HSV, and Chlamydia by testing; clinical course and resolution of follicles following discontinuation of brimonidine support the diagnosis.
Primary Diagnosis: Unilateral toxic chronic follicular conjunctivitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Nontoxic: none
Differential Diagnosis: Adenoviral keratoconjunctivitis; Adult chlamydial (inclusion) conjunctivitis; Herpes simplex/EBV keratoconjunctivitis; Toxic (medicamentosa) follicular conjunctivitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: punctal stenosis; subconjunctival scarring; symblepharon; obliteration of conjunctival fornices; loss of conjunctival goblet cells; superficial punctate keratopathy; large epithelial erosions; corneal ulceration/melting


Plan
All Medications: tobramycin–dexamethasone ophthalmic suspension; topical tobramycin–dexamethasone; topical corticosteroids; tobramycin; brimonidine; preservative-free artificial tears/ointment; topical cyclosporine A 1%; topical tacrolimus ointment; topical anesthetics; trifluridine; idoxuridine; gentamicin; pilocarpine; timolol; apraclonidine; epinephrine; dipivefrin
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up every 2–3 weeks until improvement is noted; monitor for recurrence or occult infection.
Patient Education: Discontinue the offending eye drop (brimonidine) immediately. Use preservative‑free artificial tears or ointment for comfort. Apply cold compresses as needed for irritation. Avoid other topical medications with preservatives until healed. Return for follow-up every 2–3 weeks until symptoms and follicles improve, and sooner if pain, vision loss, increasing redness, or discharge occur. If a glaucoma medication is still needed, discuss switching to a preservative‑free alternative with your eye doctor.


CASE 03 — Acute Bacterial Conjunctivitis
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine follow-up 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty of the right eye; mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling.
DURATION: 15 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had 'strep throat' or 'mononucleosis'."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old female, 15 months post–right penetrating keratoplasty, with comfortable eyes but 6-week history of right-sided preauricular lymphadenopathy and mild preauricular discomfort/swelling following a febrile illness 6 weeks earlier; denied ocular symptoms during that illness. Currently unilateral right-sided mild lid puffiness, mild lid scurf, prominent inferior and superior tarsal follicles and papillae, mild diffuse keratoplasty epitheliopathy, VA 20/60 improving to 20/40 pinhole OD, IOP 28 mmHg OD, on loteprednol, brimonidine, timolol, and methylcellulose; no other systemic symptoms, otherwise healthy. Treated with oral penicillin for presumed strep/mono at time of febrile illness with resolution of fever but persistent preauricular lymphadenopathy.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old female patient presents for a routine follow-up visit 15 months after penetrating keratoplasty and anterior segment reconstruction for trauma to the right eye. Her eyes are comfortable, but she complains of mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling. She recalls that 6 weeks previously she developed a fever and swollen glands, particularly on the right side of her face, and her internist suspected “strep throat” or “mononucleosis.” She had no ocular symptoms at that time. Throat culture and Epstein–Barr virus serology were taken and were negative. She was treated with oral penicillin for 14 days, and she recovered symptomatically except for the persisting preauricular lymphadenopathy. The patient is otherwise healthy with a negative review of systems. Her past ocular history is also significant for medically treated traumatic angle recession glaucoma of her right eye. Her left eye is normal with no ocular history. Her ocular medications for the right eye were loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily, brimonidine 0.2% twice daily, timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily, and methylcellulose at bedtime. She had last been seen 3 months previously, and no ocular inflammation was seen. Her current examination reveals a visual acuity of 20/60 + with pinhole to 20/40 in the right eye. Intraocular pressures were 28 mm Hg in the right eye and 14 mm Hg in the left eye. She has a slightly tender right preauricular lymph node. External exam reveals slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left. Biomicroscopy of the right eye shows mild lid scurf and no other lesions. The conjunctiva reveals prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area. No granulomas are present. The keratoplasty has mild diffuse epitheliopathy. The anterior segment exam is otherwise noncontributory. The left eye shows mild pretarsal papillae, without follicles, and an otherwise normal exam. The patient denied using any eye drops other than the prescription medications already listed. She denied close contact with animals, including cats and birds. She had not traveled to potentially endemic areas for other infectious etiologies. She denied any gynecologic complaints and reported being monogamous.
Symptom Onset: Chronic ( >16 days ), with onset ~6 weeks prior (insidious/chronic)
Symptom Duration: 6 weeks
Progression: Acute (hours to days)
Characteristics: Red eye with conjunctival injection, chemosis, purulent/mucopurulent discharge, ocular irritation, eyelids stuck together in morning; may begin unilateral and spread, can include papillary reaction and inflammatory (pseudo)membranes; onset acute (hours–days) or hyperacute (<24 h) in severe cases.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Touching the eyes and sharing towels or washcloths while symptomatic
Alleviating Factors: Warm compresses and artificial tears; topical antibiotic drops (e.g., trimethoprim–polymyxin B)
Associated Symptoms: mild right-sided preauricular discomfort and swelling; fever 6 weeks prior; tender right preauricular lymph node; slight puffiness of lids; ocular irritation; stuck/shut eyelids in morning due to discharge (glued eyelids)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes of conjunctivitis reported
Prior Treatments: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% twice daily; brimonidine 0.2% twice daily; timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% twice daily; methylcellulose at bedtime; 14 days oral penicillin (for suspected strep/mononucleosis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild—eyes comfortable with only mild preauricular discomfort; symptoms improved with treatment and no significant functional impairment reported (able to work/perform routine activities).
Systemic Symptoms: Fever and swollen (preauricular) glands 6 weeks prior; otherwise negative review of systems.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Use of chronic topical preserved medications (timolol, brimonidine) leading to toxic conjunctival effects; recent febrile systemic infection with preauricular lymphadenopathy; prior penetrating keratoplasty/ocular surgery; chronic topical corticosteroid use (loteprednol); immunocompetent adult (less risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60+
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart with pinhole
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left; mild lid scurf
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area (right eye); mild pretarsal papillae in the left eye
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Slight puffiness of the lids, right worse than left.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild diffuse epitheliopathy, including punctate keratitis within the keratoplasty wound margin (hurricane epitheliopathy)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open (postoperative peripheral anterior synechiae from angle recession)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Biomicroscopy of the right eye shows mild lid scurf and no other lesions. The conjunctiva reveals prominent follicles and papillae in the inferior fornix and superior pretarsal area. The keratoplasty has mild diffuse epitheliopathy. The anterior segment exam is otherwise noncontributory. The left eye shows mild pretarsal papillae, without follicles, and an otherwise normal exam.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Streptococcal pneumoniae acute bacterial conjunctivitis of the right eye with inflammatory pseudomembrane formation. Unilateral conjunctival injection with purulent discharge, papillary reaction and mild keratoplasty epithelial changes; preauricular lymphadenopathy present. Managed with topical trimethoprim–polymyxin B drops four times daily for 7 days with early symptomatic improvement. Continued monitoring for resolution and to rule out atypical or chlamydial infection given chronic follicular features and topical steroid use.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal pneumoniae bacterial conjunctivitis with inflammatory pseudomembranes
Secondary Diagnoses: 1. Penetrating keratoplasty, status post (right eye) 2. Anterior segment reconstruction, status post (right eye) 3. Traumatic angle recession glaucoma (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Viral conjunctivitis (adenovirus, EBV, HSV); Chlamydial (adult inclusion) conjunctivitis; Toxic follicular conjunctivitis from topical medications; Conjunctival lymphoma/masquerade syndrome
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: Risk of corneal involvement including corneal perforation (particularly in hyperacute/gonococcal cases); transmission to other eye/contacts; rare systemic spread in severe infections; formation of inflammatory membranes or pseudomembranes; potential for decreased vision.


Plan
All Medications: loteprednol etabonate 0.5% (eye drops) brimonidine 0.2% (eye drops) timolol gel-forming solution 0.5% (eye drops) methylcellulose (eye drops) oral penicillin (14 days) trimethoprim sulfate–polymyxin B drops (four times daily for 7 days) azithromycin 1000 mg PO once (as treatment option) doxycycline 100 mg PO twice daily (7–10 days; option) erythromycin 500 mg PO four times daily (7–10 days; option) bacitracin (topical; option) aminoglycosides (topical; option) fluoroquinolones (topical; option) ceftriaxone (intramuscular one-time; for gonococcal) intravenous ceftriaxone (inpatient; if cornea involved) gentamicin (topical; option) ciprofloxacin (topical; option) besifloxacin (topical; option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up if symptoms worsen or do not improve within 48–72 hours; return sooner for increasing pain, vision loss, marked swelling, or copious purulent discharge; seek immediate care for signs of hyperacute infection (rapid onset, copious purulence) or concern for corneal involvement.
Patient Education: Use prescribed antibiotic eye drops as directed (trimethoprim–polymyxin B four times daily for 7 days). Expect symptom improvement within a few days; most cases resolve within 1–2 weeks. Apply warm compresses and artificial tears for comfort. Avoid touching eyes, sharing towels, or close contact to reduce transmission. Return promptly for worsening symptoms (increasing pain, vision loss, copious purulent discharge, or spreading redness) or if no improvement within 48–72 hours. If hyperacute signs (rapid onset, marked purulence, severe pain) occur, seek immediate care—this may require systemic antibiotics.
Work Restrictions: Avoid touching eyes or sharing towels/washcloths while symptomatic.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate emergency care if you develop severe eye pain, marked vision loss, copious purulent discharge, rapidly worsening redness/swelling, signs of corneal involvement (white spot on the cornea), fever/systemic illness, or if symptoms rapidly worsen despite treatment.


CASE 04 — Pterygium
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye for 3 days
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "my eye has been stuck shut for the past 2 days"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 60-year-old woman with 3 days of decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in the right eye. Eye stuck shut for 2 days requiring a warm, wet washcloth to open. Denies trauma, surgery, ill contacts, fevers, chills, malaise, or other systemic symptoms. Past medical history notable for hypertension; takes metoprolol. No medication allergies. Wears glasses.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presents with complaints of decreased vision, irritation, and discharge in her right eye over the past 3 days. She reports that her eye has been stuck shut for the past 2 days, requiring a warm, wet washcloth to open the eye. She denies any trauma or surgery. She denies any contacts with ill individuals and does not have any fevers, chills, malaise, or any other ill symptoms. Her past medical history includes hypertension and a hysterectomy 10 years ago. She reports no allergies to medications and uses metoprolol for hypertension. She denies any family history of eye disease. The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems other than her ocular complaints. She is wearing glasses.
Symptom Onset: 3 days
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Progressive growth impacting vision
Characteristics: Fibrovascular overgrowth of bulbar conjunctiva, typically nasal interpalpebral fissure; may have Stocker’s iron line; can be fleshy or atrophic; may be inflamed with prominent vessels, epithelial irregularity, and Bowman’s layer opacification; often UV-exposure–related and slow‑growing but can be active/progressive.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Ultraviolet light exposure and chronic dry eye (wind/UV exposure), activities causing chronic sun/wind exposure
Alleviating Factors: Uses a warm, wet washcloth to open the eye (implies warmth/moisture helps); wearing glasses and use of preservative‑free artificial tears and UV‑blocking sunglasses may alleviate symptoms.
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision, irritation, discharge, eye stuck shut (due to crusting), mucopurulent discharge, tearing, foreign body sensation.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision OD (20/40 unaided, 20/30 with refraction), irritation, discharge, eye stuck shut for 2 days, requiring warm compresses; affects activities requiring clear vision and comfort (reading/driving), but left eye largely functional.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with ill individuals
Risk Factors: Exposure to ultraviolet (UV) light, chronic dry eye disease, wind/irritation, living closer to the equator, male sex (less applicable here), age (young to middle adulthood more common), prior ocular surface inflammation/trauma.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Manifest (with correction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Right conjunctiva injected and edematous; left conjunctiva normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no evidence of scarring or inflammation
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (grade 3)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Right: mild cataractous changes; Left: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right conjunctiva injected and edematous; mucopurulent discharge from lower lid; inferior fornix covered with a fibrin–mucus pseudomembrane; inferior and superior palpebral conjunctiva with 2+ papillary reaction; corneas clear without scarring or inflammation; anterior chambers quiet; right lens with mild cataractous changes; left eye slit lamp normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.4 in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 60-year-old woman with bilateral pterygia, right greater than left, presenting with decreased vision, irritation, and mucopurulent discharge OD. Exam shows injected, edematous right conjunctiva with an inferior fornix pseudomembrane and 2+ papillary reaction of the palpebral conjunctiva; corneas clear, quiet anterior chambers, mild right cataract, posterior poles normal, IOPs 18 OD and 15 OS, CD ratio 0.4 OU. Gram stain and cultures of right eye grew Streptococcus pneumoniae sensitive to penicillin, ciprofloxacin, vancomycin, and TMP/SMX, indicating concurrent bacterial conjunctivitis with pseudomembrane formation. Pterygia are present bilaterally; likely chronic, nonmalignant fibrovascular overgrowths (typical nasal interpalpebral location) with no corneal involvement or vision-threatening progression at this time. Management: treat acute bacterial conjunctivitis (topical antibiotic guided by sensitivities) and address inflammation/supportive care for pterygium with lubrication, UV protection, and topical steroid short course if active inflammation persists; monitor pterygium size and induced astigmatism for possible surgical removal if progressive or vision‑threatening, counseled regarding recurrence risk and adjunctive measures if surgery pursued.
Primary Diagnosis: Pterygium, bilateral
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Status post hysterectomy (10 years ago)
Differential Diagnosis: Pinguecula; Pseudopterygium (conjunctival scar); Conjunctival intraepithelial neoplasia (including squamous cell carcinoma)
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: persistent epithelial defects and scleral necrosis


Plan
All Medications: metoprolol preservative-free artificial tears topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory medications topical steroid (eg, mild topical steroid dosed four times daily and tapered over 2–3 weeks) ultraviolet-blocking sunglasses (protective measure, non-medication) topical ophthalmic antibiotics (broad-spectrum) penicillin ciprofloxacin vancomycin trimethoprim/sulfamethoxazole mitomycin C topical antibiotic (postoperative, four times a day) topical corticosteroid
All Procedures: Gram stain and conjunctival culture (mucus sampling)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up dictated by severity of symptoms and proximity to visual axis; earlier follow-up if topical steroids are used (to assess response and check intraocular pressure); postoperative follow-up until surface re-epithelializes with topical antibiotic qid and steroid tapered per inflammation.
Patient Education: A pterygium is a benign, fibrovascular growth of conjunctiva often related to UV exposure and dry eye. If small and not affecting vision, it can be observed and managed conservatively with frequent preservative‑free artificial tears, UV‑blocking sunglasses, and avoidance of irritants. For active inflammation, a short course of mild topical steroid (e.g., four times daily with a 2–3 week taper) or topical NSAIDs can reduce symptoms. Surgery is considered if the pterygium grows over the visual axis, induces significant astigmatism, causes severe symptoms, or for cosmetic concerns; discuss recurrence risk (commonly up to ~35–40% without adjuncts) and options to lower recurrence (conjunctival autograft, amniotic membrane, mitomycin C with caution). If surgery is performed, expect topical antibiotic until re‑epithelialization and a corticosteroid taper postoperatively. Seek prompt care for rapid growth, atypical appearance, or persistent inflammation to rule out malignancy.


CASE 05 — Recurrent Erosion/Epithelial Basement Membrane Dystrophy
==================================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "intermittent symptoms of bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; episodes consistent with recurrent epithelial erosions on background of epithelial basement membrane (map-dot-fingerprint) dystrophy; visual acuity 20/20 OU.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; visual acuity 20/20 OU; slit‑lamp exam shows fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes.
Symptom Onset: Intermittent (chronic), onset at least prior to presentation — patient is a 32-year-old lifeguard with intermittent bilateral symptoms (recurrent episodes).
Symptom Duration: intermittent (chronic), duration: recurrent episodes over years (chronic/intermittent)
Progression: Recurrent
Characteristics: Map lines, fingerprint lines, and dots


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye rubbing; eyelid traction on loosely adherent epithelium; dryness/insufficient lubrication; contact lens wear (bandage lens tightness)
Alleviating Factors: Application of prophylactic topical antibiotic ointment, cycloplegia, bandage contact lens or self‑retained amniotic membrane grafts, epithelial debridement/scraping, anterior stromal micropuncture, excimer laser PTK, and maintenance lubrication (nonpreserved artificial tears and 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment)
Associated Symptoms: red eye, dryness, irritation, foreign body sensation
Previous Episodes: Intermittent episodes of spontaneous recurrent corneal erosions (history of bilateral recurrent symptoms)
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent symptoms (redness, dryness, irritation, foreign body sensation) with preserved 20/20 vision — mild to moderate impact (discomfort that may interfere with work as a lifeguard, occasional activity limitation)
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: - Family history (autosomal dominant inheritance) - Female sex (female predominance) - History of corneal trauma (for traumatic recurrent erosions; distinguishes causes) - Occupation-related ocular surface exposure (e.g., lifeguard with environmental exposure) - Presence of epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map-dot-fingerprint changes) - Prior recurrent corneal erosions


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen — 20/20 (OU)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes bilaterally
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Fibrovascular overgrowth of the bulbar conjunctiva extending onto the cornea of both eyes (bilateral pterygium-like fibrovascular invasion)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome with epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map‑dot‑fingerprint changes), bilateral, with superficial gray‑white opacities and diffuse epithelial sloughing, no stromal involvement.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens (phakic, no lens pathology)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction or diplopia
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Careful slit-lamp examination reveals superficial gray‑white opacities characteristic of “map” and “dot” changes; diffuse sloughing of the epithelium as a large sheet may be seen in recurrent erosion.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to assess — no fundus/Macula exam information provided in the case.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome, epithelial basement membrane dystrophy (map-dot-fingerprint dystrophy) of both eyes, with intermittent bilateral red eye, dryness, irritation, and foreign body sensation; visual acuity 20/20 OU; slit-lamp exam consistent with EBMD (superficial gray-white map and dot changes) and diffuse sheet-like epithelial sloughing during acute episodes. Plan: acute management to promote epithelial healing and comfort (topical prophylactic antibiotic ointment and pressure patch 24–48 hrs, cycloplegia as needed, consider soft bandage contact lens or self-retained amniotic membrane graft), gentle total epithelial debridement if indicated acutely with avoidance of Bowman’s layer, and for recurrent or visually significant disease consider anterior stromal micropuncture or excimer laser PTK; maintain lubrication with nonpreserved artificial tears 4–8× daily and ointment at bedtime, consider 5% sodium chloride drops/ointment for 3+ months to promote epithelial adherence; careful follow-up until defect resolution and monitor for infectious keratitis.
Primary Diagnosis: Recurrent epithelial erosion syndrome, basement membrane dystrophy type
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent corneal epithelial erosion from epithelial basement membrane dystrophy; Traumatic recurrent corneal erosion; Herpetic keratitis causing recurrent epithelial defects; Dry eye–related epithelial breakdown
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Delayed corneal wound healing; refractive changes; secondary infectious keratitis; “tight lens syndrome” from bandage contact lens; subepithelial scarring (if Bowman’s layer violated)


Plan
All Medications: topical antibiotic ointment; cycloplegia; soft bandage contact lens (therapeutic device); self‑retained amniotic membrane grafts (Prokera, AmbioDisk; biologic grafts); topical antibiotic ointment (post‑procedural); 5% sodium chloride drops; 5% sodium chloride ointment; nonpreserved artificial tears; artificial tear ointment
All Procedures: - Prophylactic topical antibiotic ointment application - Pressure patching (24–48 hours) - Cycloplegia - Soft bandage contact lens placement - Self-retained amniotic membrane graft placement (cryopreserved Prokera or dehydrated AmbioDisk) - Epithelial debridement / scraping (manual epithelial removal) - Total epithelial debridement over entire cornea - Anterior stromal micropuncture (15–25 micropunctures with bent 25‑g needle) - Excimer laser phototherapeutic keratectomy (PTK)
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful follow-up until the epithelial defect has fully healed; monitor closely for infectious keratitis. After resolution, maintain lubrication with non‑preserved artificial tears 4–8×/day and artificial tear ointment at bedtime. Consider 5% sodium chloride drops during the day and 5% sodium chloride ointment at bedtime for ≥3 months as alternative/adjunct to promote epithelial adherence. Consider corneal topography if irregular astigmatism or haze/visual changes.
Patient Education: Explain that epithelial basement membrane (map‑dot‑fingerprint) dystrophy is a common, usually bilateral corneal condition that causes recurrent corneal erosions (sudden pain, tearing, light sensitivity). Reassure that vision is often normal between episodes but irregularity or scarring can affect vision. Acute care aims to promote healing and prevent infection: antibiotic ointment, pressure patch or bandage contact lens, cycloplegic drops for pain, and possible self‑retained amniotic membrane. Report increasing pain, discharge, worsening vision, or fever immediately. Long‑term options to reduce recurrences include regular lubrication with preservative‑free artificial tears 4–8×/day and ointment at night, consider 5% sodium chloride drops by day and ointment at bedtime for several months, and, for frequent recurrences, procedures such as epithelial debridement, anterior stromal micropuncture, or PTK may be recommended. Advise follow‑up until the epithelial defect heals and routine monitoring with corneal topography if visual symptoms persist.
Work Restrictions: Avoid swimming and activities with high risk of ocular trauma; avoid contact lens wear until healed; may require limited duties while symptomatic or until epithelial defect heals (24–48 hr patching/bandage) and reduced duty during recovery.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you develop worsening eye pain, rapidly decreased vision, increasing redness, purulent discharge, or signs of infection; for severe persistent pain not controlled with prescribed medications; or if symptoms suggest corneal ulceration (white spot on cornea) — go to an emergency eye clinic or the nearest emergency department.


CASE 06 — Fuchs’ Corneal Dystrophy
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: worse vision in the morning (diurnal blur) due to corneal edema
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "I awoke from sleep with sudden onset of pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening my eyes."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35-year-old woman with a history of multiple similar episodes in either eye awoke from sleep with sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes; symptoms began upon awakening that morning, with no antecedent trauma, and her mother has had similar episodes.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman awoke from sleep with sudden onset of unilateral pain, foreign body sensation, lacrimation, photophobia, and blurred vision immediately upon opening her eyes. The patient had a previous history of multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye. The patient’s mother has also been affected with similar episodes. She had no history of prior ocular trauma. Visual acuity is 20/400 in the involved eye and 20/20 in the fellow eye. Slit-lamp examination of the involved eye revealed a large, discrete area of epithelial sloughing with “heaped-up” edges and pooling of fluorescein over the epithelial defect; no infiltrate was apparent. Examination of the fellow eye was remarkable for diffuse, superficial gray-white opacities in a “map” and “dot” configuration.
Symptom Onset: Upon awakening / immediately upon opening her eyes
Progression: Slowly progressive
Characteristics: slowly progressive dystrophy with corneal stromal edema, loss of endothelial cell density, corneal guttata, diurnal vision fluctuation (worse in morning), increased corneal thickness on pachymetry


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Morning hours (diurnal variation with worse vision in the morning), activities that induce corneal edema (e.g., eye closure/sleep), advancing endothelial failure (progression of disease)
Alleviating Factors: - Hypertonic sodium chloride (e.g., NaCl 5%) drops (especially upon awakening) - Hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime - Using a hair dryer on lowest setting directed at the cornea at arm’s length for several minutes
Associated Symptoms: corneal pain; foreign body sensation; tearing (lacrimation); photophobia; blurred vision; diurnal vision fluctuation (worse in morning); morning blur
Previous Episodes: Multiple similar episodes occurring in either eye
Impact on Daily Activities: May experience morning visual blurring that interferes with morning activities; progressive vision decline may eventually limit daily tasks, particularly reading/driving, unless treated.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Mother
Risk Factors: Age (older age), female sex, positive family history/genetic predisposition, endothelial cell loss (corneal endothelial dysfunction), corneal guttata/pachymetry changes (thicker cornea), prior ocular surgery/trauma (as exacerbating factor)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (manifest distance visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy with corneal stromal and epithelial edema, guttata, increased pachymetry (thickened cornea), and features of bullous keratopathy in advanced cases.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris without abnormalities detected
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open/normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Cataract OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (about 3–4 mm)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Large number of central corneal guttata with mild stromal edema, worse centrally and noted on pachymetry; Descemet changes consistent with Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy OU, no active epithelial defect, no stromal infiltrate.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula: flat, normal and with no edema or hemorrhage


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 1. Fuchs’ endothelial corneal dystrophy OU — slowly progressive endothelial cell loss with corneal guttata and episodic stromal/epithelial edema producing morning blur; increased central corneal thickness on pachymetry and reduced endothelial cell count on specular microscopy expected. 2. Cataract OU contributing to decreased visual potential; consider combined surgery planning when corneal transplantation indicated. 3. Conservative measures instituted for mild edema: hypertonic NaCl 5% drops (and bedtime ointment) and temporary corneal dehydration techniques; monitor for progression. 4. Surgical indications when vision functionally impaired despite medical therapy: endothelial transplantation (DSAEK or DMEK preferred for isolated endothelial failure) versus PKP if there is significant corneal scarring; counsel regarding risks, refractive shifts (DSAEK hyperopic shift ~1.0–1.5 D), and perioperative planning including combined cataract extraction/IOL when lens opacity present. 5. Postoperative plan: topical antibiotics and prolonged steroid taper (6–12 months), minimize preservative exposure, supine positioning after endothelial grafts until bubble resolves, and suture/astigmatism management after PKP with topography-guided adjustments and visual rehabilitation with spectacles or RGP as needed.
Primary Diagnosis: Fuchs’ corneal dystrophy OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Cataract OU
Differential Diagnosis: Recurrent corneal epithelial erosion syndrome (secondary to map‑dot‑fingerprint dystrophy); Map‑dot‑fingerprint (epithelial basement membrane) dystrophy; Traumatic corneal abrasion; Other anterior basement membrane dystrophies (Meesmann’s, Reis–Bücklers)
Laterality: OU


Plan
All Medications: hypertonic sodium chloride ophthalmic solution (NaCl 5% drops), hypertonic sodium chloride ointment at bedtime, topical antibiotic drops, topical corticosteroid drops, nonpreserved artificial tears
All Procedures: DSAEK; DMEK; penetrating keratoplasty (PKP); corneal transplantation; cataract extraction with intraocular lens (IOL) placement; simultaneous cataract extraction and IOL placement; suturing techniques (16‑bite interrupted, running, combined running–interrupted); selective suture lysis; running suture adjustment; astigmatic keratotomy; compression sutures; wedge resection; repeat PKP; LASIK
Follow-Up Instructions: - Postoperative topical antibiotic and corticosteroid drops; discontinue antibiotics after surface re-epithelialization and taper steroids slowly over 6–12 months. - Use ointments and/or nonpreserved artificial tears as needed to rehabilitate the ocular surface. - Minimize number and frequency of preserved topical drops to avoid surface toxicity. - After DSAEK/DMEK: remain supine until the air bubble is absorbed to facilitate graft attachment. - After PKP: manage postoperative astigmatism with selective suture lysis and/or running suture adjustment guided by topography; prescribe glasses or RGP lenses as appropriate. - Consider surgical interventions for high astigmatism (astigmatic keratotomy, compression sutures, wedge resection, or repeat PKP) or LASIK only after all sutures removed and wound secure.
Patient Education: In early Fuchs’ dystrophy you may be asymptomatic or notice “morning blur” from corneal swelling; measures to reduce symptoms include using hypertonic sodium chloride drops (e.g., 5% NaCl) on awakening and hypertonic ointment at bedtime, and simple dehydration with a hair dryer on the lowest setting held at arm’s length for several minutes. As disease progresses, these measures may become inadequate and endothelial transplantation (DSAEK or DMEK) or, if scarring is present, penetrating keratoplasty may be required. Postoperatively you’ll use topical antibiotics until the surface heals and corticosteroid drops tapered over 6–12 months; after endothelial grafting you must remain supine until the air bubble resolves to allow graft attachment.


CASE 07 — Keratoconus
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: slowly progressive loss of vision (left greater than right)
DURATION: slowly progressive (over years)
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 60-year-old woman with slowly progressive loss of vision, worse in the left than right eye; vision initially worse on awakening and cleared during the day but more recently remained poor throughout the day.
History Narrative: A 60-year-old woman presented with a history of slowly progressive loss of vision in the left greater than the right eye. Initially, her vision was worse upon awakening and gradually cleared as the day went on; more recently, however, her vision remained poor throughout the day.
Symptom Onset: Slowly progressive; initially worse upon awakening and gradually cleared during the day, more recently remained poor throughout the day.
Symptom Duration: Slowly progressive (over years)
Progression: May progress (left eye may eventually show changes); currently mild keratoconus with potential to worsen over time
Characteristics: Progressive, bilateral (asymmetric) noninflammatory corneal ectasia with central thinning, irregular astigmatism, iron deposition in epithelium, breaks in Bowman's membrane, possible conical protrusion and scarring.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Vision worse upon awakening; eyelid closure/sleep-related increased corneal edema.
Alleviating Factors: Vision improved gradually as the day went on (worse on awakening, cleared during the day)
Associated Symptoms: Progressive decreased vision (worse upon awakening initially), monocular diplopia/ghosting from irregular astigmatism, glare/halos, and contact lens intolerance.
Prior Treatments: Rigid gas permeable contact lenses
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate; able to function with rigid gas-permeable lens in right eye (20/30) but left eye poor vision and reduced acuity impacting daily activities, may limit tasks requiring binocular vision or fine detail.
Risk Factors: Eye rubbing; atopy/allergic eye disease; family history/genetic predisposition; younger age at onset; male sex; systemic connective tissue disorders (e.g., Down syndrome, Marfan); contact lens wear (poorly fitting)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Counting fingers at 4 feet
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: Counting fingers at 4 feet
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity with correction (rigid gas permeable lenses)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet, without injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Thinning and ectasia consistent with keratoconus; right eye mild keratoconus, left eye greater involvement with corneal ectasia and possible scarring.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and deep in both eyes
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic cataract, moderate (bilateral)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive (no anisocoria noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal to light in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restriction or diplopia reported
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Central microcystic epithelial edema and stromal edema with folds in Descemet’s membrane, with corneal guttatae and endothelial pigmentation most prominent centrally; anterior chamber deep and quiet; iris normal; moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts bilaterally; hazy view of posterior poles.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, intact neuroretinal rim, no optic disc edema or hemorrhage
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal through a hazy view
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (peripheral retina without lesions)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 60-year-old woman with bilateral keratoconus, right eye mild and functioning well with rigid gas-permeable contact lens (achieving 20/30), left eye more advanced with decreased vision; not correctable with glasses or soft contacts. Plan: continue rigid gas-permeable lens management for right eye, monitor left eye for progression and consider corneal collagen cross-linking to slow progression if indicated; surgical options (DALK or PKP, or intracorneal ring segments) reserved for contact lens intolerance, progressive thinning/scarring, or inadequate vision with hard contacts. Follow with serial corneal topography/pachymetry and routine contact lens fitting/rehabilitation.
Primary Diagnosis: Keratoconus in the right eye
Differential Diagnosis: Fuchs endothelial corneal dystrophy; Pseudophakic/aphakic bullous keratopathy; Posterior polymorphous dystrophy; Corneal hydrops from keratoconus
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: residual astigmatism, myopia, glaucoma, cataract, mydriasis, risk of graft rejection


Plan
All Procedures: glasses; soft contact lenses; rigid gas permeable contact lenses; scleral lenses; specialty hybrid contact lenses; ultraviolet light/riboflavin corneal collagen cross-linking; intracorneal ring segments (INTACS); penetrating keratoplasty (PKP); deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK); Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); Descemet’s membrane endothelial keratoplasty (DMEK)
Follow-Up Instructions: This patient has done well in a rigid gas permeable contact lens for the right eye. The left eye may eventually show changes consistent with keratoconus.
Patient Education: Keratoconus is a noninflammatory thinning and bulging of the cornea that usually affects both eyes but often unequally. It causes irregular astigmatism and decreased vision that may be worse on waking. Mild disease is managed with glasses, soft lenses, or rigid gas-permeable/scleral/specialty lenses; your vision may improve markedly with a properly fitted rigid lens. Progressive disease can be slowed with corneal collagen cross-linking. If contact lenses no longer provide good vision or scarring develops, surgical options include intracorneal ring segments, deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty (DALK), or penetrating keratoplasty (PKP). Regular follow-up with corneal topography and pachymetry is recommended to monitor progression. If you have new pain, sudden vision loss, or intolerance of contacts, contact your eye doctor promptly.


CASE 08 — Microbial Keratitis
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year
DURATION: Over the past year
LATERALITY: Left eye
PATIENT QUOTES: "I don't see clearly."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 19-year-old man with a 9‑year history of glasses and contact lens wear presents with gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year. Despite a recent change in his prescription and new contact lenses, he does not “see clearly.” He reports his vision was clear and correctable to 20/20 as a child and young adult until last year. He denies trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection. He has seasonal allergies. He is wearing contact lenses. Corrected visual acuity with contacts is 20/50 OD and 20/25 OS; manifest refraction OD –2.00 –7.50 ×60 corrects to 20/100, manifest refraction OS –2.25 –1.00 ×28 corrects to 20/40. Rigid gas permeable contact lens over‑refraction corrects the right eye to 20/30.
History Narrative: A 19-year-old man presents with complaints of gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year. He has worn glasses and contacts for 9 years, and despite a recent change in his prescription and new contact lenses, he does not “see clearly.” He denies any trauma, surgery, or recent eye infection. He reports his vision in both eyes was clear as a child and young adult and until last year was correctable to 20/20. His past medical history is significant for seasonal allergies. He reports allergies to penicillin and uses acetaminophen occasionally. He denies any family history of eye disease. The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems. He is wearing contact lenses.
Symptom Onset: gradual decreased vision in his right eye over the past year
Symptom Duration: over the past year
Characteristics: Central, dense, gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect and partial re-epithelialization; may have central thinning and risk of perforation; associated pain, decreased vision, and inflammatory stromal opacity.


Subjective (cont.)

Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision, pain (implied with corneal ulcer), photophobia, tearing, foreign body sensation, redness, contact lens wear.
Previous Episodes: Denies any recent eye infection.
Systemic Symptoms: The patient is healthy with a negative review of systems.
Risk Factors: Previous contact lens wear; history of trauma; compromised ocular surface (dry eye/tear dysfunction); atopy/seasonal allergies; eye rubbing.

Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg

Use topical steroids only cautiously after adequate antimicrobial coverage and clinical improvement.
Primary Diagnosis: Streptococcal corneal ulcer, left eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Allergic rhinitis (seasonal); Contact lens wear; Penicillin allergy
Differential Diagnosis: Bacterial keratitis (e.g., Streptococcal keratitis); Acanthamoeba keratitis; Fungal keratitis; Herpetic (HSV) keratitis
Severity: Central (moderate)
Laterality: Left eye
Complications: - Corneal scarring - Persisting decreased vision - Corneal thinning and ectasia - Corneal perforation and globe collapse - Need for therapeutic penetrating keratoplasty or graft failure - Risk of graft reinfection or poor graft survival - Possible need for glue or conjunctival/surgical repair complications

All Medications: acetaminophen penicillin fortified antibiotics fluoroquinolones aminoglycosides cephalosporins penicillins synthetic penicillins erythromycin bacitracin polymyxin chloramphenicol vancomycin tetracycline sulfonamides rifampin moxifloxacin prednisolone sodium phosphate doxycycline
All Procedures: diagnostic stains, culture, sensitivities, topical fortified antibiotics, topical fluoroquinolones, subconjunctival antibiotic injection, hospitalization for IV antibiotics, topical corticosteroid drops (adjunctive), cycloplegics, doxycycline (enzyme inhibitor), collagen shields, bandage contact lenses, self‑retained amniotic grafts, corneal glue, penetrating keratoplasty, deep anterior lamellar keratoplasty, conjunctival flap (not indicated), phototherapeutic keratectomy, rigid gas permeable and scleral contact lens fitting
Follow-Up Instructions: Once infection is successfully treated, evaluate and correct risk factors for recurrence (stop or modify contact lens wear, treat lid/lacrimal/conjunctival dysfunction); consider rehabilitation for scarring (rigid gas-permeable or scleral lenses, PTK, deep anterior lamellar or penetrating keratoplasty) as indicated.
Patient Education: Avoid contact lens wear until the infection is fully treated and follow all medication instructions exactly. Report any worsening pain, redness, light sensitivity, or decreased vision immediately. Complete the full course of prescribed topical antibiotics and attend all follow-up visits so healing can be monitored. Once healed, address and modify risk factors (proper contact lens hygiene, reduce eye rubbing, treat dry eye or lid disease) to reduce recurrence; discuss rehabilitation options (rigid gas-permeable or scleral lenses, possible corneal procedures) if scarring impairs vision.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care for worsening pain, sudden vision loss, increasing redness, discharge, or any signs of corneal perforation (sudden decrease in eye size/shape or aqueous leakage). If perforation or impending perforation suspected, avoid eye rubbing and cover the eye with a protective shield; do not apply pressure. Present to emergency department or corneal specialist urgently; bring current antibiotic bottles and contact lens case.


CASE 09 — Keratoconjunctivitis Sicca—Dry Eye
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in her left eye for the previous 48 hours
DURATION: 48 hours
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in my left eye for the past 48 hours."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 39-year-old woman with contact lens wear presenting with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and purulent discharge in the left eye; VA 20/20 OD and CF 3′ OS at presentation.
History Narrative: A 39-year-old woman with a history of contact lens wear presented with 48 hours of decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, and discharge in her left eye. Visual acuity was 20/20 OD and count fingers at 3 feet OS. Right eye exam was unremarkable. Left external exam showed mild lid and conjunctival edema, a papillary conjunctival reaction, and purulent discharge. The corneal stroma had a central, dense, gray-white, necrotic-appearing infiltrate with loss of the overlying epithelium; edges were indistinct and extended beyond the stromal opacity. The anterior chamber had 2+ cell and flare and a 1-mm hypopyon. Iris, lens, and retinal exams were unremarkable. Subsequent exams: at 4 days vision 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS with minimal lid/conjunctival edema, scant purulent discharge, a condensing gray-white corneal opacity with defined borders and epithelial healing at the edges; at 11 days vision 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS with further condensation of the stromal opacity and continued re-epithelialization; at 1 month vision 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS with a central corneal stromal opacity.
Symptom Onset: 48 hours
Symptom Duration: 48 hours
Progression: Improved (vision OS from CF to 20/50 over one month)
Characteristics: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency (neurotrophic keratopathy from diabetes) with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Contact lens wear, decreased blink rate, floppy eyelids, diabetes mellitus–related neurotrophic keratopathy
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, purulent discharge
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — decreased vision, pain, photophobia, redness, discharge and reduced OS acuity (CF → 20/50) limiting visual tasks and comfort.
Risk Factors: Contact lens wear; ocular surface disease (neurotrophic keratopathy from diabetes mellitus); decreased blink rate; floppy eyelids; contact lens hygiene/overnight wear; ocular trauma (general risk factor)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 82 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: CF 3'
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: mild lid edema
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival edema and purulent discharge
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Left cornea: central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect initially; later condensing gray-white stromal opacity with re-epithelialization and residual central stromal scar (opacity)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ cell and flare, 1-mm hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open to scleral spur (wide)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restriction or diplopia noted
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Left eye: mild lid and conjunctival edema, papillary conjunctival reaction, purulent discharge; central dense gray-white necrotic-appearing stromal infiltrate with overlying epithelial defect, indistinct edges extending beyond stromal opacity; anterior chamber with 2+ cell and flare and 1-mm hypopyon. Right eye: unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal abnormalities detected)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency from diabetic neurotrophic keratopathy, associated with delayed tear clearance from decreased blink rate and floppy eyelids.
Primary Diagnosis: Keratoconjunctivitis sicca due to aqueous tear deficiency secondary to diabetes mellitus–induced neurotrophic keratitis, associated with delayed tear clearance from reduced blink rate and floppy eyelids
Secondary Diagnoses: Neurotrophic keratopathy secondary to diabetes mellitus; Delayed tear clearance from decreased blink rate and floppy eyelids
Differential Diagnosis: Bacterial keratitis (contact lens–related bacterial corneal ulcer); Fungal keratitis; Acanthamoeba keratitis; Neurotrophic keratitis (noninfectious)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: Corneal scarring (central stromal opacity) leading to reduced vision


Plan
All Medications: proparacaine; Fluress (fluorescein 0.25%); artificial tears (preservative-free); ophthalmic gels and ointments; Lacrisert; autologous serum eye drops; pilocarpine; cevimeline; topical cyclosporine; corticosteroid eye drops/ointments (prednisolone, fluorometholone, loteprednol etabonate); oral tetracyclines; oral or topical macrolides; topical antibiotics (as above)
All Procedures: Schirmer test (fluorescein clearance test with Schirmer strips) Corneal ulcer scraping for culture (corneal specimen collection and plating) Contact lens and contact lens case/solution culture Fluorescein staining and tear breakup time testing Ocular surface staining with rose bengal and lissamine green Punctal occlusion (punctal plugs or thermal cautery) Tarsorrhaphy or botulinum toxin–induced ptosis Application of scleral or bandage contact lenses (specialty contact lenses) Placement of amniotic membrane (self-retained) Warm compresses/thermal pulsation (e.g., LipiFlow) Intense-pulse light therapy and lid massage
Patient Education: Use preservative‑free artificial tears frequently; apply ophthalmic gels/ointments at night. Practice lid hygiene and warm compresses to improve oil flow. Blink more often and consider protective measures (goggles/eye shields, avoiding drafts). For persistent symptoms, treatments include punctal plugs or cautery, topical cyclosporine, short course topical steroids for inflammation, oral tetracyclines or macrolides if indicated, tear‑stimulating pills (pilocarpine/cevimeline) for severe aqueous deficiency, and specialty options such as autologous serum drops, scleral/contact bandage lenses, or amniotic membrane. Seek prompt follow‑up for worsening vision, pain, or redness.


CASE 10 — Postsurgical Corneal Edema
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: ocular irritation in both eyes
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, and redness, more in his right eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 56-year-old man with diabetes mellitus referred for a second opinion for right-eye–predominant ocular irritation after cataract surgery, complaining of burning, foreign body sensation, sandy-gritty feeling, redness worse in the morning and late day, with reduced blink rate, worsened reading/driving, preserved emotional lacrimation, sleeps on right side; exam notable for reduced corneal sensitivity, tear breakup time <2s, low tear meniscus, diffuse conjunctival injection, positive fluorescein and rose bengal staining (more right), delayed fluorescein clearance, and postoperative decreased vision in the operated eye (initial BCVA 20/40 OD, 20/20 OS) from corneal edema attributed to preexisting low endothelial cell density.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old man with diabetes mellitus presented with bilateral ocular irritation and was referred for a second opinion by an ophthalmologist for presumed unstable ocular surface/dry eye in the right eye. He complained of burning, foreign body sensation, sandy‑gritty feeling, and redness, worse in the right eye, with symptoms worse in the morning and later in the day, impairing reading and driving. He had preserved emotional lacrimation and reported sleeping on his stomach, favoring his right side. On history he had reduced blink rate. Best-corrected visual acuity was 20/40 OD and 20/20 OS. External exam showed no rosacea or palpable preauricular nodes; lid position and relationship to the globe were normal with loose, floppy lid tension (graded 2+ OD, 1+ OS). Slit-lamp exam showed clear, easily expressible meibomian secretions, low tear meniscus height OU, diffusely injected tarsal and bulbar conjunctiva with mixed papillary and follicular response, markedly reduced ocular sensitivity by Cochet–Bonnet esthesiometry, tear breakup time <2 seconds OU, fluorescein and rose bengal staining with superficial punctate staining and positive rose bengal over the exposure zone (more prominent OD). Intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg OD and 16 mm Hg OS. Fluorescein clearance test showed marked delayed dye clearance and decreased tear secretion in both eyes. Lens and fundus were unremarkable. After further evaluation, specular microscopy of the fellow eye revealed low endothelial cell density without guttata; the unexpected postoperative corneal edema in the operated eye was attributed to preexisting low endothelial density of undetermined etiology. He was managed with topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline four times daily for one week, with complete resolution of corneal edema and final BCVA 20/20.
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Postsurgical corneal edema following cataract surgery, often transient but may persist; risk factors include low endothelial cell density, Fuchs’ dystrophy, complicated surgery; presents with decreased vision (e.g., 20/40 → 20/20 after treatment), corneal haze/stromal swelling; managed medically (topical steroids, hypertonic saline, aqueous suppressants) or surgically (PK, DSAEK, DMEK)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sleeping on his stomach preferring his right side; reduced blink rate; low tear meniscus/poor tear film (dry eye); diabetes mellitus
Associated Symptoms: burning; foreign body sensation; sandy-gritty feeling; redness; worse in morning and late day; reduced blink rate; inability to read or drive comfortably; preserved emotional lacrimation
Prior Treatments: Topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline solution four times a day for 1 week
Impact on Daily Activities: Unable to read or drive comfortably; significant difficulty with reading and driving.
Risk Factors: preexisting low endothelial cell density; Fuchs’ endothelial dystrophy (corneal guttata); complicated surgery (surgical trauma, prolonged endothelial exposure during phacoemulsification, Descemet membrane detachment, vitreous prolapse, malpositioned IOL, wound leaks/hypotony, thermal injury); diabetes mellitus; past acute IOP elevation (e.g., acute angle-closure glaucoma); toxicity from intraocular/medication contaminants (e.g., intracameral epinephrine with sodium bisulfite, preservative-containing lidocaine, contaminated instruments); excessive postoperative inflammation; postoperative IOP elevation; long-term topical ophthalmic medication use (preservative-related endothelial damage)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Both tarsal and bulbar conjunctivae were diffusely injected
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No palpable preauricular nodes
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Corneal edema (postsurgical), resolved with treatment; history notable for low endothelial cell density without guttata.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Meibomian secretion clear and easily expressible; low tear meniscus height upper and lower lids OU; diffusely injected tarsal and bulbar conjunctiva with mixed papillary and follicular response; markedly reduced ocular sensitivity; tear breakup time <2 seconds OU; superficial punctate staining with positive rose bengal over exposure zone (more OD); lens and fundus unremarkable OU
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal appearing optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 56-year-old man with postoperative corneal edema of the operated eye attributed to preexisting low corneal endothelial cell density (specifically low endothelial cell count without guttata) likely related to diabetes; fellow eye exam confirmed low endothelial cell density. Managed medically with topical corticosteroids and hypertonic saline, resulting in complete resolution of edema and restoration of BCVA to 20/20.
