Continued monitoring for endothelial dysfunction and consideration of endothelial keratoplasty if edema recurs or fails to respond to medical therapy.
Primary Diagnosis: Postsurgical corneal edema
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Preexisting endothelial disease/dysfunction (e.g., Fuchs’ or low endothelial cell density); Surgical trauma to endothelium/Descemet detachment; Toxic/endothelial chemical injury (intracameral agents, contaminants); Excessive postoperative inflammation or elevated IOP
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: wound leak, flat anterior chamber, iris incarceration in the wound, IOP elevation, primary endothelial failure, endophthalmitis, epithelial defect, graft down-growth


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% (topical corticosteroid), topical hypertonic 5% sodium chloride (solution or ointment), topical ophthalmic aqueous suppressants (antiglaucoma medications), systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, preservative-free epinephrine (intracameral), preservative-free lidocaine (intracameral), antibiotics (topical/subconjunctival), cyclosporin A (systemic immunotherapy)
All Procedures: Topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone acetate 1% qid) Topical hypertonic saline solution (5% sodium chloride qid)
Patient Education: Postoperative corneal edema is often temporary and usually improves with time and appropriate treatment. Causes include preexisting endothelial disease (like low endothelial cell density or Fuchs’ dystrophy), surgical trauma, medication toxicity, inflammation, or postoperative IOP elevation. Early medical management focuses on controlling inflammation (frequent topical corticosteroids such as prednisolone acetate 1%), lowering IOP if elevated (topical or systemic agents), and reducing corneal swelling with topical hypertonic saline (5% sodium chloride solution or ointment). A therapeutic soft contact lens may help comfort and vision in mild cases. If edema persists despite medical therapy, surgical options include endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK or DMEK) or, less commonly now, penetrating keratoplasty; decisions are typically deferred 2–3 months to allow for possible spontaneous recovery. Follow all medication instructions, report worsening vision, pain, redness, or discharge promptly, and attend scheduled follow-up visits for monitoring.
Referrals: Referred by an ophthalmologist for a second opinion.


CASE 11 — Dellen
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: right eye
PATIENT QUOTES: "decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year." "persistent blurred vision without major discomfort."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55‑year‑old man with 1‑year history of decreased vision (worse at night) in both eyes, with well‑controlled diabetes mellitus for 10 years, presenting for evaluation; visual acuity 20/50 OD and 20/100 OS, and cataract diagnosed OU with recommendation for left eye cataract surgery. Postoperatively on day 1 after uncomplicated left phacoemulsification via temporal clear corneal incision, he had persistent blurred vision and slit‑lamp exam showed diffuse central and incision‑site corneal edema OS with visual acuity 20/100 and IOP 18 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man with a 10-year history of well-controlled diabetes mellitus presented with a 1-year history of decreased vision, worse at night, in both eyes. Past ocular and family histories were unremarkable. He was found to have bilateral moderate nuclear sclerotic cataracts (left worse than right) with best corrected visual acuity 20/50 OD and 20/100 OS, normal pupils and motility, IOPs 12 mm Hg OD and 13 mm Hg OS, no visual field loss by confrontation, and no diabetic retinopathy on fundus exam. Potential acuity testing showed 20/25 in each eye. A diagnosis of bilateral cataract was made and left eye cataract surgery was recommended. He underwent uncomplicated temporal clear corneal phacoemulsification with posterior chamber acrylic IOL implantation in the left eye. On postoperative day one his visual acuity was 20/100, IOP 18 mm Hg, and slit-lamp exam showed diffuse central and incision-site corneal edema with persistent blurred vision.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Characteristics: - Area of peripheral, localized corneal thinning due to desiccation of epithelial and subepithelial tissues - Occurs adjacent to areas of limbal elevation (e.g., filtering blebs, dermoids, conjunctival elevation) - Results from poor tear coverage/tear film interruption over a specific area - Typically well-circumscribed peripheral lesion - Noninfiltrated, relatively quiet (noninflammatory) process - Associated with dry eye and may be accompanied by pooling of fluorescein in the area


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Poor tear coverage from dry eye and lid disease (meibomian gland dysfunction/blepharitis/meibomitis), adjacent limbal elevation (e.g., filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation/chemosis), postoperative conjunctival elevation, tear film instability, and any mass disrupting tear distribution (e.g., pyogenic granuloma)
Alleviating Factors: lubrication with preservative-free tears, ophthalmic ointment, punctal occlusion, oral doxycycline and vitamin C (for persistent cases), bandage contact lens or self‑retained amniotic membrane graft, heat compresses and meibomian gland massage
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision (especially at night), persistent blurred vision, corneal edema, and minimal discomfort
Impact on Daily Activities: Decreased vision (especially at night) in both eyes for 1 year causing difficulty with nighttime activities; otherwise no major discomfort—moderate impact on daily activities, particularly nocturnal tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Inadequate tear distribution/dry eye, lid disease (meibomitis/blepharitis), meibomian gland dysfunction, presence of limbal elevation (e.g., filtering bleb, dermoid, conjunctival elevation), recent ocular surgery (e.g., conjunctival or scleral surgery), tear film instability, diabetes mellitus (predisposing to ocular surface disease).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J2+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen (best corrected)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Unremarkable — no lid or lash abnormality noted
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Filter­ing bleb adjacent to peripheral corneal dellen (conjunctival elevation from filtering bleb)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Dellen (peripheral localized corneal thinning adjacent to limbal elevation)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: moderate nuclear sclerosis in both eyes, left worse than right


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive (normal size)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: unremarkable
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Equal and reactive, no RAPD
Pupils – Other: unremarkable
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no motility restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Diffuse corneal edema in the central cornea and around the temporal clear corneal incision; otherwise slit-lamp exam normal (moderate nuclear sclerosis of both crystalline lenses, left worse than right).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, optic nerve clinically normal (no evidence of optic neuropathy).
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular pathology; macula appeared normal (no diabetic retinopathy)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: No vascular abnormalities detected (no diabetic retinopathy; vessels within normal limits)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal pathology; fundus exam showed no diabetic retinopathy in the periphery.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Delle in right eye due to dry eye in the setting of a filtering bleb.
Primary Diagnosis: Delle in right eye due to dry eye in the setting of a filtering bleb
Secondary Diagnoses: Diabetes mellitus (well-controlled)
Differential Diagnosis: Dellen secondary to localized tear-film disruption (adjacent to conjunctival/limbal elevation); Peripheral ulcerative keratitis from autoimmune/inflammatory disease (eg, rheumatoid arthritis, Wegener’s); Terrien’s marginal degeneration (or other peripheral degenerations such as pellucid marginal degeneration)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: corneal thinning (dellen) leading to potential keratolysis and risk of progressive corneal thinning/nonhealing requiring bandage contact lens, amniotic membrane graft, tarsorrhaphy, or surgical intervention (possible removal of offending conjunctival elevation)


Plan
All Medications: preservative-free artificial tears; bland ophthalmic lubricating ointment; oral doxycycline; vitamin C; bandage contact lens (therapeutic contact lens); self-retained amniotic membrane graft; heat compresses (lid hygiene); LipiFlow thermal pulsation system (device)
All Procedures: phacoemulsification via temporal clear corneal incision with implantation of a posterior chamber acrylic intraocular lens (left eye)
Follow-Up Instructions: Patients should be observed and treated long-term for signs and symptoms of dry eye contributing to dellen formation, with periodic follow-up to monitor healing and corneal stability and to adjust therapy as needed.
Patient Education: Dellen are localized areas of corneal thinning caused by poor tear coverage, often next to a raised area at the limbus (e.g., filtering bleb, conjunctival mass). They are not the same as inflammatory ulcers but need treatment to prevent progression. Treatment focuses on improving tear protection and lid hygiene: use preservative‑free artificial tears frequently, apply lubricating ointment at bedtime, consider punctual occlusion if directed, warm compresses and gentle lid massage for meibomian gland dysfunction, and treat blepharitis/meibomitis as instructed. If healing is slow, your doctor may add a bandage contact lens, oral doxycycline and vitamin C, or place an amniotic membrane graft; rarely a temporary tarsorrhaphy or removal of an offending mass is needed. Return promptly for worsening pain, increasing redness, a new white spot, or vision loss. Keep long‑term follow‑up to manage dry eye and prevent recurrence.


CASE 12 — Graft Rejection Following Penetrating Keratoplasty
============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision, red eye, photophobia, and foreign body sensation two weeks after surgery
LATERALITY: OD
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with a history of glaucoma treated with multiple filtration procedures who developed elevated intraocular pressure on maximal medical therapy leading to a repeat trabeculectomy with a new flap at the 7 o’clock position; two weeks postoperatively he was found to have a moderately elevated filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock and peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to the bleb with fluorescein pooling and a small area of epithelial cell loss.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man with a history of glaucoma that required multiple filtration procedures in the right eye was found to have elevated intraocular pressure on maximal medical therapy. A repeat trabeculectomy was performed with the flap established at the 7 o’clock position to avoid the prior surgical site, resulting in a moderately elevated filtering bleb extending from the 5 to 8 o’clock positions. Two weeks after surgery, peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to the filtering bleb was noted; fluorescein instillation revealed pooling with a small area of epithelial cell loss.
Symptom Onset: Two weeks after surgery
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Characteristics: - Ciliary injection, keratic precipitates, stromal edema, anterior chamber cell and flare; may include superficial and deep vascularization or a linear Khodadoust’s line in advanced cases.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Broken sutures - Suture abscess - Stromal vascularization - Large-diameter grafts - Eccentric grafts - Repeat grafts - Any postoperative inflammation (iritis, mild trauma, epithelial or subepithelial rejection) - Perilimbal conjunctival removal / exposed sclera (leading to localized dryness)
Alleviating Factors: Rehydration of the area with viscous lubricating agents (e.g., 1% methylcellulose or ophthalmic ointment) and patching the eye for 15–30 minutes leading to stromal thickening and resolution of the delle
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, decreased vision
Prior Treatments: Repeat trabeculectomy (filtration surgery) with prior multiple filtration procedures; maximal medical therapy for glaucoma (topical IOP-lowering medications).
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — patient experiences decreased vision, photophobia, foreign body sensation requiring prompt evaluation and frequent treatment, likely interfering with daily activities until resolved.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Stromal vascularization - Large-diameter grafts - Eccentric grafts - Repeat grafts - Any postoperative inflammation (e.g., iritis, trauma, epithelial or subepithelial rejection, broken sutures)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JNB (Jäger no. 8)
Visual Acuity – Method: Hand motions (HM)
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (Goldmann applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Moderately injected with a filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock; peripheral conjunctival elevation adjacent to a dellen with exposed/thinned perilimbal area.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: within normal limits
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Endothelial corneal graft rejection with stromal edema and keratic precipitates; broken suture with suture abscess; peripheral corneal thinning (dellen) adjacent to filtering bleb.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Cell and flare present (anterior chamber reaction)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Brisk and reactive, equal in size (round)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction or pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: peripheral corneal thinning adjacent to an elevated filtering bleb from 5 to 8 o’clock with pooling of fluorescein and a small area of epithelial cell loss; broken suture with suture abscess; keratic precipitates, stromal edema, ciliary injection and anterior chamber cell/flare consistent with endothelial graft rejection. Left eye: keratoconus.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal-looking macula


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: OD: Broken suture with suture abscess and endothelial corneal graft rejection — patient presents with signs consistent with endothelial rejection (ciliary injection, keratic precipitates, stromal edema, anterior chamber cell/flare) likely precipitated by broken suture/abscess; urgent aggressive topical corticosteroids +/- cycloplegic and antibiotics for suture abscess with consideration of culture; remove broken suture at slit lamp; if severe or recalcitrant consider systemic or periocular steroids. OS: Keratoconus.
Primary Diagnosis: Endothelial corneal graft rejection
Secondary Diagnoses: (1) Broken suture with suture abscess (OD). (2) Keratoconus (OS).
Differential Diagnosis: Broken suture with suture abscess; Endothelial corneal graft rejection; Iritis (post-PK anterior uveitis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: Broken suture with suture abscess; endothelial corneal graft rejection; corneal edema; stromal vascularization; potential graft failure requiring repeat PK or DSAEK; decreased vision; photophobia; foreign body sensation; risk of permanent endothelial damage.


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1%; methylcellulose 1% (ophthalmic lubricating solution); ophthalmic lubricating ointment; Polytrim; bacitracin ointment; topical fluoroquinolone (e.g., ciprofloxacin); scopolamine 0.25%; systemic prednisone (e.g., 80 mg PO daily); triamcinolone (40 mg/mL) subconjunctival/transseptal; cyclosporin A
All Procedures: trabeculectomy; penetrating keratoplasty (PK); suture removal at the slit lamp; corneal culture (if suture abscess present); topical antibiotic therapy (e.g., Polytrim, bacitracin, fluoroquinolone drops); topical corticosteroid therapy (e.g., prednisolone acetate 1%); cycloplegic agent (e.g., scopolamine 0.25%); systemic corticosteroids (e.g., oral prednisone); subconjunctival/transseptal steroid injection (e.g., triamcinolone 40 mg/mL); repeat penetrating keratoplasty (repeat PK); Descemet’s stripping automated endothelial keratoplasty (DSAEK); perioperative prophylactic systemic immunosuppression (e.g., systemic steroids or cyclosporin A)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 2–4 days for severe endothelial rejection or weekly for less severe cases until rejection is controlled; then decrease visit frequency as steroids are tapered and proceed with visual rehabilitation (suture lysis, glasses, or rigid contact lens fitting as appropriate).
Patient Education: Patients must be educated to seek evaluation within 24 hours of any new symptom of photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, or decreased vision.
Emergency Instructions: Seek evaluation within 24 hours for any new photophobia, foreign body sensation, red eye, or decreased vision.


CASE 13 — Blepharitis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in his right eye for 1 week
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 30-year-old man with a history of keratoconus and right eye penetrating keratoplasty 5 months prior presented with 1 week of right eye redness, photophobia, and blurred vision. One month earlier his right eye VA was 20/60 improving to 20/30 with pinhole. On current exam the right eye VA was 20/400 (no pinhole improvement), IOP 17 mm Hg OU, with 2+ ciliary flush, a broken exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at the 11 o’clock meridian of the graft, marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft, a line of keratic precipitates at the edge of edema, and 1+ cell and flare in the anterior chamber. The left eye was quiet with contact lens wear and known central corneal thinning and mild protrusion from keratoconus. He also had symptoms and exam findings of dry eye with inferior punctate keratopathy, diminished tear lake, and a positive Schirmer’s test.
History Narrative: A 30-year-old man presented with complaints of redness, photophobia, and blurred vision in his right eye for 1 week. Past ocular history included keratoconus, for which he had undergone penetrating keratoplasty (PK) in the right eye 5 months earlier. At his examination 1 month earlier, a visual acuity of 20/60 in the right eye with improvement to 20/30 with pinhole had been recorded.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Chronic, relapsing condition
Characteristics: Staphylococcal anterior blepharitis with crusting debris at eyelash bases, disruption of eyelid margin architecture, and madarosis (loss of eyelashes).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Frequent or rough handling of the eyelids (mechanical irritation), contact lens wear, exposure to infected individuals, dermatologic conditions (eczema/rosacea/seborrhea), seasonal allergens, poor eyelid hygiene, meibomian gland dysfunction, dry eye/aqueous tear deficiency, recurrent ocular surface inflammation.
Alleviating Factors: Eyelid hygiene (mechanical debridement/lid scrubs), warm compresses, gentle eyelid massage to express meibomian secretions, preservative‑free artificial tears (4–8×/day), topical antibiotic ointment at bedtime, short‑course topical steroids, omega‑3 fatty acid supplementation
Associated Symptoms: redness, photophobia, blurred vision
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant ocular surface discomfort with redness, photophobia, blurred vision in right eye (VA reduced to 20/400) affecting vision-dependent activities; chronic, relapsing condition requiring ongoing eyelid hygiene and treatments.
Risk Factors: Staphylococcal eyelid colonization (S. aureus, S. epidermidis); seborrheic dermatitis; meibomian gland dysfunction; rosacea (ocular acne rosacea); contact lens wear; dermatologic conditions (eczema); dry eye disease/aqueous tear deficiency; recurrent eyelid inflammation/chronic relapsing eyelid hygiene neglect; prior ocular surgery (e.g., PK) as a potential contributor; exposure to infected individuals; age-related meibomian gland changes; use of topical ocular medications (medicamentosa).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 74 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 17 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: crusting debris at the base of the eyelashes; disruption of normal eyelid margin architecture with loss of eyelashes (madarosis)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2 + ciliary flush in the right eye; left eye quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft from a broken, exposed interrupted suture at the 11 o’clock meridian; line of keratic precipitates demarcating central edge of stromal edema; 1+ anterior chamber cell and flare; inferior punctate epithelial erosions (associated aqueous tear deficiency); prior penetrating keratoplasty in right eye with visual acuity reduced to 20/400 without pinhole improvement.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 1+ cell and flare


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pinhole improvement to 20/30 in the right eye noted at prior visit
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion bilaterally (intact)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2+ ciliary flush OD; broken, exposed interrupted suture with surrounding infiltrate at the 11 o’clock meridian of the right graft; marked stromal edema extending 2 mm into the graft from the suture site; line of keratic precipitates demarcating the central edge of stromal edema; anterior chamber with 1+ cell and flare; left eye quiet with well‑positioned RGP contact lens, central corneal thinning and mild protrusion, Fleischer ring; eyelid findings consistent with blepharitis (crusting debris, disruption of lid margin, madarosis)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unable to visualize; view limited by media opacity — peripheral retina not assessable (no posterior exam due to corneal graft edema)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type, chronic and relapsing, associated with dry eye/aqueous tear deficiency. Patient also has history of right penetrating keratoplasty with prior visual acuity decline and current findings consistent with blepharitis (lid margin crusting, madarosis) and ocular surface disease (inferior punctate keratopathy, diminished tear lake, positive Schirmer). Management plan: emphasize meticulous eyelid hygiene (lid scrubs, warm compresses), topical antibiotic ointment at bedtime (e.g., erythromycin/bacitracin) for bacterial colonization, preservative-free artificial tears 4–8×/day for tear deficiency, consider short-pulse topical steroid for significant inflammation, and oral doxycycline or topical azithromycin if meibomian involvement or refractory disease. Consider culture if refractory, and evaluate for eyelid malignancy if unilateral/recurrent or atypical. Follow-up in 3–6 weeks or sooner if steroids used; long-term maintenance regimen required.
Primary Diagnosis: Anterior blepharitis, staphylococcal type.
Secondary Diagnoses: Keratoconus; Status post penetrating keratoplasty (right eye); Dry eye disease (aqueous tear deficiency)
Differential Diagnosis: Allergic conjunctivitis; Dry eye syndrome; Giant papillary conjunctivitis; Pediculosis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: - Chronic, relapsing ocular surface disease with recurring symptoms - Structural eyelid damage (poliosis, madarosis, lid ulceration, loss of eyelash/lid architecture) - Meibomian gland atrophy and dysfunction leading to evaporative dry eye - Inferior punctate epithelial erosions, corneal infiltrates, neovascularization, thinning, phlyctenules - Marginal keratitis and sterile marginal infiltrates - Recurrent chalazia and potential need for surgical correction (lid malposition/scarring) - Secondary infection from bacterial colonization (including MRSA) - Misdiagnosis of underlying malignancy (sebaceous cell carcinoma, basal cell carcinoma, squamous cell carcinoma, rarely melanoma)


Plan
All Medications: erythromycin ointment sulfacetamide ointment bacitracin ointment topical steroids (short-pulse) preservative-free artificial tears hypochlorous acid 0.01% lid cleansing solution topical azithromycin ophthalmic solution oral minocycline doxycycline 50–100 mg orally twice daily tetracycline 250 mg orally four times daily oral omega-3 fatty acid (DHA/EPA) 1–3 g/day
All Procedures: Lid hygiene/lid scrubs (mechanical debridement with cotton‑tip applicator or mild shampoo), topical antibiotic ointment application (e.g., erythromycin, sulfacetamide, bacitracin) at bedtime, warm compresses, gentle eyelid massage (meibomian expression), topical steroids (short‑pulse), preservative‑free artificial tears (4–8×/day), topical azithromycin ophthalmic solution, oral antibiotics for MGD (doxycycline, minocycline, tetracycline), oral omega‑3 fatty acid supplementation (1–3 g/day), eyelid bacterial culture and sensitivity testing, eyelid biopsy (for refractory/unilateral suspicious cases), LipiFlow thermal pulsation, intense pulsed light (IPL)
Follow-Up Instructions: Mild blepharitis: follow as needed or at next routine visit. More severe cases: return in 3–6 weeks (sooner if on topical steroids to monitor response and IOP). Emphasize ongoing maintenance eyelid hygiene, artificial tears, and warm compresses; tailor regimen to symptom severity.
Patient Education: Blepharitis is a chronic, relapsing eyelid condition; meticulous, daily eyelid hygiene is essential. Perform gentle lid scrubs (cotton-tip or commercial lid cleansers, e.g., hypochlorous 0.01% or mild shampoo) once or twice daily and use warm compresses to soften debris and help express glands. Avoid rough or frequent handling of the lids. Use preservative‑free artificial tears 4–8×/day for associated dry eye. Apply topical antibiotic ointment at bedtime if prescribed for staphylococcal disease. Short courses of topical steroids may be used briefly for severe inflammation under close follow-up (monitor IOP). Consider oral omega‑3 supplements (1–3 g/day DHA/EPA) for meibomian dysfunction; oral antibiotics (doxycycline, minocycline, tetracycline) may be used for refractory cases but are contraindicated in children and pregnant/nursing women. Return for follow-up in 3–6 weeks or sooner if symptoms worsen; report unilateral, persistent, or unusual lid changes (nodules, recurrent chalazia, ulceration, loss of normal lid architecture) as these may require biopsy to exclude malignancy. Maintenance therapy is usually lifelong to prevent recurrences.


CASE 14 — A Nearly Mature Cataract in a Patient with Glaucoma
=============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Nearly mature cataract in the only seeing eye with decreased vision and history of glaucoma
DURATION: Two years
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A pediatrician noted white pupils in a 10‑day‑old infant; the infant had poor wince to light in both eyes, reactive pupils, normal motility, soft globes, clear normal‑diameter corneas, and central white lens opacities bilaterally with no view to the posterior segment.
History Narrative: A pediatrician noticed white pupils in a 10-day-old infant and made the diagnosis of bilateral cataracts. The mother had an uncomplicated pregnancy with no rash or febrile illness, the infant was born at term, and maternal serologies were all negative. There was no family history of congenital or childhood cataracts. The infant demonstrated a poor wince to light in both eyes, reactive pupils with no relative afferent pupillary defect, normal extraocular movements, soft globes to palpation, normal eyelids and conjunctiva, clear corneas of normal diameter, and central white lens opacities bilaterally. There was no view to the posterior segment in either eye; B-scan ultrasonography revealed no retinal detachment or mass. The patient underwent laboratory testing and genetic consultation, which failed to reveal an underlying genetic syndrome.
Symptom Onset: 10 days of age
Characteristics: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, advanced glaucoma, functioning filtering bleb, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies, small pupil (requiring iris-expander), monocular (blind phthisical fellow eye)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: monocular status, advanced glaucoma with prior trabeculectomy and functioning bleb, zonular weakness/compromise, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies/small pupil, history of multiple prior intraocular surgeries (risk to corneal endothelium), tenuous corneal endothelium leading to risk of corneal decompensation
Associated Symptoms: poor wince to light, decreased vision (white pupils), no relative afferent pupillary defect, soft globes to palpation
Prior Treatments: multiple prior intraocular surgeries including trabeculectomy with a functioning filtering bleb; intermittent use of glaucoma medications and bleb massage
Impact on Daily Activities: She uses a computer at work, grows flowers, and is very aware of colors and brightness of objects.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: congenital glaucoma, prior intraocular surgeries (trabeculectomy/bleb), zonular weakness/compromise, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies/small pupil, dense (nearly mature) cataract, monocular status, prior corneal endothelial cell loss (risk for corneal decompensation)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: low teens
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear and normal in diameter
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and formed
Anterior Segment – Iris: Atrophic with radial sphincterotomies and corectopia (iridectomy present)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open with peripheral anterior synechiae from prior surgeries (functioning trabeculectomy) — overall open angle but with areas of synechial closure, i.e., partially open/compromised angle.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, capsular tension ring and 6‑D foldable silicone IOL in the capsular bag; posterior capsular defect with vitreous prolapse managed by anterior vitrectomy.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: reactive pupils with corectopia and atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies (irregular, displaced/decentered pupil)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: reactive with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Poor view of the posterior segment due to central white lens opacities (nearly mature cataract) with clear cornea of normal diameter, small/atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies and posterior synechiae, soft globe to palpation, functioning filtering bleb, no view of fundus on exam.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No view to the posterior segment in either eye on initial exam; posterior segment not visualized (dense cataract)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Mature cataract with zonular compromise in a monocular patient with advanced glaucoma status post multiple prior surgeries (including functioning trabeculectomy with bleb), atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies and small pupil, and a blind, phthisical fellow eye — presenting high risk for zonular dehiscence/dropped nucleus, corneal endothelial compromise, postoperative inflammation and IOP spike; cataract surgery planned with measures to protect endothelium, stabilize the capsular bag (capsular tension ring), mechanically expand the pupil (iris‑expander ring), and be prepared for anterior vitrectomy to manage vitreous prolapse.
Primary Diagnosis: Mature cataract with zonular compromise, advanced glaucoma status post multiple surgeries including a trabeculectomy with a functioning filtering bleb, atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies, and blind phthisical fellow eye
Secondary Diagnoses: congenital glaucoma; zonular weakness (zonular compromise); atrophic iris with radial sphincterotomies; functioning trabeculectomy with filtering bleb; phthisical blind fellow eye
Differential Diagnosis: Congenital cataract (idiopathic); Intrauterine infection–related cataract (TORCH: rubella, CMV, toxoplasmosis); Metabolic disorder–related cataract (galactosemia); Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV)
Severity: severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Vitreous prolapse through the superior nasal iridectomy (required anterior vitrectomy); risk of corneal decompensation; potential IOP spike/postoperative glaucoma exacerbation.


Plan
All Medications: Viscoat, Provisc, Healon 5, prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, diclofenac, timolol, brimonidine
All Procedures: Paracentesis; Clear corneal incision; Side-port incision; Viscoat and Provisc viscoelastic injection (soft-shell technique); Lysis of posterior synechiae (blunt and viscodissection); Insertion of Morcher iris‑expander ring; Continuous curvilinear capsulorrhexis; Insertion of Morcher capsular tension ring; Cortical cleaving hydrodissection and hydrodelineation; Phacoemulsification (choo-choo chop and flip technique) with nucleus disassembly; Irrigation and aspiration of cortex; Injection of dispersive Viscoat into capsulorrhexis; Insertion of 6‑diopter foldable silicone IOL into capsular bag; Closure of main wound with suture; Split‑port anterior vitrectomy via separate paracentesis; Removal of iris‑expander ring; Removal of residual viscoelastic with vitrector; Stromal hydration to seal incisions; Superior nasal peripheral iridectomy (existing/managed)
Follow-Up Instructions: Start topical prednisolone acetate, ofloxacin, and diclofenac TID. Follow closely for IOP spikes—examine twice daily for 3 days. Start timolol and brimonidine prophylactically for IOP control; avoid prostaglandin analogs and oral/topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors.


CASE 15 — Retained Lens Material after Cataract Extraction
==========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in the right eye with pain, blurred vision, and elevated intraocular pressure.
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 34-year-old Caucasian woman referred for evaluation of a nearly mature right cataract with complex ocular history including a phthisical left eye and congenital glaucoma; prior multiple right-eye surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy); presenting with finger-counting vision in the right eye, dense nearly mature right cataract, 3-mm pupil with 360° posterior synechiae and iris atrophy, functioning superior-nasal filtering bleb, IOP 20 mm Hg, deep quiet anterior chamber, absence/questionable zonules, no view of the right fundus.
History Narrative: A 34-year-old Caucasian woman was referred by a glaucoma specialist for evaluation of a nearly mature cataract in her right eye. She had a complex ocular history including a phthisical left eye and congenital glaucoma status post numerous surgical procedures in the right eye including goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, and a trabeculectomy. On examination, visual acuity was finger counting in the right and no light perception in the left. Examination of the right eye suggested that the patient was using her superior nasal peripheral iridotomy as an entrance pupil. There was a relatively thick-walled but functioning filtering bleb in the superior nasal quadrant. Intraocular pressure (IOP) was 20 mm Hg without glaucoma medications. A few corneal guttae were observed. The anterior chamber was deep and quiet. The iris was highly atrophic throughout its entire periphery, with very little sphincter tissue. The pupil measured 3 mm and did not dilate due to 360 degrees of posterior synechiae. There were multiple sphincterotomies at the pupillary margin and a large radial cut at the 12 o’clock position. There was a dense, nearly mature cataract, an absence of zonules visible through the large superior nasal iridectomy, and questionable zonular status in the areas of broad peripheral iridectomies in two other quadrants. There was no view of the right fundus.
Progression: Resolved


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: eye pain; blurred vision; decreased vision; conjunctival injection; inflammation; ocular hypertension; floaters; possible red eye
Prior Treatments: timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine, topical atropine, prednisolone acetate
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate temporary impairment (difficulty with vision-related tasks for days–weeks, improving to minimal impact after recovery)
Risk Factors: - Complex ocular history including congenital glaucoma with multiple prior intraocular surgeries (goniotomies, peripheral iridectomies, sphincterotomies, trabeculectomy) - Phthisical contralateral eye (reduced reserve) - Poor pupillary dilation due to 360° posterior synechiae and iris atrophy - Questionable/absent zonules - Nearly mature/dense cataract - Large axial length (high myopia, axial length 30 mm) - Large white-to-white (>14 mm) - Corneal guttae (endothelial compromise)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5
Visual Acuity – Method: finger counting
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system without apparent abnormality
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: A few corneal guttae
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: highly atrophic throughout its entire periphery with very little sphincter tissue, 360° posterior synechiae, multiple sphincterotomies and a large radial cut at 12 o’clock, pupil 3 mm not dilating
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Dense, nearly mature cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm, using superior nasal peripheral iridotomy as an entrance pupil (irregular/ectopic pupil with multiple sphincterotomies)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Did not dilate
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD detected
Pupils – Other: 3 mm and did not dilate due to 360 degrees of posterior synechiae
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior chamber was deep and quiet; a few corneal guttae were observed; the iris was highly atrophic with 360° posterior synechiae, multiple sphincterotomies at the pupillary margin, a large radial cut at 12 o'clock, pupil ~3 mm not dilating, and lens dense, nearly mature with absence/questionable zonules visible through peripheral iridectomies.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Flat.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Flat


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens particle glaucoma — patient with retained lens fragments after cataract extraction presenting weeks after lens capsule disruption with elevated IOP and anterior chamber lens remnants. Gonioscopy demonstrated open angles, B-scan showed flat retina. Clinical picture distinguished from angle-closure, phacolytic, phacoantigenic glaucoma, iridocyclitis, and angle-recession glaucoma based on history and exam. Management initiated with multiple aqueous suppressants (topical beta-blocker, alpha-2 agonist, topical and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), frequent topical corticosteroids, and cycloplegic/mydriatic (atropine). Given persistent elevated IOP and need for IOL placement, patient underwent pars plana vitrectomy, lensectomy, and IOL implantation 4 days after presentation. Postoperatively IOP normalized to 12 mm Hg off medications and final uncorrected visual acuity improved to 20/30.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens particle glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Lens particle glaucoma; congenital glaucoma; phthisical left eye; corneal guttae; posterior synechiae; iris atrophy; prior trabeculectomy with functioning filtering bleb
Differential Diagnosis: Angle-closure glaucoma; Phacolytic glaucoma; Phacoantigenic (phacoantigenic) glaucoma; Iridocyclitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: lens particle glaucoma; ocular hypertension; inflammation; retinal detachment; hemorrhage; chronic inflammation; disabling visual floaters; refractory cystoid macular edema


Plan
All Medications: timolol; dorzolamide; brimonidine; atropine; prednisolone acetate; topical beta-blocker; alpha-2-adrenergic agonist; topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitor; systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitor; glycerin (mentioned as to avoid in diabetics); isosorbide; mannitol; frequent topical corticosteroids; periocular or systemic corticosteroids; prostaglandin analogs (typically not used)
All Procedures: pars plana vitrectomy; lensectomy; intraocular lens (IOL) placement; gonioscopy; keratometry; corneal topography; axial length measurement; B-scan ultrasonography; topical ocular hypotensive therapy (timolol, dorzolamide, brimonidine); topical atropine; topical prednisolone acetate
Patient Education: Explain that retained lens fragments after cataract surgery can cause inflammation and high eye pressure (lens particle glaucoma), requiring urgent treatment to protect vision; initial care includes multiple pressure-lowering eye drops, frequent steroid drops, and cycloplegics to reduce inflammation and prevent synechiae; if pressure or inflammation persists or if vitreous prolapse, hemorrhage, retinal detachment, or disabling symptoms occur, pars plana vitrectomy with removal of lens fragments (and possible secondary IOL placement) is recommended—timing is individualized but often within days to weeks; risks of delayed surgery include persistent inflammation, glaucoma, and retinal detachment; report any worsening pain, redness, vision loss, flashes/floaters, or increased discharge immediately; attend all follow-up visits and adhere strictly to prescribed drops and systemic medications (e.g., oral acetazolamide or IV mannitol in emergencies) so optic nerve damage can be prevented.
Referrals: Glaucoma specialist


CASE 16 — Fibrin Deposition on Intraocular Lenses
=================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 2-month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 47-year-old man with history of bungee cord trauma to the left eye and complicated cataract surgery of that eye 9 months earlier, lost to follow-up, presenting to retina clinic with 2 months of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye; vision 20/400 OS, IOP OS 38 mm Hg, exam notable for mild conjunctival injection, 2+ AC cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, prolapsed vitreous, lens fragments inferiorly on gonioscopy, crystalline lens subluxated inferiorly in anterior vitreous.
History Narrative: A 47-year-old man with a history of bungee cord trauma to the left eye had undergone a complicated cataract surgery 9 months earlier. He was lost to follow-up and presented to the retina clinic with a 2-month history of decreased vision, redness, and pain in the left eye. His visual acuity was 20/20 OD and 20/400 OS. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 38 mm Hg OS. Pupils were reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect. Right eye exam was unremarkable aside from a mild cataract. Slit-lamp biomicroscopy of the left eye revealed mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous. Gonioscopy of the left eye demonstrated a wide open angle with lens fragments noted inferiorly. Dilated fundus examination of the left eye revealed a posterior vitreous detachment with the crystalline lens subluxated inferiorly in the anterior vitreous; macula, retinal vessels, and optic nerve were unremarkable.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive — subacute worsening over 2 months with chronic postoperative inflammation leading to decreased vision, redness, and pain.
Characteristics: Fibrin deposition over the intraocular lens implant occurring 2–14 days postoperatively; presents with decreased vision, red eye, pain, photophobia; slit‑lamp shows cellular anterior chamber reaction without hypopyon, fibrin as strands on IOL/pupillary plane/iris or dense membrane covering pupil; immune‑mediated due to blood–aqueous barrier breakdown allowing fibrinogen leakage and fibrin formation; associated macrophages/giant cells; complications: posterior synechiae, IOL membrane formation/dislocation, glaucoma, cystoid macular edema, posterior capsular opacification.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: surgical trauma, increased blood–aqueous permeability, prolonged surgery, prior intraocular surgery, surgical manipulation of the iris, incomplete removal of lens cortex/epithelial cells, intraoperative long‑acting miotic use, diabetes, hypertension, pseudoexfoliation syndrome, uveitis
Alleviating Factors: Topical corticosteroids (e.g., prednisolone acetate 1% drops), intracameral tPA, systemic steroids in severe cases, preoperative topical NSAIDs/steroids, intraoperative subconjunctival steroid injection, anterior chamber washout/fibrin membranectomy if persistent.
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision, redness, pain, photophobia
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant—marked decrease in left-eye visual function (VA 20/400 OS), with pain, redness, photophobia and risk of glaucoma and further complications likely impairing daily activities (driving, reading); right eye normal.
Risk Factors: diabetes; hypertension; pseudoexfoliation syndrome; uveitis; prolonged surgery; previous intraocular surgery; surgical manipulation of the iris; incomplete removal of lens cortex and epithelial cells; intraoperative long-acting miotic use; prior recent intraocular surgery (even fellow eye)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 38 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild conjunctival injection, cornea clear (no corneal edema described)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: 2+ cell and flare, fibrin strands/membrane over IOL and pupillary plane, mild conjunctival injection, no hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, prolapsed vitreous
Anterior Segment – Angle: Wide open angle
Anterior Segment – Lens: Subluxated crystalline lens with ectopia lentis and prolapsed lens fragments; prior complicated cataract surgery with intraocular lens present and fibrin on IOL.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive to light; no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: Reactive to light with no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild conjunctival injection, 2+ anterior chamber cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis, and prolapsed vitreous
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina flat and unremarkable (no tears or detachment)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 47-year-old man with history of complicated cataract surgery OS presenting with 2 months of decreased vision, redness, and pain OS. Exam OS: VA 20/400, IOP 38 mmHg, 2+ AC cell and flare, iridodonesis, ectopia lentis with prolapsed vitreous, lens fragments inferiorly in the angle, and subluxated crystalline lens in the anterior vitreous. Findings consistent with postoperative fibrin deposition on the IOL with secondary inflammatory reaction and elevated IOP—diagnosis: postoperative fibrin deposition on the intraocular lens OS with associated lens-particle/secondary glaucoma and vitreo-lenticular subluxation following prior complicated cataract surgery. Management focused on controlling intraocular inflammation (topical corticosteroids; consider systemic steroids if severe) and IOP, with consideration of intracameral tPA for refractory fibrin and surgical intervention (AC washout, membranectomy or lens/IOL removal) if medically refractory or if IOL/lens is causative. Follow-up for IOP, inflammation, and risk of cystoid macular edema and infection.
Primary Diagnosis: Postoperative fibrin deposition on the intraocular lens
Differential Diagnosis: Fibrinous postoperative anterior uveitis (fibrin deposition on IOL); Acute postoperative endophthalmitis; Lens-particle/inflammatory glaucoma from retained lens fragments; Persistent uveitic reaction (including autoimmune or infectious anterior uveitis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: AC turbidity; corneal edema; elevated intraocular pressure; bleeding; corneal toxicity; band keratopathy; endophthalmitis; posterior synechiae; loss of iris function; membrane formation on the IOL; dislocation of the IOL; glaucoma; cystoid macular edema; posterior capsular opacification; infectious keratitis


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% (topical) — one drop 4–6 times daily, up to hourly as needed; topical nonsteroidal antiinflammatory agents (topical NSAIDs) (preoperative use); systemic steroids (for severe cases); intraoperative subconjunctival steroid injection; recombinant tissue plasminogen activator (tPA) — intracameral injection (doses as low as 3 μg)
All Procedures: intracameral tPA injection topical prednisolone acetate administration intraoperative subconjunctival steroid injection anterior chamber washout (fibrin membranectomy) IOL removal (explantation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Regular eye examinations at regular intervals to monitor visual acuity, intraocular pressure, and signs of infection or recurrent inflammation.
Patient Education: Fibrin on the lens is inflammation after eye surgery that can cause decreased vision, redness, pain, and light sensitivity. It usually appears days to two weeks after surgery. The main treatment is frequent topical steroid drops (e.g., prednisolone acetate 1%) to reduce inflammation; you may need them hourly at first and then tapered as the eye improves. In severe cases, a medication called tPA can be injected into the front of the eye to dissolve fibrin, but this has rare risks (corneal swelling, higher eye pressure, bleeding, infection). Surgery (washout or lens removal) is rarely needed unless the lens is causing ongoing problems. Follow-up visits are important to monitor vision, pressure, and signs of infection or recurrent inflammation; report worsening pain, sudden vision loss, increasing redness, discharge, or fever immediately. Use drops exactly as prescribed and avoid rubbing the eye.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care for sudden severe eye pain, marked vision loss, worsening redness, discharge, new floaters or flashes, or if IOP becomes very high (severe eye pressure symptoms). If signs of infection (fever, purulent discharge) or trauma occur, go to the emergency department or call your eye surgeon right away.


CASE 17 — Subluxated Crystalline Lens
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photophobia
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 59-year-old Caucasian woman presented 5 days after combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber IOL implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye for photophobia; she had undergone the same procedures in the left eye 1 month earlier. Past medical history significant for hypertension. She was using prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours and ofloxacin four times a day in both eyes. Visual acuity 20/60 OD and 20/200 OS; slit-lamp OD: mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb, clear cornea, moderate cells and flare in the anterior chamber without hypopyon, and a fibrin meshwork on the IOL. OS: low bleb, clear cornea, rare anterior chamber cells, IOL in good position. Dilated fundus: moderate (OD) and severe (OS) glaucomatous cupping; poor vision OS due to advanced glaucoma.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old Caucasian woman presented to the eye clinic after intraocular surgery in the right eye, with a chief complaint of photophobia. Five days earlier, she had undergone combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens (IOL) implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye. Her left eye had undergone the same procedures 1 month prior to presentation. Her past medical history was significant only for hypertension. Her ocular medications were prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours and ofloxacin four times a day in both eyes. Her visual acuity was 20/60 OD and 20/200 OS. Slit-lamp examination of the right eye revealed mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb. The cornea was clear. The anterior chamber had moderate cells and flare with no hypopyon. There was a meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL. Examination of the left eye revealed a low bleb, clear cornea, and rare cells in the anterior chamber. The IOL was in good position. Dilated fundus exam was unremarkable in both eyes except for moderate and severe glaucomatous cupping of the right and left optic nerves, respectively. The poor vision in the left eye was secondary to advanced glaucoma.
Symptom Onset: 5 days earlier
Symptom Duration: 5 days
Characteristics: Upward (superotemporal) subluxation of the crystalline lens associated with Marfan’s syndrome, often bilateral, with zonular laxity and risk of associated systemic features (tall stature, arachnodactyly, aortic root dilation)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia
Prior Treatments: combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (right eye 5 days prior; left eye same procedures 1 month prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — photophobia and reduced vision (20/60 OD, 20/200 OS) impair activities such as reading, driving, and light tolerance, though some function retained with corrective measures.
Systemic Symptoms: tall stature; long, thin extremities; arachnodactyly; joint laxity; pectus excavatum; kyphoscoliosis; decreased subcutaneous fat; cardiac abnormalities (mitral or aortic valve disease, progressive ascending aortic dilation)
Risk Factors: Marfan’s syndrome; trauma; pseudoexfoliation; eye rubbing associated with atopy; hereditary systemic disorders (e.g., homocystinuria, Weill–Marchesani syndrome, hyperlysinemia, sulfite oxidase deficiency)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (distance visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: NAD. Normal-appearing lids and lashes.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: moderate cells and flare with no hypopyon
Anterior Segment – Iris: Upward subluxation of the crystalline lens with associated iridodonesis (iris tremulousness)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Upward subluxation of crystalline lens (ectopia lentis)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: mild conjunctival injection with a low bleb; clear cornea; anterior chamber with moderate cells and flare, no hypopyon; meshwork of fibrin deposition on the IOL. Left eye: low bleb; clear cornea; rare cells in the anterior chamber; IOL in good position.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: moderate and severe glaucomatous cupping of the right and left optic nerves, respectively
Fundus Exam – Macula: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal abnormalities detected (no detachments); peripheral retina unremarkable.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) consistent with Marfan’s syndrome — upward subluxation of the crystalline lens in the left eye. Significant zonular laxity with risk for progressive displacement, pupillary block, and retinal detachment. Visual impairment OU with advanced glaucomatous cupping (worse OS); conservative optical management may be attempted but surgical intervention (phacoemulsification with capsular support devices or pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy and appropriate IOL fixation) should be considered based on degree of decentration, visual symptoms, and complications. Recommend systemic evaluation for Marfan’s (including echocardiography) and family screening.
Primary Diagnosis: Lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) associated with Marfan’s syndrome
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Traumatic lens subluxation; Marfan’s syndrome-associated ectopia lentis; Homocystinuria (hereditary metabolic zonulopathy)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Cardiac abnormalities (aortic root dilation, mitral/aortic valve disease, risk of dissecting aortic aneurysm) - Retinal detachment - Glaucoma (including angle‑closure from pupillary block, optic nerve cupping/vision loss) - Corneal endothelial cell loss and corneal decompensation (from anterior chamber lens dislocation) - Amblyopia (in pediatric cases) - Intraoperative/postoperative complications of surgery: vitreous prolapse, intraocular hemorrhage, endophthalmitis, severe anterior chamber inflammation/fibrinous uveitis, hypotony - IOL complications: decentration/tilt, pigment dispersion, unpredictable refractive error, suture degradation/breakage with late IOL dislocation, conjunctival erosion from exposed scleral knots - Hemorrhage and haptic damage with intrascleral fixation


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1% every 2 hours; ofloxacin four times a day
All Procedures: combined cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens implantation and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (left eye); phacoemulsification; pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy; anterior chamber IOL implantation; sulcus implantation of three-piece foldable IOL; sutured intracapsular device placement; three-piece posterior chamber IOL with iris or scleral suture fixation; intrascleral tunnel haptic fixation (glued IOL); anterior vitrectomy; peripheral laser iridotomy; Nd:YAG laser zonulysis; aspiration of anterior chamber and vitreous for culture and intravitreal antibiotic injection
Follow-Up Instructions: Return to clinic for postoperative follow-up as scheduled for wound and IOP check; monitor for increasing pain, redness, decreased vision, hypopyon, or vitritis (seek urgent care if these occur). Continue topical prednisolone and antibiotic as prescribed; taper steroids per surgeon’s plan. Arrange echocardiography to evaluate for aortic/valvular disease if Marfan’s not previously assessed. Long-term: regular ophthalmic follow-up for IOP and retinal exam; consider amblyopia/visual rehabilitation or secondary IOL planning as needed.
Patient Education: - Explanation that lens subluxation (ectopia lentis) is displacement of the natural lens caused by weak or broken zonules, which can cause blurred vision, double vision, glare, and increased risk of glaucoma or retinal detachment. - If bilateral and nontraumatic, importance of systemic evaluation for inherited conditions (especially Marfan’s syndrome) and referral for cardiology evaluation (echocardiography) because of risk of aortic dilation/dissection. - Conservative options: glasses or contact lenses may correct refractive changes; pupillary agents (miotics or cycloplegics) or laser iridotomy may be used temporarily to manage symptoms or pupillary block. - Indications for surgery: significant visual impairment, lens-induced glaucoma, corneal endothelial compromise, or progressive anterior dislocation; surgical options include phacoemulsification with capsular support devices, or pars plana lensectomy with vitrectomy if lens is posteriorly dislocated or vitreous prolapse. - If surgery planned, discuss possible need for capsule tension rings, capsular hooks, scleral- or iris-fixated IOLs, anterior chamber IOLs, or leaving aphakic with later secondary IOL; review risks (infection, hemorrhage, IOL decentration, retinal detachment, glaucoma). - Postoperative expectations: steroid and antibiotic eye drops, monitoring for inflammation/infection, and regular glaucoma and retinal checks; report worsening pain, vision loss, or increasing redness immediately. - Family screening and genetic counseling recommended when hereditary cause suspected.
Referrals: Echocardiography for evaluation of mitral/aortic valve abnormalities and aortic dilation (cardiology referral)


CASE 18 — Congenital Cataract
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in the left eye
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 41-year-old man with Marfan’s syndrome presenting with variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye; no history of trauma; family history of Marfan’s syndrome. Visual acuity 20/70 OS with −5.00 D correction and 20/20 OD with −0.75 D. Left crystalline lens subluxated superiorly with diaphanous zonules exposed across at least six clock hours inferiorly; no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse. Dilated exam of right eye shows very subtle inferior zonular dialysis. Intraocular pressure and fundus exam normal.
History Narrative: A 41-year-old man with Marfan’s syndrome presents with complaints of variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in his left eye. The patient has a family history of Marfan’s syndrome, but no history of trauma. Visual acuity is 20/70 with –5.00 D correction in the left eye, compared to 20/20 with a –0.75 D correction in the right eye. The left crystalline lens is subluxated superiorly with diaphanous zonules exposed across an area of at least six clock hours inferiorly. There is no phacodonesis and no vitreous prolapse. Dilated examination of the asymptomatic right eye revealed a very subtle inferior zonular dialysis. The intraocular pressure and fundus examination are normal.
Symptom Onset: Present at birth or within the first year of life
Characteristics: Bilateral, idiopathic congenital central (nuclear) lens opacities causing leukocoria and amblyopia risk; often bilateral (two-thirds), variable density, may allow peripheral clear cortex; visually significant if present before 2–3 months.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: variable decreased vision; bothersome glare
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in left eye causing some visual impairment but not bedridding; right eye normal saves daily function.
Risk Factors: Marfan’s syndrome; zonular weakness/subluxated lens; family history of Marfan’s syndrome; age (adult with longstanding congenital cataract)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (measured): 20/70 (left eye); 20/20 (right eye)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Left crystalline lens subluxated superiorly with exposed diaphanous zonules inferiorly over at least six clock hours; no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupil constriction confirms excellent centration of the IOL (normal, round, reactive)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Pupil constriction confirms excellent centration of the IOL
Extraocular Movements: Full range of extraocular movements intact (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Left crystalline lens subluxated superiorly with diaphanous zonules exposed across at least six clock hours inferiorly; no phacodonesis, no vitreous prolapse; right eye with very subtle inferior zonular dialysis; otherwise anterior segment unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: WNL


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral idiopathic congenital cataract
Secondary Diagnoses: Marfan’s syndrome
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (PHPV)/persistent fetal vasculature; Coats’ disease; Retinal detachment
Severity: Variable decreased vision and bothersome glare in left eye; left VA 20/70 with -5.00 D — Moderate severity
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior capsular opacification; secondary membrane formation; glaucoma; retinal detachment


Plan
All Medications: atropine
Follow-Up Instructions: Excellent refractive correction and aggressive amblyopia therapy (occlusion/patching) with immediate optical rehabilitation (contact lens for unilateral aphakia; spectacles for bilateral), regular postoperative pupil dilation as needed, close long‑term monitoring for glaucoma (lifelong IOP checks) and posterior capsule/opacification, and frequent follow‑up visits to adjust refraction and manage inflammation and complications.
Patient Education: Explain congenital cataract, its risks (amblyopia if not treated early), and the need for timely surgery for visually significant cataracts; discuss benefits/risks of IOL implantation vs aphakia correction (spectacles or contact lens), including likely need for future refractive changes and possible complications (posterior capsular opacification, glaucoma, retinal detachment); emphasize importance of strict postoperative refractive correction and occlusion therapy (patching) to prevent amblyopia; instruct on postoperative care (pupil dilation, medication adherence, monitoring for inflammation/infection); arrange and stress long-term follow-up for refractive adjustments and glaucoma surveillance; provide resources/support and answer family questions.


CASE 19 — Ocular Hypertension
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: shadow blocking her temporal vision
DURATION: 20-year
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "a 'shadow' since her surgery, which was blocking her temporal vision. This was so debilitating that she almost ran over a small boy with her car."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented 8 months after combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implantation, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye, complaining of a temporal “shadow” since her surgery that was so debilitating it nearly caused a motor vehicle accident; vision in the affected eye is 20/30 and IOP is 7 mm Hg.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman with a 20-year history of glaucoma presented for consultation 8 months following combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye. She complained of a “shadow” since her surgery, which was blocking her temporal vision. This was so debilitating that she almost ran over a small boy with her car. The visual acuity was 20/30 in the affected eye and the intraocular pressure (IOP) was 7 mm Hg. A large, ischemic filtering bleb was present with a negative Seidel’s test. The cornea was clear, the anterior chamber deep and quiet, and the cup-to-disc ratio was 0.8. The peripheral fundus was not visible due to a small and fibrotic pupil. A B-scan ultrasound was performed, which showed 360-degree ciliochoroidal detachments with serous fluid inside the detachments. There was a large nasal choroidal detachment that measured 8 mm. There was no retinal detachment overlying the choroidal detachment.
Symptom Onset: 8 months ago
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: No progression.
Characteristics: Ocular hypertension: elevated intraocular pressure with normal-appearing optic nerves and visual fields, absence of glaucomatous optic neuropathy, may have normal slit-lamp and gonioscopy unless secondary cause present, risk factors include age, race, family history, thin CCT, often asymptomatic.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: "shadow" blocking temporal vision, near-miss driving incident (almost ran over a child)
Prior Treatments: combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, and trabeculectomy with mitomycin C in the right eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — shadowing blocked temporal vision causing near-accident while driving
Risk Factors: - Elevated intraocular pressure (IOP) - Thin central corneal thickness (CCT) - Race (particularly black) - Positive family history of glaucoma - Older age


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 7 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 7 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Intraocular lens implant (pseudophakia)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: small and fibrotic pupil
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: small and fibrotic
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The cornea was clear, the anterior chamber deep and quiet, and there was a small, fibrotic pupil.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio 0.8
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral fundus not visible due to a small and fibrotic pupil.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ocular hypertension — elevated intraocular pressure without optic nerve or visual field evidence of glaucomatous damage; manage based on risk factors and consider observation versus IOP‑lowering therapy individualized to age, CCT, race, family history, and other risks.
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic ocular hypertension; Primary open-angle glaucoma; Secondary glaucoma from pigment dispersion or pseudoexfoliation
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Large, ischemic filtering bleb; ciliochoroidal (serous) detachments (including large nasal choroidal detachment); shadowing/temporal visual field loss; low intraocular pressure (hypotony) — postoperative complications after trabeculectomy (with mitomycin C)


Plan
All Procedures: combined cataract extraction, intraocular lens implant, trabeculectomy with mitomycin C, slit-lamp exam, gonioscopy, spectral domain optical coherence tomography (SD-OCT), perimetry/visual field testing, pachymetry, B-scan ultrasound, laser trabeculoplasty (not recommended), glaucoma filtration surgery (not recommended), dilated optic nerve examination
Follow-Up Instructions: Initially seen at least every 6–12 months. Yearly visual field testing and/or OCT and dilated optic nerve exam. If started on IOP-lowering therapy, recheck within 4 weeks; after stabilization, exam every 4–6 months for at least 2 years. If stable for many years, exam every 6–12 months.
Patient Education: Yearly counseling on the nature of ocular hypertension, its risk of progressing to glaucoma, and the importance of adherence to follow-up; instructions to report new visual symptoms promptly; lifestyle and medication adherence discussion if treated; and recommendation for scheduled follow-up visits (initially every 6–12 months, with yearly visual field/OCT and dilated optic nerve exams; if treated, recheck within 4 weeks and then every 4–6 months until stable).
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care if you experience sudden vision loss, new or worsening severe eye pain, new flashes/floaters, a marked change in visual field (e.g., new shadow blocking vision), or signs of infection (increasing redness, discharge, or light sensitivity). If vision or safety is acutely threatened (e.g., nearly ran someone over due to vision change), go to the emergency department or an eye clinic urgently.


CASE 20 — Open-Angle Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: High eye pressures noted by optometrist (possible glaucoma)
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 42‑year‑old Caucasian man was referred to the eye clinic after an optometrist noted elevated eye pressures and suggested he might have glaucoma. He had no prior ocular symptoms; past medical and family histories were unremarkable and he was not taking any medications. On exam his visual acuity was 20/20 OU, pupils and motility were normal, anterior segment unremarkable, and IOPs were 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy showed 360° open angles to the ciliary body band with moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation. Dilated fundus exam revealed symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping (cup-to-disc ratio 0.2) and intact neuroretinal rims OU. A 24-2 Humphrey visual field showed no defects in either eye.
History Narrative: A 42-year-old Caucasian man was referred to the eye clinic after being told by an optometrist that his eye pressures were high and that he might have glaucoma. Past medical history and family history were unremarkable. He was not taking any medications. Ocular examination revealed visual acuity of 20/20 OU without correction. Pupils and motility were normal. Anterior segment biomicroscopic examination was unremarkable with a clear cornea and lens. Intraocular pressures (IOPs) were 27 mm Hg OD and 28 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy revealed angles open to the ciliary body band 360 degrees with moderate pigmentation of the trabecular meshwork. Dilated funduscopic examination revealed symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping; the cup-to-disc ratio was 0.2 with an intact neuroretinal rim OU. The macula, vessels, periphery, and vitreous were normal in appearance. A 24–2 Humphrey automated perimetry was performed, which revealed no visual field defects in either eye.
Characteristics: Primary open-angle glaucoma with elevated intraocular pressures (IOP 27 mm Hg OD, 28 mm Hg OS), open angles 360° to ciliary body band with moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation, normal visual acuity (20/20 OU), normal pupils and motility, clear cornea and lens, symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping (cup-to-disc ratio 0.2 OU) and intact neuroretinal rim, no visual field defects on 24-2 Humphrey perimetry, no corneal/endothelial or iris transillumination signs of pigmentary or pseudoexfoliation glaucoma, unremarkable macula/vessels/periphery/vitreous.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: None reported
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities; normal visual acuity and no visual field defects.
