Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Optometrist (who first noted high IOP)
Risk Factors: Age 42 (young adult), Caucasian race, elevated intraocular pressure (ocular hypertension), moderate trabecular meshwork pigmentation on gonioscopy, male sex, normal central corneal thickness not stated (N/A)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 27 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Quiet, normal conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or abnormalities)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris OU
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open to ciliary body band 360 degrees
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: NORMAL
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Normal
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular motility without restriction (EOMI)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment biomicroscopic examination was unremarkable with a clear cornea and lens.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping; cup-to-disc ratio 0.2 OU with intact neuroretinal rim, no focal notching or hemorrhages.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal in appearance
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 42-year-old Caucasian man with bilateral ocular hypertension and open angles consistent with primary open-angle glaucoma. Visual acuity 20/20 OU, pupils and motility normal. IOP elevated: 27 mm Hg OD, 28 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy: angles open to ciliary body band 360° with moderate TM pigmentation. Dilated fundus exam: symmetric optic nerves with normal cupping, C/D 0.2 OU, intact neuroretinal rims, no RNFL defects noted. 24-2 Humphrey visual fields: no defects OU. No history or signs suggestive of secondary causes (no pigment dispersion or pseudoexfoliation findings). Impression: primary open-angle glaucoma (early/ocular hypertensive stage) — elevated IOP with open angles but without optic nerve damage or visual field loss; recommend measurement of central corneal thickness, consider initiation of IOP-lowering therapy with target ≥20% reduction from baseline or close observation with risk stratification and follow-up, and baseline optic nerve imaging/photography and serial visual field monitoring.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Primary open‑angle glaucoma; Secondary open‑angle glaucomas (pigmentary glaucoma, pseudoexfoliation); Chronic angle‑closure glaucoma; Anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (optic nerve atrophy)
Severity: mild
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: β-blockers topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors prostaglandin analogs adrenergic agonists miotics apraclonidine 5-fluorouracil mitomycin C
All Procedures: gonioscopy dilated funduscopic examination 24–2 Humphrey automated perimetry (automated perimetry) Goldmann applanation tonometry tonopen measurement pneumotonometry slit-lamp examination laser peripheral iridotomy stereoscopic fundus examination with fundus contact lens / Hruby / 78-D or 90-D lens red-free slit-lamp photography / RNFL assessment SD-OCT and optic nerve head imaging (confocal scanning laser ophthalmoscopy, scanning laser polarimetry, OCT) central corneal thickness measurement (ultrasound pachymetry, anterior segment OCT, corneal topography/tomography) visual field testing (automated static threshold and manual kinetic/static perimetry) laser trabeculoplasty (selective laser trabeculoplasty, argon laser trabeculoplasty) trabeculectomy (with antimetabolites: 5‑fluorouracil, mitomycin C) seton device implantation (Baerveldt, Molteno, Ahmed implants) transscleral cyclodestructive procedures (cryoablation, cyclophotocoagulation) endoscopic cyclophotocoagulation micropulse transscleral cyclophotocoagulation minimally invasive glaucoma surgery procedures (trabecular meshwork micro-bypass stents, intracanalicular scaffolding devices, supraciliary microstents, ab interno trabeculotomy, ab interno canaloplasty, subconjunctival filtering devices) stereoscopic optic nerve photography
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up frequency based on disease severity, target IOP achievement, evidence of progression, and duration of control; at each visit check medication side effects and compliance, visual acuity, IOP, and examine the optic nerve; repeat visual field testing and optic nerve imaging as indicated; obtain baseline stereoscopic optic nerve photos and adjust therapy for failure to reach target IOP, progressive nerve or field loss, or medication side effects/noncompliance.
Patient Education: You have primary open-angle glaucoma (POAG), a condition where eye pressure is elevated and can damage the optic nerve over time. Key points and recommendations: - Nature of disease: POAG is usually slow and painless. Early disease often has no symptoms but can cause irreversible vision loss if untreated. - Goal of treatment: Lower eye pressure to a “target” level (often ≥20% below baseline) to prevent or slow further optic nerve damage and vision loss. - Tests and monitoring: Expect regular visits for eye pressure checks, gonioscopy, optic nerve examination (stereoscopic photos or OCT), and visual field tests. Central corneal thickness may be measured to help interpret pressures. - Medical therapy: First-line treatment is usually pressure-lowering eye drops (prostaglandin analogs, beta-blockers, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, alpha agonists, or combinations). Start in one eye initially to assess response and side effects. Take drops exactly as prescribed; missed doses reduce effectiveness. - Possible side effects and adherence: Report any local irritation, vision changes, cardiac/respiratory symptoms (with beta-blockers), or systemic effects. Bring all eye drops to visits. If cost, dosing frequency, or side effects interfere with adherence, tell your doctor—alternatives exist. - Laser and surgery options: If drops don’t reach target pressure or adherence is poor, treatments include selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT) and various surgical procedures (trabeculectomy, tube implants, minimally invasive glaucoma surgery). SLT can be an initial or adjunct option and requires a short post-procedure pressure check. - When to seek urgent care: Sudden vision loss, sudden severe eye pain, halos around lights, persistent nausea/vomiting, or acute marked increase in redness/IOP—seek emergency care. - Lifestyle and general advice: Keep regular ophthalmology follow-ups, control systemic health conditions, avoid smoking, and use protective eyewear. Maintain a list of medications and inform other providers you have glaucoma. - Prognosis: With consistent treatment and monitoring, progression can often be slowed or halted. Early detection and adherence are important to preserve vision. - Next steps for you: Start/continue prescribed IOP-lowering therapy as directed (or discuss SLT if appropriate), schedule regular follow-up visits with IOP, optic nerve imaging/photography, and automated visual fields, and report any side effects or adherence issues promptly.


CASE 21 — Primary Angle-Closure Glaucoma
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye exam
DURATION: acute (onset described as acute)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam with no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints; he denied repeated or prior episodes and reported an acute onset of the problem in the left eye.
History Narrative: A 51-year-old African American man presented for a routine eye exam. He had no past ocular problems and no current visual complaints. Past medical history was remarkable for mild emphysema for which he used inhalers. Examination revealed visual acuities of 20/20 in each eye, with a refraction of –3.00 +2.25 × 110 OD and –2.25 +2.00 × 86 OS. The pupils reacted normally with no afferent pupillary defect. Anterior slit-lamp examination was unremarkable, and pressures by applanation tonometry were 26 mm Hg OD and 32 mm Hg OS. Gonioscopy was performed and the angles were noted to be grade IV in both eyes with a clear view of the ciliary body band for 360 degrees. There was 1+ pigment of the trabecular meshwork. On funduscopic exam, the optic nerves were as pictured in the report. Automated visual fields were also obtained.
Symptom Onset: Acute onset (sudden), involving the left eye (acute presentation)
Symptom Duration: Acute onset (no prior repeated brief episodes)
Characteristics: Narrow anterior chamber angle in right eye (occludable); acute primary angle‑closure glaucoma in left eye with iris bombé (midperipheral iris bowed anteriorly) and peripheral iris‑corneal touch; 1+ trabecular meshwork pigment; corneas clear enough for exam; optic nerves with glaucomatous changes (as pictured); IOPs 26 mmHg OD, 32 mmHg OS; no prior ocular problems; African American male, 51 years old; refraction myopic with moderate astigmatism (−3.00 +2.25 ×110 OD; −2.25 +2.00 ×86 OS)


Subjective (cont.)
Previous Episodes: The patient denied repeated, brief episodes and had no past ocular problems.
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities; patient had no visual complaints and 20/20 acuity in each eye.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: - Age (51 years) - Female sex: N/A (patient is male) - Hyperopia: N/A (patient is myopic) - African American race - Shallow/narrow anterior chamber angle (anatomic predisposition) - Pupillary block mechanism (iris bombe) - Lens-related factors: N/A (no lens findings) - Use of precipitating medications: None reported - History of prior intermittent attacks: None


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (Jaeger 1)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 26 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 32 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection or conjunctival abnormalities
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: OD: deep (grade IV), clear view of ciliary body band 360°; OS: shallow peripherally with iris bombé and peripheral iris–corneal touch (angle closed)
Anterior Segment – Iris: midperipheral iris bowed anteriorly with iris bombé and peripheral iris-cornea touch (iris bombe)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Right eye: grade IV (open, ciliary body band visible 360°); Left eye: closed (acute angle-closure with iris bombé and peripheral iris–cornea touch)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no abnormalities (no cataract or lens-induced changes)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: reactive, normal size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted normally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD
Pupils – Other: Pupils reacted normally with no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior slit-lamp examination unremarkable (no corneal edema, no anterior chamber cells/flare, iris normal)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Nerve cupping with increased cup-to-disc ratio consistent with glaucomatous optic neuropathy (as pictured), likely asymmetric with greater cupping OS than OD
Fundus Exam – Macula: No abnormalities detected
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No abnormalities of the retina or vessels.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma. Left eye: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma; Left eye: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Mild emphysema
Differential Diagnosis: Primary (pupillary-block) angle-closure glaucoma; Plateau iris configuration; Lens-induced (phacomorphic/ectopia) angle-closure glaucoma; Secondary angle-closure from neovascular/uveitic/choroidal effusion causes
Severity: Narrow anterior chamber angle, at risk for subsequent angle-closure glaucoma (fellow eye: prophylactic risk) — Left eye: Acute primary angle-closure glaucoma (acute, severe)
Laterality: Left eye


Plan
All Medications: isosorbide glycerol mannitol acetazolamide topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors pilocarpine 2% topical beta-blockers alpha-2 agonists prostaglandin analogs topical corticosteroids analgesics antiemetics
All Procedures: Laser iridotomy; prophylactic iridotomy in fellow eye; laser iridoplasty; therapeutic paracentesis; laser or surgical goniosynechialysis; cataract extraction (if lens component present)
Follow-Up Instructions: - Measure IOP within 30–120 minutes after laser surgery. - Re-examine within 1 week post-op (more frequently if inadequate response). - Re-examine again at 4–8 weeks post-op. - Taper topical corticosteroids during the postoperative period. - Perform pupillary dilation with post-dilation IOP check and gonioscopy to confirm angle remains open; consider provocative testing if indicated.
Patient Education: You have acute primary angle‑closure glaucoma in the left eye and a narrow, at‑risk angle in the right eye. Immediate goals are to lower the eye pressure and open the drainage angle to prevent vision loss. Initial medical treatment may include oral or IV osmotic agents (isosorbide, glycerol, or IV mannitol) and intravenous acetazolamide to rapidly reduce intraocular pressure, plus topical medications such as beta‑blockers or alpha‑agonists and pilocarpine to attempt to open the pupil if the iris can respond. Topical steroids reduce inflammation and analgesics/antiemetics relieve symptoms. The definitive treatment is a laser peripheral iridotomy (or iridoplasty if needed) to relieve pupillary block; the fellow eye usually receives a prophylactic iridotomy because it is at high risk. You will need close follow‑up after treatment (IOP checks within 30–120 minutes post‑procedure, at 1 week, and again at 4–8 weeks) and instruction to report worsening pain, vision loss, or nausea immediately. Avoid medications known to precipitate angle closure and bring a list of current drugs to your appointments.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic emergency care for sudden eye pain, decreased vision, nausea/vomiting, or colored halos; do not dilate the pupil; go to an ER or eye clinic promptly for urgent IOP-lowering treatment (osmotic agents, acetazolamide, topical meds) and urgent laser iridotomy or other surgical management as directed by the ophthalmologist.


CASE 22 — Pigmentary Glaucoma
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye
DURATION: several hours
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old woman presented with several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye. She reported acute onset of blurry vision and eye discomfort; vision was 20/20 OD and 20/60 OS. No prior similar episodes documented in the case.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old woman presented with a history of several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye. She reported no prior similar episodes mentioned. Examination at presentation showed vision of 20/20 in the right eye and 20/60 in the left eye. Intraocular pressures were 14 mm Hg in the right eye and 52 mm Hg in the left eye. The left pupil was sluggish and mid-dilated. She had mild conjunctival and episcleral vessel congestion, mild corneal epithelial edema, and a shallow peripheral anterior chamber. The midperipheral iris was markedly bowed anteriorly. The lens showed mild nuclear sclerosis. Gonioscopy of the left eye revealed marked convexity of the iris contour with no visible anterior chamber angle structures; gonioscopy of the right eye demonstrated an open, narrow anterior chamber angle. The optic nerve cups were small in both eyes. The left eye was treated with medical therapy and laser iridotomy, which opened the anterior chamber angle and reduced the IOP to normal.
Symptom Onset: several hours
Symptom Duration: several hours
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: - A form of secondary open-angle glaucoma caused by pigment liberation from posterior iris epithelium due to iris–zonule chafing (often with posterior iris bowing); affects young myopic men more commonly; features Krukenberg spindle on corneal endothelium, dense even trabecular meshwork pigmentation, Sampaolesi’s line, exercise-induced pigment release and IOP spikes, blurry vision with exercise; may present with acute IOP elevation or chronic IOP elevation leading to trabecular sclerosis and glaucomatous optic neuropathy.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exercise
Associated Symptoms: blurred vision, eye discomfort/pain, colored halos around lights, eye redness/congestion, blurry vision with exercise
Previous Episodes: several hours of discomfort and blurred vision in the left eye; no prior similar episodes documented
Prior Treatments: Medical therapy and laser peripheral iridotomy (LPI)
Impact on Daily Activities: Blurred vision with exercise and intermittent acute IOP spikes causing episodes of reduced vision (e.g., 20/60 in affected eye), likely intermittent functional limitation during exertion but otherwise mild impact on daily activities.
Risk Factors: - Young age (most commonly affects younger adults) - Male sex (affects men approximately twice as often as women) - Myopia (near-sightedness) - Exercise (can provoke pigment release and IOP spikes) - Family history of glaucoma (general increased risk)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (OD), N8 (OS)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 52 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mildly hyperemic
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild corneal epithelial edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Left eye: Shallow peripheral anterior chamber with absent peripheral anterior chamber (iris bombé); Right eye: Narrow, shallow anterior chamber.
Anterior Segment – Iris: Midperipheral iris markedly convex (iris bombé) with anterior bowing of midperipheral iris and middilated sluggish pupil; iris transillumination defects present.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Left eye: Closed (convex iris/iris bombé pre-iridotomy; angle opened after iridotomy). Right eye: Narrow but open.
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild nuclear sclerosis


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Left pupil middilated and sluggish; right pupil not specifically described (assumed normal).
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Left sluggish
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Sluggish
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: sluggish and middilated
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no EOM restriction or diplopia reported
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The conjunctiva and episclera were mildly hyperemic, and the cornea was mildly edematous. The pupil was middilated and the iris had a markedly convex configuration.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic nerves small in both eyes, small cups (no glaucomatous cupping described)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Periphery flat, no retinal breaks or detachments; no peripheral retinal degeneration or hemorrhage detected


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 55-year-old woman with acute left eye angle-closure attack manifested by mid-dilated pupil, conjunctival injection, corneal epithelial edema, shallow peripheral anterior chamber and markedly convex (bowed) iris with IOP 52 mm Hg OS and 14 mm Hg OD. Gonioscopy OS showed closed anterior chamber angle with no visible angle structures; OD had an open but narrow angle. Optic nerves small OU. After medical therapy and laser peripheral iridotomy OS, the angle opened and IOP normalized. Diagnosis: left eye acute angle-closure glaucoma secondary to pupillary block with iris bombé, successfully treated with laser iridotomy; fellow eye narrow/occludable angle—recommend prophylactic iridotomy. Manage IOP and follow for glaucomatous damage; consider gonioscopic surveillance and visual field testing when stable.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: Moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma with superior arcuate defect; Left eye: Moderate-stage pigmentary glaucoma with early nasal step defect
Secondary Diagnoses: Nuclear sclerosis (mild)
Differential Diagnosis: Primary open-angle glaucoma with heavy trabecular pigmentation; Pseudoexfoliative glaucoma; Uveitic glaucoma; Intraocular melanosis/melanoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Progressive glaucomatous optic nerve damage with visual field loss leading to irreversible vision loss; acute IOP spikes causing corneal edema and pain; trabecular meshwork sclerosis with chronic IOP elevation; medication/laser/surgical treatment–related complications (medication side effects, IOP spike after selective laser trabeculoplasty, surgical risks such as infection, hypotony, bleb failure, or implant-related complications).


Plan
All Medications: pilocarpine prostaglandin analogs beta-blockers carbonic anhydrase inhibitors alpha-adrenergic agonists
All Procedures: laser iridotomy argon laser trabeculoplasty (ALT) selective laser trabeculoplasty (SLT) trabeculectomy Ex-Press glaucoma mini-shunt implantation glaucoma drainage implant surgery iStent trabecular micro-bypass implantation gonioscopy-assisted transluminal trabeculotomy (GATT) endocyclophotocoagulation
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up individualized to treatment response and disease stability; schedule visits based on IOP control and progression risk, monitor visual acuity, IOP, gonioscopy, optic nerve appearance and visual fields at appropriate intervals; more frequent follow-up after treatment changes or inadequate control.
Patient Education: Pigmentary glaucoma is a type of open‑angle glaucoma caused by pigment from the back of the iris blocking drainage of eye fluid, which can raise eye pressure and damage the optic nerve. It most often affects younger, nearsighted men but can occur in others. Key points - Symptoms: blurry vision (sometimes worsened with exercise), eye discomfort, halos around lights, episodic eye redness. Some patients have no symptoms early on. - Why treatment matters: uncontrolled high eye pressure can cause irreversible vision loss. Regular monitoring and treatment aim to prevent progression. - Treatments: eye‑drop medications to lower pressure (prostaglandin analogs, beta‑blockers, carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, alpha agonists); laser trabeculoplasty to improve drainage; laser peripheral iridotomy in selected cases to reduce pigment release; and surgical options if other treatments fail. - Practical advice: use glaucoma medications exactly as prescribed; report side effects; bring all eye drops to visits; avoid heavy exercise if it causes vision changes until pressure is controlled; keep scheduled follow-ups for IOP checks, optic nerve exams, and visual fields. - When to seek urgent care: sudden severe eye pain, marked vision loss, persistent headache, or nausea/vomiting with eye symptoms—these may signal an acute pressure spike. - Follow‑up: regular visits are required—frequency depends on disease severity and response to treatment. Early detection and consistent treatment greatly reduce risk of vision loss.


CASE 23 — Neovascular Glaucoma
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Routine eye examination, no complaints
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old myopic man presented for a routine eye examination with no visual complaints and no significant past ocular or medical history; examination revealed elevated intraocular pressures (28 mm Hg OD, 26 mm Hg OS), vertically oriented pigment on the corneal endothelium, numerous spoke-like iris transillumination defects bilaterally, dense trabecular meshwork pigmentation with open angles, and optic nerve/RNFL and visual field changes consistent with early/moderate glaucomatous damage.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old myopic man presents for routine eye examination. He currently has no complaints and denies any significant past ocular or medical history.
Characteristics: Secondary angle-closure glaucoma with iris and trabecular meshwork neovascularization due to ocular ischemia (neovascular glaucoma)


Subjective (cont.)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely minimal — patient is asymptomatic with 20/20 acuity and no complaints, so daily activities unaffected.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies any systemic symptoms
Risk Factors: Diabetic retinopathy; retinal vein occlusion (eg, central retinal vein occlusion); retinal artery occlusion; carotid occlusive disease; sickle cell retinopathy; chronic uveitis; intraocular tumor; chronic retinal detachment; ocular ischemia.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: best corrected
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 26 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Narrow/closed (angle-closure from fibrovascular membrane)
Anterior Segment – Iris: numerous spokelike iris neovascularization with transillumination defects and ectropion uveae features described (fine capillary tufts at the pupillary margin progressing toward iris root)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (360 degrees)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: no afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Pupils – Reaction to Light: no afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect in either eye
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: vertically oriented pigment deposition on each corneal endothelium and numerous spokelike iris transillumination defects in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Right optic nerve: cup-to-disc ratio ~0.7 with inferotemporal rim thinning; Left optic nerve: cup-to-disc ratio ~0.55
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma (NVG) with elevated IOP due to anterior segment neovascularization; requires urgent IOP-lowering therapy, evaluation/treatment of underlying ischemic retinal cause (e.g., panretinal photocoagulation if view permits) and consideration of intravitreal anti-VEGF; likely need for glaucoma drainage device surgery if IOP not controlled medically.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute ocular hypertension secondary to neovascular glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Neovascular glaucoma (secondary to ischemic retinal disease, e.g., CRVO or proliferative diabetic retinopathy); Acute angle-closure glaucoma; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; Traumatic/angle-recession glaucoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; topical beta-blockers; topical alpha-2 agonists; topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; cholinergic agents (miotics); prostaglandin analogs; hyperosmotic agents; topical prednisolone; topical cycloplegics; intravitreal anti‑VEGF agent
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up with ophthalmology urgently for IOP control and treatment planning; arrange prompt panretinal photocoagulation if posterior view adequate or intravitreal anti‑VEGF injection once retinal detachment/tumor ruled out; monitor IOP, optic nerve status, visual fields, and OCT serially (at least every few weeks initially) and schedule glaucoma surgery (aqueous shunt) if IOP not controlled medically.
Patient Education: Explain that neovascular glaucoma (NVG) is a secondary form of glaucoma caused by abnormal new blood vessels in the eye that can block fluid drainage and rapidly raise eye pressure, potentially damaging vision. Emphasize the need for prompt treatment to lower intraocular pressure (medications, sometimes oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, and possibly urgent procedures) and to treat the underlying cause of ischemia (e.g., panretinal photocoagulation or intravitreal anti-VEGF injections). Describe common symptoms that should prompt immediate care (eye pain, redness, sudden vision change, headache, nausea/vomiting). Explain that medical therapy often controls pressure temporarily but surgical drainage devices are frequently required; if neovascularization is active, anti-VEGF may be given before surgery to reduce bleeding risk. Advise adherence to medications, follow-up appointments, and testing (IOP checks, optic nerve photos, visual fields, OCT). Discuss potential side effects/precautions for key treatments (oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors—watch renal function; hyperosmotics caution in diabetics/heart disease; possible transient vision changes after injections). Stress prevention: control systemic vascular disease (diabetes, hypertension), retina evaluation for ischemia, and regular eye exams if at risk. Provide guidance on pain management (topical steroids and cycloplegics, antiemetics for nausea) and explain prognosis: early detection and treatment improve chances of preserving vision, but advanced disease may require multiple interventions and may have guarded prognosis. Encourage immediate contact/emergency visit for severe pain or sudden vision loss.
Emergency Instructions: Lower intraocular pressure immediately and seek urgent ophthalmology care. If severe pain, vision loss, or nausea/vomiting occur, go to the emergency department. Do not take oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors if you have kidney disease or sickle cell disease without physician approval. Apply prescribed topical IOP-lowering drops as directed, use antiemetics if needed, and avoid heavy exertion. Urgent treatments may include oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, topical aqueous suppressants, topical steroids and cycloplegics for comfort, anterior chamber paracentesis if IOP is dangerously high, and intravitreal anti‑VEGF plus prompt retinal evaluation for panretinal photocoagulation or surgical drainage (tube shunt) as indicated. Seek immediate care if symptoms worsen or do not improve.


CASE 24 — Inflammatory Glaucoma
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: a bad headache on the right side of my head, extreme redness and light sensitivity of the right eye, and nausea/vomiting
DURATION: Half a day
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "a bad headache on the right side of my head." "sick to my stomach." "a bad headache on the right side of my head." "pink eye" "colored halos around lights."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 72-year-old man with sudden 12-hour history of severe right-sided headache, extreme redness, photophobia, nausea with one episode of vomiting, and rapid onset ocular symptoms. He reports prior abrupt, painless severe vision loss in the right eye ~3 months ago diagnosed as ischemic central retinal vein occlusion. Current right eye vision hand motions; examination notable for severe corneal edema, conjunctival injection, small hyphema, cell and flare in anterior chamber, minimally reactive iris with fine irregular branching surface vessels, and markedly elevated IOP (67 mm Hg) in the right eye. Left eye asymptomatic.
History Narrative: A 72-year-old man presents to the emergency department complaining of “a bad headache on the right side of my head.” He also notes extreme redness and light sensitivity of the right eye and feels “sick to my stomach.” His symptoms have developed quickly over half a day. Past medical history is remarkable for hypertension, diabetes, and arteriosclerosis. A review of systems is positive for nausea and one episode of vomiting. The patient had experienced an abrupt, painless, severe decline in vision in the right eye approximately 3 months earlier and was diagnosed at that time with an ischemic central retinal vein occlusion by his ophthalmologist.
Symptom Onset: half a day
Symptom Duration: half a day
Progression: Acute presentation with rapid onset over half a day; prior painless severe vision loss 3 months earlier from CRVO; current NVG with markedly elevated IOP (right eye) indicating rapid/acute progression to secondary glaucoma.
Characteristics: Acute inflammatory secondary open‑angle glaucoma with severe IOP elevation, corneal edema, conjunctival injection, anterior chamber cell/flare and small hyphema, iris neovascularization, minimally reactive pupil, unilateral severe vision loss and systemic symptoms (nausea/vomiting).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nausea and vomiting, corneal edema, hyphema, iris neovascularization, prior central retinal vein occlusion, active intraocular inflammation (uveitis)
Associated Symptoms: bad headache on the right side, extreme redness of the right eye, light sensitivity (photophobia) of the right eye, nausea, vomiting, blurry vision (severe decline in vision)
Previous Episodes: Abrupt, painless, severe decline in vision in the right eye ~3 months earlier (ischemic CRVO); no prior recurrent uveitis episodes reported.
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe — acute vision loss and pain (right eye hand motions acuity, severe corneal edema, photophobia, headache, nausea/vomiting, IOP 67 mmHg) likely markedly limits daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: nausea and one episode of vomiting, feeling “sick to my stomach,” headache on the right side
Risk Factors: Hypertension, diabetes, arteriosclerosis, prior ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (leading to retinal ischemia/neovascularization)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 150/88 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 67 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 19 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Severe conjunctival injection
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or extraocular motion abnormalities noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Severe corneal edema with a “steamy” appearance
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Small hyphema with cell and flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: fine, irregular branching/stellate surface vessels with patchy iris atrophy; pupil minimally reactive (no iris bombe)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Obscured by corneal edema in the right eye; wide open in the left eye.
