Pupils – Reaction to Light: minimally reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Minimally reactive
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect (Marcus Gunn)
Pupils – Other: minimally reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in both eyes with no limitation of extraocular movements
Slit Lamp Exam Description: severe corneal edema, conjunctival injection, small hyphema, cell and flare in the anterior chamber; iris minimally reactive with fine irregular branching surface vessels and patchy atrophy; anterior chamber view limited by corneal edema
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: appears normal in the left eye; right optic disc not visualized due to corneal edema (only a dull red reflex)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Only a dull red reflex appreciated on ophthalmoscopy of the right eye; right fundus not visible due to corneal edema. Left fundus notable only for severe hypertensive retinopathy (no specific macular findings described).
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Severe hypertensive retinopathy


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis with secondary open‑angle inflammatory glaucoma of the right eye, presenting with marked corneal edema, hyphema, iris neovascularization, and a critically elevated IOP (67 mm Hg). Clinical picture most consistent with neovascular glaucoma secondary to prior ischemic CRVO, with associated inflammatory findings suggestive of herpetic iridocyclitis; left eye without acute findings (IOP 19 mm Hg) and notable for hypertensive retinopathy. Management priorities: control intraocular inflammation with topical/systemic corticosteroids and antiviral therapy as indicated, aggressive IOP lowering with aqueous suppressants and adjunctive agents, cycloplegia for comfort and synechiae prevention, and urgent consideration of surgical intervention (glaucoma drainage device or filtering surgery) if IOP remains uncontrolled, while treating underlying ischemic/neovascular drive (panretinal photocoagulation) when feasible. Close follow‑up with gonioscopy, optic nerve/visual field monitoring, and imaging as inflammation and corneal clarity permit.
Primary Diagnosis: Herpes simplex anterior uveitis
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; arteriosclerosis; ischemic central retinal vein occlusion (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Neovascular glaucoma from ischemic CRVO; Herpetic anterior uveitis (herpes simplex/zoster) with secondary open-angle glaucoma; Fuchs’ heterochromic iridocyclitis; Posner–Schlossman syndrome (glaucomatocyclitic crisis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: band keratopathy; cystoid macular edema (CME); steroid‑induced IOP elevation; posterior synechiae; peripheral anterior synechiae (PAS); secondary angle closure; glaucomatous optic neuropathy; bleb failure; intensified postoperative inflammation (after cyclophotocoagulation); reactivation of herpes simplex keratouveitis


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone 1%; dexamethasone 0.1%; rimexolone; loteprednol; flurbiprofen; ketorolac; diclofenac; methotrexate; azathioprine; timolol; levobunolol; dorzolamide; brinzolamide; apraclonidine; dipivefrin; latanoprost (prostaglandin analogs); trifluridine; acyclovir; atropine; cyclopentolate; 5‑fluorouracil; mitomycin C
All Procedures: gonioscopy slit-lamp examination ophthalmoscopy B-scan ultrasonography serologic workup body imaging topical corticosteroid therapy (prednisolone 1%, dexamethasone 0.1%, rimexolone, loteprednol) topical nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory agents (flurbiprofen, ketorolac, diclofenac) systemic immunosuppressive therapy (methotrexate, azathioprine) topical aqueous suppressants (timolol, levobunolol) topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (dorzolamide, brinzolamide) topical adrenergic agonists (apraclonidine, dipivefrin) prostaglandin analog therapy (latanoprost) topical trifluridine oral acyclovir cycloplegic agents (atropine, cyclopentolate) laser peripheral iridotomy surgical iridectomy trabeculectomy with antimetabolite (5-fluorouracil or mitomycin C) aqueous drainage device implantation (Ahmed valve, Molteno implant) laser trabeculoplasty (contraindicated in active inflammation) laser cyclophotocoagulation
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequent follow-up with serial IOP checks, slit‑lamp and gonioscopic examinations to monitor inflammation, cornea, angle neovascularization/PAS; optic nerve evaluation and visual field testing to detect glaucomatous progression; adjust anti‑inflammatory and IOP medications as needed; monitor for complications (CME, band keratopathy); defer glaucoma surgery until inflammation controlled unless IOP dangerously elevated despite maximal medical therapy.
Patient Education: Your eye pain, redness, nausea, and very high eye pressure are caused by inflammation and abnormal new blood-vessel growth in the affected eye (neovascular/inflammatory glaucoma). Immediate goals are to lower the pressure to prevent optic nerve damage and to control the inflammation and any underlying eye disease. What to expect now - Urgent medical treatment to lower IOP (eye drops and possibly oral medication). You may have temporary blurry vision from corneal swelling. - Anti-inflammatory treatment (frequent steroid eye drops and possibly antiviral or other medications if infection is suspected). - Cycloplegic drops (e.g., atropine) to relieve pain and prevent synechiae. - Additional tests (gonioscopy, imaging, bloodwork) to find the cause of the inflammation and new vessels. - If medications do not control the pressure, laser or surgery (glaucoma drainage device or filtering surgery) may be recommended. Important warnings - Very high pressure can cause permanent vision loss; take medications exactly as prescribed and come to all follow-up visits. - If you develop worsening vision, severe eye pain, increasing headaches, nausea/vomiting, or new light sensitivity, seek care immediately. Self-care and follow-up - Keep scheduled ophthalmology appointments; frequent visits are often needed initially. - Avoid rubbing the eye. - Bring a list of all current medications and medical conditions (e.g., diabetes, vascular disease). - If prescribed topical steroids or antivirals, use them as directed and do not stop abruptly without instruction. Long-term issues - This condition may require ongoing monitoring and sometimes surgery; glaucoma can develop even after inflammation settles. - Control of systemic diseases (diabetes, hypertension) and treatment of the underlying cause of uveitis are important to reduce recurrence. If you have questions about your medications or what to expect with treatment, ask your ophthalmologist or clinic staff before you leave.


CASE 25 — Primary Congenital Glaucoma
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye for 2 days
DURATION: 2 days
LATERALITY: Left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 38-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain of the left eye.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-day history of blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain involving his left eye. His ocular history is notable for one episode of herpes simplex keratitis involving the left eye, which occurred 12 months ago and which resolved after treatment with trifluridine eye drops.
Symptom Onset: 2 days
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Characteristics: Primary congenital glaucoma: corneal enlargement with clouding, epiphora, photophobia, elevated IOP, ocular enlargement (buphthalmos), Haab’s striae (Descemet breaks), high flat iris insertion with peripheral stromal thinning, onset within first 2 years of life (often bilateral), autosomal recessive inheritance tendency


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Elevated intraocular pressure (ocular hypertension / glaucoma)
Associated Symptoms: blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, pain
Previous Episodes: One episode of herpes simplex keratitis in the left eye 12 months ago
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — activities limited by photophobia, tearing, blurred vision in affected eye; can see well with right eye (20/20) so overall daily function mostly preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Autosomal recessive inheritance (family history in 10–40%), consanguinity, male sex slight predilection, early infancy (presentation within first 2 years), certain ethnic/consanguineous populations


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation and central, steady, maintained (CSM) testing
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 46 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen (hand-held applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal conjunctiva and sclera (no redness or injection noted)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Both corneas enlarged (right > left) with left moderate central corneal haze and right subtle epithelial edema; Haab’s striae (horizontal Descemet breaks) present.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep centrally and peripherally
Anterior Segment – Iris: Patchy iris atrophy with transillumination defects; no iris nodules, heterochromia, or posterior synechiae
Anterior Segment – Angle: open to the ciliary band with moderate pigmentation and prominent iris processes
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye unremarkable. Left cornea with fine, stellate keratic precipitates scattered diffusely; anterior chamber deep centrally and peripherally with 2+ cells and aqueous flare; patchy iris pupillary sphincter atrophy on transillumination; no iris nodules, heterochromia, or posterior synechiae. Lens clear.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Optic discs were symmetric with healthy rims
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits; peripheral retina without lesions or pathology


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 38-year-old Caucasian man with a history of prior left-herpetic keratitis presented with 2 days of left eye blurry vision, photophobia, tearing, and pain. Exam shows OD 20/20, OS 20/60 improving to 20/40 with pinhole. IOPs: OD 12 mmHg, OS 46 mmHg. Left slit-lamp: fine stellate keratic precipitates, 2+ anterior chamber cells and flare, patchy iris pupillary sphincter atrophy on transillumination, deep anterior chamber, clear lens. Gonioscopy: angles open to ciliary band OU, moderate TM pigmentation and prominent iris processes, no PAS. Fundus: clear vitreous and symmetric healthy optic discs. Clinical picture consistent with unilateral herpetic anterior uveitis with secondary marked elevation of IOP (inflammatory glaucoma) in the left eye. Differential includes other causes of corneal haze and pediatric glaucomas in general discussion, but given history and findings, herpetic keratouveitis causing secondary glaucoma is most likely. Immediate management: lower IOP medically (topical/oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, careful beta‑blocker use) and initiate appropriate antiviral/anti-inflammatory therapy as indicated, with prompt ophthalmic follow-up and consideration of further intervention if IOP not controlled.
Primary Diagnosis: Primary congenital glaucoma
Differential Diagnosis: Primary congenital glaucoma; Peters anomaly (anterior segment dysgenesis); congenital hereditary endothelial dystrophy; secondary glaucoma from ocular tumor (masquerade syndrome)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: trifluridine; carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (dorzolamide, oral acetazolamide); selective beta‑blockers; topical alpha‑2 agonists (contraindicated in infants); topical cycloplegics; topical steroids; brimonidine
All Procedures: goniotomy; trabeculotomy; examination under anesthesia; Tonopen/hand‑held applanation IOP measurement (Perkins); A‑scan ultrasonography (axial length measurement); dilated funduscopic examination and stereoscopic disc photography; topical and oral medical therapy (preoperative) including topical carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, beta‑blockers, oral acetazolamide; placement of glaucoma drainage device (Baerveldt shunt); trabeculectomy with mitomycin C (rare); cyclodestructive procedures; topical cycloplegics and steroids (postoperative)
Follow-Up Instructions: After initial exam under anesthesia and angle surgery: topical cycloplegics and steroids for 1–2 weeks; repeat EUA at 8–10 weeks with all measurements and dilated fundus/disc photos if IOP acceptable. Subsequent EUAs q2–3 months until IOP stable or patient can be examined in clinic; then clinic exams every 3–4 months, later every 6 months once stable. By age 3–4 years, transition to office examination.
Referrals: Pediatrician and anesthesiologist clearance for general anesthesia; surgeon experienced in pediatric angle surgery (glaucoma specialist)


CASE 26 — Ocular Hypotony
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: "Cloudy eyes and tearing"
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "cloudy eyes and tearing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-month-old female infant referred for 3–4 weeks of bilateral “cloudy eyes and tearing”; photophobic and more comfortable in dim light; diffuse corneal haze OU (R>L); estimated corneal diameters 12 mm OD and 11 mm OS; IOPs measured with iCare while nursing 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old female infant was referred after her mother noted “cloudy eyes and tearing” for 3 to 4 weeks. The child was full-term with an uneventful pregnancy and delivery without use of forceps. There was no known family history of glaucoma. She had a 3-year-old brother without ocular problems. On office exam she was photophobic and more comfortable in dim illumination. Both eyes had diffuse corneal haze, right greater than left. Estimated corneal diameters were 12 mm OD and 11 mm OS. IOP measured with iCare while the infant nursed on a bottle was 45 mm Hg OD and 36 mm Hg OS.
Symptom Onset: 3 to 4 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 weeks
Progression: Resolved after surgical revision (improved to low‑teens IOP; no recurrent hypotony at 6 years)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Photophobia, tearing, cloudy eyes, shallow anterior chamber, maculopathy (chorioretinal folds), disc swelling
Prior Treatments: Cycloplegic agents; autologous blood patch; two additional scleral flap sutures (surgical revision)
Risk Factors: - Filtration surgery (trabeculectomy) postoperative state - Use of antimetabolites leading to thin, highly filtering bleb - Early postoperative period before conjunctival contraction - Wound leak (bleb leak) - Cyclodialysis cleft (surgical trauma) - Overuse or continued use of aqueous suppressant medications - Inflammation (postoperative or chronic) - Ocular ischemia - Occult globe perforation (surgical/retrobulbar injury)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mm Hg
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 45 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 36 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: iCare rebound tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Diffuse corneal haze (OU), R>L; corneal diameters: OD 12 mm, OS 11 mm
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Shallow
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (gonioscopy performed; cyclodialysis ruled out)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Both eyes had a diffuse corneal haze, the right more than the left.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Disc swelling and chorioretinal folds
Fundus Exam – Macula: Maculopathy with chorioretinal folds
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration following filtration surgery, with associated maculopathy (disc swelling and chorioretinal folds). Initial medical measures including cycloplegia and topical therapy failed; autologous blood patch was ineffective. Surgical revision with placement of two additional scleral flap sutures restored IOP into the 20s, with subsequent suture lysis lowering IOP to 13 mm Hg and normalization of axial length, refraction, and visual acuity. Long-term IOP has remained in the low teens with no recurrent hypotony.
Primary Diagnosis: Hypotony due to primary overfiltration
Differential Diagnosis: Primary overfiltration; Hyposecretion of aqueous (underproduction); Bleb leak; Cyclodialysis cleft
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: aqueous suppressants, cycloplegic agents, topical corticosteroids, autologous blood
All Procedures: Seidel test; fundoscopy; gonioscopy; iCare IOP measurement; examination under anesthesia (pressure measurement, corneal diameter measurement, ultrasonography including axial length and biomicroscopy, anterior segment exam, gonioscopy, dilated fundus exam/optic disc photos); cycloplegic therapy; discontinuation of aqueous suppressants; topical corticosteroids; bandage contact lens; autologous blood patch; bleb compression sutures (external/mattress sutures); Nd:YAG laser bleb remodeling; resuturing of scleral flap via transconjunctival sutures or open bleb revision (dissection to bare sclera and direct scleral flap suturing); reformation of anterior chamber with viscoelastic; suture lysis
Follow-Up Instructions: Cycloplegic agents and conservative observation; if persistent, autologous blood patch (failed in this case) then surgical revision with additional scleral flap sutures; postoperative follow-up with IOP monitoring, axial length/refraction/visual acuity checks, and suture lysis as indicated (performed 1 month postrevision in this case)


CASE 27 — PosttrabeculectomyWound Leak
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: worsening vision
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "worsening vision"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 45-year-old woman with inflammatory glaucoma in the aphakic right eye referred for surgical management; preop vision 20/20 with aphakic correction, IOP 40 mm Hg on maximum medical therapy, 1+ AC cell/flare and fine KPs, vitreous at pupillary plane, cup-to-disc 0.9 with inferior notch and superior altitudinal field defect. Underwent trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy; postoperative IOP 9–12 mm Hg for first year. One year after surgery developed worsening vision: VA decreased to 20/60 (improving to 20/20 with +1.50 D), highly elevated avascular cystic bleb, moderate chorioretinal folds at posterior pole and macula, IOP 3 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman was referred for surgical management of inflammatory glaucoma in her aphakic right eye. Her left eye had only light perception since birth due to persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous. Preoperative visual acuity with aphakic correction was 20/20. Intraocular pressure (IOP) was 40 mm Hg on maximum medical therapy including oral agents. Central corneal thickness was 535 μm in each eye. There was 1+ cell and flare in the anterior chamber with scattered, fine keratic precipitates. There was vitreous at the pupillary plane. The optic disc was markedly excavated with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.9 and a notch inferiorly. The visual field had a superior altitudinal defect. A trabeculectomy with mitomycin C and subtotal vitrectomy was performed without incident. Postoperatively, visual acuity remained 20/20. IOP was well controlled in the range of 9 to 12 mm Hg for the first postoperative year. One year after surgery, the patient presented with worsening vision. Visual acuity was 20/60, which improved to 20/20 with an increase in hyperopic correction of +1.50 diopters. The bleb was highly elevated, avascular, and cystic. The remainder of the examination was unchanged from baseline except for moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula. The IOP was 3 mm Hg.
Symptom Onset: 1 year after surgery
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Worsening vision over 1 year with development of late bleb leak and hypotony/maculopathy
Characteristics: Highly elevated, avascular, cystic bleb, Seidel-negative (late bleb leak), large and ischemic.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C) leading to thin, avascular, cystic bleb and chronic hypotony
Associated Symptoms: worsening vision, hyperopic shift (+1.50 D), chorioretinal folds involving posterior pole and macula, hypotony symptoms (visual acuity decrease to 20/60)
Prior Treatments: maximum medical therapy including oral agents
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — temporary visual blurring (20/60, correctable to 20/20 with +1.50D) and metamorphopsia from macular folds causing decreased visual function until hypotony resolved; risk of lasting visual impairment if untreated.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Use of antimetabolites (mitomycin C/5‑FU) leading to thin, avascular/cystic blebs; young age; high myopia; prior trabeculectomy/overfiltration; aphakia; inflammatory eye disease; prolonged topical corticosteroid use; conjunctival buttonholes/trauma; bleb ischemia/thin conjunctiva


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 3 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Thin, avascular, highly elevated, cystic bleb overlying sclera (ischemic conjunctiva)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: NAD
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal decompensation; central corneal thickness 535 μm in each eye
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal depth
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open with no neovascularization; gonioscopy excluded a cyclodialysis cleft.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The bleb was highly elevated, avascular, and cystic; eye quiet and noninflamed; Seidel-negative.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: markedly excavated with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.9 and an inferior notch
Fundus Exam – Macula: Moderate chorioretinal folds involving the posterior pole and macula
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Engorgement and tortuosity
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal detachment; peripheral fundus otherwise unremarkable (no hemorrhage or lesions)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Late bleb leak with chronic hypotony and hypotony maculopathy in the right aphakic eye following trabeculectomy with mitomycin C. Clinical findings: large, elevated, avascular cystic bleb (Seidel-negative), IOP 3 mm Hg, decreased vision to 20/60 reversible to 20/20 with +1.50 D hyperopic shift, moderate chorioretinal (macular) folds, axial length shortened by 0.75 mm, quiet eye without intraocular inflammation, gonioscopy negative for cyclodialysis cleft, no retinal or choroidal detachment, not on aqueous suppressants. Recommended management directed at reducing excessive aqueous outflow and resurfacing the ischemic bleb (initial conservative measures: discontinue aqueous suppressants, observation/bandage contact lens, consider autologous blood injection or laser/needle interventions; definitive surgical bleb revision or conjunctival advancement/patch graft if persistent).
Primary Diagnosis: Late bleb leak
Secondary Diagnoses: Inflammatory glaucoma; Aphakia; Persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous (left eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Late bleb leak; Overfiltration; Choroidal effusion (hypotony-related)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right
Complications: - hypotony - chorioretinal folds (hypotony maculopathy) - decreased visual acuity (reversible with refraction) - potential cataract - corneal decompensation - synechiae - choroidal effusions - blebitis/endophthalmitis


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors topical corticosteroids topical phenylephrine beta-blockers
All Procedures: Trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; Subtotal vitrectomy; Seidel test; Gonioscopy; Autologous blood injection (as a described treatment option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow closely for recurrent leaks if an ischemic bleb remains; monitor IOP, visual acuity, and maculopathy; if definitive surgical repair was successful, continue routine postoperative visits as risk of recurrence will be low.


CASE 28 — Failing Filtering Bleb
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Failing Filtering Bleb
DURATION: 5 months
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye; five months postoperatively she presented with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye, with best-corrected visual acuity 20/100 OD, a very shallow anterior chamber and intraocular pressure 2 mm Hg.
History Narrative: A 59-year-old woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. The scleral flap was closed with two 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule was closed with a running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture, and the conjunctiva was closed with a running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture. Five months postoperatively she presented with a dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye; best-corrected visual acuity was 20/100 OD and 20/30 OS, the anterior chamber OD was very shallow, and IOP OD was 2 mm Hg.
Symptom Onset: 5 months postoperative
Symptom Duration: 5 months
Progression: Late filtration failure
Characteristics: Late filtration failure; flat (low) filtering bleb with very shallow anterior chamber and hypotony (IOP 2 mm Hg)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Young age; inflammatory conditions (e.g., uveitis); prior failed filtering or other conjunctival surgery; neovascular glaucoma; aphakia; iatrogenic factors (excessive cautery, poor hemorrhage control, excessive intraocular manipulation); race
Associated Symptoms: dramatic decrease in vision in the right eye
Prior Treatments: Trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C (limbus‑based trabeculectomy with MMC)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual decrease in right eye (20/100) causing impairment but not complete loss; likely interferes with activities requiring good vision (reading, driving) while left eye functions at 20/30.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: young age; race; inflammatory conditions such as uveitis; history of prior failed filtering procedure or other conjunctival procedure; neovascular glaucoma; aphakia


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 36.8 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J2+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart, best-corrected (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 2 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Conjunctiva intact with flat filtering bleb; no external leak; conjunctiva over bleb likely scarred/fibrotic (episcleral fibrosis)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Very shallow anterior chamber in the right eye; cornea not otherwise abnormal (no corneal edema or pathology reported).
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: very shallow
Anterior Segment – Angle: very shallow/flat (shallow anterior chamber with likely closed/very narrow angle)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive (normal size)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full and unrestricted
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Flat filtering bleb; very shallow anterior chamber in the right eye; conjunctiva over bleb appears low/flat; possibly hypotony signs (shallow AC)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Advanced cupping consistent with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 59-year-old woman with history of limbus‑based trabeculectomy with MMC in the right eye presenting 5 months postoperatively with dramatic decrease in vision OD. Exam: BCVA 20/100 OD, shallow anterior chamber OD, IOP 2 mm Hg OD, flat filtering bleb on slit‑lamp. Diagnosis: late filtration failure with hypotony and shallow anterior chamber (failed/flat bleb) OD; consider differential including overfiltration, bleb leak, or bleb wall fibrosis/encapsulation; plan evaluation with fluorescein for leak, gonioscopy to assess sclerostomy patency, and management likely surgical (needle revision or more aggressive bleb revision) with close follow‑up and possible IOP‑lowering meds as needed.
Primary Diagnosis: Late filtration failure
Differential Diagnosis: Encapsulated filtering bleb (Tenon’s cyst); Tight scleral flap sutures; Occlusion of the sclerostomy (tissue incarceration or membrane over ostium); Episcleral fibrosis/bleb scarring
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Hypotony (very shallow anterior chamber, IOP 2 mm Hg)


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin C 10–0 nylon 8–0 Vicryl (dissolvable suture) topical anti-inflammatory agents IOP-lowering medication
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C application; scleral flap closure with 10–0 nylon sutures; Tenon’s capsule closure with running locking 8–0 Vicryl suture; conjunctival closure with running nonlocking 8–0 dissolvable suture; argon laser suture lysis; releasable suture release; suture lysis with suture lysis lens; digital ocular massage; gonioscopy; needle revision of bleb; surgical revision of filtering bleb
Follow-Up Instructions: Once the IOP is controlled, follow closely for signs of recurrent failure; continue regular IOP checks and clinical bleb examinations; maintain IOP-lowering medications as needed even after successful surgical revision.


CASE 29 — Flat Anterior Chamber
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Flat anterior chamber
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: right eye
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 53‑year‑old African American woman with advanced primary open‑angle glaucoma (target IOP 15 mm Hg) who underwent a limbus‑based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye; at 1 year postop IOP was at target with a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts, and three months later she presented with IOP 28 mm Hg, a deep anterior chamber but an almost flat filtering bleb, later found to have central and peripheral shallowing of the anterior chamber with a patent peripheral iridectomy and no choroidal effusion or bleb leak, consistent with aqueous misdirection.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old African American woman with advanced primary open-angle glaucoma and a target IOP of 15 mm Hg underwent a limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C in the right eye. At 1 year postop the IOP was at target with a moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts. Three months later she presented with IOP of 28 mm Hg; the anterior chamber was flat; the filtering bleb was almost flat. There was a patent peripheral iridectomy, no bleb or wound leak, the eye was not hypotonous, and funduscopy and ultrasound demonstrated no choroidal elevation. The ciliary processes were easily visible through the iridectomy. Diagnosis: aqueous misdirection (ciliary block) of the right eye.
Symptom Onset: 3 months after the 1-year postoperative visit (i.e., 15 months after surgery)
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progression: worsened over time, with initial IOP at target at 1 year, then 3 months later IOP elevated to 28 mm Hg with flat bleb, subsequently diagnosis of aqueous misdirection with progressive shallowing and possible further IOP elevation if untreated.
Characteristics: Aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma): central and peripheral anterior chamber shallowing with patent peripheral iridectomy, anterior rotation of ciliary body, no choroidal elevations, normal or elevated IOP, visible ciliary processes through iridectomy, occurs after intraocular surgery in eyes predisposed to angle closure.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Prior laser or incisional glaucoma surgery (trabeculectomy with mitomycin C), short axial length/angle-closure–predisposed eye, discontinuation of cycloplegics, use of miotics, anterior rotation of ciliary body from hypotony/overfiltration or choroidal effusion
Alleviating Factors: Cycloplegics (e.g., atropine 1%) with phenylephrine, topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors, oral/IV osmotic agents, intracameral viscoelastic injection, Nd:YAG or argon laser procedures (anterior hyaloid disruption or ciliary process shrinkage), and pars plana vitrectomy with anterior vitreous removal.
Associated Symptoms: Flat (very shallow) central and peripheral anterior chamber; elevated intraocular pressure (IOP 28 mm Hg); patent peripheral iridectomy; no iris bombé; no choroidal effusion or hemorrhage; visible ciliary processes through iridectomy; prior trabeculectomy with mitomycin C; failing/low flat bleb with microcysts earlier; possible vision pain not described explicitly.
Prior Treatments: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C
Risk Factors: - Short axial length - Predisposition to angle-closure glaucoma (chronic angle-closure) - Prior laser or incisional intraocular surgery (e.g., trabeculectomy) - Phakic status (presence of lens) - Use of antimetabolites (e.g., mitomycin C) contributing to bleb changes - Discontinuation of cycloplegics - Eyes with prior filtration surgery and postoperative hypotony/overfiltration (as precursor)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart with correction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 28 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Flat (very shallow)—central and peripheral
Anterior Segment – Iris: Patency of peripheral iridectomy; iris normal without iris bombé; ciliary processes visible through iridectomy
Anterior Segment – Angle: Closed (peripheral anterior chamber shallowing with central shallowing due to aqueous misdirection causing angle closure)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive
Pupils – RAPD: none
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no extraocular movement restriction.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderately elevated diffuse ischemic bleb with microcysts at 1 year post‑op; at presentation 3 months later bleb was almost flat. Anterior chamber was deep at 1 year visit and later centrally and peripherally very shallow/flat (uniformly shallow) with patent peripheral iridectomy; no iris bombé; ciliary processes visible through iridectomy; no bleb or wound leak.
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular abnormality detected
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No choroidal elevations; fundus otherwise unremarkable in the periphery


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Patent peripheral iridectomy present with uniform central and peripheral shallowing of the anterior chamber, no iris bombé, normal-to-elevated IOP (28 mm Hg), no bleb or wound leak, no hypotony, fundus and ultrasound without choroidal elevation, ciliary processes visible through the iridectomy. Findings most consistent with aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma).
Primary Diagnosis: Aqueous misdirection
Differential Diagnosis: Aqueous misdirection (ciliary block/malignant glaucoma); Overfiltration leading to hypotony with anterior rotation; Serous choroidal effusion; Pupillary block
Severity: Advanced
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Aqueous misdirection (malignant glaucoma) leading to a flat/very shallow anterior chamber with potential elevated IOP; possible need for laser or pars plana vitrectomy; risk of recurrence requiring long-term cycloplegia.


Plan
All Medications: Atropine 1% (cycloplegic–mydriatic) Phenylephrine 2.5% Topical aqueous suppressants (eg, beta-blocker or alpha agonist — unspecified) Oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitor Oral or intravenous osmotic agent Miotics (avoid) Intracameral viscoelastic (for anterior chamber reformation) Mitomycin C (intraoperative)
All Procedures: limbus-based trabeculectomy with intraoperative mitomycin C
Follow-Up Instructions: Maintain cycloplegia (e.g., atropine 1% daily) — may be indefinite; avoid miotics; monitor for recurrence (re-evaluate anterior chamber depth and IOP periodically); in fellow eye consider prophylactic laser iridotomy and avoid miotics; if vitrectomy performed, cycloplegia may be stopped with caution in phakic eyes.
Patient Education: You have a condition called aqueous misdirection (also called ciliary block or malignant glaucoma), which causes the front chamber of your eye to become very shallow and can raise eye pressure. It most often occurs after glaucoma surgery. Initial treatment is medical: eye drops to dilate and relax the eye (cycloplegics/mydriatics such as atropine), medications to reduce eye fluid production (topical aqueous suppressants and oral carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), and sometimes an osmotic agent to reduce fluid in the back of the eye. Miotic drops (which constrict the pupil) should be avoided because they can make the problem worse. If medicines and laser treatments (YAG to open the anterior hyaloid or argon to shrink ciliary processes) do not help, a vitrectomy (surgical removal/disruption of vitreous) may be needed to restore normal fluid flow. You may need to continue dilating drops long-term to prevent recurrence. If you have symptoms such as increasing eye pain, vision loss, or a very shallow front chamber, seek urgent ophthalmic care. In the fellow eye, a preventive peripheral iridotomy and avoiding miotics are recommended if you need future eye surgery.


CASE 30 — Persistent Choroidal Detachment
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Shallow anterior chamber and decreased vision in the right eye 1 day after trabeculectomy
DURATION: 1 day
LATERALITY: Right eye
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 53-year-old woman with bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma presented 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy with a shallow anterior chamber in the right eye; she had decreased vision (20/400 OD, 20/40 OS), IOPs 34 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS, a moderately elevated filtration bleb negative for Seidel, central and peripheral anterior chamber shallowing with iridocorneal contact to within 1 mm of the pupil, central shallowing with a posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to the endothelium, and 3+ anterior chamber cells; B-scan of the right eye showed a flat retina and no choroidal effusions.
History Narrative: A 53-year-old woman with a history of bilateral chronic angle-closure glaucoma presented 1 day after a mitomycin trabeculectomy in the right eye with a shallow anterior chamber. She reported visual decrease in the right eye; examination that day revealed right eye visual acuity 20/400 and left eye 20/40. Intraocular pressures were 34 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS. She had a moderately elevated filtration bleb on the right that was Seidel-negative. The right anterior chamber was shallow centrally and peripherally with iridocorneal touch extending from the periphery to within 1 mm of the pupillary margin and a posterior chamber intraocular lens located 0.5 mm posterior to the corneal endothelium; anterior chamber cells were 3+. A surgical iridectomy was patent with ciliary processes visible. The left eye had a filtration bleb, deep anterior chamber, patent surgical iridectomy, and pseudophakia. Fundus exam of the right eye had a poor view but excellent red reflex; B-scan ultrasonography of the right eye showed a flat retina without choroidal effusions.
Symptom Onset: 1 day after trabeculectomy
Symptom Duration: 1 day
Progression: Persistent
Characteristics: Serous, smooth, orange/tan, bullous, typically four lobes, echolucent on B-scan


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Observation; elevation of IOP; discontinuation of systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors and topical aqueous suppressants in the affected eye; discontinuation of topical beta-blocker in the contralateral eye; topical steroid and cycloplegic therapy.
Associated Symptoms: Shallow anterior chamber, decreased vision (20/400 OD), anterior chamber cells 3+, poor fundus view in right eye, moderate pain absent (no pain reported)
Prior Treatments: Mitomycin trabeculectomy
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant short-term impairment (visual axis blockage and profound visual loss when effusions are large) with generally full recovery after resolution or drainage; therefore temporary major impact on daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Glaucoma filtration surgery (trabeculectomy with mitomycin), postoperative hypotony/overfiltration, prior chronic angle-closure glaucoma, pseudophakia (posterior chamber IOL), use of topical/systemic aqueous suppressants (e.g., carbonic anhydrase inhibitors), recent tapering of steroids/iridocyclitis, bleb leak or thin ischemic bleb, surgical trauma (cyclodialysis cleft)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N6
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 34 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Moderately elevated filtration bleb, negative Seidel’s test
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or orbital abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Moderately elevated filtration bleb; central corneal endothelium with posterior chamber IOL located 0.5 mm posterior to the corneal endothelium; lenticulocorneal touch present (anterior chamber shallow with iridocorneal contact extending to within 1 mm of pupillary margin)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow with iridocorneal touch peripherally and central shallowing; posterior chamber IOL ~0.5 mm posterior to endothelium
Anterior Segment – Iris: Iridocorneal touch extending from the periphery to within 1 mm of the pupillary margin; surgical iridectomy present and patent
Anterior Segment – Angle: closed
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (pseudophakia)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderately elevated filtration bleb negative for Seidel; shallow anterior chamber with iridocorneal contact from periphery to within 1 mm of pupillary margin; central shallowing with posterior chamber IOL 0.5 mm posterior to corneal endothelium; anterior chamber cells 3+; surgical iridectomy patent with ciliary processes visible.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: moderate glaucomatous cupping
Fundus Exam – Macula: Excellent red reflex
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Poor view with excellent red reflex in right eye; left eye moderate glaucomatous cupping, otherwise unremarkable retina


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: Persistent serous choroidal detachment.
Primary Diagnosis: Persistent serous choroidal detachment
Secondary Diagnoses: Chronic angle-closure glaucoma (bilateral); Pseudophakia (right and left eyes)
Differential Diagnosis: Overfiltration/bleb leak; Serous choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage; Retinal detachment
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye


Plan
All Medications: mitomycin (used during trabeculectomy), topical steroid, cycloplegic agent, topical beta-blocker (contralateral eye discontinued recommended), topical aqueous suppressants (discontinued recommended), systemic carbonic anhydrase inhibitors (discontinued recommended; oral acetazolamide mentioned as sometimes used), balanced salt solution (BSS) or viscoelastic (used intraoperatively), subconjunctival antibiotics and steroids
All Procedures: mitomycin trabeculectomy; slit-lamp examination; B-scan ultrasonography; Seidel’s testing; gonioscopy; drainage of choroidal effusions (sclerostomy with suprachoroidal drainage)
Follow-Up Instructions: Examined 1 day postoperatively; place on a brief course of topical steroid, antibiotic, and cycloplegic (if phakic); monitor for persistent effusion/drainage and expect prompt resolution with dramatic visual recovery if visual axis was involved.
Patient Education: You have a serous choroidal detachment (fluid between the choroid and sclera) after glaucoma surgery. This commonly occurs with low eye pressure and is usually benign. Most cases improve with observation and conservative measures: stopping any medicines that lower eye pressure in the affected eye (and possibly in the fellow eye), using topical steroid and cycloplegic drops, and close monitoring. If vision is threatened (touching the lens/cornea or blocking the visual axis), or the detachment does not resolve, a simple surgical drainage can be performed with very good visual recovery. You will need frequent follow-up and possibly ultrasound exams to ensure the retina is flat and the fluid is resolving. Seek urgent care for sudden severe pain, a rapid drop in vision, or signs of infection.


CASE 31 — Nonproliferative Diabetic Retinopathy
===============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: progressive visual difficulties at night
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "clumsy" and often walked into surrounding objects she could not see well.
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Progressive night vision difficulty since childhood beginning around age 10, worsening over years; stopped driving at night one year ago due to unsafe vision; longstanding clumsiness with walking into objects; otherwise healthy, no family ocular history, no consanguinity.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old woman with progressive visual difficulties at night was evaluated. Since age 10 she has had trouble performing outdoor activities after dusk. A year ago she stopped driving at night because she no longer felt safe. For many years she has been “clumsy” and often walked into surrounding objects she could not see well. She was otherwise healthy. Family history was negative for ocular problems, and there was no consanguinity.
Symptom Onset: Since age 10
Symptom Duration: Since age 10 — progressive night vision difficulty for many years; stopped driving a year ago. Symptom duration: approximately 14 years (since age 10 to current age 24).
Characteristics: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy in both eyes with microaneurysms, intraretinal hemorrhages (approaching severe NPDR threshold), venous beading and areas of capillary nonperfusion; no proliferative changes; visual acuity 20/25 OU; no RAPD.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Duration of diabetes, poor glycemic control, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia
Associated Symptoms: progressive difficulty with night vision (nyctalopia), clumsiness, bumping into objects, stopped driving at night due to unsafe vision
Prior Treatments: Maximal systemic glucose control (medical management); no prior ocular laser or intravitreal treatments indicated (none documented)
Impact on Daily Activities: Stopped driving at night a year ago and unable to perform outdoor activities after dusk; frequent night-time clumsiness and walking into objects, indicating significant impairment of night vision affecting mobility and driving.
Risk Factors: - Duration of diabetes mellitus - Poor glycemic control (hyperglycemia) - Hypertension - Hypercholesterolemia - Severity of baseline retinopathy


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N14/15
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear, without injection
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses, no significant cataract changes noted


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No relative afferent pupillary defect (i.e., pupils briskly reactive to light)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full extraocular movements intact
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macula flat, no clinically significant macular edema; foveal vascular zone somewhat irregular.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Attenuation of the retinal vasculature in the area of retinal atrophy
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Atrophy of the midperipheral and peripheral retina with pigment clumping ("bone spicules") and attenuation of retinal vasculature in areas of atrophy.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU.
Primary Diagnosis: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU
Differential Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa; Branch or central retinal vein occlusion; Radiation retinopathy
Severity: Moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy, OU
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Macular edema (diabetic macular edema) - Progression to proliferative diabetic retinopathy (severe/very severe NPDR → high-risk PDR) - Visual loss - Vitreous hemorrhage - Retinal neovascularization (with tractional detachment risk) - Peripheral/capillary nonperfusion leading to ischemia


Plan
All Medications: Doxycycline, intravitreal steroid, anti–vascular endothelial growth factor
All Procedures: panretinal (scatter) laser photocoagulation funduscopic examination (dilated retinal exam) Goldmann visual field testing automated 30-degree visual field testing optical coherence tomography (OCT) full-field electroretinography (ERG) angiography (fluorescein angiography)
Follow-Up Instructions: Annual examination for minimal/absent NPDR; every 6–12 months for mild–moderate NPDR without macular edema; every 4–6 months if nonclinically significant edema; every 1–2 months if clinically significant macular edema; every 2–4 months for severe or very severe NPDR.
Patient Education: Control your blood sugar and blood pressure; these reduce risk of progression. Have regular eye exams at intervals based on severity (annual for minimal NPDR; 6–12 months for mild–moderate NPDR without edema; 4–6 months if nonclinically significant edema; 1–2 months for clinically significant macular edema; 2–4 months for severe/very severe NPDR). Report any sudden vision changes. Laser treatment (panretinal photocoagulation) may be recommended if disease progresses toward severe/very severe NPDR or proliferative disease. Consider treatment of macular edema if present. Screening for and management of systemic risk factors (hypertension, hypercholesterolemia) is important.


CASE 32 — Diabetic Macular Edema
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision OU for 2 months
DURATION: 2-month history
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had blurred vision for the past two months."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presenting with a 2-month history of blurred vision in both eyes (OU).
History Narrative: A 53-year-old man with a 10-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented with a 2-month history of blurred vision OU. Medical history is positive for hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, and a history of hepatitis C. Best corrected visual acuity was 20/20 OU. Slit-lamp examination was unremarkable. The intraocular pressure was 11 mm Hg in the right eye and 12 mm Hg in the left eye. Funduscopic examination on the right showed a normal disc, no macular edema, macular lipid, or neovascularization. Intraretinal hemorrhages in two quadrants and a mild degree in the other two quadrants were present in both eyes. There was a cotton-wool spot superior to the right macula. Fluorescein angiography demonstrated microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout, but no neovascularization.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU; early proliferative diabetic retinopathy OS; 53-year-old man with 10-year type II diabetes, hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, history of hepatitis C, 2-month blurred vision, BCVA 20/20 OU, IOP 11 mm Hg OD and 12 mm Hg OS, intraretinal hemorrhages in multiple quadrants OU, cotton-wool spot superior to right macula, microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout on FA, no neovascularization OD.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Hypertension, hypercholesterolemia (dyslipidemia), poor systemic diabetic control (longstanding diabetes), peripheral neuropathy (marker of chronic disease), prior/prone to PRP-induced worsening (if PRP performed)
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision OU for 2 months; peripheral neuropathy (systemic symptom)
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — minimal impact; visual acuity 20/20 OU with blurred vision but preserved function, able to perform daily activities with little to no limitation.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, peripheral neuropathy, history of hepatitis C
Risk Factors: Type II diabetes mellitus (10-year history), hypertension, hypercholesterolemia (implied/mentioned as common risk), peripheral neuropathy, hepatitis C, duration of diabetes, poor systemic control (inferred risk factor)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/88 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 11 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic with no cataract (clear lens)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Reactive, equal, round, and symmetric (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal OU
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal disc
Fundus Exam – Macula: No macular edema, macular lipid, or neovascularization (normal macula)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: no venous beading or vascular tortuosity
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal peripheral retina with intraretinal hemorrhages in all quadrants (no neovascularization)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 53-year-old man with 10-year history of type 2 diabetes presenting with 2 months of blurred vision OU. Exam: BCVA 20/20 OU, IOP 11 OD/12 OS, slit lamp unremarkable. Fundus: intraretinal hemorrhages and microaneurysms in multiple quadrants OU, cotton-wool spot superior to right macula; no macular lipid or visible edema on right; fluorescein angiography showed microaneurysms with mild perifoveal capillary dropout OU and no neovascularization. Diagnosis: clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU; early proliferative diabetic retinopathy OS. Plan: initiate intravitreal anti-VEGF therapy for center-involving DME with follow-up in 4–6 weeks; consider a series of three injections at 4–6 week intervals before changing therapy; address systemic risk factors (optimize BP, lipids, glucose) with medical consultation; consider focal/grid laser for non–center involving areas or adjunctive laser as indicated; monitor for need for PRP if proliferative changes progress, and evaluate for surgical intervention only if vitreomacular traction is demonstrated on SD-OCT.
Primary Diagnosis: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Peripheral neuropathy; Hepatitis C
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic macular edema (center-involving); Hypertensive retinopathy with macular edema; Cystoid macular edema (postoperative); Radiation retinopathy
Severity: Clinically significant diabetic macular edema, center-involving (ci-CSME) OU; Early proliferative diabetic retinopathy, OS
Laterality: OU


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab ranibizumab aflibercept intravitreal corticosteroids argon laser
All Procedures: intravitreal injection (anti-VEGF: bevacizumab, ranibizumab, aflibercept) intravitreal corticosteroid injection focal/grid macular laser (modified ETDRS technique) panretinal photocoagulation (PRP) pars plana vitrectomy / surgical removal of taut posterior hyaloid fundus photography fluorescein angiography spectral-domain optical coherence tomography (SD-OCT)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in 4 to 6 weeks after anti‑VEGF (with or without focal laser); retreat if persistent edema/visual loss. Consider sequence of injections; switch agents if poor response after three consecutive injections at 4–6 week intervals. Intravitreal corticosteroid or repeat laser (3–6 months after initial) may be considered for persistent or severe edema. Close follow‑up for PRP decisions and systemic optimization.
Patient Education: Education about diabetic macular edema, including that anti-VEGF injections are now first-line for center-involving DME while laser remains an option for non–center-involving DME; importance of close follow-up (typically every 4–6 weeks after treatment) and that a series of 3 injections may be needed before changing therapy; counseling that optimal control of systemic conditions (blood glucose, blood pressure, cholesterol) improves outcomes; possible need to combine treatments (injections, focal/grid laser, or steroids) or switch drugs if response is inadequate; potential risks/benefits of focal/grid laser and that surgery is reserved for tractional cases; and that visual recovery may be limited despite multiple treatments, so setting realistic expectations.
Referrals: Medical consultation for optimization of systemic conditions (hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, glycemic control)

Assessment Narrative: 58-year-old man with 20-year type I diabetes, recent hypertension and hypercholesterolemia, presenting with blurred vision. Exam: BCVA 20/30 OU, IOP 20 OU, trace nuclear sclerotic cataract, no RAPD. Fundus: moderate microaneurysms OU with clinically significant diabetic macular edema OU, circinate lipid ring around macula OD; early neovascularization of the disc (NVD) OS confirmed on FA and OCT showing macular edema OU. Right eye: proliferative diabetic retinopathy (PDR) with high-risk characteristics (neovascularization involving over one disc area, elevated, no vitreous hemorrhage). Left eye: moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy with early NVD and macular edema. Management to date: bilateral intravitreal anti-VEGF injections for macular edema; planned follow-up in 1 month to reassess OS NVD and retreat OU; subsequent visit: third anti-VEGF given and focal laser to OD for persistent edema; OS improved to 20/20 with normal macular OCT and no further treatment recommended. Plan: continue anti-VEGF therapy and focal laser as indicated for macular edema OD; monitor OS closely for PDR progression, consider panretinal photocoagulation if neovascularization progresses or fails to regress; educate patient about diabetes control and schedule close follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Proliferative diabetic retinopathy (right eye) and moderately severe nonproliferative diabetic retinopathy (left eye)
Secondary Diagnoses: Type I diabetes mellitus, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, diabetic macular edema (OU)
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy (proliferative vs nonproliferative); retinal vein occlusion with collateralization; radiation retinopathy; proliferative sickle cell retinopathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Vitrectomy; vitreous hemorrhage; exacerbation of macular edema; decreased night vision/peripheral field loss; post-PRP pain; choroidal detachment (from excessive treatment); early persistent visual loss (≥2 lines)


Plan
All Medications: anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF) injections (intravitreal)
All Procedures: intravitreal anti-VEGF injections OU; focal laser treatment OD; planned follow-up examinations; (consideration of panretinal photocoagulation/PRP as mainstay treatment)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within a few weeks after completion of PRP; if retinopathy progresses consider vitrectomy. If regressed and stable, continue conservative observational follow-up with periodic exams and visual acuity monitoring. Repeat PRP and/or anti‑VEGF for recurrent vitreous hemorrhages or incomplete regression of neovascularization.
Patient Education: You have proliferative diabetic retinopathy in the right eye and significant diabetic macular edema in both eyes. Key points: - This is a sight‑threatening complication of long‑standing diabetes; good control of blood glucose, blood pressure, and cholesterol is essential to slow progression. - Treatments aim to prevent further vision loss: panretinal laser photocoagulation (PRP) is the standard for proliferative disease; intravitreal anti‑VEGF injections are effective for macular edema and can help regress neovascularization. - PRP may require multiple sessions, can cause temporary pain and decreased night vision, and may worsen macular edema temporarily; combining or giving anti‑VEGF beforehand can reduce this risk. - Anti‑VEGF injections are usually given monthly initially and may be repeated based on OCT and symptoms; expect follow‑up OCT to monitor macular thickness. - Watch for sudden vision decrease or new floaters/hemorrhage — these may signal vitreous hemorrhage or retinal detachment and require urgent evaluation. - Regular dilated retinal exams and OCT are required; adherence to follow‑up visits and treatment is critical to preserve vision. - Maintain coordination with your primary care/endocrinologist for tight systemic control and with your ophthalmologist to plan PRP, injections, or possible vitrectomy if bleeding or traction develops.


CASE 34 — Retinal Arterial Occlusion
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Second opinion regarding possibility of diabetic retinopathy; asymptomatic on presentation
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 50-year-old man with a 5-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented for a second opinion regarding possibility of diabetic retinopathy; asymptomatic on initial presentation.
History Narrative: A 50-year-old man with a 5-year history of type II diabetes mellitus presented for a second opinion regarding possible diabetic retinopathy. He was asymptomatic on presentation. Visual acuity was 20/20 in each eye. Intraocular pressures were 12 mm Hg OD and 13 mm Hg OS. Slit-lamp exam and lenses were unremarkable and clear. Funduscopic exam OD showed mild hard exudates at the posterior pole without definite macular thickening, questionable neovascularization elsewhere at the distal portions of both temporal arcades, and definite neovascularization of the disc exceeding one disc area. OS had intraretinal hemorrhages with microaneurysms in all four quadrants, exceeding standard photograph 2A for hemorrhages in two quadrants.
Progression: Stable
Characteristics: Branch retinal artery occlusion (inferotemporal) with Hollenhorst plaque and retinal whitening, limited arterial filling on fluorescein angiogram, in a patient with diabetes mellitus.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Atherosclerosis (systemic vascular disease), carotid plaque/stenosis, cardiac arrhythmias, diabetes mellitus
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Systemic Symptoms: asymptomatic
Risk Factors: Age 50; Type II diabetes mellitus (5 years); Atherosclerotic vascular disease risk (implied)

Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20

Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry

Anterior Segment – Lens: clear

Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric
