Primary Diagnosis: Branch retinal artery occlusion
Secondary Diagnoses: Type II diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Central retinal artery occlusion; Branch retinal artery occlusion; Retinal vein occlusion (ischemic branch/central); Giant cell arteritis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Neovascularization (retinal and disc), iris neovascularization/rubeosis iridis, neovascular glaucoma, retinal atrophy with permanent visual field defect, increased risk of stroke/MI (systemic vascular events)


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide
All Procedures: digital ocular massage; lowering intraocular pressure (IV acetazolamide, topical IOP-lowering drops, or anterior chamber paracentesis); inhalation of carbogen (5% CO2/95% O2); retrobulbar or systemic calcium channel blockers; full-scatter panretinal photocoagulation; fluorescein angiography; spectral-domain optical coherence tomography; stereoscopic fundus photography; slit-lamp biomicroscopy with precorneal or contact lens examination
Follow-Up Instructions: Monitor for subsequent neovascularization; reinforce internist-prescribed systemic regimens; consider low-vision aids/prism glasses as needed.
Patient Education: Retinal arterial occlusion is a sudden blockage of an artery in the eye that can cause sudden, often permanent vision loss; it is important because it may signal serious systemic vascular disease (like atherosclerosis, carotid plaque, or heart rhythm problems) and requires prompt medical evaluation. There is no proven ocular treatment to restore vision, but early measures (digital ocular massage, lowering intraocular pressure, or paracentesis when within 24 hours) are sometimes attempted. You should be referred promptly for systemic workup by your primary care doctor or cardiologist (carotid imaging, cardiac rhythm evaluation, vascular risk factor control—blood pressure, diabetes, cholesterol, smoking cessation). Follow-up eye exams are important to monitor for neovascularization, which may require panretinal photocoagulation. Seek immediate care for any new sudden vision changes.
Referrals: Referral for systemic medical workup (internist/cardiovascular evaluation for atherosclerosis, carotid plaque assessment, and arrhythmia evaluation)


CASE 35 — Central Retinal Vein Occlusion
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden visual loss in the right eye 4 weeks prior
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 69-year-old woman with sudden visual loss in the right eye 4 weeks prior to presentation.
History Narrative: A 69-year-old woman presented 4 weeks after sudden visual loss in the right eye. Medical history included a 6-year history of systemic hypertension and a 1-year history of diabetes mellitus. She was taking oral contraceptives. No afferent pupillary defect was noted.
Symptom Onset: 4 weeks
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: Stable for 4 weeks since onset (subacute); risk of progression to ischemic CRVO with potential neovascularization within 3–6 months.
Characteristics: Intraretinal hemorrhages in all four quadrants with tortuous veins, macular edema, possible disc collaterals later, and variable ischemia (nonperfused vs perfused) leading to risk of neovascularization.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Oral contraceptive use
Associated Symptoms: sudden visual loss in the affected eye (acute decreased vision)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Cessation of oral contraceptives; aspirin 80 mg daily; no prior ocular treatments noted (no prior PRP, intravitreal injections, or surgery)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — some difficulties with tasks requiring fine central vision (reading, driving) but able to perform most activities with reduced efficiency.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, diabetes mellitus
Risk Factors: Hypertension, diabetes mellitus, oral contraceptive use, hypercoagulable states (e.g., thrombophilia, hyperviscosity syndromes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/86 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jaeger 2 (J2)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal-looking orbit; no proptosis, palpable masses, or external abnormalities noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no corneal abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep, and formed
Anterior Segment – Iris: No rubeosis (no iris neovascularization)
Anterior Segment – Lens: early nuclear sclerosis in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk in each eye
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no limitation)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Early nuclear sclerosis of the lens in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Macula: Marked intraretinal hemorrhage and retinal whitening in the posterior pole with marked macular edema (intraretinal edema)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: tortuous
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal abnormalities noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CRVO OD with secondary macular edema causing visual loss. 69-year-old woman with 4 weeks of sudden right-eye visual loss; exam shows intraretinal hemorrhages in all quadrants, tortuous veins, no afferent pupillary defect, IOP 16 mm Hg OU, early nuclear sclerosis OU. OCT confirms marked intraretinal/macular edema OD. Risk factors: hypertension and diabetes; oral contraceptive use noted previously and cessation recommended. Plan: systemic evaluation/management with primary physician, consider aspirin, monitor for ischemic conversion and neovascularization; initiate intravitreal anti-VEGF or corticosteroid therapy for macular edema with OCT-guided retreatment and monthly follow-up for at least 6 months; consider PRP if ≥5 disc areas of nonperfusion or rubeosis develops; treat neovascular glaucoma with anti-VEGF, PRP, and glaucoma surgery/shunt if needed.
Primary Diagnosis: CRVO with secondary macular edema, OD
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; early nuclear sclerosis of the lens; history of oral contraceptive use; Hollenhorst plaque (cholesterol embolus)
Differential Diagnosis: Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO)/hemiretinal vein occlusion; Diffuse diabetic retinopathy (or radiation retinopathy) with widespread intraretinal hemorrhage
Severity: Moderate (CRVO with secondary macular edema)
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: - Neovascularization of the anterior segment (rubeosis iridis) leading to neovascular glaucoma - Macular edema - Vitreous hemorrhage - Increased intraocular pressure/glaucoma (including neovascular glaucoma) - Vision loss (ranging from mild to severe; potential permanent visual impairment) - Retinal ischemia/nonperfusion - Collateral vessel formation at the optic disc - Need for vitrectomy or glaucoma/shunt surgery in advanced cases


Plan
All Medications: Aspirin 80 mg daily; intravitreal anti-VEGF injections; intravitreal triamcinolone (corticosteroid); cessation of oral contraceptives
All Procedures: Cessation of oral contraceptives; referral to primary care for systemic evaluation; monthly ophthalmic follow-up for 6 months; panretinal photocoagulation (PRP) if ≥5 disc areas of nonperfusion or rubeosis iridis; intravitreal anti-VEGF injections for macular edema; intravitreal corticosteroid injection (triamcinolone) for macular edema; pars plana vitrectomy or cryopexy if media opacity prevents laser; glaucoma shunt surgery for neovascular glaucoma; grid photocoagulation (not efficacious for macular edema)
Follow-Up Instructions: Monthly follow-up examinations for 6 months after diagnosis (if injection therapy not given); continued monitoring thereafter with attention to any decrease in visual acuity or conversion to nonperfused CRVO (rubeosis risk declines after 1 year).
Patient Education: You have a central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO), which is a blockage of the main retinal vein causing swelling and bleeding in the retina. This can reduce vision mainly from macular edema (swelling in the central retina) and, in severe ischemic cases, can lead to abnormal new blood vessel growth (rubeosis) and neovascular glaucoma. Important points and actions: - Systemic risks: CRVO is commonly associated with hypertension, diabetes, and in younger patients hormonal contraceptives or clotting disorders. You should follow up with your primary doctor for blood pressure, diabetes control, and evaluation of any other clotting risk factors; stopping oral contraceptives was recommended. - Vision treatment: Macular edema is often treatable and commonly managed with repeated intravitreal injections (anti-VEGF or corticosteroid) guided by OCT imaging; this can improve or stabilize vision in many patients. - Glaucoma risk: If the retina is severely ischemic, abnormal vessels can develop 3–6 months after onset and may cause painful high eye pressure; close ophthalmic follow-up (monthly for the first 6 months) is important to detect rubeosis early so laser (panretinal photocoagulation) and/or anti-VEGF and glaucoma surgery can be done if needed. - Monitoring: Expect regular eye exams and OCT scans; report any sudden worsening of vision, eye pain, redness, or new floaters immediately. - Prognosis: Visual outcome varies—some improvement is possible with treatment of macular edema, but outcomes depend on the degree of retinal ischemia. - Lifestyle/medication: Control blood pressure and diabetes, discuss antiplatelet or anticoagulant therapy with your physicians as appropriate, and avoid smoking.


CASE 36 — Branch Retinal Vein Occlusion
=======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had decreased vision in my right eye for three weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 45-year-old woman with a 3-week history of decreased vision in the right eye; vision gradually diminished during the first week after onset then stabilized. No hypertension; taking oral contraceptives. Prior pseudophakia OD. Presented with BCVA 20/100 OD and 20/20 OS, right afferent pupillary defect, macular edema involving the fovea, and intraretinal hemorrhages in segmental distribution.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman presented with a 3-week history of decreased vision in the right eye. Her only medications were oral contraceptives and she did not have hypertension. The vision had gradually diminished during the first week after onset and then stabilized. Examination disclosed best corrected visual acuity of 20/100 OD and 20/20 OS. There was a right afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp examination showed no signs of iris neovascularization; the patient was pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule. Intraocular pressures were 12 and 14 mm Hg, respectively. Funduscopic examination on the right showed intraretinal hemorrhage distributed throughout each quadrant with mild disc edema, but no definite collaterals. Macular edema was evident over about a disc area at the fovea. The cup-to-disc ratio was 0.3. Examination of the left eye was normal. Fluorescein angiography showed good perfusion in the midperiphery and late dye leakage at the fovea. OCT showed marked central macular edema. Intravitreal triamcinolone was administered with resolution of the macular edema and improvement of vision to 20/30 at 3 months. Edema and visual loss recurred at 6 months; a second intravitreal triamcinolone produced similar resolution but recurred 3 months later. The patient has been maintained on intravitreal bevacizumab about every 3 months with a stable OCT and visual acuity of 20/30.
Symptom Onset: 3-week history
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Recurrent with periods of resolution (chronic recurrent course)
Characteristics: Intraretinal hemorrhage distributed segmentally in the inferotemporal quadrant with macular edema involving the fovea, mild disc edema, pseudophakia, right afferent pupillary defect, IOP 12 mm Hg OD and 14 mm Hg OS, cup-to-disc ratio 0.3, OCT showing marked central macular edema, fluorescein angiography with late foveal leakage and good midperipheral perfusion.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Systemic hypertension, diabetes, cardiovascular disease, glaucoma, increased body mass, hyperopia, high serum cholesterol, low HDL, coagulation disorders, alcohol use, oral contraceptives
Alleviating Factors: Intravitreal triamcinolone and intravitreal anti‑VEGF injections (bevacizumab/aflibercept) which reduced macular edema and improved vision
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision in the right eye for 3 weeks.
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: intravitreal triamcinolone (two injections), intravitreal bevacizumab maintenance every ~3 months
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision in the right eye (20/100 improving to 20/30 with treatment) causing visual impairment but not complete loss, requiring ongoing treatments and follow-up.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Systemic hypertension; diabetes; other cardiovascular disease; glaucoma; increased body mass at age 20; hyperopia; higher serum cholesterol; lower HDL; coagulation disorders (various); alcohol consumption; oral contraceptive use.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen eye chart (best corrected visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear/conjunctiva without injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbit exam within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea with no signs of inflammation; pseudophakic right eye with a clear posterior capsule
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep, without cells or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: No iris neovascularization
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right afferent pupillary defect (pupils reactive asymmetric)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: Right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: no signs of iris neovascularization; the patient was pseudophakic with a clear posterior capsule
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: cup-to-disc ratio was 0.3
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular edema involving the fovea (about one disc area)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Intraretinal hemorrhages segmentally in the distribution of the inferotemporal branch retinal vein; no definite collaterals; mild disc edema.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Intraretinal hemorrhages throughout each quadrant in right fundus with good perfusion in midperiphery on FA; no definite peripheral neovascularization.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: BRVO of the right eye with foveal-involving macular edema causing decreased vision (initial BCVA 20/100 OD), chronic recurrent edema responsive to intravitreal triamcinolone and maintained on serial intravitreal anti-VEGF (bevacizumab) with stabilized OCT and vision of 20/30 OD; no iris neovascularization or vitreous hemorrhage; IOPs 12–14 mm Hg; fellow eye normal.
Primary Diagnosis: BRVO with macular edema, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Pseudophakia OD; History of oral contraceptive use; No hypertension; Right afferent pupillary defect; Macular edema OD; Intraretinal hemorrhages OD; Mild optic disc edema OD; Prior intravitreal triamcinolone OD; Ongoing intravitreal bevacizumab treatment OD
Differential Diagnosis: Diabetic retinopathy; Central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO); Radiation retinopathy; Sickle cell retinopathy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: Macular edema; intraretinal hemorrhage; disc edema; retinal neovascularization (possible but not present initially); vitreous hemorrhage (possible with neovascularization); recurrence of edema after treatment; potential epiretinal membrane; vision loss.


Plan
All Medications: oral contraceptives; intravitreal triamcinolone; intravitreal bevacizumab; intravitreal aflibercept; aspirin
All Procedures: intravitreal triamcinolone injection; intravitreal bevacizumab injections; focal/grid laser photocoagulation; sectoral/scatter laser photocoagulation; vitrectomy with membrane peeling; vitrectomy for nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage; optic nerve sheath decompression (experimental); lysis/decompression of common adventitial sheath (experimental)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up every 3–6 months initially if vision >20/32; if being treated for macular edema, initiate intravitreal therapy (typically every 4–6 weeks) and then extend interval based on OCT/clinical response; perform segmental scatter photocoagulation if retinal neovascularization develops; consider vitrectomy for nonclearing vitreous hemorrhage or tractional/rhegmatogenous macular detachment.
Patient Education: You have a branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO), which means one of the small veins that drains your retina is blocked. This can cause blood and fluid to leak into the retina and produce swelling of the macula (macular edema), which is why your vision dropped. Key points: - Cause and risk factors: BRVO often occurs spontaneously but is more common with high blood pressure, diabetes, high cholesterol, smoking, and other vascular risk factors. Because you are younger, your doctor may advise targeted systemic evaluation and will notify your primary care physician. - Symptoms to watch for: worsening blurry or distorted central vision, new floaters, sudden loss of vision, or increasing pain (seek urgent care if these occur). - Treatment overview: Macular edema is commonly treated with intravitreal medicines (anti‑VEGF injections or corticosteroid injections) to reduce swelling and improve or stabilize vision; focal or sectoral laser is used in some cases, and scatter laser is used if abnormal new vessels (neovascularization) develop. Surgery (vitrectomy) is reserved for persistent nonclearing hemorrhage or tractional problems. - What to expect: Multiple injections may be needed initially (often every 4–12 weeks) and then less frequently when stable. OCT scans and periodic eye exams monitor response. Vision may improve but can recur, requiring additional treatment. - Systemic care: Control blood pressure, lipids, blood sugar, and stop smoking; follow up with your primary care doctor for risk-factor management. - Follow-up schedule: Regular ophthalmology visits (initially every 4–12 weeks while receiving injections, then every 3–6 months when stable) and prompt return for any sudden vision changes. If you have questions about the treatments, risks, or need help coordinating systemic evaluation, ask your ophthalmologist.
Referrals: Notify patient's primary care physician.


CASE 37 — Nonexudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision OS with a central scotoma for 2 months
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "he reported was well controlled on medications"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 66-year-old man with a 2-month history of decreased vision in the left eye characterized by a central scotoma centered superotemporally; history notable for long-standing hypertension well controlled on medications.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old man had a 2-month history of decreased vision OS characterized by a central scotoma that was centered superatemporally. He had a 20-year history of hypertension that he reported was well controlled on medications. The visual acuity was 20/200 at first presentation, OS. A branch retinal vein occlusion (BRVO) was diagnosed. The right eye had previously incurred a central retinal vein occlusion (CRVO) that responded to several injections of several anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF) agents, as well as a couple of intravitreal triamcinolone injections. The visual acuity OD had improved from 20/400 to 20/80 after resolution of the macular edema. Intravitreal bevacizumab was administered without improvement, followed by aflibercept which had a better effect and has been applied for about a year every 6 weeks. The visual acuity has stabilized at 20/70. The intraocular pressures were 15 mm Hg in each eye. Funduscopic examination OD showed a dry macula with collateral vessels at the optic nerve head. In the left eye, there was an anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade with dot-and-blot hemorrhages extending from the midportion of the inferotemporal arcade posteriorly into the macula. There was moderate to marked macular edema.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Stable (no progression noted over follow-up; vision stabilized at 20/70 OS with ongoing monitoring recommended)
Characteristics: Nonexudative (atrophic) age-related macular degeneration characterized by drusen with variable RPE atrophy, no exudation on FA/OCT, possible geographic atrophy, bilateral (OU) involvement.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Smoking (tobacco use)
Associated Symptoms: central scotoma; metamorphopsia
Previous Episodes: CRVO OD (treated with multiple anti-VEGF and intravitreal triamcinolone); BRVO OS prior to treatment
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal bevacizumab (no improvement), then intravitreal aflibercept every 6 weeks for ~1 year; prior anti-VEGF injections and intravitreal triamcinolone OD for CRVO.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate impairment — difficulty with fine central tasks (reading, recognizing faces), may require low-vision aids but retains some functional vision for many activities.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension (well controlled on medications); otherwise none reported
Risk Factors: Age, smoking (tobacco use), hypertension, bilateral drusen/advanced age-related macular degeneration in the fellow eye, prior retinal vascular disease (history of CRVO/BRVO)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/70
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N24
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet, no injection
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea; no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic cataracts, mild-to-moderate in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – RAPD: None
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Clear corneas, deep and quiet anterior chambers, and 2+ nuclear sclerotic cataracts OU.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal-appearing optic nerve head
Fundus Exam – Macula: Dry macula with drusen and geographic atrophy consistent with nonexudative AMD; left macula with moderate–marked macular edema and dot‑and‑blot hemorrhages from inferotemporal BRVO.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Collateral vessels at the optic nerve head; anomalous arteriovenous crossing along the inferotemporal arcade
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits for both eyes


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Nonexudative (atrophic) age-related macular degeneration, OU — characterized by drusen with variable RPE atrophy of the macula. No evidence of exudation or choroidal neovascularization on imaging (OCT and fluorescein angiography). Visual function reduced (history of central scotoma OS; prior CRVO OD with residual visual deficit), but retinal thickness is thin with preservation of remaining retinal structure suggesting relatively spared photoreceptors. Recommend AREDS2 vitamin supplementation, smoking avoidance, routine monitoring every 3–12 months, patient education on Amsler grid and prompt return for any increase in metamorphopsia or new visual symptoms; consider low-vision referral if functional impairment progresses.
Primary Diagnosis: Nonexudative age-related macular degeneration, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Branch retinal vein occlusion; Central retinal vein occlusion; Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Occult exudative (neovascular) AMD; Pattern dystrophy (or inherited macular dystrophy, e.g., Stargardt); Inflammatory- or trauma-induced RPE atrophy; Optic neuropathy or amblyopia (coexisting causes of disproportionate vision loss)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OU
Complications: development of exudative (neovascular) AMD


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab, aflibercept, intravitreal triamcinolone, AREDS2 vitamins, antihypertensive medications (for hypertension)
Follow-Up Instructions: Examine every 3–12 months depending on stability; use Amsler grid daily to monitor each eye and report any new or increased metamorphopsia or other visual changes immediately.
Patient Education: Use AREDS2 vitamin supplements, avoid tobacco smoking, perform daily Amsler grid self-monitoring for each eye, report any new visual changes (especially increased metamorphopsia) immediately, and consider low-vision evaluation/ aids as needed; follow-up every 3–12 months depending on stability.
Referrals: Low vision evaluation
Emergency Instructions: Any sudden change in vision—especially new or worsening metamorphopsia, sudden increase in central scotoma, new floaters, flashes, or marked vision loss—promptly return for immediate evaluation for possible exudative (neovascular) conversion.


CASE 38 — Exudative Age-Related Macular Degeneration
====================================================

CASE 39 — Myopic Degeneration
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision in her right eye
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "decreased vision in my right eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 87-year-old woman with prior nonexudative AMD in both eyes presenting with decreased vision in the right eye; exam showed VA 20/40 OD and 20/50 OS, no RAPD, pseudophakia OU, normal IOPs; fundus OD with drusen, RPE changes, new subretinal fluid and pigment epithelial detachment without hemorrhage; OCT confirmed PED with adjacent subretinal fluid OD and drusen without fluid OS; FA OD showed delayed stippled hyperfluorescence with late leakage consistent with occult CNVM; underwent intravitreal bevacizumab and instructed to follow up in 4 weeks.
History Narrative: An 87-year-old woman presented complaining of decreased vision in her right eye. She had a prior history of nonexudative age-related macular degeneration (AMD) in both eyes. Examination showed vision of 20/40 in the right eye and 20/50 in the left eye. There was no definite afferent pupillary defect. Slit-lamp examination showed the patient to be pseudophakic in both eyes. Intraocular pressures were within normal limits in each eye. Funduscopic examination on the right showed drusen with retinal pigment epithelium (RPE) changes in the macula with new subretinal fluid and pigment epithelial detachment. No hemorrhage was noted. On the left, there were drusen with RPE changes in the macula but no hemorrhage was noted. Optical coherence tomography (OCT) of the right eye showed a pigment epithelial detachment with adjacent subretinal fluid; the left eye showed drusen with no evidence of macular fluid. Fluorescein angiography of the right eye showed delayed stippled hyperfluorescence with late leakage, consistent with an occult choroidal neovascular membrane (CNVM). The patient underwent an intravitreal injection of bevacizumab and was instructed to follow up in 4 weeks.
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Characteristics: Myopic degeneration characterized by atrophic versus neovascular forms, often with lacquer cracks, posterior staphyloma, peripapillary RPE atrophy, drusen-like RPE changes, retinal lattice degeneration, pigment epithelial detachments, subretinal fluid/CNV (often occult or subfoveal), and Fuchs’ spot in late stages


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Decreased vision
Previous Episodes: Recurrent choroidal neovascularization (prior CNVM with recurrence)
Prior Treatments: Intravitreal bevacizumab injection (one)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely significant central vision impairment for tasks requiring fine detail (reading, recognizing faces, driving), with need for low-vision aids and increased difficulty with near work and visual tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: High axial myopia (high myopia, >6 D or axial length >26.5 mm); presence of lacquer cracks; posterior staphyloma; retinal lattice degeneration; prior chorioretinal scars; older age (increased risk of progressive CNV)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/72 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jäger 2 (OU)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chartje (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White and quiet conjunctiva; no scleral injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakic in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD) — pupils equal and reactive, normal in size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No relative afferent pupillary defect (no RAPD)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: No definite afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: No definite afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restriction or limitation
Slit Lamp Exam Description: the patient to be pseudophakic in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits (no optic disc pallor or edema; tilted optic nerve head noted in myopic fundus)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Drusen with retinal pigment epithelium changes, subretinal fluid and pigment epithelial detachment in the right macula; drusen with RPE changes and no macular fluid in the left macula.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course (no specific vessel narrowing, beading, or tortuosity noted)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Lattice degeneration without atrophic holes in the peripheral retina.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Subretinal choroidal neovascularization with recurrence secondary to myopic degeneration, OU, with active occult CNVM in the right eye manifesting as pigment epithelial detachment and adjacent subretinal fluid on OCT and late leakage on FA; left eye myopic degenerative changes with lacquer crack and focal CNVM changes nasally but without significant macular fluid.
Primary Diagnosis: Subretinal choroidal neovascularization secondary to myopic degeneration, OU
Secondary Diagnoses: Age-related macular degeneration (nonexudative) OU; pseudophakia OU; retinal lattice degeneration OD; high myopia (−10 D)
Differential Diagnosis: Pathologic myopia with choroidal neovascularization; Age-related macular degeneration with CNV; Presumed ocular histoplasmosis syndrome with CNV; Traumatic choroidal rupture with secondary CNV
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OU
Complications: - Choroidal neovascularization (CNV) with subretinal fluid and pigment epithelial detachment - Subfoveal scarring/Fuchs' spot - Progressive RPE and chorioretinal atrophy leading to central vision loss - Retinal detachment (associated with lattice degeneration and posterior staphyloma) - Lacquer cracks (breaks in Bruch’s membrane)


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab
All Procedures: Intravitreal injection of bevacizumab; optical coherence tomography; fluorescein angiography; photodynamic therapy with verteporfin; laser photocoagulation; pars plana vitrectomy with CNVM excision; Amsler grid monitoring; OCT at each follow-up
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in 4 weeks for clinical exam and OCT; thereafter interval may be extended based on response and stability, with OCT at each visit and prompt return for new distortion or vision loss (use Amsler grid monitoring).
Patient Education: - Myopic degeneration can cause central vision loss; monitor for new distortion or decreased central vision and report promptly. - Use an Amsler grid daily to check for metamorphopsia or scotomas and bring any changes to your ophthalmologist immediately. - Follow-up visits are typically every 4 weeks after starting anti-VEGF treatment, then less frequently based on response; OCT will be performed at visits to monitor for fluid. - Intravitreal anti-VEGF injections (e.g., bevacizumab, ranibizumab) are the main treatment for neovascular complications and can stabilize or improve vision; discuss risks and benefits with your doctor. - There is no proven treatment for atrophic (nonexudative) myopic degeneration; low-vision aids (magnifiers, high‑plus lenses, telescopes, video magnification) and rehabilitation can help maximize remaining vision. - Report signs of possible complications of injections (increasing pain, redness, marked vision loss) immediately as they may indicate infection or retinal detachment.


CASE 40 — Idiopathic Central Serous Chorioretinopathy
=====================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye
DURATION: 1 month
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "finding everything to be cloudy."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 38-year-old urologist with a 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye; told previously he had a CNVM not amenable to treatment. Presented with a 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye. Later (6 years after initial treatment) returned with a 1-month history of decreased central vision in the left eye described as everything appearing cloudy.
History Narrative: A 38-year-old urologist presented with a 1-year history of visual loss in the right eye. He had been told he had a choroidal neovascular membrane (CNVM) in the right eye that was not amenable to treatment. At presentation he reported a 1-month history of distortion with a paracentral scotoma in the left eye. He was a high myope with spherical equivalent –10 D. He later returned 6 years after initial treatment with a 1-month history of decreased central vision in the left eye, describing vision as “cloudy.” He also had a history of glaucoma treated initially with Propine OU and later a beta‑blocker OU.
Symptom Onset: 1 month
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Chronic/recurrent over 1 year with episodes (initial unilateral long-standing, new left-eye episode after 6 years; overall chronic recurrent)
Characteristics: Detached neurosensory retina from the underlying retinal pigment epithelium with subretinal fluid (serous macular detachment), typically painless, in young to middle‑aged adults, often self‑limited, may show focal RPE leaks on fluorescein angiography and neurosensory retinal elevation on OCT.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Corticosteroids; Psychotropic medications; Type A personality/stress; Pregnancy; Cushing’s disease; Hypertension; Systemic lupus erythematosus; Helicobacter pylori infection; Idiopathic factors
Alleviating Factors: Stopping systemic corticosteroids
Associated Symptoms: Central visual disturbance (blurry/cloudy vision), metamorphopsia (distortion), paracentral scotoma, painless vision loss
Previous Episodes: 1 year of visual loss in right eye; 1-month history in left eye; later returned after 6 years with recurrence in left eye — prior episodes: right eye chronic 1 year; left eye initial episode treated 6 years earlier with recurrence.
Prior Treatments: None (no prior treatment)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — impaired central vision with metamorphopsia causing difficulty with fine visual tasks (reading, near work) but retained some functional vision (20/30 in affected eye at presentation), occasional significant episodes (20/200) limiting activities.
Recent Travel: Returned to Peru for further follow-up examination locally
Risk Factors: Corticosteroids; Psychotropic medications; Hypertension; Systemic lupus erythematosus; Pregnancy; Cushing’s disease; Helicobacter pylori infection; Male gender; Type A personality; Excess stress; Aldosterone/mineralocorticoid excess (e.g., aldosterone-related conditions); Idiopathic


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 36.8 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear corneas, no abnormalities noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: open-angle
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size (appears round, no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal to light in both eyes
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The slit-lamp examination was unremarkable.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Tilted (myopic) disc with peripapillary atrophy
Fundus Exam – Macula: Cloudy vision with subretinal fluid under the fovea and leakage on fluorescein angiography (serous neurosensory detachment of the macula)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course (no specific vascular abnormalities noted)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retinal lattice degeneration in the periphery of both eyes, no atrophic holes


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Central serous chorioretinopathy of the left eye with subfoveal subretinal fluid on OCT and focal fluorescein leakage; chronicity noted with recurrent symptoms beyond typical spontaneous-resolution window. Right eye with chronic RPE atrophy and prior/presumed inactive CNVM with longstanding reduced vision (20/200). No signs of intraocular inflammation. Risk factors/considerations: male gender, stress/type A, possible steroid exposure; monitor for recurrence and consider focal laser or photodynamic therapy for persistent/recurrent subretinal fluid.
Primary Diagnosis: Central serous chorioretinopathy
Secondary Diagnoses: Myopia (high myopia), Glaucoma, Retinal lattice degeneration, Choroidal neovascular membrane (right and left)
Differential Diagnosis: Central serous chorioretinopathy; Choroidal neovascular membrane (eg, myopic CNV or AMD-related CNV); Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada disease (or other posterior inflammatory chorioretinopathies); Choroidal tumor (eg, melanoma)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left eye
Complications: scotomas; pigment epithelial mottling/atrophy; chronic subretinal fluid with persistent visual acuity loss; recurrences; secondary choroidal neovascularization


Plan
All Medications: Propine (dipivefrin) OU; topical beta‑blocker OU; oral rifampin; methotrexate; carbonic anhydrase inhibitors; metoprolol; propranolol; ketoconazole; mifepristone; eplerenone; spironolactone; finasteride; anti‑vascular endothelial growth factor (anti‑VEGF) injections
All Procedures: argon blue-green laser treatment OU (left eye treated) krypton laser treatment (left eye)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for monitoring until resolution within ~3 months; arrange serial visits with visual acuity, fundus exam and OCT to document subretinal fluid resolution; consider follow-up every 4–6 weeks initially; if persistent/chronic or recurrent, consider photodynamic therapy or focal laser and refer for treatment planning.
Patient Education: Central serous chorioretinopathy (CSCR) is a usually self‑limited condition in which fluid collects under the central retina, causing blurred or distorted vision. Risk factors include corticosteroid use, stress/type A personality, pregnancy, hypertension, and certain medications. Most cases improve spontaneously within 3 months; avoid or discuss stopping systemic steroids with your prescribing physician. If fluid persists or vision is functionally significant, treatments that may be offered include focal laser or photodynamic therapy (PDT) to help resolve subretinal fluid. Regular follow‑up with retinal imaging (OCT and possibly fluorescein/ICG angiography) is recommended to monitor resolution or recurrence. Report any new or worsening central vision changes promptly.


CASE 41 — Epiretinal Membrane
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurriness in left eye for 3 weeks
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: Right eye
PATIENT QUOTES: "I deny any past medical history, trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroids, or previous surgeries."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old engineer with 3-week history of blurriness in the left eye, central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid, no trauma, no past medical history, diabetes, hypertension, steroids, or prior surgeries. Visual acuity 20/20 OD and 20/50 OS (improving to 20/30 with +2.00), anterior segment and IOP unremarkable, dilated fundus: left circumscribed neurosensory macular detachment; OCT showed subretinal fluid under the fovea; FA showed expanding macular leakage. Observed with spontaneous resolution of subretinal fluid and vision returned to 20/20 after one month.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old engineer presented with blurriness in his left eye for 3 weeks. He denies any past medical history, trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroids, or previous surgeries. On examination, the visual acuity was 20/20 in the right eye and 20/50 in the left eye. With a +2.00 lens, the vision in the left eye improved to 20/30. Anterior segment examination and intraocular pressures were unremarkable in both eyes. A dilated fundus examination of the right eye was normal. The left eye had an elevated, circumscribed neurosensory detachment of the retina. There were no retinal tears, hemorrhages, or exudates. The patient noted central metamorphopsia on Amsler grid testing of his left eye. On optical coherence tomography (OCT), there was subretinal fluid under the fovea in the left eye. Fluorescein angiography (FA) of the left eye revealed an expanding area of leakage in the macula. The patient was observed closely. One month later, the subretinal fluid completely resolved, and the vision returned to 20/20.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Improved (postoperative vision improved from 20/50 to 20/60 at 3 months? actually preop left eye 20/50, post-op improved—text: "visual acuity had improved to 20/60" but that is worse; however overall metamorphopsia improved and OCT improved)
Characteristics: Translucent preretinal membrane causing macular surface wrinkling, vascular distortion, and foveal contour distortion with retinal thickening on OCT and associated metamorphopsia and decreased visual acuity.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Metamorphopsia and blurriness (decreased vision) of the affected eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision (20/50 OS improving to 20/30 with +2.00) with central metamorphopsia affecting visual tasks; improved after surgery to 20/60 then macular appearance improved.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms (no trauma, diabetes, hypertension, steroid use, or other systemic illness).
Risk Factors: Previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment; prior retinal tear/detachment; posterior vitreous separation; intraocular inflammation; retinal vascular disease; ocular trauma; prior intraocular surgery; increasing age


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (20/40)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet (no conjunctival injection; sclera normal)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: brisk
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (no restriction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment examination unremarkable in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: elevated, circumscribed neurosensory detachment of the macula (left eye)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: distorted (vascular distortion temporal to the fovea)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No retinal tears, hemorrhages, or exudates; peripheral retinal break noted (history of prior peripheral retinal break)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: ERM secondary to previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment, right eye. Patient presenting with decreased vision and metamorphopsia; OCT and clinical exam consistent with an epiretinal membrane causing foveal distortion and retinal thickening. Surgical removal via vitrectomy and ERM peel performed; postoperative course with improved foveal contour and decreased thickness on OCT and visual acuity improvement by 3 months.
Primary Diagnosis: ERM secondary to previous peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment, right eye
Secondary Diagnoses: Peripheral retinal break and retinal detachment (right eye)
Differential Diagnosis: Cystoid macular edema; Vitreomacular traction; Hypotony-related retinal folds
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: retinal detachment; progressive nuclear sclerosis (accelerated cataract formation)


Plan
All Medications: Topical corticosteroid drops; topical antibiotic drops
All Procedures: Vitrectomy with epiretinal membrane peel (with internal limiting membrane peel as performed)
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative topical corticosteroid and antibiotic drops; routine follow-up visits with visual acuity and OCT at ~1 week, 6 weeks, and 3 months (visual recovery usually rapid over first 6 weeks and maximal by 3–6 months); monitor for complications (retinal detachment, cataract progression) and treat cataract if needed.
Patient Education: Epiretinal membranes (macular pucker) are a common cause of visual distortion (metamorphopsia) and mild vision loss. They are diagnosed by clinical exam and OCT. If vision and symptoms are minimal, observation is reasonable because many membranes stabilize and may not worsen. If vision declines to about 20/40–20/60 or distortion is disabling, pars plana vitrectomy with membrane peel (often with internal limiting membrane removal) can improve anatomy and symptoms over months; recovery is fastest in the first 6 weeks and may continue up to 3–6 months. Postoperative care includes topical steroid and antibiotic drops. Risks of surgery are low but include retinal detachment and a high likelihood of cataract progression in phakic eyes. If you notice sudden worsening, new flashes, showers of floaters, or increased distortion, contact your ophthalmologist promptly.


CASE 42 — Vitreomacular Traction
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: progressively decreasing vision in the right eye for 18 months with metamorphopsia
DURATION: 18 months
LATERALITY: OD
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have been noticing progressively decreasing vision in my right eye for the past 18 months."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 73-year-old woman with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye, associated metamorphopsia; prior retinal detachment repair with pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas in that eye 2 years earlier.
History Narrative: A 73-year-old woman presented with an 18-month history of progressively decreasing vision in the right eye. She had metamorphopsia and best corrected Snellen visual acuity of 20/200. A retinal detachment was repaired with a primary pars plana vitrectomy, endolaser, and gas 2 years previously. Funduscopic examination of the right eye showed a prominent epiretinal membrane (ERM) with moderate vascular distortion. Optical coherence tomography (OCT) demonstrated an ERM with an abnormal foveal contour.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Vitreomacular traction with focal vitreofoveal attachment causing retinal traction and distortion, associated epiretinal membrane and abnormal foveal contour on OCT, symptomatic with progressive decreased vision and metamorphopsia (OD); OS nonprogressive.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressive vitreoretinal traction from epiretinal membrane and prior retinal surgery (posterior vitreous separation alteration) leading to increased retinal distortion and cystoid changes
Alleviating Factors: Observation; spontaneous release of traction (especially with focal attachment)
Associated Symptoms: progressively decreasing vision and metamorphopsia
Prior Treatments: Pars plana vitrectomy with endolaser and gas (retinal detachment repair)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — progressive decreased vision (20/200) with metamorphopsia significantly impacting visual function and daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Age, epiretinal membrane (ERM), prior retinal surgery/retinal detachment, uveitis, retinal vascular disease (e.g., vein occlusion, diabetic retinopathy), macular telangiectasia


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+3
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White/pale, no conjunctival injection or scleral erythema
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, unremarkable (no corneal pathology noted)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia (right eye), Pseudophakia (left eye)

Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light

Follow-Up Instructions: Educate patient on Amsler grid self-monitoring and to report any worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotoma; schedule periodic clinical evaluations with OCT monitoring as indicated.
Patient Education: Educate patient on self-monitoring with Amsler grid for worsening metamorphopsia, decreased vision, or new scotomas and report promptly; explain observation may be appropriate if symptoms minimal and that treatments (vitrectomy, ocriplasmin injection, or expansile gas) are options if progression occurs.


CASE 43 — Vitreous Hemorrhage
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye for 3 months, interfering with driving and reading
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 78-year-old woman with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye, interfering with driving and reading; vision 20/60 OD and 20/40 OS.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia in the right eye interfering with driving and reading abilities. The vision was 20/60 OD and 20/40 OS. Slit-lamp examination demonstrated clear cornea, absence of cataract, and intraocular pressure of 13 mm Hg in both eyes. Fundoscopic examination showed focal areas of adherent posterior vitreous to the macula associated with raised retinal tissue, OD, and a nonspecific loss of foveal reflex, OS. Optical coherence tomography (OCT) in OD demonstrated a hyperreflective layer correspondent to the posterior hyaloid partially attached to the macula, leading to elevation and distortion of the retina. The OCT OS also showed focal vitreomacular attachment with distortion of the inner foveal layers. An intravitreal ocriplasmin injection was performed OD, without improvement in vision or OCT appearance after 1 month. A pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) was performed OD with release of the vitreomacular traction (VMT) and improvement of the foveal contour. The vision was 20/30 OD 1 month postoperatively. The OS has remained unchanged with vision of 20/40 without clinically important or progressive symptoms, so it has been observed.
Symptom Onset: 3-month history
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressively worsening over 3 months
Characteristics: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization (associated with wet age-related macular degeneration)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia interfering with driving and reading; posterior vitreous detachment with retinal tear risk and breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization (wet AMD)
Alleviating Factors: Sleeping in a slightly inclined (head-elevated) position to allow blood to settle inferiorly and facilitate examination
Associated Symptoms: progressively worsening blurred vision and metamorphopsia interfering with driving and reading
Impact on Daily Activities: Interfering with driving and reading abilities
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: age (elderly), history of choroidal neovascularization/age-related macular degeneration, posterior vitreous detachment (PVD)/vitreomacular traction, prior retinal vascular disease risk (implicit), non-diabetic (nondiabetic) status


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 13 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear conjunctiva and sclera
External Exam – Lacrimal System: WNL
Anterior Segment – Cornea: clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear cornea, absence of cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive bilaterally, no abnormality detected
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU, no restrictions or pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: clear cornea, absence of cataract
Fundus Exam – Macula: breakthrough subretinal hemorrhage from choroidal neovascularization with loss of foveal reflex
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Nonspecific; no peripheral retinal tears or detachments identified on exam


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Vitreous hemorrhage of the left eye due to breakthrough bleeding from choroidal neovascularization associated with wet age-related macular degeneration. Visual potential limited (20/200) with good fellow-eye vision; continued observation recommended with consideration of vitrectomy only if bilateral visual decline or nonclearing hemorrhage.
Primary Diagnosis: Vitreous hemorrhage due to breakthrough bleeding associated with choroidal neovascularization, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: Choroidal neovascularization (wet age-related macular degeneration)
Differential Diagnosis: Breakthrough vitreous hemorrhage from choroidal neovascularization (wet AMD); Posterior vitreous detachment with retinal tear; Proliferative retinopathy (eg, proliferative diabetic retinopathy or vein occlusion); Vitreous opacities from posterior uveitis (pars planitis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS


Plan
All Medications: ocriplasmin
All Procedures: intravitreal ocriplasmin injection OD pars plana vitrectomy (PPV) OD
Follow-Up Instructions: Close clinical follow-up with repeated fundus exams and B‑scan ultrasound as needed; observe for spontaneous clearing for 3–6 months (unless signs of retinal tear/detachment or nonclearing hemorrhage warrant earlier vitrectomy); consider vitrectomy if no clearing by 3–6 months or sooner if retinal tear, detachment, or other indications; counsel patient about limited visual potential and ensure good fellow‑eye function.
Patient Education: You have a vitreous hemorrhage—blood inside the gel of the eye—most likely from bleeding related to your macular (choroidal neovascular) disease. This can cause blurry vision and distortion. Important points: - Immediate threats to vision include retinal tears or detachment; these are diagnosed with dilated exam and B‑scan ultrasound when the view is limited. - In many cases we observe closely because blood can clear on its own; if it does not clear or if there is an underlying treatable cause, vitrectomy (surgical removal of the vitreous) may be recommended after weeks to months depending on cause and visual potential. - If the hemorrhage is due to neovascular macular degeneration, treatment focuses on controlling the underlying neovascularization (often with injections or laser when appropriate), but visual recovery may be limited. - Positioning (sleeping slightly upright) can help the blood settle inferiorly to improve examination but does not speed clearance. - Seek prompt care for sudden worsening, new flashes, increased floaters, or a curtain/shadow—these may indicate a retinal tear or detachment requiring urgent treatment. - Follow-up with scheduled exams and imaging (OCT and/or B‑scan) is essential to monitor clearing and detect treatable complications.


CASE 44 — Retinitis Pigmentosa
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden decreased vision in the left eye for 3 months
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 90-year-old woman with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye; prior vision 3 years earlier was 20/50 OD and 20/200 OS from atrophic AMD OD and previously treated subfoveal CNV OS; currently VA 20/40 OD and 2/200 OS, IOP 12 mm Hg OU; slit-lamp: right posterior chamber IOL well positioned with vitreous adherent to superior wound; left eye 2+ nuclear sclerosis and dense vitreous hemorrhage precluding posterior view; B-scan showed vitreous hemorrhage with PVD OS, no retinal tear or detachment. She was observed without treatment for 3 months, during which vision improved to 20/400 and a subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery became visible. No history of diabetes or hypertension.
History Narrative: This 90-year-old woman presented with a 3-month history of sudden decreased vision in the left eye. She had prior vision of 20/50 OD and 20/200 OS three years earlier from atrophic age-related macular degeneration OD and a previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS. Medical history negative for diabetes and hypertension. On presentation visual acuity was 20/40 OD and 2/200 OS; intraocular pressures 12 mm Hg OU. Slit-lamp exam showed a well-positioned posterior chamber lens implant OD with vitreous adherent to the superior wound; OS had 2+ nuclear sclerosis and a very dense vitreous hemorrhage precluding posterior view. B-scan ultrasonography demonstrated vitreous hemorrhage with posterior vitreous detachment OS, no retinal tear or detachment, and an indeterminate left macular disciform scar. She was observed without treatment for 3 months; on follow-up VA improved to 20/400 and exam revealed a subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery.
Symptom Onset: between the second and fifth decades of life
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: moderately advanced
Characteristics: Progressive rod and cone photoreceptor dysfunction and death with midperipheral retinal atrophy, pigmentary clumping ("bone spicules"), vascular attenuation, nyctalopia and progressive peripheral vision loss, often with preserved central vision until late stages


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Exposure to bright/ultraviolet sunlight
Alleviating Factors: Observation/monitoring; treatment of cataract or cystoid macular edema if present; sunglasses (reduce UV exposure); possible oral supplements (vitamin A palmitate, DHA, lutein) and low-vision aids; retinal implant for very advanced disease
Associated Symptoms: Gradual progressive nyctalopia and progressive loss of peripheral vision
Prior Treatments: Previously treated subfoveal choroidal neovascular membrane OS; cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens OD.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — progressive peripheral vision loss and nyctalopia impair mobility and night activities but central vision relatively spared until late, allowing many daily tasks with limitations.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Age (typically onset 2nd–5th decades) — family history/genetic inheritance (autosomal recessive, autosomal dominant, X-linked); approximately 50% sporadic cases; multiple causative genes (>150); posterior subcapsular cataract; prior ocular surgery/trauma not specified; no diabetes or hypertension.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 2/200
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or icterus noted)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology evident
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris (no abnormality noted)
Anterior Segment – Angle: open-angle
Anterior Segment – Lens: Right eye: posterior chamber intraocular lens, well-positioned; Left eye: 2+ nuclear sclerosis


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size and shape
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and painless
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right eye: well-positioned posterior chamber lens implant; vitreous adherent to the superior wound. Left eye: 2+ nuclear sclerosis.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: mild optic nerve pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: Subfoveal laser scar with contiguous subretinal hemorrhage extending inferiorly to the midperiphery
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Attenuated
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Midperipheral retinal atrophy with pigmentary clumping ("bone spicules") and vascular attenuation, more apparent inferiorly near the vascular arcades, extending toward the midperiphery.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa, sporadic, moderately advanced
Secondary Diagnoses: Retinal detachment: N/A
Differential Diagnosis: Retinitis pigmentosa; Choroideremia; Gyrate atrophy; Vitamin A deficiency
Severity: moderately advanced
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: - Posterior subcapsular cataract (stellate PSC) - Cystoid macular edema (CME) - Vitreous hemorrhage - Posterior vitreous detachment (PVD) - Retinal atrophy with vascular attenuation and pigmentary clumping (“bone spicules”) - Optic nerve atrophy - Weak zonular lens support leading to challenging cataract extraction and posterior capsular fibrosis requiring multiple YAG capsulotomies - Progressive peripheral visual field loss leading to potential severe vision loss (advanced stages may lead to bare light perception or no light perception)


Plan
All Medications: vitamin A palmitate (15,000 IU daily); vitamin E; lutein; docosahexaenoic acid (DHA); acetazolamide; dorzolamide
Follow-Up Instructions: If cataract or CME develops, consider treatment; periodic yearly liver function tests may be helpful for patients on vitamin A therapy; provide counseling regarding visual prognosis and disease susceptibility; consider low-vision aids and routine follow-up for monitoring and management.
Patient Education: - RP is an inherited retinal disease causing gradual loss of night and peripheral vision; central vision often preserved until late. - Expect slow progression; course varies widely between people. - Avoid excessive ultraviolet light exposure—wear sunglasses outdoors. - Report new sudden vision changes (flashes, new floaters, curtain/field loss) immediately. - Consider genetic counseling/testing to clarify inheritance, rule out syndromic forms, and explore clinical trial eligibility. - Low-vision aids and rehabilitation can help maximize function as vision declines. - If considering vitamin A or other supplements (vitamin A palmitate, DHA, lutein), discuss risks and uncertain benefits with your specialist and consider genotype before starting; periodic liver tests may be advised if on vitamin A. - Cataract or cystoid macular edema are treatable—ask about evaluation and treatment if symptoms arise. - In very advanced disease, retinal implant options exist but provide limited, prosthetic vision.


CASE 45 — Choroiditis
=====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: decreased vision and “blind spots” in both eyes for 1 week
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "blind spots"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with 1-week history of decreased vision and "blind spots" in both eyes; reported fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior to ocular symptoms. Best-corrected visual acuity 20/30 in each eye. No eye pain, no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation noted.
History Narrative: A 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with a 1-week history of decreased vision and "blind spots" in both eyes. He reported recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior to his ocular symptoms. His best-corrected visual acuity was 20/30 in each eye. Intraocular pressures were normal, and there was no afferent pupillary defect. The anterior segment was normal, and there was no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation. The posterior segment contained multiple, flat, deep, creamy, placoid lesions in the posterior pole of both eyes.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Improved with prompt systemic corticosteroid treatment; gradual taper over 4–6 months with risk of recurrence—requires frequent follow-up.
Characteristics: Bilateral, multiple flat deep creamy placoid posterior pole lesions; outer retinal and RPE disruption on OCT; early hypofluorescence with late staining on FA and corresponding hypofluorescence on ICG; no anterior or vitreous inflammation; acute onset after systemic viral-like prodrome; young healthy adult.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior (systemic viral/flu-like illness)
Associated Symptoms: decreased vision; “blind spots” (scotomas); recent fevers; headaches; photopsias; photophobia; floaters
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision and central scotomas in both eyes likely impair reading, driving, and detailed tasks but not complete loss of function.
Systemic Symptoms: recent fevers and headaches 3 weeks prior
Risk Factors: Recent febrile illness/headaches 3 weeks prior; young adult (26-year-old) healthy Caucasian man; systemic autoimmune predisposition (VKH association) — HLA-related susceptibility (e.g., HLA-DR4) inferred.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 122/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.2 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Best-corrected visual acuity (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep, no cells or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear crystalline lens, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal size and reactive (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: no afferent pupillary defect (normal pupillary light reaction)
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion, no restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior segment was normal, and there was no anterior or vitreous cellular inflammation.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Multiple, flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole involving the macula of both eyes; corresponding outer retinal and RPE disruption on OCT.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing retinal vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior pole of both eyes; peripheral fundus otherwise without noted lesions (no peripheral involvement)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 26-year-old healthy Caucasian man with a 1-week history of bilateral decreased vision and scotomas following recent febrile illness, exam notable for normal anterior segment and no vitritis but multiple flat, deep, creamy placoid lesions in the posterior poles bilaterally. Multimodal imaging (FA: early hypofluorescence with late staining; ICG: corresponding hypofluorescent areas; OCT: outer retinal hyperreflectivity, disruption of photoreceptor outer segments and RPE abnormalities) is characteristic of severe bilateral choroiditis consistent with Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome. Differential includes APMPPE, serpiginous choroiditis, MEWDS, and other white-dot syndromes, but the bilateral placoid lesions, imaging pattern, lack of anterior/vitreous inflammation, and prodromal systemic symptoms support VKH as the leading diagnosis. Recommend prompt initiation of high-dose systemic corticosteroid therapy and close follow-up for monitoring response and potential need for steroid-sparing immunosuppression.
Primary Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada syndrome
Differential Diagnosis: APMPPE; Serpiginous choroiditis; MEWDS; VKH syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: posterior synechiae; angle-closure glaucoma; choroidal neovascularization; cataract; exudative retinal detachments


Plan
All Medications: prednisone (oral systemic corticosteroids 80–200 mg/day; may use IV steroids in severe cases), prednisolone acetate 1% (topical), cycloplegic agent, ranitidine 150 mg twice daily, cyclosporine, cyclophosphamide, chlorambucil, azathioprine
All Procedures: fluorescein angiography indocyanine green angiography optical coherence tomography anterior chamber paracentesis intravenous steroid therapy systemic corticosteroid therapy (oral prednisone) topical prednisolone acetate 1% cycloplegic agent administration of H2‑blocker (ranitidine) immunosuppressive therapy (cyclosporine, cyclophosphamide, chlorambucil, azathioprine) cataract surgery (if indicated) surgical intervention for angle‑closure glaucoma (if indicated) surgical treatment for choroidal neovascularization (if indicated)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow frequently in the initial stages to adjust corticosteroid treatment and prevent sequelae; after inflammation and exudative detachment decrease, examine less frequently and taper systemic steroids gradually (slow taper over ~4–6 months, up to 6 months after complete resolution); if steroids inadequate or not tolerated, consider systemic immunosuppressants; monitor regularly for cataract, choroidal neovascularization, and angle‑closure glaucoma.
Patient Education: Explain the diagnosis (Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada syndrome) in simple terms: an autoimmune inflammation affecting the eye’s pigment layer and retina that can cause blurred vision, blind spots, light sensitivity, and sometimes headaches or hearing/skin changes. Emphasize urgency: start systemic corticosteroid treatment promptly to reduce inflammation and prevent permanent vision loss. Describe treatment plan basics: high-dose oral steroids (or IV in severe cases), possible topical steroid and cycloplegic drops for comfort, stomach protection (e.g., ranitidine or PPI), and that steroid taper is slow (months) to lower relapse risk. Discuss monitoring: frequent eye exams early, OCT/angiography as needed, and alertness for side effects (increased blood sugar, weight gain, mood changes, stomach upset, infection risk). Advise reporting immediately any worsening vision, new floaters, severe eye pain, fever, or symptoms suggesting systemic involvement (hearing loss, tinnitus, headache, hair/skin changes). Outline possible additional therapies: if steroids are insufficient or intolerable, other immunosuppressants may be used. Discuss long-term risks and follow-up: potential cataract, glaucoma, or choroidal neovascularization that may require treatment; importance of adherence to medication and appointments. Offer supportive advice: avoid live vaccines while immunosuppressed, maintain good bone, stomach, and glucose care (calcium/vitamin D, diet), and communicate steroid side effects to the team. Provide reassurance but stress close follow-up and prompt reporting of new symptoms.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate ophthalmic care for sudden vision loss, new or worsening blind spots, severe eye pain, marked redness, flashes/floaters, or symptoms of increased intraocular pressure; if on high-dose systemic steroids, go to the emergency department for severe abdominal pain, black/tarry stools, vomiting blood, or signs of infection (fever/chills).


CASE 46 — Retinitis
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness
DURATION: 1 month
LATERALITY: OU
PATIENT QUOTES: "constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old Hispanic woman with 3 weeks of constant right eye pain, 1 week of redness, tearing and blurred vision in the right eye, and 1 month of headache and neck stiffness; recent 2-day flulike symptoms; no trauma. Initial vision 20/20 OU, then 1 week later marked decreased vision in right eye to 3/200 with persistent pain; left eye developed mild inflammation and focal subretinal fluid.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old Hispanic woman complained of constant pain in the right eye for 3 weeks, blurred vision, redness, and tearing for 1 week, and a 1-month history of headache and neck stiffness. She noted flulike symptoms for 2 days previously and denied any history of trauma. Computed tomography (CT) of the brain was normal. A lumbar puncture showed a mild pleocytosis.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Infectious retinal inflammation (usually progressive, caused by viral/fungal/parasitic/opportunistic organisms) leading to retinal necrosis, often unilateral initially, with vitreous cells, optic disc edema, serous/exudative retinal detachment, multifocal yellow‑white RPE/retinal lesions, granulomatous anterior chamber reaction, diffuse choroidal thickening on B‑scan, pinpoint RPE hyperfluorescence with late leakage on FA, CSF lymphocytic pleocytosis if CNS involvement, high risk of retinal detachment.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics
