Associated Symptoms: pain in the right eye, blurred vision, redness, tearing, headache, neck stiffness, flu-like symptoms, decreased vision (markedly decreased in right eye)
Prior Treatments: Topical prednisolone acetate 1% and cycloplegics
Impact on Daily Activities: Markedly decreased visual acuity in the right eye to 3/200 with pain and vision-related impairment likely causing severe interference with daily activities (significant disability).
Systemic Symptoms: flu-like symptoms for 2 days, headache and neck stiffness, tinnitus not reported but neurologic symptoms (meningism) implied
Risk Factors: HIV infection/AIDS, immunodeficiency (especially low CD4 count), systemic immunosuppression (e.g., chemotherapy, corticosteroids), organ transplantation, opportunistic infections, advanced age (lesser), prior ocular surgery/trauma (as potential contributory)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 86 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Redness (injection)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Moderate cell and flare in the right eye; mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction in the left eye.
Anterior Segment – Iris: granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium with moderate anterior chamber cell and flare (right > left)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (wide)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk/reactive
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: briskly reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restrictions or pain on movement
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Moderate anterior chamber cell and flare in the right eye with granulomatous keratic precipitates on the corneal endothelium; mild anterior chamber inflammatory reaction in the left eye.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: optic disc edema (swollen optic nerve)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Serous retinal detachment with macular retinal folds and focal yellow-white RPE lesions; subretinal fluid in macula.
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course, no retinal vascular occlusion or neovascularization detected
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retinal whitening and focal areas of necrosis in the peripheral retina with multiple areas of necrosis/retinal breaks leading to high risk of retinal detachment


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: CMV retinitis, OU, in a patient with AIDS.
Primary Diagnosis: CMV retinitis, OU, in a patient with AIDS
Secondary Diagnoses: AIDS; CMV retinitis; posterior uveitis; optic disc edema; serous (exudative) retinal detachment; choroidal thickening; vitreous inflammation (vitritis); granulomatous anterior uveitis
Differential Diagnosis: Vogt–Koyanagi–Harada (VKH) syndrome; sympathetic ophthalmia; infectious posterior uveitis (e.g., CMV, toxoplasmosis); sarcoidosis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Retinal detachment; cataract formation; long-term visual loss (severe visual loss)


Plan
All Medications: prednisolone acetate 1%; cycloplegics; ganciclovir (intravenous and intravitreal and implant); foscarnet (intravenous and intravitreal); cidofovir
All Procedures: topical prednisolone acetate 1% administration; cycloplegic therapy; computed tomography (CT) of the brain; lumbar puncture with CSF analysis; slit-lamp examination; B-scan ultrasonography of the right eye; fluorescein angiography; intravenous ganciclovir induction therapy (5 mg/kg once daily) — alternative: intravenous foscarnet induction (60 mg/kg three times daily); intravitreal ganciclovir injection (2000 µg) — alternative: intravitreal foscarnet (1.2 mg); intravitreal ganciclovir implant insertion; pars plana vitrectomy with silicone oil tamponade; retinal laser demarcation (for small peripheral detachments); complete blood count and serologic testing (RPR, FTA-ABS, ANA, ACE, Lyme titer), PPD and chest X-ray as indicated
Follow-Up Instructions: Close follow-up with photographic documentation.


CASE 47 — The White Dot Syndromes
=================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of the right and left eyes for 2 weeks"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of AIDS (CD4 64) on HAART presented with 2 weeks of new onset multiple black spots (floaters) in both visual fields. She reported no anterior segment symptoms; systemic history notable for long-standing HIV/AIDS. Examination: BCVA 20/30 OD and 20/40 OS, IOP 10 mm Hg OU; fundus showed creamy yellowish-white retinitis with hemorrhage and vitritis OD and peripheral retinitis temporally OS.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old woman with a 10-year history of acquired immune deficiency syndrome (AIDS) presented with new onset of multiple black spots in the visual field of both eyes for 2 weeks. She had a CD4 count of 64 and was on highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART). Examination revealed best corrected visual acuity 20/30 OD and 20/40 OS, intraocular pressure 10 mm Hg OU, an unremarkable anterior segment without anterior chamber cell or flare. Fundus exam OD showed creamy yellowish-white retinitis with prominent hemorrhage within the lesion, active vitritis, and vascular sheathing superior to the disc extending along the superotemporal arcade; OS had a peripheral area of retinitis temporally.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Characteristics: less fulminant inflammatory chorioretinal white spots at the level of the choroid (often deep, creamy/white or yellow lesions), variably associated with minimal anterior/vitreous inflammation, characteristic imaging findings on FA/ICG/OCT, and potential for vision loss or complications (e.g., CNVM, CME) depending on the specific syndrome.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: viral prodrome, headaches
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — transient visual disturbances (central vision decrease, scotomas, photopsias) that may temporarily impair reading/driving but often improve to near baseline with good prognosis
Systemic Symptoms: viral prodrome, headaches
Risk Factors: Younger age (20–40 years), female sex (many entities), Caucasian ethnicity, myopia (particularly MEWDS, MCP, PIC), HLA-A29 positivity (birdshot), recent viral prodrome, and immunosuppression (e.g., low CD4 in HIV/CVID for CMV retinitis); prior tuberculosis or systemic infections (for serpiginous-like presentations).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (N5)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity (BCVA)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, noninjected
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: No anterior chamber cell or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive (no abnormality noted)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable anterior segment with no anterior chamber cell or flare.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Numerous deep cream-colored placoid lesions in the posterior pole involving the macula (granular macular appearance with small white and orange specks)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Right eye: confluent wedge of retinitis with hemorrhage extending from superior nerve head into midperiphery; vascular sheathing superior to disc along superotemporal arcade. Left eye: peripheral area of retinitis temporally.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 32-year-old woman with AIDS (CD4 64) on HAART presenting with 2 weeks of new floaters and multiple black spots OU. Exam: BCVA 20/30 OD, 20/40 OS; IOP 10 mm Hg OU; quiet anterior segments. Fundus OD: confluent, creamy yellow–white retinitis with prominent intralesional hemorrhage and active vitritis with vascular sheathing along the superotemporal arcade; OS: peripheral temporal retinitis without hemorrhage. Clinical picture most consistent with CMV retinitis (fulminant/hemorrhagic form OD, granular form OS) in the context of advanced immunosuppression. Differential includes acute retinal necrosis, toxoplasmosis, syphilitic placoid retinitis, and other opportunistic infections, but lack of significant anterior chamber inflammation, location and hemorrhagic necrosis tracking along arcades, and low CD4 favor CMV. Recommend prompt ophthalmic management with intraocular and/or systemic anti-CMV therapy and coordination with infectious disease for systemic antiviral treatment and optimization of HAART; baseline and serial wide-field fundus photography/indirect ophthalmoscopy and consideration of prophylactic measures for retinal detachment; screen for other opportunistic infections and perform diagnostic testing as indicated (e.g., PCR of intraocular fluid if atypical or poor response).
Primary Diagnosis: Cytomegalovirus (CMV) retinitis
Differential Diagnosis: CMV retinitis; Acute retinal necrosis (herpes zoster); Ocular toxoplasmosis; Syphilitic retinitis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Retinal detachment; choroidal neovascularization (CNVM); cystoid macular edema (CME); epiretinal membrane (ERM); RPE atrophy and scarring; optic neuropathy; neovascular glaucoma


Plan
All Medications: highly active antiretroviral therapy (HAART) oral corticosteroids periocular steroids systemic steroids immunosuppression / immunomodulatory therapy (e.g., cyclosporine, mycophenolate, azathioprine) antituberculosis therapy intravitreal steroids anti-vascular endothelial growth factor (anti-VEGF)
All Procedures: Funduscopic examination with indirect ophthalmoscopy; fluorescein angiography; optical coherence tomography (OCT); indocyanine green angiography (ICG); fundus autofluorescence (FAF); electroretinogram (ERG); Goldmann and Humphrey visual field testing; fundus photography; Amsler grid testing; laboratory evaluation for infectious etiologies (syphilis, tuberculosis) (standard-of-care)
Patient Education: Your eye condition involves inflammation of the back of the eye (choroid/RPE), which often causes sudden changes in vision such as blurred vision, scotomas (blind spots), and photopsias (flashes). Many “white dot” syndromes follow a viral-like illness and most are self-limited and improve over weeks to months. Eye imaging (fluorescein angiography, OCT, and sometimes indocyanine green or autofluorescence) helps confirm the diagnosis and monitor healing. Most entities (MEWDS, APMPPE, ARPE, PIC) have a good prognosis without treatment and vision often recovers substantially, but some forms (serpiginous, birdshot, multifocal choroiditis) are chronic, can recur, and may require immunosuppressive therapy to prevent permanent vision loss. Seek prompt ophthalmic care for any new floaters, flashes, or visual field loss; if your lesions involve the central macula or you have neurologic symptoms (headache, focal deficits), treatment (often oral corticosteroids) or neurologic evaluation may be indicated. Regular follow-up with retinal imaging is important to monitor activity and detect complications such as cystoid macular edema or choroidal neovascularization, which may need additional treatments (steroids, immunomodulation, or anti‑VEGF injections).


CASE 48 — Vascular Tumor
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for 3 days (2-week history of photopsias, blurred central vision for 3 days)
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had photopsias and blurred central vision in my right eye for the past 3 days."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 55-year-old Caucasian man with a 2-week history of photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old Caucasian man presented with a 2-week history of photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days. Past medical history was notable for hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and skin cancer. Past ocular history was remarkable for macular degeneration.
Symptom Onset: 2 weeks
Symptom Duration: 2 weeks
Progression: Decreased in size (height reduced from 4.5 mm to 2.3 mm) with resolution of overlying neurosensory detachment and progressive pigmentary alterations; visual acuity improved to 20/40.
Characteristics: Dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal choroidal mass with intrinsic vascularity, low internal reflectivity on A-scan, regular internal structure, choroidal excavation, associated exudative retinal detachment, thickness 4.5 mm (decreased to 2.3 mm posttreatment), base ~11.9 × 10.1 mm, progressive late leakage on fluorescein angiography, no extrascleral extension.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Photopsias; blurred central vision; visual acuity loss; persistent photopsias; visual field defects
Previous Episodes: 2-week history of photopsias and blurred central vision in the right eye for the previous 3 days.
Prior Treatments: Iodine-125 radioactive plaque therapy with fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling.
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — transient visual disturbance in right eye (blurred central vision and photopsias) causing some difficulty but overall preserved function (20/40–20/60), no major functional impairment.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, skin cancer
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: First-degree relatives (family members)
Risk Factors: Hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, history of skin cancer, age (55 years), Caucasian race, family history (possible—phakomatosis noted in discussion though not explicit), von Hippel–Lindau disease (as a related condition)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 F
Vitals – Respirations: 14 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Within normal limits
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear corneas with mild nuclear sclerotic changes noted on slit-lamp (no corneal pathology)
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild nuclear sclerotic lens changes bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: 1+
Pupils – Other: 1+ right afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild nuclear sclerotic alterations in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Exudative retinal detachment involving the macula (blurred central vision) with resolution posttreatment and no radiation-related vasculopathy.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Unremarkable


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 55-year-old Caucasian man with a 2-week history of photopsias and 3 days of blurred central vision in the right eye. Exam: VA 20/60 OD, 20/20 OS; IOP 15 mm Hg OU; 1+ right afferent pupillary defect; mild nuclear sclerotic changes OU. Dilated fundus exam OD: dome-shaped moderately pigmented inferotemporal choroidal mass with an associated anterior exudative retinal detachment. Lesion thickness 4.5 mm with basal dimensions approximately 11.9 × 10.1 mm by ultrasonography. B-scan: dome-shaped, regularly structured mass with choroidal excavation and exudative retinal detachment; no extrascleral extension. A-scan: low internal reflectivity and regular internal structure. Fluorescein angiography: intrinsic vascularity with progressive late leakage. Systemic metastatic workup (LFTs, chest X-ray) unremarkable. Diagnosis: medium-sized posterior uveal (choroidal) melanoma. Management: fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling at time of iodine-125 plaque radiotherapy. Gene expression profiling: Class 1A tumor (low metastatic risk) — plan annual liver ultrasound surveillance. Seven-month follow-up: VA OD 20/40 with progressive nuclear sclerosis; tumor regressed to height 2.3 mm with pigmentary changes and resolution of overlying neurosensory detachment; no radiation-related retinal vasculopathy.
Primary Diagnosis: Choroidal melanoma
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; hypercholesterolemia; skin cancer; macular degeneration
Differential Diagnosis: Choroidal hemangioma; Metastatic carcinoma to choroid; Choroidal nevus (with secondary exudation)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: - Exudative retinal detachment - Progressive nuclear sclerosis (cataract) - Vision loss / decreased visual acuity - Vitreous hemorrhage (occasionally) - Epiretinal membrane formation and vitreoretinal traction - Radiation-related changes (potential)


Plan
All Medications: hypertension medication (unspecified), hypercholesterolemia medication (unspecified), iodine-125 radioactive plaque (radiation therapy, not a medication)
All Procedures: fine-needle aspiration biopsy for gene-expression profiling; iodine-125 radioactive plaque placement (plaque radiotherapy); B-scan ultrasonography; A-scan ultrasonography; fluorescein angiography; liver ultrasound (metastatic evaluation); chest X-ray; fundus photography; optical coherence tomography (OCT) with enhanced depth imaging; pars plana vitrectomy; laser photocoagulation; cryotherapy (double or triple freeze–thaw cryotherapy); transpupillary thermotherapy; intravitreal or systemic antiangiogenic injections; external beam radiotherapy; genetic testing; 24-hour urine collection for pheochromocytoma; systemic imaging of CNS, thorax, and abdomen
Follow-Up Instructions: Annual to biannual ophthalmic examinations for affected individuals and first‑degree relatives; periodic systemic screenings (CNS, thoracic and abdominal imaging) and 24‑hour urine collection for pheochromocytoma; coordinate care with appropriate specialists.
Patient Education: Recommend evaluation for von Hippel–Lindau (VHL) disease given retinal angiomas; discuss genetic testing and autosomal dominant inheritance with implications for first‑degree relatives. Explain need for regular ophthalmic follow‑up (annual to biannual indirect ophthalmoscopy) to monitor for new or growing angiomas. Review systemic screening recommendations for VHL: CNS and thoracic/abdominal imaging (eg, MRI abdomen/CNS), and 24‑hour urine catecholamines/metanephrines for pheochromocytoma. Discuss treatment options for retinal angiomas (observation, laser photocoagulation, cryotherapy, plaque or external beam radiotherapy, intravitreal or systemic anti‑VEGF in select cases, possible pars plana vitrectomy for complications), expected course after treatment, and potential complications (vision loss, exudative detachment, cataract, traction, recurrence). Advise prompt reporting of new symptoms (vision changes, headaches, systemic symptoms) and recommend family members undergo screening/genetic counseling.


CASE 49 — Choroidal Melanoma
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 20-year-old man asymptomatic; peripheral retinal lesion in left eye found on routine ophthalmic exam; no other ocular history; no known systemic medical problems; family history positive for von Hippel–Lindau disease in father and two siblings; visual acuity 20/20 bilaterally; external exam unremarkable; no afferent pupillary defect; visual field and motility unremarkable; slit-lamp normal; left fundus with dilated tortuous arterioles and venules from optic disc leading to reddish‑pink nodules surrounding large reddish‑orange lesion from retina with small area of subretinal fluid and adjacent exudate; right fundus normal.
History Narrative: A 20-year-old man presents for evaluation of a peripheral retinal lesion in the left eye found on routine ophthalmic examination. He is asymptomatic with no other past ocular history and no known systemic medical problems. Family history is notable for his father and two siblings with von Hippel–Lindau disease. Visual acuity is 20/20 bilaterally. External examination is unremarkable. There is no afferent pupillary defect. Visual field and motility examinations are unremarkable. Slit-lamp examination is normal. Funduscopic examination of the left eye shows several dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally, leading to a collection of reddish-pink nodules surrounding a large reddish-orange lesion on the retinal surface with a small associated area of subretinal fluid and adjacent exudate. Right eye fundus is normal.
Symptom Duration: Asymptomatic
Characteristics: Pigmented (often), dome- or mushroom‑shaped choroidal lesion with low-to-medium internal reflectivity on A‑scan, intrinsic vascularity on B‑scan, may show orange‑pigmented lipofuscin (hyperautofluorescence) and associated subretinal fluid on OCT


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Asymptomatic
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: - Fair skin / light iris color - Older age (highest risk in middle-aged to older adults) - Caucasian race - Presence of choroidal nevus (pre-existing melanocytic lesion) - Male sex (slightly higher risk) - Genetic predisposition (germline mutations; e.g., BAP1 alterations) - UV light exposure (possible association)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 124/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Unremarkable
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lens, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: No afferent pupillary defect (no mention of abnormal size/shape) — pupils equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: unremarkable
Slit Lamp Exam Description: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules emanating from the optic disc and traveling nasally
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Several dilated, tortuous arterioles and venules nasally with a collection of reddish‑orange nodules and small adjacent subretinal fluid/exudate.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right eye: medium-sized choroidal melanoma.
Primary Diagnosis: Right eye: medium-sized choroidal melanoma
Differential Diagnosis: Choroidal nevus; Metastatic carcinoma to the choroid; Choroidal hemangioma; Melanocytoma
Severity: Medium
Laterality: Right eye
Complications: Radiation-related complications including retinopathy, optic neuropathy, and cataract; radiation-vasculopathy and optic neuropathy (occurring in ~30–50% at 5 years for tumors adjacent to optic nerve or fovea)


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up every 3 months for the first year, then every 6 months for 2 years, then annually thereafter; perform clinical exam, fundus photography, and ultrasonography at each visit, with AF and OCT as needed; for patients with gene expression profiling: class 1A—annual liver imaging; class 1B—annual liver imaging and liver enzymes staggered by 6 months; class 2—liver imaging and liver enzymes every 6 months staggered by 3 months.
Patient Education: Diagnosis: choroidal melanoma — a malignant tumor in the pigmented layer of the eye. Treatment options depend on tumor size/location and include plaque (brachytherapy) radiation to save the eye, local resection, or enucleation for very large tumors or poor vision. Small tumors may be observed or treated with transpupillary thermotherapy in select cases. Expected follow-up: frequent eye exams with fundus photos and ultrasound (every 3 months first year, then every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly) to monitor tumor regression, recurrence, and radiation complications. Systemic monitoring for metastasis (especially liver) is required long-term; timing guided by tumor risk (gene expression profile): low-risk — annual liver imaging; intermediate — annual imaging with liver enzymes staggered 6 months; high-risk — liver imaging and liver enzymes every 6 months staggered by 3 months. Possible side effects/complications: vision loss, radiation retinopathy, optic neuropathy, cataract, and need for further treatment; enucleation results in loss of the eye and requires orbital prosthesis. Symptoms that should prompt immediate contact: new sudden vision loss, increasing floaters, flashes, pain, or rapid changes in appearance of the eye. Discuss genetic testing and systemic workup for metastatic disease; if relevant, referral for oncology and for genetic counseling. Offer educational materials, support resources, and review treatment risks/benefits to assist shared decision-making.
Referrals: Ocular oncology (retina/ocular oncology specialist) and medical oncology for metastatic workup and long-term systemic surveillance (including liver imaging).


CASE 50 — Dislocated Posterior Chamber Intraocular Lens
=======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: OS
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 78-year-old woman presented 4 days after a complicated left cataract surgery with acute-onset severe left eye pain and loss of vision; past ocular history notable for high myopia (−9.00 D) and primary open-angle glaucoma; on timolol, dorzolamide, and topical prednisolone in the left eye. Vision was hand motions OS, IOP 39 mmHg OS; exam showed temporal clear corneal incision closed with nylon sutures, shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, conjunctival injection, and a bullous appositional retinal elevation; B‑scan showed appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages.
History Narrative: A 78-year-old woman presented 4 days after a complicated cataract surgery in her left eye, complaining of acute-onset severe pain and loss of vision in the left eye. Past medical history was notable for cardiac bypass surgery 2 years previously and systemic hypertension. Past ocular history was significant for myopia (–9.00 sphere) and primary open-angle glaucoma. Ocular medications included timolol 0.5% in both eyes twice daily, dorzolamide 2% in both eyes three times per day, and prednisolone acetate 1% in the left eye four times per day.
Symptom Onset: 4 days after a complicated cataract surgery
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Improved to 20/20 at 6 years postoperatively
Characteristics: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: advanced age; atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease; systemic anticoagulation; hypertension; myopia; glaucoma; aphakia; recent intraocular surgery
Alleviating Factors: Observation, topical anti-inflammatory agents, topical ocular hypotensive agents, topical miotics
Associated Symptoms: Severe pain; loss of vision; glare and monocular diplopia mentioned as possible with decentration (in context of management)
Prior Treatments: timolol 0.5% OU twice daily; dorzolamide 2% OU three times daily; prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times daily; recent complicated cataract surgery OS (intraoperative termination with incision sutured)
Impact on Daily Activities: Severe — unable to perform normal visual tasks (hand motion vision OS, pain, significant functional loss)
Systemic Symptoms: cardiac bypass surgery 2 years previously; systemic hypertension
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: advanced age; atherosclerotic cardiovascular disease; systemic anticoagulation; hypertension; myopia; glaucoma; aphakia; recent intraocular surgery; compromise of zonular integrity (surgical trauma, nonsurgical trauma, pseudoexfoliation syndrome)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 12 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 39 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 2+ conjunctival injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal appearing orbit
Anterior Segment – Cornea: temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow
Anterior Segment – Lens: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2 + conjunctival injection, a temporal clear cornea incision reapproximated with interrupted nylon sutures, a shallow anterior chamber, aphakia, and the appearance of a bullous appositional retinal detachment posterior to the iris plane


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens, left eye (OS), with acute-onset severe pain, hand motion vision, elevated IOP (39 mm Hg), shallow anterior chamber and aphakia after complicated cataract surgery; presentation and ultrasound consistent with appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages causing bullous retinal/choroidal elevation; persistent suprachoroidal clot with subsequent liquefaction requiring pars plana vitrectomy and suprachoroidal hemorrhage drainage and fluid–gas exchange; postoperative course with IOP medically controlled and visual recovery to 20/400 at 3 months.
Primary Diagnosis: Dislocated posterior chamber IOL, OS
Secondary Diagnoses: myopia (−9.00 D); primary open-angle glaucoma; systemic hypertension; history of coronary artery bypass graft (2 years prior)
Differential Diagnosis: Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Exudative (serous) retinal detachment/choroidal effusion; Suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: elevated intraocular pressure; cystoid macular edema; retinal detachment; suture knot erosion; endophthalmitis; hemorrhage; IOL torsion or malposition; recurrent dislocation due to suture breakage; endothelial trauma; postsurgical astigmatism


Plan
All Medications: timolol 0.5% OU twice daily; dorzolamide 2% OU three times daily; prednisolone acetate 1% OS four times per day
All Procedures: Pars plana vitrectomy with drainage of suprachoroidal hemorrhage and fluid–gas exchange; cataract incision closure with interrupted nylon sutures; medical management of elevated IOP (topical ocular hypotensives); scleral fixation techniques / IOL repositioning or exchange discussed (transscleral suture fixation with 9–0 polypropylene as standard option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up with ophthalmology for serial echography and clinical exams; monitor IOP and retinal status; if suprachoroidal hemorrhage/retinal apposition persists, plan pars plana vitrectomy with drainage when liquefaction occurs (typically within days–weeks); postoperative visits to assess visual acuity, IOP, and macula (OCT) with peripheral retinal exam for breaks; routine follow-up intervals per surgeon (early post-op, 1 week, 1 month, and ongoing as needed).


CASE 51 — Cystoid Macular Edema
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after cataract surgery
DURATION: 6 months
LATERALITY: OD
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64-year-old woman presented with sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an uncomplicated extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens insertion; during the procedure a central posterior capsular rupture occurred and the IOL was placed into the ciliary sulcus, with postoperative findings of 2+ superior microbullous corneal edema, moderate anterior chamber cells, vitreous incarceration in the wound, a round pupil, clear pupillary space, and a freely mobile PCIOL within the vitreous cavity inferiorly; vision was 20/30 with an aphakic correction at presentation.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with a sudden decreased vision in the left eye 2 days after an uncomplicated extracapsular cataract extraction surgery with posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) insertion. During the procedure, a central posterior capsular rupture was noted, but the IOL was placed anterior to the remaining anterior capsule into the ciliary sulcus. Her vision was 20/30 in the left eye with an aphakic correction. Slit-lamp examination showed a 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate cells in the anterior chamber. The pupil was round, but there was vitreous incarceration in the wound. The pupillary space was clear. Residual capsule was not noted. Funduscopic examination disclosed a freely mobile PCIOL within the vitreous cavity inferiorly. The retina was attached. Surgery was recommended and performed with repositioning of the dislocated PCIOL into the ciliary sulcus using a scleral suturing technique to fixate the haptics. The scleral fixation sutures were placed through two partial-thickness superotemporal and inferonasal scleral flaps to cover the suture knots. The IOL was in good position 1 month postoperatively with vision at 20/60. The vision returned to 20/20 within 6 months and remained so 6 years after the surgical repair. The IOL remained centered and well positioned, with no sign of complication. In the right eye, the vision was 20/400 due to nuclear sclerosis.
Symptom Onset: 2 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Progression: Resolved (vision returned to 20/20 within 6 months and remained stable for 6 years)
Characteristics: Cystoid macular edema following intraocular surgery (postoperative/pseudophakic), characterized by macular thickening with cystic intraretinal spaces on OCT and macular fluorescein pooling on angiography, often associated with inflammation, vitreous prolapse/incarceration, and possible IOL dislocation after complicated cataract extraction.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Recent intraocular surgery (complicated cataract extraction with posterior capsular rupture and vitreous prolapse/incarceration), vitreous incarceration/prolapsed vitreous, retained lens fragments, iris trauma, intraocular inflammation
Associated Symptoms: sudden decreased vision, glare, monocular diplopia, pain
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — temporary decreased vision affecting activities (impaired reading/driving) for weeks to months with eventual full recovery in affected eye.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: - Recent intraocular surgery (especially complicated cataract extraction) - Posterior capsular rupture - Vitreous prolapse/incarceration into wound or anterior chamber - Dislocated or malpositioned IOL - Iris trauma - Retained lens fragments - Vitreomacular traction/adhesion - Intraocular inflammation - Preexisting retinovascular disease


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity with correction (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Within normal limits (no abnormalities of lids or lashes noted)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal, quiet conjunctiva and sclera
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: 2+ microbullous corneal edema superiorly
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: moderate cells in the anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Iris: Round pupil
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (PCIOL) in ciliary sulcus with prior posterior capsular rupture; later dislocated into vitreous then surgically scleral-fixated and ultimately well-centered and stable.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Round, reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Vitreous incarceration in the wound
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, no restriction or diplopia noted
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 2 + microbullous corneal edema superiorly and moderate cells in the anterior chamber.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal appearing
Fundus Exam – Macula: Cystoid macular edema (CME) with macular thickening and cystic changes
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Retina attached, no peripheral retinal breaks or detachments noted; periphery otherwise unremarkable


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema of the right eye (OD) following complicated cataract extraction with posterior capsular rupture and subsequent intraocular lens dislocation; clinical exam and imaging (FA/OCT) demonstrate macular thickening with cystic changes. Initial management with topical NSAID and corticosteroid therapy is recommended with close follow-up in 4–6 weeks; consider periocular or intravitreal steroid injection or pars plana vitrectomy if medical therapy fails or if vitreomacular traction/retained lens material is present. Continue monitoring for recurrence and associated retinal pigment epithelium atrophy.
Primary Diagnosis: Postoperative (pseudophakic) cystoid macular edema, OD
Differential Diagnosis: Pseudophakic (Irvine–Gass) cystoid macular edema; Vitreomacular traction–induced macular edema; Inflammatory/postoperative uveitic macular edema; Retained lens fragment–associated macular edema
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OD
Complications: - Posterior capsular rupture - Vitreous prolapse/incarceration in the wound - Dislocated PCIOL into the vitreous cavity (IOL dislocation) - Microbullous corneal edema - Anterior chamber inflammation (cells) - Cystoid macular edema


Plan
All Medications: flurbiprofen (topical), suprofen (topical), ketorolac 0.5% (topical), prednisolone 1% (topical), periocular corticosteroid injection, intravitreal corticosteroid injection, ranibizumab (intravitreal anti-VEGF), bevacizumab (intravitreal anti-VEGF), aflibercept (intravitreal anti-VEGF)
All Procedures: extracapsular cataract extraction with posterior chamber intraocular lens insertion; repositioning of dislocated posterior chamber intraocular lens with scleral suturing fixation via pars plana approach (scleral fixation through partial-thickness superotemporal and inferonasal scleral flaps); pars plana vitrectomy (as a general option)
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe within 4 to 6 weeks after initiating medical treatment; follow carefully thereafter with ongoing monitoring for recurrence.
Patient Education: Cystoid macular edema (CME) is swelling in the central retina that can occur after eye surgery. It often causes blurred or decreased central vision but may improve with treatment or on its own over weeks to months. Diagnosis is made by eye exam and retinal imaging (OCT, fluorescein angiography). First-line treatment is topical anti-inflammatory eye drops: a steroid (e.g., prednisolone) and a nonsteroidal anti-inflammatory drug (e.g., ketorolac) used together. If there is no improvement after 4–6 weeks, additional options include periocular or intravitreal steroid injections, anti-VEGF injections (less predictable), or surgical treatment (vitrectomy) if vitreous traction or retained lens material is present. Keep all follow-up appointments; vision may gradually improve but can recur, and chronic cases can cause lasting vision loss. Report new pain, marked vision loss, flashes, or new floaters promptly.


CASE 52 — Endophthalmitis
=========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased visual acuity in the right eye 8 weeks after phacoemulsification
DURATION: 8 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "the visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 66-year-old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification complaining of decreased visual acuity in the right eye. An acrylic intraocular lens had been placed in the ciliary sulcus at the time of surgery because of a central posterior capsular rupture. Vision was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. She reported no other contributory medical or ophthalmic history, including diabetes or hypertension.
History Narrative: A 66-year-old woman presented 8 weeks after phacoemulsification complaining of decreased visual acuity in the right eye. An acrylic intraocular lens was inserted into the ciliary sulcus at the time of surgery due to a central posterior capsular rupture. The patient reported that the visual acuity was excellent postoperatively but declined substantially after the first 4 weeks. The patient reported no other contributory medical or ophthalmic history including diabetes mellitus or hypertension.
Symptom Onset: 8 weeks after phacoemulsification
Symptom Duration: 8 weeks
Characteristics: Intraocular infection causing severe intraocular inflammation, often postoperative, presenting with pain, decreased vision, conjunctival hyperemia, hypopyon, fibrin, and vitreous opacities; can be acute or chronic, exogenous (postoperative, post-injection, posttraumatic, bleb-associated) or endogenous (hematogenous), variable onset and severity depending on organism virulence.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: - Intraocular surgery (especially cataract surgery) - Posterior capsular rupture, vitreous loss, retained nuclear or cortical material - Intravitreal injections - Ocular trauma - Bleb-related surgery (filtering blebs) - Immunodeficiency, diabetes mellitus, intravenous drug use, indwelling catheters, malignancy (risk for endogenous endophthalmitis)
Associated Symptoms: Decreased visual acuity (progressive vision loss), painless inflammation (characteristic of late-onset cases), no pain, no conjunctival hyperemia or other systemic symptoms reported.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — decreased vision to 20/80 in the affected eye likely causes noticeable difficulty with activities requiring fine vision (reading, driving) but some function preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: No other systemic symptoms reported
Risk Factors: - Recent intraocular surgery (especially cataract surgery) - Intravitreal injections - Ocular trauma - Retained lens material, posterior capsular rupture, vitreous loss - Bleb-associated surgery (filtration bleb) - Immunodeficiency - Diabetes mellitus - Intravenous drug use - Indwelling catheters - Malignancy - Use of prostaglandin analogs (postoperative risk factor) - Use of preservative-containing intravitreal triamcinolone (sterile inflammation mimic) - Lack of povidone-iodine antisepsis or breaches in sterile technique


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jäger 6 (J6)
Visual Acuity – Method: Best corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Conjunctival hyperemia
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: The cornea was clear and of normal thickness
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: The iris appeared normal without peaking
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open and deep
Anterior Segment – Lens: Acrylic IOL in ciliary sulcus (pseudophakia)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: normal
Extraocular Movements: Full without limitation
Slit Lamp Exam Description: conjunctival hyperemia, hypopyon within the anterior chamber, and fibrin on the intraocular lens
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: The optic nerve head was normal with a cup-to-disc ratio of 0.2.
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular thickening with cystic spaces in the fovea (cystoid macular edema).
Fundus Exam – Vessels: The retinal vessels were normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal without holes, breaks, or tears


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute postoperative endophthalmitis, left eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute postoperative endophthalmitis
Differential Diagnosis: Infectious endophthalmitis (acute postoperative); Toxic anterior segment syndrome; Retained lens material–induced inflammation; Exacerbation of preexisting uveitis
Severity: Acute postoperative (moderate–severe)
Laterality: Left eye
Complications: Endophthalmitis


Plan
All Medications: vancomycin ceftazidime amikacin dexamethasone topical vancomycin aminoglycoside povidone-iodine aflibercept triamcinolone fourth-generation fluoroquinolones
All Procedures: vitreous tap and intravitreal injection of antibiotics (vancomycin and ceftazidime)
Follow-Up Instructions: Prompt intravitreal antibiotics (vancomycin + ceftazidime or amikacin) after vitreous tap; consider addition of intravitreal dexamethasone based on inflammation/fungal suspicion; obtain vitreous cultures and smears; follow clinically with frequent examinations to monitor response and vision; perform PPV promptly if presenting vision is light perception only or if deterioration despite tap-and-inject; for chronic or suspected P. acnes consider PPV with partial/total capsulectomy and possible IOL exchange/removal; involve infectious disease for endogenous cases and give systemic antibiotics/antifungals as indicated.
Patient Education: You have an infection inside your eye called endophthalmitis, most often occurring after eye surgery. It can cause decreased vision and may become serious if not treated promptly. Treatment generally involves taking a sample of the eye fluid and injecting strong antibiotics directly into the eye; surgery (vitrectomy) is recommended if vision is very poor or if the infection is severe. If the infection is delayed or indolent, a more extensive surgery to remove lens/capsule material may be needed. Because some infections can come from elsewhere in the body, blood-borne causes may require systemic antibiotics or antifungals and coordination with your physician. Prevention measures around surgery or injections include sterile technique and povidone‑iodine; routine topical antibiotics have not been shown to reduce risk. Promptly report any increasing pain, redness, dramatic vision loss, or new floaters after surgery or injections so treatment can begin quickly.


CASE 53 — Suprachoroidal Hemorrhage
===================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 10/10 left eye pain and decreased vision in left eye
DURATION: 5 days
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I woke up with 10 out of 10 eye pain and my vision was decreased in my left eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 59-year-old woman awoke 5 days after an uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure with sudden severe (10/10) left eye pain and decreased vision. On presentation vision was hand motion; exam showed pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, a shallow anterior chamber with anterior chamber reaction and hypopyon, fibrin on the intraocular lens, temporal clear corneal cataract incision reapproximated with nylon sutures, and no view to the posterior pole. B‑scan ultrasonography demonstrated moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities and appositional (kissing) suprachoroidal hemorrhages.
History Narrative: This 59-year-old woman awoke with sudden severe left eye pain (10/10) and decreased vision 5 days after an uneventful combined cataract and LASIK procedure. She reported rapid onset of symptoms shortly following the recent intraocular surgery.
Symptom Onset: Awoke with sudden onset (upon awakening) — 5 days after surgery
Symptom Duration: Awoke with symptoms 5 days after surgery (acute onset that morning)
Progression: Improved to 20/20 several months after initial presentation.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Severe ocular pain, decreased vision, conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, anterior chamber reaction with hypopyon, fibrin on the IOL.
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Combined cataract and LASIK procedure 5 days prior; vitreous tap and intravitreal vancomycin, ceftazidime, dexamethasone; hourly topical fortified vancomycin and ceftazidime, prednisolone QID, cyclopentolate TID; later laser capsulotomy.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant temporary functional impairment (severe pain and decreased vision to hand motion initially with gradual recovery to 20/20 over months after treatment)
Risk Factors: - Diabetes - Posterior capsular rupture - Wound leaks - Intraoperative hypotony - Myopia (especially high myopia) - Preexisting ocular hypertension/elevated intraocular pressure - Systemic hypertension (blood pressure/tachycardia) - Advanced age - Use of antimetabolites (in bleb-associated eyes) - Blebs with leakage or inferior surgical location - Vitreous prolapse/incarceration in wound or bleb


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motion
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Pronounced conjunctival hyperemia
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: temporal clear cornea cataract incision reapproximated with nylon sutures, corneal edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: shallow or flat
Anterior Segment – Iris: iris apposed to anterior retina with anterior displacement (iris touch)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow to closed (shallow/flat anterior chamber with choroidal apposition)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakia


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Pronounced conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, shallow anterior chamber with anterior chamber reaction and hypopyon, fibrin on the intraocular lens, aphakia, temporal clear corneal cataract incision reapproximated with nylon sutures.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with choroidal elevations involving multiple quadrants, preventing view of the posterior pole (no clear peripheral retinal view)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 59-year-old woman with sudden-onset severe left eye pain and vision loss 5 days after combined cataract/LASIK surgery. Exam: left eye hand-motion vision, conjunctival hyperemia, corneal edema, shallow/flat anterior chamber with anterior chamber reaction and hypopyon, fibrin on IOL, aphakia noted at initial photo, no fundus view. B-scan ultrasonography demonstrates appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhages with moderately dense membranous vitreous opacities. Diagnosis: appositional suprachoroidal hemorrhage of the left eye (kissing choroidals) likely postoperative in setting of recent intraocular surgery. Management: emergent vitreous tap with intravitreal vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone for presumed acute postoperative endophthalmitis; topical hourly fortified antibiotics, topical steroid and cycloplegic. Serial echography for liquefaction; conservative medical management with IOP and pain control while awaiting liquefaction for possible delayed surgical drainage; surgical drainage/pars plana vitrectomy considered if uncontrolled IOP, persistent severe pain, persistent kissing choroidals, macular/retinal involvement, or retinal detachment. Prognosis guarded; factors for poor outcome include retinal apposition >14 days, >2 quadrants involvement, poor presenting acuity, afferent pupillary defect, or concurrent retinal detachment.
Primary Diagnosis: Appositional (“kissing”) suprachoroidal hemorrhage
Secondary Diagnoses: Suprachoroidal hemorrhage; Appositional ("kissing") suprachoroidal hemorrhage; Aphakia; Postoperative endophthalmitis due to methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus epidermidis
Differential Diagnosis: Rhegmatogenous retinal detachment; Exudative (serous) retinal detachment; Serous choroidal effusion
Severity: Severe
Laterality: left


Plan
All Medications: vancomycin (intravitreal; topical fortified), ceftazidime (intravitreal; topical fortified), dexamethasone (intravitreal), prednisolone (topical), cyclopentolate (topical), povidone-iodine, cefuroxime (intracameral), systemic steroids (unproven benefit), balanced salt solution, air, intraocular gas tamponade (gas), perfluorocarbon liquid
All Procedures: combined cataract and LASIK procedure; vitreous tap; intravitreal injections of vancomycin, ceftazidime, and dexamethasone; hourly topical fortified vancomycin and ceftazidime; topical prednisolone; topical cyclopentolate; laser capsulotomy
Follow-Up Instructions: Monitor clinically with serial examinations and serial echography to assess liquefaction and extent of hemorrhage; manage ocular hypertension and pain medically as needed; proceed to surgical drainage if indicated (uncontrolled IOP, severe pain, kissing choroidals with retinal apposition, macular involvement/iris touch, associated retinal detachment) typically after 7–14 days when clot liquefies; provide rehabilitation and monitor for risk factors for poor outcome (retinal detachment, >2 quadrants hemorrhage, wound vitreous incarceration, afferent pupillary defect, poor initial VA, prolonged retinal apposition).


CASE 54 — Ruptured Globe
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Decreased vision in the left eye after being struck in the eye with a metal hammer.
DURATION: 25 years
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "It felt like a piece of dust went onto the eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old man working aboard a docked marine biology research ship was repairing a metal banister when he struck the metal rail with a metal hammer and felt a minor pain in his left eye. Over the next 2 hours he noticed a subtle but definite decrease in vision. On presentation his visual acuity was 20/30; slit-lamp exam showed a deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare, a defect in the temporal iris ~1 mm from the limbus, a slitlike temporal corneal defect not leaking on Seidel, and a sectoral white opacity in the peripheral lens. Posterior exam revealed a small intraocular foreign body embedded in the retinal midperiphery with a collar of retinal edema but no hemorrhage.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man working aboard a docked marine biology research ship was repairing a metal banister when, while striking the metal rail with a metal hammer, he experienced a minor pain in his left eye. Over the ensuing 2 hours he noticed a subtle but definite decrease in vision. On presentation his visual acuity was 20/30. Slit‑lamp exam showed a deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare. There was a defect in the temporal iris approximately 1 mm from the limbus with a corresponding slitlike corneal defect that was not leaking on Seidel testing. Posterior to the iris there was a sectoral white opacity in the peripheral lens. Posterior pole exam disclosed a small intraocular foreign body embedded in the midperipheral retina with a collar of retinal edema surrounding it but no hemorrhage.
Symptom Onset: 2 hours after the injury
Symptom Duration: 2 hours
Progression: Resolved/stable
Characteristics: Ruptured globe involving cornea, iris, lens, and posterior segment with intraocular metallic (magnetic) foreign body causing retinal impact and localized retinal edema; self-sealing slitlike corneal entry ~1 mm from limbus; minimal anterior inflammation; posteriorly embedded IOFB in midperipheral retina with collar of edema; no APD; initial VA 20/30.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Striking metal on metal without protective eyewear
Associated Symptoms: pain; decreased vision
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none — patient regained and maintained 20/20 vision in the affected eye, allowing normal daily activities.
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: Young male population, metal-on-metal work without eye protection (e.g., hammering metal), prior ocular surgery (previous incision), rural/farm settings (increased endophthalmitis risk), lack of protective eyewear


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N8
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (measured at 20 feet)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry (Goldmann)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Extensive subconjunctival hemorrhage and chemosis temporally and superiorly
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Slitlike temporal peripheral corneal defect (entry wound) approximately 1 mm from limbus, self-sealing (no Seidel leak)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Defect in the temporal iris approximately 1 mm from the limbus
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (wide)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Traumatic cataract with sectoral white peripheral lens opacity; lensectomy performed (aphakia post-op with IOL implantation)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect (APD)
Extraocular Movements: Full without restriction, intact, and symmetric.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: deep anterior chamber with minimal cell and flare; defect in the temporal iris ~1 mm from the limbus; slitlike temporal peripheral corneal defect not leaking on Seidel; sectoral white opacity in the peripheral lens posterior to the iris
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Small intraocular foreign body embedded in midperipheral retina temporally with a collar of retinal edema surrounding impact site; no hemorrhage; retina attached postoperatively.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ruptured globe, left eye (OS) with intraocular foreign body penetrating cornea, iris, lens and embedded in midperipheral retina; small temporal corneal/limbal entry with corresponding iris defect and sectoral peripheral lens opacity; anterior chamber deep with minimal inflammation; no afferent pupillary defect; vitreous hemorrhage minimal; retina attached after surgical intervention. Status post primary repair with vitrectomy, lensectomy, intraocular lens implantation, magnetic extraction of metallic IOFB, laser photocoagulation around retinal defect and placement of a low encircling scleral buckle. Postoperative course favorable with retina attached and visual acuity restored to 20/20, stable over 25 years.
Primary Diagnosis: Ruptured globe
Differential Diagnosis: Intraocular foreign body; Ruptured globe (full‑thickness corneoscleral laceration); Occult globe rupture with scleral dehiscence (including previous cataract wound dehiscence)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: - Endophthalmitis - Retinal detachment - Vitreous hemorrhage - Traumatic optic neuropathy (affecting afferent pupillary defect) - Sympathetic ophthalmia - Persistent vitreous traction/fibrous proliferation leading to proliferative vitreoretinopathy - Irregular corneal astigmatism/aphakia requiring rehabilitation - Need for enucleation (in severe cases)


Plan
All Medications: parenteral broad-spectrum antibiotics; tetanus toxoid vaccine; intravenous antibiotics (continued ~36 hours)
All Procedures: vitrectomy; lensectomy; intraocular lens (IOL) implantation; magnetic extraction of intraocular foreign body (IOFB); low encircling scleral buckle placement; laser photocoagulation (endolaser) around retinal defect; anterior chamber entry and corneoscleral laceration closure (primary globe repair)
Follow-Up Instructions: As long as anatomic and visual stabilization is observed, lengthen follow-up intervals; cicatricial response completed by ~6 weeks. Consider additional surgery (epiretinal membrane removal or secondary IOL) at ~3 months if needed. Recommend shatter‑proof safety glasses; coordinate vocational rehabilitation as indicated.
Patient Education: Wear shatter‑proof safety glasses to protect the other eye; keep a protective eye shield over the injured eye until cleared; report fever, increasing pain, worsening vision, redness, or discharge immediately (signs of infection/endophthalmitis); ensure tetanus immunization is up to date; expect close short‑term follow‑up and possible further surgeries (vitrectomy, lensectomy, IOL later) and discuss vocational rehabilitation if vision is permanently reduced.
Work Restrictions: Avoidance of activities risking ocular trauma; wear shatter‑proof safety glasses; restrict duties involving metal‑on‑metal work (e.g., hammering) until fully healed and cleared.
Emergency Instructions: Place a protective eye shield over the eye, avoid pressure on the globe, administer broad‑spectrum parenteral antibiotics, check tetanus status and give booster if >5 years, and arrange urgent ophthalmic surgical evaluation for globe repair.


CASE 55 — Intraocular Foreign Body
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Poor vision after a fall (noticed upon waking the morning after the fall)
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: An 80‑year‑old nursing‑home woman fell onto a piece of furniture the prior day; periocular ecchymoses suggested direct ocular trauma. She had no immediate pain or noticed visual loss but awoke the next morning with poor vision and sought care. She had prior cataract surgery (extracapsular nuclear expression) 3 years earlier and preinjury vision 20/30 limited by atrophic macular degeneration. On presentation vision was hand motions, IOP 10 mm Hg; cornea relatively clear, formed AC, conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly, scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with posterior chamber IOL in subconjunctival space, dense vitreous hemorrhage limiting posterior view, pupil not peaked, no vitreous anterior to iris.
History Narrative: An 80-year-old woman presented the morning after falling at her nursing home. Although the exact history was unclear, periocular ecchymoses suggested direct ocular trauma on a piece of furniture. Initially, the patient had not noticed pain or visual loss, but upon waking the next morning she realized poor vision and sought consultation. Cataract surgery with a nuclear expression extracapsular technique had been performed 3 years previously. Before the injury, the vision had been documented as 20/30, limited by some atrophic macular degeneration changes. Upon presentation, visual acuity was hand motions and the intraocular pressure was 10 mm Hg. The cornea appeared relatively clear with a formed anterior chamber. However, there was conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly. While raising the upper lid, there was no conjunctival laceration, but a scleral dehiscence was evident, apparently at the previous cataract wound; upon examination of the superotemporal quadrant, the posterior chamber implant was seen in the subconjunctival space. The view posteriorly was limited by a dense vitreous hemorrhage. The pupil was not peaked, and there was no vitreous anterior to the iris.
Symptom Onset: Morning after fall (symptoms noted upon waking the next morning)
Symptom Duration: 1 day
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Retained magnetic metallic intraocular foreign body, posterior-segment (vitreous) with scleral wound/dehiscence at prior cataract incision; associated vitreous hemorrhage, focal subretinal hemorrhage, no retinal detachment, aphakia after IOL removal.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Fall (traumatic globe injury with prior cataract wound dehiscence)
Associated Symptoms: poor vision, periocular ecchymoses, conjunctival chemosis, dense vitreous hemorrhage, subretinal hemorrhage, hand motions visual acuity (severe vision loss)
Prior Treatments: Cataract surgery with a nuclear expression extracapsular technique 3 years prior
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — persistent visual impairment (20/100 OS, aphakic) limiting activities like reading and driving, but able to perform some tasks with correction.
Risk Factors: Industrial trauma (metal-on-metal impact, workplace without protective eyewear), rural/farm injuries, failure to wear eye protection, penetrating ocular trauma (previous surgical wound dehiscence), elderly falls


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: Hand motions
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen, best corrected — hand motions (HM)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 10 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Periocular ecchymoses temporally and superiorly; no conjunctival laceration; scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound; upper lid lift revealed subconjunctival IOL implant.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly; scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound (superotemporal)
External Exam – Orbit: Periocular ecchymoses temporally and superiorly with conjunctival chemosis; scleral dehiscence at prior cataract wound with subconjunctival intraocular lens visible.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: relatively clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: formed anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Iris: scleral dehiscence at previous cataract wound with posterior chamber IOL displaced into subconjunctival space; pupil not peaked; no vitreous anterior to iris
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow/partially obscured by vitreous traction with no angle recession noted
Anterior Segment – Lens: Aphakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: not peaked
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The cornea appeared relatively clear with a formed anterior chamber; conjunctival chemosis temporally and superiorly; no conjunctival laceration; scleral dehiscence at the previous cataract wound with the posterior chamber implant seen in the subconjunctival space; pupil not peaked; no vitreous anterior to the iris.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normal appearing
Fundus Exam – Macula: atrophic macular degeneration changes
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Dense vitreous hemorrhage with a focal subretinal hemorrhage in the superotemporal midperiphery; no retinal detachment; minimal epiretinal membrane temporally at three months.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body OS with globe dehiscence at prior cataract wound, dense vitreous hemorrhage, subretinal hemorrhage superotemporally, no retinal detachment, aphakia post-IOL removal; urgent vitrectomy, IOFB removal, encircling scleral buckle placed; postop attached retina with minimal epiretinal membrane and BCVA 20/100 aphakic.
Primary Diagnosis: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Intraocular foreign body (metallic/siderotic IOFB); Retained nonmetallic IOFB (glass/plastic); Globe rupture with intraocular hemorrhage without retained IOFB; Traumatic endophthalmitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: OS
Complications: Retained magnetic intraocular foreign body; vitreous hemorrhage; scleral wound dehiscence with extruded IOL; subretinal hemorrhage; epiretinal membrane; risk of endophthalmitis; potential retinal detachment; aphakia


Plan
All Medications: prophylactic antibiotics (systemic); intravitreal antibiotics (when indicated); systemic antibiotics (IV) postoperatively; topical corticosteroids; topical antibiotics; cycloplegic agents
All Procedures: vitrectomy; placement of an encircling scleral band (scleral buckling); removal of intraocular lens (IOL explantation); lensectomy (as part of vitrectomy)
Follow-Up Instructions: Monitor postoperatively with IV antibiotics and topical corticosteroids, antibiotics, and/or cycloplegics as indicated; discontinue systemic antibiotics if no endophthalmitis within 24–48 hours and clinical progress is good. Observe at ~1–2 week intervals during the first month, then less frequently; monitor for recurrent retinal detachment and visually significant epiretinal membrane. Consider secondary IOL implantation or aphakic contact lens if patient remains aphakic.
Patient Education: Avoid MRI until foreign body is confirmed nonmetallic. Wear protective eyewear in high‑risk settings. Seek immediate care for eye trauma; early removal of metallic intraocular foreign bodies reduces infection and toxicity risk. Expect possible surgery (vitrectomy, lens removal, scleral buckle), antibiotics (often IV and/or intravitreal), and follow‑up visits to monitor for infection, retinal detachment, or epiretinal membrane; vision may remain reduced and secondary lens surgery may be needed.


CASE 56 — Optic Neuritis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: headaches and anisocoria
DURATION: several years (worse over the last few months)
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurry"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old student nurse with several years of daily diffuse headaches that have worsened over the past few months; headaches not associated with nausea or vomiting. She reports chronic “blurry” vision, worse with headaches. No diplopia or other eye problems. Evaluated by neurologist who noted anisocoria and referred for ophthalmology. Taking medications only for headaches. On exam visual acuity 20/20 OU; pupils 8 mm OD nonreactive to light or near, 4 mm OS briskly reactive; no RAPD; normal motility, no ptosis; slit-lamp shows smooth round pupils without segmental contraction; fundus normal without disc swelling.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old student nurse with a several-year history of daily, diffuse headaches that had worsened over the past few months, referred for anisocoria noted by a neurologist. She reported chronically “blurry” vision, worse with headaches, denied diplopia or other eye problems, and was taking medications only for her headaches. Examination elsewhere had shown visual acuity 20/20 OU, normal color vision and full visual fields, pupils 8 mm OD and 4 mm OS with the right pupil nonreactive to light or near and the left pupil reacting briskly; no relative afferent pupillary defect, normal motility without ptosis, normal facial sensation and strength, and slit-lamp and fundus exams without iris irregularity or optic disc swelling. Clinical impression was most consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis rather than third nerve palsy or tonic pupil.
Symptom Onset: Several years ago, worsening over the last few months
Symptom Duration: several years
Progression: Acute, usually unilateral loss of visual acuity and/or visual field with eventual visual improvement (most improve ≥1 line by day 15 and ≥1 line by day 30; recovery often nearly complete by 30 days, may continue for months)
Characteristics: Acute unilateral vision loss with periocular pain (worse on eye movement), decreased color vision, relative afferent pupillary defect, young adult (<40), normal fundus (retrobulbar optic neuritis), typically MRI-positive for demyelinating lesions, improved with IV steroids per ONTT.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Head movement/eye movement (periocular pain worse with eye movement)
Associated Symptoms: Headaches, blurry vision (worse with headaches), anisocoria, eye pain on movement not reported in this case but general — however specific to this patient: headaches and blurry vision.
Systemic Symptoms: Headache (chronic, worse over months), no nausea or vomiting, no diplopia, otherwise denied systemic symptoms.
Risk Factors: - Young adult age (<40 years) - History of demyelinating lesions on MRI (white matter lesions) - Three or more demyelinating lesions on MRI - History of nonspecific neurologic symptoms - History of previous optic neuritis - Increased cerebrospinal fluid IgG - Monocular involvement or occupational need to recover vision rapidly (consideration for treatment)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Both pupils smoothly round without irregularities; right pupil dilated (pharmacologic mydriasis)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right 8 mm, left 4 mm; both smoothly round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil did not react to light; left pupil reacted briskly to light.
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Right pupil did not react to near; left pupil reacted briskly to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect documented
Pupils – Other: 8mm on the right; 4mm on the left; right pupil nonreactive to light or near; left pupil briskly reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: both pupils to be smoothly round without irregularities, and there was no segmental contraction of the right pupil to light
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal with no disc swelling
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right pharmacologic mydriasis causing anisocoria; no evidence of third nerve palsy or tonic pupil. Vision 20/20 OU, color vision and fields full, normal motility, no ptosis, and normal fundus without disc edema. Headaches chronic; likely accidental exposure to topical/mydriatic agent given nursing occupation. Observation recommended with dilute pilocarpine testing to confirm pharmacologic blockade; no surgical intervention. Monitor for resolution; if persistent or other neurologic signs develop, pursue further neuro-ophthalmic workup.
Primary Diagnosis: Optic neuritis
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisocoria; Pharmacologic mydriasis; Headaches (chronic/diffuse)
Differential Diagnosis: Pharmacologic mydriasis; Tonic (Adie) pupil; Third cranial nerve palsy; Iris sphincter damage
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Unilateral
Complications: - Increased risk of developing multiple sclerosis (MS) - Potential progression to neuromyelitis optica (NMO) with worse visual prognosis - Persistent visual impairment (incomplete recovery) - Recurrent optic neuritis attacks - Steroid-related complications from treatment (e.g., systemic corticosteroid side effects)


Plan
All Medications: headache medications (unspecified) methylprednisolone (IV high-dose per ONTT regimen) prednisone (oral)
All Procedures: MRI of the brain and orbits with contrast; slit-lamp examination; pupillary testing including pilocarpine testing; visual acuity and visual field testing; color vision testing (HRR plates)
