Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up with neurology and ophthalmology; obtain brain and orbital MRI to evaluate for demyelinating lesions; monitor visual acuity and visual fields with repeat eye exams (within weeks) and consider IV methylprednisolone if indicated (abnormal MRI, severe vision loss, or need for rapid recovery); test for NMO and MOG antibodies if atypical features or poor recovery.
Patient Education: - Optic neuritis is inflammation of the optic nerve that commonly causes sudden, usually one‑eye, vision loss, pain with eye movement, and decreased color vision. - Most typical cases occur in young adults and improve gradually, often beginning within 2 weeks and continuing over months; most patients regain substantial vision by 1 month. - MRI of the brain and orbits is recommended to look for demyelinating lesions (risk factor for multiple sclerosis) and to help guide treatment. - High‑dose IV corticosteroids (e.g., IV methylprednisolone followed by an oral steroid taper) speed recovery and may reduce short‑term MS risk; standard‑dose oral prednisone alone is not recommended. - Atypical features (both eyes affected at once, lack of pain, severe disc swelling, worsening or no improvement by 3–4 weeks, or other systemic signs) require further testing for infectious or inflammatory causes such as neuromyelitis optica (NMO) or MOG antibody disease. - If NMO or MOG is suspected, additional blood tests and longer or different immunosuppressive treatment may be needed. - Report any new neurologic symptoms (weakness, numbness, balance problems), worsening vision, or lack of improvement; follow neuro‑ophthalmology and neurology as advised. - Use supportive measures: avoid activities that markedly increase body temperature if vision is worse with heat, and arrange for visual aids and safety measures while vision is impaired.


CASE 57 — Optic Disc Edema with Macular Star (Neuroretinitis)
=============================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Blurred vision in the right eye for 12 days (acute onset)
DURATION: 12 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I noticed sudden blurred vision in my right eye about 12 days ago, with moderate retro-orbital pain that worsens when I move my eye. My vision got worse over the first few days then stabilized. When I try to do aerobic exercise my vision becomes worse. I don’t have any other neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches for many years, and I’m otherwise healthy with no family history of visual problems."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old woman with 12 days of acute onset blurred vision in the right eye, associated with moderately severe retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements. Vision deteriorated over first 3–4 days then stabilized; exercise worsened vision. No precipitating factors, no neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches for years. Otherwise healthy; denies family history of visual impairment.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with acute onset 12 days earlier of blurred vision in the right eye associated with moderately severe retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements. Vision deteriorated over the first 3–4 days then stabilized; exercise (aerobic activity) worsened vision. She denied precipitating factors, neurologic symptoms except rare diffuse headaches for many years, was otherwise healthy, and had no family history of visual impairment. She owned three cats.
Symptom Onset: 12 days ago
Symptom Duration: 12 days
Progression: Acute (vision deteriorated over first 3–4 days, then stabilized)
Characteristics: Unilateral optic disc edema with macular star (neuroretinitis), subacute painful vision loss, retro‑orbital pain worsened by eye movement, relative afferent pupillary defect, macular exudates forming a star, hyperemic swollen optic disc, typically associated with infectious etiology (Bartonella/cat‑scratch)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: aerobic exercise and heat exposure (e.g., hot shower, sauna)
Associated Symptoms: Blurred vision in right eye; moderately severe retro-orbital pain worsened by eye movements; vision worsened with exercise/heat (Uhthoff’s symptom); rare diffuse headaches.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulty with exercise (vision worsens during aerobic activity), impaired reading/near tasks in affected eye (20/80), some color vision loss and visual field defect, but overall able to perform many daily activities with one good eye.
Systemic Symptoms: Flulike illness (preceding), rare diffuse headaches; otherwise otherwise healthy, no other systemic symptoms.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Three cats at home
Risk Factors: Exposure to cats (three cats at home); recent flu-like illness (preceding systemic infection)

Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm

Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/80

Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20

Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep and formed
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal

Pupils – Reaction to Light: react to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near

Pupils – Other: Significant relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) on the right
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions without restriction or pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The slit-lamp examination was normal.

Clinical picture and positive Bartonella serology support neuroretinitis; alternate causes (other infections, inflammatory, demyelinating, compressive, ischemic) considered less likely. Management: typically benign and may resolve over months; treat identified infectious cause with appropriate antibiotics; no surgical intervention indicated. Follow-up for visual recovery and possible late macular star formation; ODEMS not predictive of MS.
Primary Diagnosis: ODEMS (neuroretinitis secondary to cat-scratch disease)
Differential Diagnosis: Demyelinating optic neuritis (typical ON); Neuroretinitis due to Bartonella henselae (cat-scratch disease); Other infectious causes (syphilis, Lyme, toxoplasmosis, tuberculosis); Inflammatory/NMO spectrum disorder (NMOSD)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Optic atrophy; macular retinal pigment epithelial changes; possible residual visual disability; recurrence in same or fellow eye


Plan
All Medications: antibiotics (unspecified)
Follow-Up Instructions: Reevaluate within 2 weeks for development of a macular star; routine ophthalmic follow-up to monitor visual recovery and for signs of optic atrophy or recurrence.
Patient Education: ODEMS (neuroretinitis) from cat-scratch disease often improves over weeks to months and usually has a good visual prognosis; avoid strenuous exercise or activities that worsen vision until improved; return for follow-up in 1–2 weeks to recheck the macula (a macular star can appear later) and sooner for any worsening; if Bartonella infection confirmed, take prescribed antibiotics as directed and finish the course; report new symptoms such as increasing vision loss, severe pain, fever, or neurologic changes immediately.


CASE 58 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Nonarteritic
=========================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Sudden painless vision loss upon awakening
DURATION: 3 weeks
LATERALITY: OS
PATIENT QUOTES: "blurred vision in her right eye"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 22-year-old woman with 3 weeks of blurred vision in the right eye, onset about one week after a nonspecific “flulike” illness, associated with minimal right periorbital pain, occasional mild diffuse headaches, and 1+ vitreous cells; no prior medical, ophthalmologic, or neurologic illnesses; three cats at home.
History Narrative: A 22-year-old woman noted the onset of blurred vision in her right eye 3 weeks ago. She noted minimal right periorbital pain. She denied any history of previous medical, ophthalmologic, or neurologic illnesses, but she did complain of recent occasional headaches of mild and diffuse nature and noted that the visual blurring seemed to have started a week or so after a nonspecific “flulike” illness. She has three cats at home. Examination revealed visual acuity to be 20/60 on the right and 20/15 on the left. She identified 4 of 10 pseudoisochromatic plates on the right and 10 of 10 on the left. Visual field examination revealed a cecocentral scotoma on the right and was normal on the left. Pupils were 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near, but there was a right relative afferent pupillary defect. Motility examination was normal. The right fundus exam revealed significant optic disc edema with peripapillary and macular exudates, the latter in a star configuration. There were 1+ vitreous cells on the right. The left fundus examination was normal.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks ago
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Progression of visual loss beyond 2 to 4 weeks.
Characteristics: Unilateral optic disc edema with macular star, vitreous cells (1+), cecocentral scotoma, afferent pupillary defect (right RAPD), dyschromatopsia, recent flu‑like prodrome, young age (22), minimal periorbital pain, visual acuity 20/60 OD and 20/15 OS, normal fellow fundus, positive Bartonella henselae serology (high IgM/IgG)


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Sudden painless vision loss upon awakening; minimal right periorbital pain; recent mild diffuse headaches; recent nonspecific “flu‑like” illness; occasional headaches; right eye dyschromatopsia.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — some difficulty with reading and color tasks (20/60 OD, cecocentral scotoma, color dyschromatopsia) but able to perform many activities with the left eye (20/15 OS).
Systemic Symptoms: flulike illness and occasional mild diffuse headaches
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: 3 cats at home
Risk Factors: Hypertension; diabetes mellitus; hyperlipidemia; small cup-to-disc ratio (contralateral crowded disc); age (elderly)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 138/86 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/15
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/15
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, no cataract mentioned


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5mm bilaterally, equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Equally reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 1 + vitreous cells on the right
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Significant optic disc edema with peripapillary exudates and macular star (right eye); left fundus normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Macular star (peripapillary and macular exudates in a star configuration)
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal abnormalities detected)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION) of the right eye. Sudden, painless vision loss with sectoral disc edema, macular exudates, right relative afferent pupillary defect, color dyschromatopsia, and cecocentral scotoma consistent with NAION. Fellow eye shows small cup-to-disc ratio, increasing risk for involvement. Vascular risk–factor control recommended; no proven acute therapy—consider aspirin and discuss corticosteroids risks/benefits. Monitor fellow eye and visual function; counsel regarding ~12% lifetime risk to fellow eye and possible spontaneous improvement by 6 months.
Primary Diagnosis: Nonarteritic AION, OS
Differential Diagnosis: Neuroretinitis (infectious or idiopathic); Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION); Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (giant cell arteritis); Central or branch retinal vein occlusion
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: OS
Complications: Risk of AION in the fellow eye (≈12% lifetime); potential permanent visual loss in affected eye; increased risk of stroke and myocardial infarction (associated vascular risk factors)


Plan
All Medications: phenytoin; erythropoietin; intravitreal anti-vascular endothelial growth factor therapies; topical brimonidine; levodopa; aspirin; corticosteroids
Follow-Up Instructions: The risk of AION in the fellow eye is approximately 12% in the patient’s lifetime. Control vascular/stroke risk factors. Consider aspirin to possibly decrease fellow‑eye AION and cardiovascular risk (unproven). Arrange ophthalmic follow‑up to monitor vision and disc status (eg, repeat exam and visual fields); provide vision rehabilitation as needed.
Patient Education: Nonarteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (NAION) is sudden, usually painless vision loss due to decreased blood flow to the optic nerve. Key points: - Cause and risk: It is often associated with vascular risk factors (hypertension, diabetes, high cholesterol) and a small optic nerve “cup”; it is not caused by infection in typical NAION. - Prognosis: Some spontaneous visual improvement can occur (≈40% improve by 6 months), but many patients have permanent visual field loss. There is a roughly 10–15% lifetime risk of involvement of the fellow eye. - What to watch for: Report any new or worsening vision loss, new headaches, jaw claudication, scalp tenderness, or systemic symptoms immediately (these could suggest giant cell arteritis and require urgent evaluation). - Risk reduction: Optimize control of vascular risk factors—blood pressure, blood sugar, lipids—and discuss aspirin with your clinician (may lower stroke/heart attack risk and possibly fellow-eye risk, though benefit for NAION is unproven). - No proven treatment: There is no consistently effective therapy for acute NAION; some clinicians consider corticosteroids in select cases but this is controversial. Surgical optic nerve sheath fenestration is not helpful and may be harmful. - Follow-up and rehabilitation: Regular ophthalmology follow-up for monitoring, visual field testing, and low-vision rehabilitation as needed; consider referral for occupational or mobility training if vision impairment affects daily activities. - When to seek urgent care: sudden new vision loss in either eye, or systemic symptoms suggestive of giant cell arteritis.


CASE 59 — Anterior Ischemic Optic Neuropathy—Arteritic
======================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe visual loss in the left eye upon awakening 3 days ago
DURATION: 3 days
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "on awakening from sleep 3 days ago she noted severe visual loss in the left eye." "She denied any headache, eye pain, jaw claudications, episodes of transient visual loss, or any other neurologic or ophthalmologic complaints." "She related a past history of hypertension, increased cholesterol, and diabetes mellitus."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 65-year-old woman with sudden severe visual loss in the left eye noted on awakening 3 days ago; denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic complaints; past history of hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and diabetes mellitus.
History Narrative: A 65-year-old woman reports sudden severe vision loss in the left eye noted on awakening 3 days ago. She denies headache, eye pain, jaw claudication, transient visual loss, or other neurologic/ophthalmologic complaints. Past medical history notable for hypertension, hypercholesterolemia, and diabetes mellitus.
Symptom Onset: 3 days ago
Symptom Duration: 3 days
Progression: Sudden onset with acute severe visual loss noted on awakening 3 days prior, with possible slight worsening over 2–4 weeks described as typical but no further progression reported.
Characteristics: Elderly patient with sudden, painless monocular vision loss on awakening; dense inferior altitudinal visual field defect; ipsilateral RAPD; diffusely swollen, pallid (chalk‑white) optic disc with peripapillary hemorrhages; elevated ESR and CRP; temporal artery biopsy positive for giant cell arteritis (transmural lymphocytic infiltration with multinucleated giant cells and internal elastic lamina disruption).


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nocturnal hypotension
Associated Symptoms: sudden severe visual loss in the left eye on awakening, headache (denied), jaw claudication (denied), scalp tenderness (noted as possible in discussion but denied by patient), constitutional symptoms—history of polymyalgia rheumatica mentioned in discussion; pallid (chalk-white) disc edema, peripapillary hemorrhages, elevated ESR and CRP, transient visual loss (amaurosis fugax) possible prodrome (denied)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — marked left-eye vision loss (20/200) with dense inferior visual field defect causing difficulty with activities requiring depth perception, reading, driving, and monocular visual tasks; requires monitoring and possible assistance.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported (denies headache, jaw claudication, other constitutional symptoms)
Risk Factors: Hypertension, increased cholesterol (hyperlipidemia), diabetes mellitus, age (elderly)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N24/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear and quiet bilaterally
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear and deep, no cells or flare noted
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 3mm bilaterally, reacted well to light on the right and poorly to light on the left
Pupils – RAPD: Left relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: left relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Left optic disc diffusely swollen, pale (pallid) with peripapillary hemorrhages; right optic disc normal (cup-to-disc ratio ~0.1).
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions noted / peripheral retina within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 65-year-old woman with acute, painless severe vision loss of the left eye on awakening 3 days ago. Exam: VA 20/20 OD, 20/200 OS; color vision 10/10 OD, 2/10 OS; dense inferior altitudinal field defect OS; left RAPD; left optic disc diffusely swollen, pallid with peripapillary hemorrhages; fellow optic nerve cup-to-disc 0.1. Elevated ESR 70 mm/h and CRP 20 mg/dL. Temporal artery biopsy positive for transmural lymphocytic infiltration with multinucleated giant cells and disruption of the internal elastic lamina. Clinical picture and laboratory/histopathology consistent with arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy due to giant cell arteritis. Immediate high-dose corticosteroid therapy indicated to prevent further visual loss.
Primary Diagnosis: Arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (giant cell arteritis)
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Hypercholesterolemia; Diabetes mellitus; Polymyalgia rheumatica (history suggestive)
Differential Diagnosis: Ischemic optic neuropathy (arteritic AION); Nonarteritic AION; Optic neuritis; Compressive optic neuropathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: Further visual loss in the involved or fellow eye (including bilateral blindness); systemic complications of giant cell arteritis such as stroke, large-vessel ischemia; steroid-related adverse effects from prolonged high‑dose therapy.


Plan
All Medications: prednisone (oral) 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day; methylprednisolone (IV) 1000 mg/day; methotrexate (steroid‑sparing agent)
All Procedures: Sedimentation rate (ESR) and C-reactive protein (CRP) testing; temporal artery biopsy; initiation of high‑dose corticosteroid therapy (oral prednisone 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day; consideration of IV methylprednisolone 1,000 mg/day in severe cases).
Follow-Up Instructions: Careful monitoring for visual impairment and constitutional symptoms; serial ESR/CRP to guide therapy; regular assessment for steroid adverse effects; coordination with internist/rheumatologist for systemic management and consideration of steroid‑sparing agents if needed; arrange temporal artery biopsy (if not yet done) and close ophthalmic follow‑up.
Patient Education: You have an inflammation of medium–large arteries called giant cell arteritis (GCA) that caused sudden vision loss in one eye due to arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy. Immediate treatment with high‑dose corticosteroids is necessary to reduce the risk of further vision loss in the affected or the other eye. Steroids should be started right away and will often be continued for many months with a gradual taper; some people need longer-term therapy or steroid‑sparing medicines. A temporal artery biopsy is recommended to confirm the diagnosis but should not delay starting treatment. You will need close follow‑up with your eye doctor and a rheumatologist/internist for monitoring of vision, symptoms, and steroid side effects, and periodic ESR/CRP blood tests to help gauge inflammation. Report new symptoms immediately (worsening vision, vision loss in the other eye, new severe headache, jaw pain with chewing, scalp tenderness, or systemic symptoms).
Referrals: Internist and/or rheumatologist
Emergency Instructions: Start high‑dose corticosteroids immediately (oral prednisone 1.0–1.5 mg/kg/day; consider IV methylprednisolone 1 g/day if severe vision loss <48 hours, bilateral involvement, or monocular patient). Do not delay treatment for temporal artery biopsy. Monitor vision closely and arrange urgent temporal artery biopsy and rheumatology/internist consultation.


CASE 60 — Progressive Optic Neuropathy—Tumor
============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe visual loss in the left eye (progressive over years / markedly blurry over hours)
DURATION: years
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "Over a period of hours, the vision became markedly blurry." "Over the last 2 weeks, she had noted two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye." "She denied any periocular or orbital pain, but noted that over the last several months she had diffuse, dull headaches with occasional superimposed “ice pick–like” pains affecting her left or right temples." "She had a past history of hypertension." "She reported jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica (PMR)-like symptoms."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 68-year-old woman with several months of diffuse dull headaches with occasional sharp “ice pick–like” temple pains, and over the last 2 weeks two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss in the left eye lasting minutes, who over a period of hours developed severe, markedly blurry visual loss in the left eye; she denied periocular/orbital pain. She has a history of hypertension and reported jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms.
History Narrative: A 68-year-old woman with a history of hypertension presented with acute severe visual loss in the left eye. Over a period of hours her vision became markedly blurry; in the prior two weeks she had two or three painless, transient episodes of visual loss lasting minutes in the left eye. She denied periocular or orbital pain but reported several months of diffuse dull headaches with occasional sharp “ice pick–like” pains in the temples. She also reported jaw claudication and polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms.
Symptom Onset: Over a period of hours (acute onset), with progressive visual loss over years noted in title — acute severe left visual loss over hours, with transient episodes over last 2 weeks and months of headaches.
Symptom Duration: Several months (with progressive visual loss over years noted in abstract)
Progression: Progressive over years
Characteristics: Progressive painless visual loss with afferent pupillary defect, decreased color vision, optic atrophy/pale swollen disc with peripapillary hemorrhages, compressive optic neuropathy due to optic nerve sheath meningioma on MRI


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Progressive visual loss over years; episodes of transient visual loss; headaches (temporal "ice pick–like" pains); jaw claudication; polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive visual loss, transient visual loss (amaurosis fugax) in left eye, diffuse dull headaches with intermittent "ice pick–like" temple pains, jaw claudication, systemic polymyalgia rheumatica–like symptoms, hypertension
Previous Episodes: two or three episodes of painless, transient visual loss in the left eye lasting minutes over the last 2 weeks
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — marked impairment of left-eye vision (20/400, severe color loss, visual field defect) likely interferes with depth perception, reading, driving, and daily tasks, requiring adaptation/monitoring.
Systemic Symptoms: headache, episodic “ice pick–like” temple pains, jaw claudication, weight loss/anorexia, malaise consistent with polymyalgia rheumatica-like symptoms
Risk Factors: Age >50 years; female sex; presence of headache; previous/present systemic hypertension; history suggestive of polymyalgia rheumatica/giant cell arteritis (jaw claudication, constitutional symptoms)

Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min

Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400

Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Compressive optic neuropathy of the right optic nerve secondary to an optic nerve sheath meningioma evidenced by T1 postcontrast MRI showing optic nerve sheath enhancement. Recommend close observation with serial clinical exams, visual fields, and repeat MRIs (every 6 months ×2 years then yearly if stable); consider stereotactic conformal radiation therapy if progressive visual deterioration. Surgical resection generally not recommended unless exophytic tumor or progressive intracranial extension threatens fellow eye.
Primary Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy due to optic nerve sheath meningioma
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Giant cell arteritis (suspected — history of jaw claudication and PMR-like symptoms)
Differential Diagnosis: Optic nerve sheath meningioma; compressive optic neuropathy from intracranial meningioma/pituitary adenoma/craniopharyngioma; arteritic anterior ischemic optic neuropathy (giant cell arteritis)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: Progressive vision loss; optic atrophy; permanent visual impairment; possible fellow-eye involvement if intracranial extension; need for long-term monitoring and potential radiation-related complications


Plan
All Procedures: close observation; stereotactic conformal radiation therapy; surgical debulking (for exophytic lesions); gross total resection or subtotal excision (for intracranial meningiomas with optic nerve involvement); postoperative radiation therapy (considered for nonresectable or progressive/high-grade tumors); serial clinical reevaluation with visual fields and repeat MRIs (follow-up imaging every ~6 months for 2 years then yearly)
Follow-Up Instructions: Serial clinical reevaluation (e.g., every 6 months) including visual fields and repeat MRIs (e.g., every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly if no growth)
Patient Education: You have a tumor (optic nerve sheath meningioma) compressing the optic nerve, which can cause progressive, painless vision loss, decreased color vision, and an afferent pupillary defect. MRI with contrast is the key test to follow the tumor. Treatment options depend on how the tumor behaves: small, stable tumors are often observed with regular follow-up; if vision worsens or the tumor grows, stereotactic/conformal radiation is commonly recommended. Surgery is rarely used for primary optic nerve sheath meningiomas because it often risks further vision loss; it may be considered for certain growing or intracranial tumors. Follow-up typically includes eye exams with visual acuity and visual field testing and repeat MRI every ~6 months for 2 years, then yearly if stable. Report any new or worsening vision changes, new headaches, or other neurologic symptoms promptly.


CASE 61 — Papilledema–Pseudotumor Cerebri
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: painless progressive visual loss in her right eye
DURATION: 3 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "vision was no better after the surgery"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64‑year‑old woman with a multi‑year history of painless, progressive visual loss in the right eye beginning ~3 years ago with initial mild blurring (VA 20/40 OD, 20/20 OS), gradual decline to 20/60 then to 20/100 despite right cataract surgery one year after onset that did not improve vision. She denied headache or eye pain. She has transient visual obscurations, tinnitus and bilateral disc edema noted earlier with progressive unilateral optic atrophy and a right relative afferent pupillary defect. Neuroimaging showed signs of increased intracranial pressure (bilateral optic nerve sheath CSF signal, empty sella) with normal MRV; lumbar puncture revealed elevated opening pressure (350 mm H2O) with normal CSF contents, consistent with idiopathic intracranial hypertension.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman with a three-year history of painless, progressive visual loss in the right eye. Three years earlier she noted mild visual blurring in the right eye with visual acuity 20/40 OD and 20/20 OS, attributed to nuclear sclerotic cataract and observed. Over the following year vision in the right eye worsened to 20/60 leading to right cataract surgery, but vision did not improve and continued to deteriorate. At presentation she denied headache or eye pain. She reported progressive visual decline on the right over years with associated color vision loss on the right; no systemic symptoms described.
Symptom Onset: 3 years prior
Symptom Duration: 3 years
Progression: Progressive visual loss over 3 years
Characteristics: Painless progressive visual loss in one eye with headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, bilateral disc edema, obesity, elevated intracranial pressure (opening pressure 350 mm H2O), normal CSF contents, normal MRI/MRV except signs of increased ICP (optic nerve sheath CSF signal, empty sella), no secondary cause.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Obesity; pregnancy (relative concern for acetazolamide use); worsening with activities that increase intracranial pressure (implied)
Alleviating Factors: Weight reduction (including surgically induced weight loss); acetazolamide medical therapy; repeat lumbar punctures (temporary); CSF shunting (VP or lumboperitoneal) and optic nerve sheath fenestration as surgical options; symptomatic headache treatments.
Associated Symptoms: headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations
Prior Treatments: Right cataract surgery (prior)
Impact on Daily Activities: Impaired — progressive visual loss in right eye causing decreased acuity, color vision and visual field, affecting daily activities (e.g., reading/driving)
Systemic Symptoms: headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, obesity
Risk Factors: Obesity, female sex (young woman of childbearing age), recent weight gain, possibly pregnancy considerations, and use of medications that can raise intracranial pressure (e.g., tetracyclines, vitamin A derivatives)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/100
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet.
External Exam – Orbit: Mild proptosis on the right (Hertel 22 mm OD, 19 mm OS at base 95)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right; mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5mm bilaterally, poorly reactive on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 5mm bilaterally poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive on the left; poorly reactive on the right
Pupils – RAPD: Right relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally, poorly reactive to light on the right and briskly reactive on the left
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU (normal motility)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: a posterior chamber intraocular lens on the right and a mild nuclear sclerotic cataract on the left

Fundus Exam – Macula: Unable to determine
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Papilledema due to idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri) — obese woman with headaches, tinnitus, transient visual obscurations, bilateral disc edema; neuroimaging (MRI with optic nerve sheath fluid signal and empty sella) and MRV without venous occlusion; lumbar puncture opening pressure 350 mm H2O with normal CSF contents; diagnosis consistent with IIH. Plan: serial visual function monitoring; weight reduction; medical therapy (acetazolamide 1–2 g/day unless contraindicated, consider furosemide add‑on or other CAI if intolerant); avoid routine repeated LPs; consider surgical options (optic nerve sheath fenestration or CSF shunting) if medical therapy fails or vision worsens; close follow‑up monthly for 6–12 months with visual fields, optic nerve imaging, acuity, color vision, and RAPD assessment.
Primary Diagnosis: Papilledema (Idiopathic Intracranial Hypertension / Pseudotumor cerebri)
Secondary Diagnoses: Obesity; Headaches; Tinnitus; Transient visual obscurations; Papilledema (idiopathic intracranial hypertension)
Differential Diagnosis: Compressive optic neuropathy (orbital or intracanalicular mass, e.g., meningioma); Idiopathic intracranial hypertension (pseudotumor cerebri); Optic atrophy secondary to chronic glaucoma; Inflammatory/infectious optic neuropathy (e.g., sarcoidosis, syphilis)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: acetazolamide; methazolamide (Neptazane); furosemide (Lasix); corticosteroids
All Procedures: magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) with and without gadolinium contrast; magnetic resonance venography (MRV); computed tomography (CT) of brain and orbit with and without contrast; lumbar puncture (spinal tap) with opening pressure measurement; serial visual field testing (perimetry); color vision testing; optic nerve sheath fenestration (ONSF); ventriculoperitoneal (VP) shunt; lumboperitoneal shunt; stenting for cerebral venous sinus stenosis; repeated lumbar punctures; cataract surgery; fundus photography; optical coherence tomography (OCT) of the optic nerves; stereo-optic disc photography
Follow-Up Instructions: Return monthly for 6–12 months with careful evaluation of formal visual fields, stereo optic‑disc photos, OCT of the nerves, visual acuity, color vision, and RAPD testing; lengthen interval if stable but continue close long‑term follow‑up for possible recurrence or shunt failure.
Patient Education: Weight reduction can improve papilledema and reduce intracranial pressure; discuss diet and possibly bariatric surgery if morbidly obese. Acetazolamide (1–2 g/day) is effective for most patients but avoid or use cautiously in pregnancy—consult obstetrician. Regular monitoring of vision (visual fields, acuity, color vision, optic nerve photos/OCT) is essential; return monthly for 6–12 months initially. If medical therapy fails, surgical options include CSF shunting (VP or lumboperitoneal) or optic nerve sheath fenestration; discuss risks/benefits. Headache may need symptomatic treatment; repeated lumbar punctures are generally not recommended as primary therapy. Seek urgent care for worsening vision, persistent transient visual obscurations, new focal neurologic signs, or severe headaches.


CASE 62 — Visual Field Defect—Junctional Scotoma
================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: painless progressive vision loss in the right eye
DURATION: 8 months
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I had a pulsating sound in my head"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 62-year-old presented with painless progressive vision loss in the right eye, progressive over weeks to months, with associated right afferent pupillary defect, right central scotoma, right optic atrophy, and a contralateral superior temporal defect; MRI showed a pituitary adenoma.
History Narrative: A 22-year-old obese woman with an 8-month history of almost-daily diffuse headaches, rarely with nausea, who over the last 2 months noted pulsatile, posture-sensitive tinnitus and over the last 2 weeks had brief transient visual obscurations in either eye; she denied diplopia or other neurologic symptoms, was not pregnant and took no medications associated with increased intracranial pressure.
Symptom Onset: 8 months
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Progressive, painless vision loss in the right eye over months
Characteristics: Right optic neuropathy with junctional scotoma secondary to compressive lesion of the junction of the right optic nerve with the chiasm (pituitary adenoma)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Changing posture, especially going from lying to standing
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive vision loss in the right eye; headache (almost daily, diffuse); pulsatile tinnitus (pulse-synchronous tinnitus), transient visual loss episodes in either eye; no diplopia
Previous Episodes: Transient visual loss episodes lasting seconds in the left or right eye over the last 2 weeks
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate impact — intermittent transient visual obscurations and progressive painless vision loss in the right eye with contralateral field defect causing functional limitations but preserved acuity (20/20) so activities moderately affected.
Systemic Symptoms: Headache; pulsatile tinnitus; transient visual obscurations; obesity (weight-related)
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Obesity (female, 250 lb), reproductive-age woman (childbearing years)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 135/85 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/20
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing (no conjunctival injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, clear lenses

Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally, reactive to light, no relative afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Reactive to light and accommodation (reacted well to light)
Pupils – RAPD: Right afferent pupillary defect

Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal periphery


Assessment

Secondary Diagnoses: Pituitary adenoma; Right optic neuropathy (compressive) with junctional scotoma; Right optic atrophy; Headache; Pulsatile tinnitus; Obesity; Elevated intracranial pressure/papilledema
Differential Diagnosis: Pituitary tumor (pituitary adenoma) causing junctional compression; suprasellar meningioma; supraclinoid aneurysm; craniopharyngioma
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: optic atrophy


Plan
All Medications: Dopamine agonists (for prolactin-secreting tumors)
All Procedures: Decompressive surgical removal of the pituitary (suprasellar) mass (transsphenoidal pituitary tumor decompression)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up ophthalmologic examinations with visual field testing every 4 months for 1 year, then every 6 months thereafter, with periodic MRI scans to monitor for tumor growth.
Patient Education: You have a compressive lesion at the junction of the right optic nerve and the optic chiasm (pituitary adenoma) causing vision loss in the right eye and a visual field defect in the left eye. Key points: - The tumor can press on the optic nerves causing progressive, painless vision loss and visual field defects; prompt treatment reduces risk of permanent vision loss. - Recommended care includes pituitary hormone evaluation, MRI imaging for diagnosis and monitoring, and typically surgical decompression (transsphenoidal resection) ± medical therapy (e.g., dopamine agonists for prolactinomas) or adjunctive radiation/chemotherapy depending on pathology. - Expect baseline and serial ophthalmic exams (visual acuity, color vision, pupillary testing, formal visual fields, and optic nerve imaging) and periodic MRIs after treatment to monitor tumor and recovery. - Report any new symptoms immediately: sudden vision loss, worsening visual field, new double vision, severe headache, changes in menstruation or libido, fatigue, symptoms of hormone imbalance, or signs of cerebrospinal fluid leak after surgery. - Rehabilitation includes low-vision support if deficits persist; follow-up schedule is typically every 4 months for the first year, then every 6 months with periodic MRIs. - Lifestyle and safety: avoid activities that risk head injury until cleared; arrange assistance for driving if visual fields or acuity are impaired.
Referrals: Neurosurgery (for decompressive surgical removal) and endocrinology (for pituitary hormone evaluation and replacement)


CASE 63 — Visual Field Defect—Pituitary Lesion
==============================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: progressive painless visual impairment in the right eye for 1½ years
DURATION: 1.5 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have progressive painless visual impairment in my right eye for about a year and a half."

History Narrative: A 55-year-old man complained of progressive painless visual impairment in his right eye over a period of 1½ years. He denied any visual difficulty in his left eye and denied any other history of neurologic or ophthalmologic impairment.
Symptom Onset: 1.5 years
Symptom Duration: 1.5 years
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Bitemporal hemianopia with superotemporal defect in left eye and generalized diffuse depression in right eye; painless progressive visual loss; right optic disc pallor; right relative afferent pupillary defect; respects vertical meridian

Laterality: Bilateral

Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative visual field and ophthalmologic examination as soon as tolerated; then visual fields and serial ophthalmologic exams at 3 months, then every 6 months for 2 years, then yearly for 5 years, then every 2 years thereafter; MRI at 6 months, 1 year, and then yearly for several years; endocrinologic follow-up and hormonal replacement as needed.
Patient Education: You have a lesion compressing the optic chiasm (most likely a pituitary tumor) causing loss of peripheral (temporal) vision. Important points: - This type of vision loss is typically painless and progresses slowly; early detection and treatment can prevent further vision loss. - Neuroimaging (MRI of the sella) is needed to confirm the diagnosis and to plan treatment. - Treatment is usually surgical removal of the mass; some tumors (prolactinomas) may shrink with medication. Additional treatments can include radiation or hormone replacement as needed. - Visual fields and eye exams should be repeated after treatment and then at regular intervals to monitor recovery and detect recurrence. - Report new headaches, worsening vision, double vision, nausea/vomiting, or hormonal symptoms (weakness, sexual dysfunction, weight changes) promptly. - Bring a companion when traveling or driving is necessary; avoid driving until cleared, as peripheral vision loss can impair safety.
Referrals: Neuroimaging (preferably MRI of the sella) and neurosurgical consultation; endocrinology for hormonal evaluation and postoperative follow-up.


CASE 64 — Visual Field Defect—Homonymous Hemianopia
===================================================
Subjective

Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia consistent with a retrochiasmal visual pathway lesion; recommend control of vascular risk factors, stroke prophylaxis (e.g., aspirin), and repeat visual fields and ophthalmologic exam in ~6 months with consideration of rehabilitation (saccadic training, prism glasses) as appropriate.
Primary Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarction causing a dense, congruous left homonymous hemianopia
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; hyperlipidemia
Differential Diagnosis: Right occipital lobe infarct (retrochiasmal stroke); Parietal lobe infarct causing homonymous hemianopia; Optic tract lesion (e.g., compressive mass or tumor); Demyelinating lesion of retrochiasmal pathways
Severity: Dense
Laterality: Right
Complications: Difficulty reading (losing place or missing lines), poor driving performance with motor vehicle accidents, bumping left front fender, vision-related functional impairment (reduced safety), potential persistent dense homonymous hemianopia with limited treatment options and risk of stroke-related morbidity.


Plan
All Medications: acetaminophen aspirin
Follow-Up Instructions: Repeat visual fields and ophthalmologic examination approximately 6 months after onset to assess for improvement.
Patient Education: - Explanation that vision loss is due to a stroke in the right occipital lobe causing a left homonymous hemianopia (loss of the left visual field in both eyes). - Reassure that eye structures are normal; the problem is in the brain’s visual pathway. - Risk-factor modification: control blood pressure, diabetes, and cholesterol; smoking cessation; discuss aspirin for stroke prevention with primary care/neurology. - Driving safety: advise against driving until formally evaluated and cleared; discuss reporting requirements and occupational restrictions. - Reading and daily function strategies: use a ruler or line guide, train large saccadic eye movements toward the blind side, practice systematic scanning patterns. - Low-vision/optical options: trial of peripheral prism glasses with a low-vision specialist may help; discuss limitations. - Rehabilitation options: referral for vision rehabilitation/occupational therapy for scanning training and adaptive strategies. - Follow-up: repeat visual fields and ophthalmology exam about 6 months after onset to assess for improvement. - When to seek urgent care: new or worsening weakness, speech changes, sudden visual change, severe headache, or other stroke symptoms.
Referrals: Neurology (stroke evaluation/management)


CASE 65 — Transient Monocular Visual Loss
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Transient monocular visual loss
DURATION: 6 weeks
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "lose his place" and "miss lines"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Six-week history since coronary bypass surgery of recurrent episodes of transient monocular visual loss described as “losing his place” and “missing lines” when reading and bumping the left front fender when parking; no other neurologic symptoms. Past medical history notable for hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia. Visual acuity 20/20 OU; left homonymous hemianopia on visual field testing.
History Narrative: A 57-year-old man with a past history of hypertension, diabetes, and hyperlipidemia who underwent coronary bypass surgery 6 weeks prior, with an apparently uneventful recovery. Since surgery he often “loses his place” or “misses lines” when reading and has bumped his left front fender when parking. No other neurologic symptoms.
Symptom Onset: 6 weeks prior to evaluation
Symptom Duration: 6 weeks
Progression: Stable
Characteristics: Left homonymous hemianopia, congruous, macular-sparing, no relative afferent pupillary defect


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: "lose his place" or "miss lines" when reading; bumping his left front fender when parking; no other neurologic symptoms
Prior Treatments: Coronary artery bypass graft surgery 6 weeks prior; antihypertensive therapy; lipid-lowering therapy (statin); aspirin/antiplatelet therapy (inferred standard post‑CABG)
Impact on Daily Activities: He often “lost his place” or “miss lines” when reading and bumped his left front fender several times when parking, indicating moderate interference with reading and driving/parking tasks.
Systemic Symptoms: hypertension, hyperlipidemia, diabetes
Risk Factors: Hypertension; Diabetes; Hyperlipidemia; Recent coronary artery bypass surgery; Carotid atherosclerotic disease (right carotid stenosis)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: clear
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: normal/open
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: no relative afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Normal
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal discs
Fundus Exam – Macula: Unremarkable (no macular abnormalities)

Fundus Exam – Periphery: No hemorrhages or exudates; peripheral retina without lesions (normal)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Retinal emboli from right carotid stenosis causing transient monocular visual loss; recommend vascular (carotid/aortic) and cardiac evaluation, neuroimaging (MRI), and stroke risk–factor management with antiplatelet therapy pending definitive intervention (CEA or stenting if ipsilateral carotid stenosis ≥70% and patient is surgical candidate).
Primary Diagnosis: Retinal emboli secondary to right carotid stenosis causing transient monocular visual loss
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension; Hyperlipidemia; Diabetes; Coronary artery disease (post–coronary artery bypass graft)
Differential Diagnosis: Retrochiasmal visual pathway lesion (occipital lobe infarct) ; Carotid artery embolism causing retinal/retrochiasmal ischemia ; Cardioembolic source (valvular thrombus or atrial fibrillation) ; Giant cell arteritis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: Transient monocular visual loss; retinal emboli (Hollenhorst plaque); stroke risk (ipsilateral stroke); recurrent retinal/ocular vascular occlusion; persistent homonymous hemianopia (visual field defect); carotid artery restenosis; perioperative morbidity/mortality related to carotid surgery


Plan
All Medications: aspirin
All Procedures: carotid endarterectomy; carotid stenting; anticoagulation (for cardiac valvular/atrial fibrillation sources); carotid Doppler; MR angiography; CT scan; MRI; coronary bypass surgery
Follow-Up Instructions: Medical supervision of stroke risk factors; periodic reevaluation of the carotid artery for restenosis with noninvasive studies (e.g., carotid Doppler or MR angiography).
Patient Education: Explain that his brief monocular vision losses were caused by cholesterol/embolic debris from the carotid or heart traveling to the eye (Hollenhorst plaque). Emphasize that this is a warning sign for higher stroke risk and that prompt vascular and cardiac evaluation (carotid imaging, cardiac evaluation, and possible blood tests) is needed. Review stroke risk-factor control: blood pressure, cholesterol, diabetes management, smoking cessation, diet/exercise, and adherence to prescribed antiplatelet or anticoagulant therapy if indicated. Discuss treatment options if carotid narrowing is found (medical therapy, carotid endarterectomy or stenting) and explain the need for close follow-up with vascular/neurology and periodic carotid imaging. Advise to seek emergency care for any new sudden vision loss, weakness, numbness, speech changes, or confusion.
Referrals: Neurology (neuroimaging and stroke evaluation), Vascular surgery or vascular medicine (carotid evaluation/endarterectomy consideration), Cardiology (cardiac/aortic embolic source evaluation), Rheumatology or Neuroimmunology (giant cell arteritis evaluation if indicated), Ophthalmology (retinal vascular/retina follow-up)


CASE 66 — Third Nerve Palsy—Ischemic
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 4 weeks of binocular diplopia
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I gray-out in the right eye for a period of 5 or 6 minutes."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 70-year-old patient with a history of hypertension and diabetes mellitus presented with 4 weeks of binocular diplopia, normal pupils, ptosis of the right upper lid, and mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy; exam showed no adduction, elevation, or depression with intact abduction consistent with a pupil-sparing, complete ischemic third nerve palsy.
History Narrative: A 70-year-old patient with a history of hypertension and diabetes mellitus presented with 4 weeks of binocular diplopia, normal pupils, ptosis of the right upper lid, and mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy. The patient had no adduction, elevation, or depression with intact abduction consistent with a pupil‑sparing, complete ischemic third nerve palsy.
Symptom Onset: 4 weeks
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Characteristics: Pupil-sparing, complete extraocular muscle palsy with ptosis; ischemic (vasculopathic) etiology in an older patient with vascular risk factors (diabetes, hypertension, hypercholesterolemia).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: binocular diplopia, ptosis (right upper lid drooping)
Previous Episodes: 4 weeks of binocular diplopia (patient presented with 4 weeks of symptoms)
Impact on Daily Activities: Likely moderate impairment — binocular diplopia and complete ptosis of the right eyelid causing difficulty with reading, driving, and activities requiring binocular vision, though visual acuity largely preserved.
Systemic Symptoms: Denies headaches, jaw claudication, persistent visual loss, or transient neurologic dysfunction.
Risk Factors: Hypertension, diabetes mellitus, increased cholesterol


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 142/86 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N15 (J1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right upper eyelid complete ptosis with markedly impaired levator function; otherwise lids and lashes unremarkable (no lash abnormalities noted)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Injected conjunctiva with no scleral icterus
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external adnexa without proptosis or enophthalmos; right complete ptosis with markedly impaired right levator function.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open (assumed normal)
Anterior Segment – Lens: mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally (round)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: both reacted well to ... near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: normal pupils
Extraocular Movements: No adduction, elevation, or depression of the right eye; intact abduction (right complete III nerve palsy with preserved abduction)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild bilateral nuclear sclerotic cataracts
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal (no optic disc edema or pallor)
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Embolic plaque in retinal arteriole (Hollenhorst plaque)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ischemic isolated right third nerve palsy (pupil-sparing) in a patient with vasculopathic risk factors (hypertension, diabetes, hyperlipidemia). Findings: 4 weeks of binocular diplopia, complete right ptosis with markedly impaired levator function, complete paresis of adduction, elevation, and depression with intact abduction, normal/reactive pupils, mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy. Plan: control vascular risk factors, observe with follow-up every 1–2 months; expect recovery over 3–6 months; if no improvement by 3 months obtain neuroimaging to evaluate for compressive lesion. Consider strabismus or lid surgery if residual diplopia or ptosis persists.
Primary Diagnosis: Ischemic isolated third nerve palsy
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy; increased cholesterol
Differential Diagnosis: Ischemic microvascular third nerve palsy (diabetic/hypertensive ischemia); compressive third nerve palsy (posterior communicating artery aneurysm or tumor); myasthenia gravis; Tolosa–Hunt syndrome
Severity: Complete
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: hypertension medication (unspecified), cholesterol-lowering medication (unspecified)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow at 1- to 2-month intervals to monitor for improvement; expect resolution in 3–6 months. If no improvement by 3 months, obtain neuroimaging to evaluate for a compressive lesion.
Patient Education: Control vascular risk factors (diabetes, hypertension, hyperlipidemia); expect ischemic third‑nerve palsy to improve over 3–6 months; follow up every 1–2 months to monitor recovery; consider neuroimaging if no improvement by 3 months; strabismus or eyelid surgery may be offered for persistent diplopia or ptosis.


CASE 67 — Third Nerve Palsy—Aneurysm
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Binocular diplopia with left-sided frontal–periorbital pain, left eyelid droop, and anisocoria (left pupil larger than right)
DURATION: 4 weeks
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "stabilized" and in fact had "improved" since his right eye "drooped."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 70-year-old man with a 4-week history of binocular diplopia that developed and worsened over a 5-day period, then stabilized and partially improved after his right eye “drooped.” In the first 2 weeks he had severe right retro-orbital pain that later became minimal. He reports prior bilateral cataract extraction with otherwise stable vision, history of hypertension and diabetes, one pack-per-day smoking, and denies facial numbness, recent headache, jaw claudications, or other neurologic deficits. Examination noted visual acuity 20/30 OD and 20/25 OS, pupils 3 mm bilaterally reactive to light and near, no RAPD, complete right ptosis with markedly impaired levator function, inability to adduct, elevate, or depress the right eye with full abduction and mild incyclodeviation on depression, normal left motility, no proptosis, normal facial sensation, and otherwise normal neurologic exam; slit-lamp showed bilateral posterior chamber IOLs and fundus revealed mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy without optic disc pathology.
History Narrative: A 70-year-old man had a 4-week history of binocular diplopia. The diplopia developed and worsened over a 5-day period, but then stabilized and had improved since his right eye “drooped.” In the first 2 weeks he had severe retro-orbital pain on the right which became minimal. He had a history of bilateral cataract extraction but vision otherwise stable. Past medical history included hypertension and diabetes. He smoked one pack per day. He denied facial numbness, recent headache, jaw claudication, or other neurologic deficits. Examination noted visual acuity 20/30 OD and 20/25 OS, pupils 3 mm bilaterally reactive to light and near without relative afferent pupillary defect, normal visual fields, complete ptosis of the right lid with markedly impaired levator function, inability to adduct, elevate, or depress the right eye with full abduction and mild incyclodeviation on attempted depression, normal motility in the left eye, no proptosis, normal facial sensation, otherwise normal neurologic exam, bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses, and mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy without optic disc pathology.
Symptom Onset: Acute (developed over 5 days, with 4-week history of diplopia)
Symptom Duration: 4 weeks
Progression: improved/stabilized
Characteristics: Partial, pupil-involving left third nerve palsy (aneurysmal compression) with ptosis, impaired elevation/adduction/depression, abduction spared, anisocoria (left pupil larger), acute onset with periorbital pain


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Gaze (eye movements) — attempts at elevation, adduction, and depression of the affected eye worsened symptoms/diplopia.
Alleviating Factors: Patching the eye to relieve the diplopia
Associated Symptoms: binocular diplopia; frontal–periorbital (retro-orbital) pain; lid droop (ptosis); anisocoria (left pupil larger); mild incyclodeviation on attempted depression
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — diplopia, severe ptosis, and ocular motility loss causing difficulty with reading, driving, and daily visual tasks; requires eye patching for symptomatic relief.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypertension; diabetes; smoking (1 pack/day); no headache, jaw claudication, or other neurologic deficits.
Risk Factors: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; current smoker (1 pack/day); prior bilateral cataract extraction; age (70 years)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 140/85 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right complete ptosis with markedly impaired levator function; left lids normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external exam; no proptosis.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal appearing iris with anisocoria (left pupil larger than right) but otherwise unremarkable.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Posterior chamber intraocular lens (bilateral)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm bilaterally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Both reacted well to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Both reacted well to near
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD
Pupils – Other: anisocoria (left pupil larger than right)
Extraocular Movements: Right: complete limitation of adduction, elevation, and depression; full abduction. Left: full motility.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy without optic disc pathology
Fundus Exam – Macula: no acute macular abnormality
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy only


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Partial third nerve palsy (pupil-involving) of the left eye secondary to a posterior communicating artery aneurysm, presenting with acute binocular diplopia, ptosis, anisocoria (left pupil larger), and partial paresis of elevation, adduction, and depression; recommend neuroimaging (CT/CTA, MRI/MRA) and urgent neurosurgical/endovascular consultation for aneurysm repair.
Primary Diagnosis: Posterior communicating artery aneurysm causing pupil-involving left third nerve palsy
Secondary Diagnoses: hypertension; diabetes mellitus; bilateral posterior chamber intraocular lenses; mild bilateral diabetic nonproliferative retinopathy; history of smoking (tobacco use)
Differential Diagnosis: Posterior communicating artery aneurysm compressive third nerve palsy; Ischemic (microvascular) third nerve palsy due to diabetes/hypertension; Pituitary/sellar mass compressive third nerve palsy; Cavernous sinus process (thrombosis or fistula)
Severity: Severe
Laterality: left
Complications: Aneurysmal rupture with high morbidity and mortality


Plan
All Procedures: Surgery or endovascular therapy to repair the aneurysm
Follow-Up Instructions: Patch the eye to relieve the diplopia; observe for pupil changes and arrange neuroimaging (CT/CTA ± MRI/MRA and consider cerebral angiography) with timely neurosurgical/endovascular referral for aneurysm repair.
Patient Education: Explain that the drooping eyelid, double vision, and pupil change are caused by weakness of the third cranial nerve from a posterior communicating artery aneurysm — a bulge in a brain artery that can press on the nerve. Urgent treatment (surgical clipping or endovascular repair) is recommended to prevent rupture. While awaiting definitive therapy you may need to patch the affected eye or use prism/patching for diplopia and avoid activities with high rupture risk (heavy exertion, uncontrolled high blood pressure, smoking). Report any worsening headache, sudden severe pain, nausea/vomiting, loss of consciousness, or new neurologic symptoms immediately. Control vascular risk factors (blood pressure, diabetes), stop smoking, and attend scheduled neurosurgical and ophthalmic follow-up for imaging and recovery monitoring; eyelid and eye movement may improve after aneurysm repair but can take weeks to months, and further rehabilitation or strabismus surgery may be considered later if diplopia persists.
Referrals: Neurosurgery and/or interventional neuroradiology for aneurysm repair; neuro-ophthalmology follow-up.
Emergency Instructions: Surgery or endovascular therapy to repair the aneurysm is necessary to prevent aneurysmal rupture, which carries a high morbidity and mortality.


CASE 68 — Fourth Nerve Palsy—Congenital
=======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Vertical binocular diplopia
DURATION: 8 days
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I noted the acute onset of binocular diplopia."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 10-year-old patient with acute onset of vertical binocular diplopia, noted to have a right hypertropia worse on left gaze and right head tilt; vertical fusional amplitudes of 10 PD consistent with congenital fourth nerve palsy.

Characteristics: congenital fourth nerve palsy; vertical binocular diplopia; right hypertropia worse on left gaze and right head tilt; vertical fusional amplitudes 10 PD; large vertical fusional amplitudes; hypertropia

Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect

Differential Diagnosis: Congenital fourth nerve palsy; Decompensated congenital fourth nerve palsy; Traumatic fourth nerve palsy; Superior oblique tendon palsy
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
