CASE 69 — Sixth Nerve Palsy
===========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Binocular horizontal diplopia for 3 months, worse on left gaze
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "below and to the side of the other"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 56-year-old with type II diabetes mellitus presenting with a 3-month history of binocular horizontal diplopia, primarily at distance and worse on left gaze.
History Narrative: A 56-year-old patient with type II diabetes mellitus presented with a 3-month history of binocular horizontal diplopia, primarily at distance and worse on left gaze. He had no other focal complaints reported in the history portion.
Symptom Onset: 3-month history
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Progressive over 3 months
Characteristics: Left sixth nerve palsy with limitation on abduction, 4 PD esotropia, binocular horizontal diplopia worse on left gaze, left V1 sensory loss, left-sided headache, cavernous sinus meningioma (compressive lesion)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: worse on left gaze
Associated Symptoms: Binocular horizontal diplopia (worse at distance and on left gaze), left-sided headache, left facial numbness/sensory changes in V1 distribution, limitation on abduction (left), esotropia (4 PD)
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — binocular diplopia at distance affecting reading/driving; requires patching/prisms and possible surgery.
Systemic Symptoms: left-sided headache and left facial numbness
Risk Factors: Diabetes mellitus (type II)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: white and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; external adnexa unremarkable (no ptosis), with left facial numbness noted from trigeminal V1 involvement.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal cornea, no abnormalities reported
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally, equally reactive to light and near (normal shape)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equal reaction to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Left abduction limitation; right ductions and versions intact.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Some degree of excyclotropia in the right eye.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Left sixth cranial nerve palsy with left lateral rectus paresis causing esotropia and symptomatic binocular horizontal diplopia, associated with left V1 sensory disturbance and left-sided headache; neuroimaging (MRI) demonstrated a cavernous sinus meningioma accounting for combined abducens and ophthalmic-branch trigeminal involvement. Management directed at the underlying tumor (surgery/radiation as indicated) with symptomatic diplopia control (patching, prisms, consideration of early ipsilateral medial rectus botulinum injection) and follow-up for residual ophthalmoplegia and neurologic deficits.
Primary Diagnosis: Left sixth nerve palsy secondary to cavernous sinus meningioma
Secondary Diagnoses: Type II diabetes mellitus; left ophthalmic (V1) trigeminal neuropathy (sensory changes) due to cavernous sinus meningioma; left-sided headache
Differential Diagnosis: Left sixth nerve palsy due to cavernous sinus lesion (meningioma); Ischemic (microvascular) sixth nerve palsy; Cavernous sinus thrombosis or carotid-cavernous fistula; Myasthenia gravis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left
Complications: Diplopia (binocular horizontal, worse on left gaze)


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Observe for recurrence of further neurologic and ophthalmologic deficits depending on the etiology; continue symptomatic diplopia treatment (patching, prisms, or botulinum/strabismus surgery as indicated) and follow-up for possible surgery, radiation, or observation directed at the underlying lesion.
Patient Education: Explain that the double vision is due to weakness of the sixth cranial nerve affecting the outward-moving muscle (lateral rectus) on the left side, likely caused by a mass in the cavernous sinus (meningioma). Recommend neuroimaging (MRI) if not already done and discuss treatment options directed at the underlying lesion (observation, surgery, radiation). For symptom relief: use an eye patch (full or prism) or temporary prism glasses, consider early botulinum injection to the medial rectus to help alignment, and strabismus surgery if diplopia persists and is stable. Advise monitoring for new or worsening neurological signs (worsening double vision, facial numbness, headaches, eyelid droop) and to seek prompt care if these occur. Address diabetes control as part of overall health. Provide reassurance that some cases improve with treatment, and arrange follow-up with neuro-ophthalmology and neurosurgery/oncology as appropriate.
Referrals: Neuroimaging (MRI) / Neurosurgery and/or Radiation Oncology for cavernous sinus meningioma evaluation and management; Ophthalmology/Strabismus clinic for symptomatic diplopia management (prisms/patching/botulinum injection; possible strabismus surgery)


CASE 70 — Internuclear Ophthalmoplegia
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: difficulty focusing her eyes
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "difficulty focusing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 27-year-old woman with a history of multiple sclerosis and prior right optic neuritis, not on medications, presenting with “difficulty focusing” her eyes. She has mild left-eye adduction deficit, right abducting nystagmus, and slow saccades in left adduction; convergence is intact, consistent with a left internuclear ophthalmoplegia.
History Narrative: A 27-year-old woman with multiple sclerosis and a history of right optic neuritis not on medications presented with “difficulty focusing” her eyes. She had mild adduction deficit of the left eye, right abducting nystagmus, slow saccades in adduction of the left eye, and intact convergence, consistent with left internuclear ophthalmoplegia. Additionally, a 56-year-old man reported a 3-month history of binocular diplopia—worse at distance, absent at near, and worse on left gaze—along with left forehead numbness/tingling and mild left frontotemporal headaches. He denied trauma, visual loss, or prior neurologic/ophthalmologic symptoms; he was diabetic on oral agents. Examination showed 20/20 vision OU, full color plates, pupils 4 mm equal reactive, no RAPD, full confrontational fields, a 4 PD esotropia at distance (none at near), esotropia nil on right gaze, 8 PD ET on left gaze, 4 PD ET on up/down gaze, underaction of left abduction, slightly depressed left corneal reflex, decreased soft touch sensation over left forehead, otherwise normal slit-lamp, fundus, and neurologic exam with no papilledema.
Symptom Onset: 3 months
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Characteristics: Left internuclear ophthalmoplegia: mild left eye adduction deficit, right abducting nystagmus, slow adduction saccades in left eye, intact convergence, horizontal diplopia worse at distance and on left gaze, 4 PD esotropia at distance (nil at near), underaction of left abduction.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Looking to the left (left gaze) and distance fixation.
Associated Symptoms: difficulty focusing; binocular diplopia (worse at distance, absent at near, worse on left gaze); numbness and tingling in left forehead; mild left frontotemporal headaches
Impact on Daily Activities: Difficulty focusing eyes and binocular diplopia affecting distance vision; may interfere with activities requiring distance vision (e.g., driving), but near tasks spared — mild to moderate functional impact.
Systemic Symptoms: numbness and tingling in the left forehead; mild left frontotemporal headaches; diabetes (on oral agents)
Risk Factors: Multiple sclerosis; diabetes mellitus; prior right optic neuritis; age (56 male) as vascular risk factor; possible prior neurologic disease.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear and quiet
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbits without external abnormality (no eyelid swelling or deformity)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: left corneal reflex slightly depressed; slit‑lamp otherwise normal (no corneal pathology)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear crystalline lens bilaterally (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 4 mm bilaterally and equal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 4mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Near: reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: none
Pupils – Other: 4mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Mild adduction deficit in the left eye; right abducting nystagmus; slow adduction saccades in the left eye; intact convergence; underaction of abduction in the left eye.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp exam was normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal appearing vessels
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits (no peripheral retinal lesions noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right optic neuropathy and left internuclear ophthalmoplegia (INO) due to multiple sclerosis, presenting with left adduction deficit, right abducting nystagmus, slowed adducting saccades, intact convergence, and distance-predominant binocular diplopia; consider neuroimaging and evaluation for underlying cause, with symptomatic prism or surgical strabismus options if persistent.
Primary Diagnosis: Internuclear ophthalmoplegia (left) due to multiple sclerosis
Secondary Diagnoses: multiple sclerosis; right optic neuritis; optic neuropathy on the right; left internuclear ophthalmoplegia (INO); sixth nerve palsy (left abduction deficit); diabetes mellitus
Differential Diagnosis: Multiple sclerosis–related internuclear ophthalmoplegia; brainstem (pontine/medial longitudinal fasciculus) ischemic infarct; demyelinating/inflammatory brainstem lesion (e.g., neuromyelitis optica, sarcoidosis); mass lesion/compressive brainstem pathology
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for resolution of the INO is important.
Patient Education: INO is caused by a problem in the brainstem connection between the eyes (often due to multiple sclerosis). Symptoms include double vision, trouble moving one eye inward, and outward nystagmus of the other eye. Imaging (MRI with contrast) and neurologic evaluation may be needed to find the cause. Treatment targets the underlying disease; diplopia may improve on its own, with prisms or temporary occlusion for symptomatic relief, and rarely strabismus surgery if misalignment persists. Report any new or worsening symptoms (vision loss, headache, weakness, numbness, or difficulty speaking) promptly. Follow-up exams are important to monitor recovery.


CASE 71 — Diplopia—Ocular Myasthenia Gravis
===========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months, worse as the day goes on, with intermittent left or right eyelid droop
DURATION: 6 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "difficulty focusing"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months that worsens as the day progresses, with intermittent left or right eyelid droop (ptosis); symptoms fluctuate during the day.
History Narrative: A 27-year-old woman with a history of multiple sclerosis (MS) complained of “difficulty focusing” her eyes. Her previous MS course included a history of optic neuritis in the right eye. She did have mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, and leg numbness. Her eye difficulty started several months prior to ophthalmologic evaluation. She was taking no medications. Ophthalmologic examination revealed visual acuity to be 20/20 bilaterally. She identified 7 of 10 pseudoisochromatic plates on the right and 10 of 10 on the left. Visual fields showed a small paracentral scotoma on the right. Pupils were 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near, but there was a mild right afferent pupillary defect. The patient had a 2 prism diopter left hypertropia that was comitant in left, right, up, and down gaze. There was mild paresis of adduction in the left eye but otherwise ductions were full. On gaze to the right, the adducting saccade in the left eye was quite slow compared to the abducting saccade in the right eye. Saccades to the left and vertically were normal. On attempting to hold her gaze to the right, monocular dissociated horizontal nystagmus was noted in the right eye in the direction of abduction. Convergence was normal and was able to overcome the adduction deficit in the left eye. There was no ptosis. Facial sensation and movement were normal. Slit-lamp examination was normal. Fundus exam revealed mild temporal pallor in the right eye but was otherwise normal.
Symptom Onset: 6 months
Symptom Duration: 6 months
Progression: Worsening over the course of the day (fluctuating/progressive fatigue of symptoms)
Characteristics: Intermittent diplopia worsening throughout the day with fluctuating bilateral ptosis, paresis of both lateral rectus muscles, enhanced ptosis, positive Tensilon test, negative acetylcholine receptor antibodies — consistent with ocular myasthenia gravis.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Symptoms worsen "as the day goes on" (fatigue/prolonged use of ocular muscles)
Alleviating Factors: Convergence (overcoming the adduction deficit)
Associated Symptoms: intermittent diplopia, intermittent left or right eyelid droop (ptosis), worsening fatigability of ocular symptoms as day progresses, fluctuating ocular motor weakness, mild gait instability, bladder disturbance, leg numbness, history of optic neuritis (decreased color vision, small paracentral scotoma, mild right afferent pupillary defect), oscillopsia
Previous Episodes: Intermittent diplopia for 6 months with intermittent eyelid droop; prior optic neuritis in right eye (history of MS)
Impact on Daily Activities: Intermittent diplopia and fluctuating ptosis that worsen throughout the day, causing difficulty with vision-related tasks (e.g., reading, driving, close work) and potentially requiring patching or spectacle crutches; may limit daily activities and require medical management.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Jäger 1.0
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: bilateral ptosis
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: WNL
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, no proptosis or enophthalmos, orbits without masses or tenderness on palpation
Anterior Segment – Cornea: normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: clear and quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Equal reaction to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: briskly reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: Mild right RAPD
Pupils – Other: 5mm bilaterally and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Paresis of both lateral rectus muscles; bilateral ptosis with increased ptosis on contralateral eyelid elevation (enhanced ptosis); otherwise ductions not fully specified.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Slit-lamp examination was normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: mild temporal pallor in the right eye; otherwise normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ocular myasthenia gravis presenting with fluctuating diplopia and bilateral ptosis, bilateral lateral rectus paresis, enhanced ptosis, positive Tensilon test, and negative acetylcholine receptor antibodies—consistent with purely ocular MG.
Primary Diagnosis: Ocular myasthenia gravis
Differential Diagnosis: Internuclear ophthalmoplegia from multiple sclerosis or brainstem infarct; Ocular myasthenia gravis (myasthenic pseudo-INO); Cranial nerve VI palsy (bilateral/alternating abducens involvement); Brainstem mass or demyelinating lesion (e.g., tumor, infection)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Potential generalization to generalized myasthenia gravis (risk of life‑threatening respiratory involvement), treatment side effects from corticosteroids and immunosuppressants, thymoma (if present) necessitating thymectomy, persistent diplopia and ptosis, exposure keratopathy from eyelid retraction or crutch use, and surgical complications if ptosis surgery performed.


Plan
All Medications: pyridostigmine bromide (Mestinon), prednisone, corticosteroids (general), azathioprine, cyclophosphamide, cyclosporine, intravenous immunoglobulin, plasmapheresis
All Procedures: Tensilon test Single fiber EMG Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of brain Computed tomography (CT) of mediastinum (or chest CT) for thymoma evaluation Mediastinal MRI (alternative to CT) Ptosis surgery (surgical ptosis repair) Spectacle crutch placement Plasmapheresis Intravenous immunoglobulin (IVIG)
Follow-Up Instructions: Patients need close supervision of medications used to control symptoms and should be warned of the possibility of generalization of disease; they must inform their physician immediately if new symptoms such as dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or extremity weakness develop.
Patient Education: Warn that ocular myasthenia can fluctuate and may generalize; report immediately any new symptoms such as difficulty swallowing, breathing, or limb weakness. Use patching or prism/occlusion for troublesome diplopia; consider eyelid crutch for ptosis. Take medications as instructed and attend close follow-up for dose adjustments. Undergo chest imaging (CT or MRI) to evaluate for thymoma. Seek urgent care for respiratory symptoms.
Referrals: Mediastinal CT or MRI for thymoma evaluation; neurology referral for systemic/myasthenia management and monitoring
Emergency Instructions: Inform your physician immediately if you develop dysphagia, respiratory difficulty, or weakness of the extremities.


CASE 72 — Thyroid Ophthalmopathy
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: mild to moderate eye irritation / foreign body sensation, diplopia, and bulging eyes
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "eye irritation" or "foreign body–type" sensation "diplopia" "eyes bulging" "two lines instead of one"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 35-year-old woman with history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago who presents with a 2-month history of mild to moderate eye irritation/foreign body sensation, diplopia, and bulging eyes; onsets progressive over 2 months.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with a past medical history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine one year ago presents with a 2-month history of mild to moderate eye irritation described as a foreign-body sensation, diplopia, and progressive eye bulging. She reports limitation of elevation and depression and limitation of abduction in both eyes, lid retraction, lid lag, proptosis, and symptoms consistent with mild punctate keratopathy.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Gradual over 2 months with worsening during the day (progressive worsening of symptoms)
Characteristics: Thyroid ophthalmopathy with proptosis, lid retraction/lag, diplopia, restrictive extraocular myopathy (limited elevation, depression, abduction), mild punctate keratopathy, exposure-related irritation, chronic inflammatory → fibrotic phase risk


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Smoking; elevation of body temperature (worsens symptoms); prolonged or sustained upward gaze and sustained exertion/fatigue during the day.
Alleviating Factors: rest, eyelid closure (taping or sleeping with eyes closed), head-of-bed elevation, smoking cessation, artificial tears/ointment, systemic corticosteroids (temporizing), orbital radiation (in acute cases), botulinum toxin for lid retraction
Associated Symptoms: eye irritation; foreign body sensation; diplopia; eyes bulging; limitation of elevation and depression; limitation of abduction; lid retraction; lid lag; proptosis; mild punctate keratopathy; intermittent diplopia worsening through day; ptosis; worsened ptosis with sustained upward gaze; orbicularis oculi paresis
Prior Treatments: Radioactive iodine (for hyperthyroidism) 1 year ago; supportive ocular lubricants (artificial tears/ointment) and conservative measures (head elevation, dark glasses) inferred.
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — difficulty driving (seeing double, “two lines instead of one”), worsening diplopia with gaze and as day progresses; needs symptomatic measures (patching/prisms) and lifestyle adjustments.
Systemic Symptoms: Hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine (1 year prior); no headaches, facial numbness or pain, dysphagia, dysarthria, breathing difficulty, generalized muscle weakness, or extremity paresis or sensory symptoms; history of pernicious anemia.
Risk Factors: Smoking; history of thyroid disease (hyperthyroidism, radioactive iodine treatment); autoimmune thyroid disorder.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+1.0
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen visual acuity (distance)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 18 mmHg — Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: lid retraction, lid lag, mild bilateral ptosis, scleral show"""
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild conjunctival injection with mild conjunctival irritation/exposure changes consistent with punctate keratopathy and exposure (no hemorrhage or chemosis)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis, lid retraction, lid lag, scleral show, mild periorbital edema, bilateral extraocular motility restriction (limited elevation, depression, and abduction), mild punctate keratopathy from exposure
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild punctate keratopathy
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic cataract, mild


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm and equally reactive to light and near
Pupils – Reaction to Light: equal and reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: equally reactive to near
Pupils – RAPD: No relative afferent pupillary defect (negative)
Pupils – Other: 5mm and equally reactive to light and near
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of elevation and depression in both eyes; limitation of abduction in both eyes.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: mild punctate keratopathy, otherwise slit-lamp exam normal
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 72-year-old woman with history of hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine presenting with bilateral lid retraction and lag, proptosis, diplopia, limitation of elevation, depression, and abduction in both eyes, and mild punctate keratopathy from exposure. Findings of restrictive extraocular muscle dysfunction consistent with thyroid ophthalmopathy (Graves orbitopathy) with associated dry eye/exposure keratopathy. No optic neuropathy evident on exam. Recommend optimization of thyroid status, aggressive lubrication and ocular surface protection, smoking cessation, observation during active inflammatory phase with consideration of systemic corticosteroids or orbital radiation for severe inflammation or optic nerve compromise, prisms/patching or botulinum toxin for symptomatic diplopia, and defer strabismus, orbital decompression, and lid surgery until disease is inactive and stable ≥6–12 months. Follow with endocrinology and close ophthalmic monitoring for progression to compressive optic neuropathy or worsening exposure keratopathy.
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid orbitopathy
Secondary Diagnoses: Hyperthyroidism (treated with radioactive iodine); Dry eye (exposure keratopathy/punctate keratopathy); Lid retraction and lag; Diplopia from extraocular muscle restriction; Proptosis; Ptosis; Pernicious anemia
Differential Diagnosis: Thyroid orbitopathy (Graves’ orbitopathy); Myasthenia gravis (ocular myasthenia); Cranial nerve VI palsy (bilateral abducens palsy)
Severity: Mild to moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Exposure keratopathy; diplopia from restrictive extraocular muscle involvement; compressive optic neuropathy leading to visual loss; cosmetic disfigurement (proptosis, lid retraction); globe luxation.


Plan
All Medications: radioactive iodine methylcellulose artificial tears ointment (nighttime ocular lubricating ointment) systemic corticosteroids (oral or intravenous corticosteroids) neostigmine methyl-sulfate (Prostigmin) edrophonium hydrochloride (Tensilon) botulinum toxin orbital radiation therapy
All Procedures: methylcellulose artificial tears, ointment at night, head-of-bed elevation, eye patching, prisms, botulinum toxin injection into extraocular or levator muscles, systemic corticosteroids (oral or IV), orbital radiation therapy, orbital decompression surgery, strabismus surgery (extraocular muscle recessions/resections), eyelid procedures (lateral tarsorrhaphy, Müller's muscle and levator lengthening, lower eyelid elevation, blepharoplasty with orbital fat excision), surgical tarsorrhaphy
Follow-Up Instructions: Close ophthalmologic supervision with regular follow-up to monitor visual acuity, color vision, visual fields, and fundus for optic neuropathy and to assess for corneal epithelial breakdown; coordinate care with endocrinology and other specialists; long-term monitoring over months–years with staged interventions as needed.
Patient Education: Stop smoking; use methylcellulose/artificial tears by day and ointment at night; elevate head of bed and sleep partially supine; wear dark glasses with side protection; tape lids shut at night or use humidified goggles if severe exposure; report worsening vision, severe exposure keratopathy, marked proptosis, or signs of optic neuropathy promptly; expect prolonged course and need for multidisciplinary care (endocrinology and ophthalmology) with close follow-up.
Referrals: Endocrinology referral; ophthalmology (orbital/oculoplastic and strabismus) follow-up; neuro-ophthalmology as needed.
Emergency Instructions: Seek immediate medical attention if you develop rapidly worsening vision, new visual field loss, loss of color vision, severe eye pain, sudden increase in proptosis or inability to close the eyelids, or signs of corneal breakdown (marked redness, discharge, or severe irritation).


CASE 73 — Anisocoria—Tonic Pupil
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: one pupil larger than the other (anisocoria)
DURATION: 2 months
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "one pupil larger than the other"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 35-year-old woman with a 2-month history of constant but fluctuating mild–moderate eye irritation/foreign-body sensation and binocular vertical diplopia; diplopia is constant, she often patches one eye to read or drive; husband noted recent apparent eye bulging. She has prior hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year ago and is on thyroid supplements.
History Narrative: A 35-year-old woman with a 2-month history of constant, fluctuating mild-to-moderate eye discomfort described as irritation or foreign-body sensation, and binocular constant vertical diplopia. She often patched one eye to read or drive. Husband noted recent apparent eye “bulging.” Past medical history notable for hyperthyroidism treated with radioactive iodine 1 year earlier; she takes thyroid supplements. She denied other medical problems.
Symptom Onset: 2 months
Symptom Duration: 2 months
Progression: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil — typically initial unilateral mydriasis that may become smaller over time with possible bilateral involvement developing at ~4% per year (slow, often stable or slowly improving).
Characteristics: Poor pupillary light reaction; vermiform movements of the iris to light; segmental palsy of the sphincter; light–near dissociation; cholinergic supersensitivity of denervated muscles; accommodative paresis; induced astigmatism at near; tonicity of accommodation; occasional ciliary cramp with near work


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Reassurance; unilateral patching for diplopia when needed; unequal bifocal or unilateral frosted bifocal segment for near tasks.
Associated Symptoms: eye discomfort; diplopia; vertical diplopia; foreign body–type eye irritation; fluctuating eye pain; eye bulging (proptosis); lid retraction and lid lag; mild punctate keratopathy
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: She often patched one eye in order to read or drive.
Risk Factors: Idiopathic (no specific identifiable risk factors); possible association with prior viral or inflammatory injury to ciliary ganglion; female sex predominance (young adult women)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids with lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; tear film mildly decreased (mild punctate keratopathy) — lacrimal drainage/intact.
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis with Hertel 22 mm OD and 24 mm OS (base 95 mm); lid retraction and lid lag bilaterally; no ptosis.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Right pupil 6 mm in dark with poor segmental light reaction; left pupil 3 mm, reactive. Vermiform segmental movements of iris noted in the right pupil.
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses bilaterally


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 6 mm, left pupil 3 mm; right pupil dilated with poor/segmental light reaction (tonic pupil).
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Poor pupillary light reaction
Pupils – Reaction to Near: light-near dissociation
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: Right pupil 6 mm in dark, left pupil 3 mm; right poorly reactive and segmental with light‑near dissociation (tonic pupil).
Extraocular Movements: Limitation of elevation and depression in both eyes; limitation of abduction in both eyes (worse on the left); adduction relatively normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild punctate keratopathy bilaterally.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions; fundus periphery normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil.
Primary Diagnosis: Isolated, idiopathic tonic pupil
Differential Diagnosis: Isolated idiopathic tonic pupil (Adie’s pupil); Holmes–Adie syndrome; Oculomotor nerve palsy (partial) with sectoral iris sphincter involvement; Pharmacologic mydriasis
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: thyroid supplements
Follow-Up Instructions: No treatment except reassurance; consider unequal bifocal reading aids or unilateral frosted bifocal segment for permanent accommodative paresis; monitor for pupillary size change and possible bilateral involvement (≈4%/yr); assess for Holmes–Adie features if indicated.
Patient Education: Reassure that a tonic (Adie’s) pupil is usually benign and often requires no treatment. Explain findings: one pupil may be larger, react slowly or segmentally to light, but still constricts with near focus (light–near dissociation). Symptoms may include blurred near vision or difficulty with reading; unequal pupils can become less pronounced over time. Recommend measures for comfort and function: consider unequal bifocals or a unilateral frosted bifocal segment for persistent accommodative difficulty; use reading glasses as needed. Advise return if new symptoms occur (worsening vision, pain, headache, or other neurologic signs). Discuss that rare progression to bilateral involvement can occur and that evaluation for related conditions (e.g., Holmes–Adie features like diminished reflexes) may be done if clinically indicated.


CASE 74 — Anisocoria—Horner’s Syndrome
======================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches and drooping of left upper eyelid
DURATION: 2 weeks
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "it could be due to a brain tumor."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 45-year-old woman noted anisocoria 2 weeks earlier when a friend observed one pupil larger than the other; she reports occasional headaches over several years but no other illnesses, no significant head or eye trauma, no use of eye drops, and no visual symptoms.
History Narrative: A 45-year-old woman presented for evaluation of anisocoria noted 2 weeks earlier when a friend observed one pupil larger than the other. She reported occasional headaches over several years but denied other illnesses, significant head or eye trauma, use of any eye drops, or visual symptoms. She had seen a neurosurgeon who obtained a brain MRI that was normal; the neurosurgeon suggested she might need a cerebral angiogram. On exam, visual acuity was 20/20 OU, color vision and visual fields were normal. The right pupil measured 6 mm in dark and the left 3 mm; the right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light while the left reacted briskly and symmetrically. The right pupil constricted slowly to near and slowly redilated on distance fixation; the left constricted briskly to near and redilated quickly. Extraocular motility was normal, there was no ptosis. Slit-lamp exam showed vermiform, sectoral movements of the right iris; fundus exam was normal.
Symptom Onset: 3 weeks
Symptom Duration: 3 weeks
Progression: Stable after treatment with eventual maintenance on aspirin
Characteristics: Anisocoria greater in the dark with ipsilateral miosis and 2 mm ptosis (Horner syndrome); pharmacologic testing: poor dilation with cocaine 10%, poor dilation with hydroxyamphetamine 1% (postganglionic Horner’s), associated ipsilateral headache/frontotemporal and periorbital pain.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: left frontotemporal and periorbital headaches; drooping of the left upper eyelid (ptosis)
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: Coumadin for 3 months, then maintenance aspirin
Impact on Daily Activities: None—no impairment of daily activities noted
Systemic Symptoms: Denies systemic symptoms other than occasional headaches and history of hypertension; no other illnesses, trauma, or visual symptoms.
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Hypertension; spontaneous internal carotid artery dissection (vascular disease)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/80 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+ (Jaeger 1+)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 14 mmHg — Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Left ptosis 2 mm
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, no injection
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external exam of the orbits without proptosis or globe displacement.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea; no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: vermiform, segmental iris movements; no iris injury
Anterior Segment – Angle: open and normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 6 mm (poor/segmental light reaction); Left pupil 3 mm (brisk reaction)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil reacted poorly and segmentally to light; left pupil reacted briskly and symmetrically to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Right pupil: slowly constricted to near and then slowly redilated on looking in the distance; Left pupil: constricted briskly to near and quickly redilated at distance
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: vermiform, segmental movements of the right iris
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions; motility normal.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Vermiform, segmental movements of one sector of the right iris; no iris injury.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Postganglionic Horner’s syndrome of the left eye due to spontaneous dissection of the internal carotid artery.
Primary Diagnosis: Postganglionic Horner’s syndrome due to spontaneous dissection of the internal carotid artery
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypertension
Differential Diagnosis: Tonic (Adie) pupil; Pharmacologic mydriasis; Oculomotor (III) nerve palsy; Postganglionic Horner syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Medications: cocaine 10%; hydroxyamphetamine 1%; Coumadin (warfarin); aspirin; pilocarpine 0.125%
All Procedures: Topical cocaine 10% instillation in both eyes Topical hydroxyamphetamine 1% instillation in both eyes Slit-lamp biomicroscopy Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of the brain Oral anticoagulation with warfarin (Coumadin) for 3 months Maintenance antiplatelet therapy with aspirin
Follow-Up Instructions: Close observation with periodic reassessment to monitor for development of other neurologic or medical signs or symptoms.
Patient Education: Horner’s syndrome itself does not usually require treatment, but it signals an underlying cause that must be identified and treated. The key points for you to know: - Cause: Horner’s results from disruption of the sympathetic pathway to the eye (ptosis, small pupil, sometimes decreased sweating). In this case it was due to a spontaneous internal carotid artery dissection. - Symptoms to watch for: new or worsening headache, neck pain, vision changes, weakness, numbness, speech changes, or new droopiness—report these immediately. - Testing and treatment: Imaging and vascular evaluation are necessary; treatment targets the underlying cause (for carotid dissection this commonly includes anticoagulation or antiplatelet therapy as recommended by your physician). The eye findings themselves often do not require ocular surgery. - Prognosis and follow-up: Some nerve function may recover over months; close follow-up with neurology/vascular medicine and ophthalmology is recommended to monitor for complications or recovery. - Practical tips: Use sunglasses for light sensitivity if present; consider ptosis crutch or eyelid taping only if vision is obstructed.


CASE 75 — Anisocoria—Eye Drops
==============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Long-standing headaches, blurred vision, and anisocoria
DURATION: long-standing
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I've had headaches and blurred vision and my pupils look different."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 24-year-old student nurse with long-standing headaches, blurred vision, and anisocoria. Pupils 8 mm OD and 4 mm OS; right pupil nonreactive to light or near, left pupil reacts briskly. Right pupil did not constrict to dilute pilocarpine, consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis. No other neurologic symptoms reported in initial presentation.
History Narrative: A 24-year-old student nurse with long-standing headaches, blurred vision, and anisocoria. Pupils measured 8 mm on the right and 4 mm on the left; the right pupil did not react to light or near, while the left pupil reacted briskly to light and near. The right pupil did not constrict to dilute pilocarpine drops, consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis. No other neurologic symptoms reported in this account.
Symptom Onset: Long-standing (chronic) — patient noted symptoms prior to presentation (duration not precisely specified)
Symptom Duration: long-standing
Progression: Chronic/long-standing
Characteristics: Pharmacologic mydriasis causing a dilated, nonreactive right pupil (8 mm) with anisocoria and associated headaches/blurred vision.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Reassurance and discussion of findings (no treatment required)
Associated Symptoms: Headaches, blurred vision, ptosis (drooping of upper eyelid)
Impact on Daily Activities: None — no functional limitation; activities of daily living not affected.
Systemic Symptoms: Headaches, blurred vision


Objective Exam

Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right upper eyelid ptosis 2 mm; mild left ptosis noted in other case descriptions — here prominent right eyelid ptosis with mild eyelid droop.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Clear, quiet conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or hemorrhage)
External Exam – Orbit: Mild left ptosis; mild “upside-down” ptosis; otherwise external orbit exam unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, unremarkable cornea
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Pharmacologic mydriasis with a nonreactive (8 mm) right pupil; left pupil reactive, no iris abnormalities noted.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Right pupil 8 mm, fixed dilated; left pupil 4 mm (briskly reactive)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right pupil: no reaction to light; Left pupil: brisk reaction to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Right pupil: no reaction to near; Left pupil: reacted briskly to near
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Right 8 mm, left 4 mm; right pupil nonreactive to light or near (pharmacologic mydriasis)
Extraocular Movements: Full motility in all directions of gaze; no extraocular movement restriction.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: unremarkable
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 24-year-old female with long‑standing anisocoria, headaches, and blurred vision. Examination showed a pharmacologically dilated right pupil (8 mm) that was nonreactive to light and near and did not constrict to dilute pilocarpine, consistent with pharmacologic mydriasis. No treatment required; patient counseled about causes and reassured with recommendation for follow‑up to ensure resolution.
Primary Diagnosis: Pharmacologic mydriasis
Secondary Diagnoses: Pharmacologic mydriasis
Differential Diagnosis: Pharmacologic mydriasis; Adie (tonic) pupil; Third‑order (postganglionic) Horner syndrome
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: pilocarpine (dilute), cocaine 10% (topical), hydroxyamphetamine 1% (Paredrine), apraclonidine 0.5–1%
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up to ensure resolution of the symptoms is reasonable.
Patient Education: Reassure patient that no treatment is required for pharmacologic mydriasis, explain that the dilated pupil is caused by topical medication exposure (eye drops) and typically resolves as the drug wears off, advise to avoid further exposure to unknown eye or facial products and to protect the affected eye from glare/light (sunglasses) and report any new symptoms (increasing pain, vision loss, drooping, double vision, or persistent dilation beyond expected duration). Recommend follow-up with eye care provider to confirm resolution and seek urgent care if neurologic symptoms (severe headache, weakness, numbness) develop.


CASE 76 — Leukocoria
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Leukocoria
DURATION: 6 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 3-year-old boy referred for persistent right-eye poor vision and esotropia diagnosed at age 2; treated with sporadic full‑time patching of the left eye for ~6 months without improvement. Parents noted leukocoria of the left eye on examination. Current symptoms include 20/200 vision in the right eye and 20/30 in the left, 25Δ esotropia at distance and near, a 1+ right relative afferent pupillary defect, small crowded optic discs with double‑ring sign (more pronounced on the right). Past medical history notable only for weight and height at the 15th percentile. No family history of strabismus or amblyopia.
History Narrative: A 3-year-old boy is referred by another ophthalmologist. The child was diagnosed with esotropia and amblyopia in the right eye at age 2 years. The referring ophthalmologist prescribed full-time patching of the left eye, which was done sporadically for approximately 6 months. No improvement in the child’s vision was noted, prompting referral for a second opinion and treatment recommendations. The child’s medical history is notable for a weight and height in the 15th percentile, but is otherwise unremarkable. The review of systems is completely negative with the exception of the ophthalmologic complaints and there is no family history of strabismus or amblyopia. The patient is shown in ▶Fig. 85.1. Best-corrected visual acuity is 20/200 in the right eye and 20/30 in the left eye. Motility evaluation reveals an esotropia of 25 prism diopters at distance and at near with full ductions and versions. Pupillary testing reveals a 1 + relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in the right eye. Cycloplegic refraction is +1.25 diopters in both eyes and initial evaluation of the posterior pole reveals a normal disc, macula, and vessels. The ophthalmologist is concerned by the presence of the RAPD and performs a more careful evaluation of the posterior pole. The disc of the right eye appears slightly pale. The vessels on the nerve are crowded. The disc diameter is small, and a double-ring sign is noted (▶Fig. 85.2a, b). The left disc is also slightly smaller than normal.
Symptom Onset: Age 2 years (diagnosed with esotropia and amblyopia; symptoms noted then)
Symptom Duration: Approximately 1 year (esotropia/amblyopia noted at age 2; now 3 years)
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Leukocoria; bilateral retinoblastoma (left > right); 3-year-old boy; esotropia and amblyopia history; 25Δ esotropia; right RAPD 1+; small crowded optic discs with double-ring sign (optic nerve hypoplasia features); vitreous seeding and exudative retinal detachment in affected eye; hereditary potential (recommend RB1 testing).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Leukocoria; esotropia; decreased vision in right eye (20/200) and left eye (20/30); relative afferent pupillary defect (right); small/crowded optic discs with double-ring sign; pale right optic disc; growth retardation (height and weight 15th percentile)
Prior Treatments: Full-time patching of the left eye (sporadically for ~6 months)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — impaired depth perception and vision in the right eye (20/200) and leukocoria/poor vision in the left eye with likely treatment-related monocularity will limit activities requiring binocular vision; requires protective measures and rehabilitation.
Systemic Symptoms: Unremarkable except for low height and weight (15th percentile); review of systems otherwise negative.
Risk Factors: Hereditary RB1 germline mutation; family history of retinoblastoma (if present); young age (early childhood); bilateral disease; prior radiation exposure (general known risk); increased risk of secondary malignancies (soft‑tissue and bony sarcomas)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 92 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5 (right), N5 (left)
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (best-corrected visual acuity)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: 20 mmHg – applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal-looking conjunctiva and sclera (no injection or hemorrhage noted)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Left enophthalmos with leukocoria and esotropia; evidence of left orbital involvement by retinoblastoma (left > right) with enucleation recommended for the left eye.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, formed anterior chamber
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear crystalline lens bilaterally (no cataract)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD); pupils otherwise normal in size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) in the right eye
Pupils – RAPD: 1+ RAPD OD
Pupils – Other: 1+ relative afferent pupillary defect in the right eye
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: right optic nerve appears slightly pale with crowded vessels, small disc diameter and double‑ring sign (optic nerve hypoplasia)
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: crowded
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: large retinoblastoma with vitreous seeding and exudative retinal detachment (left > right); peripheral tumor involvement noted.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: This 3-year-old boy with a history of presumed amblyopia and esotropia presents with unilateral visual loss (OD 20/200, OS 20/30), a 1+ right RAPD, small crowded pale right optic disc with double-ring sign, and bilateral retinoblastoma on imaging (left > right) with vitreous seeding and exudative retinal detachment. MRI demonstrates bilateral optic nerve involvement concern and midline evaluation for associated CNS/endocrine abnormalities; endocrine testing revealed marked growth hormone deficiency. Diagnosis: bilateral retinoblastoma (left greater than right). Plan: urgent multidisciplinary management with enucleation of the more advanced left eye with long optic nerve segment, and chemotherapy ± focal therapies (laser/cryotherapy, plaque, intra-arterial/intravitreal as indicated) for the right eye, genetic testing for RB1 mutation, frequent examinations under anesthesia every 3–4 months until age 5 if germline mutation present, long-term surveillance for secondary malignancies, and endocrinology follow-up for pituitary hormone deficiencies.
Primary Diagnosis: Bilateral retinoblastoma
Secondary Diagnoses: Esotropia; Amblyopia (right eye); Optic nerve hypoplasia; Growth hormone deficiency; Septo‑optic dysplasia; Small optic discs/bilateral optic nerve hypoplasia findings
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Coats’ disease; Persistent fetal vasculature (persistent hyperplastic primary vitreous); Optic nerve hypoplasia
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Increased risk of secondary malignancies (particularly soft-tissue and bony sarcomas), orbital recurrence after enucleation, optic nerve invasion with potential spread, vision loss/monocular status, endocrine abnormalities related to septo‑optic dysplasia (e.g., growth hormone and corticotrophin deficiency)


Plan
All Medications: steroids
All Procedures: exam under anesthesia; magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of brain and orbits; genetic testing for RB1; chemotherapy (intravenous, intra-arterial, periocular, intravitreal); laser therapy; cryotherapy; radioactive plaque therapy; enucleation; socket examinations (post-enucleation)
Follow-Up Instructions: Following local control of the right eye, examinations under anesthesia every 3–4 months until age 5 if RB1 germline mutation present; frequent examinations for several more years with annual eye exam thereafter; examine enucleated socket at each contralateral eye visit; long-term surveillance for secondary malignancies; protect remaining eye with polycarbonate safety glasses and provide eye‑safety education.
Patient Education: Protect the remaining (seeing) eye: wear polycarbonate safety glasses for all activities. Understand retinoblastoma treatment and follow-up: chemotherapy and focal treatments (laser/cryotherapy) or enucleation may be needed; genetic testing (RB1) guides prognosis. Attend frequent examinations under anesthesia (every 3–4 months until age 5 if germline RB1 positive, then regular long-term follow-up) to monitor for tumor recurrence. Be aware of increased lifetime risk of secondary malignancies (soft-tissue and bone sarcomas) with hereditary disease and maintain long-term surveillance. Seek prompt care for any new eye symptoms and coordinate care with oncology/genetics and, if indicated, endocrinology.
Referrals: Endocrinology consultation (endocrinologist) and referral to pediatrician for endocrine testing; Ophthalmology surgical oncology/retinoblastoma specialist (for EUA, imaging, chemotherapy/enucleation)


CASE 77 — The Child Who Sees Poorly Out of One Eye
==================================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left eye looks “funny”/white pupil (leukocoria) noticed for 3 months.
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "funny"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: The family of a 9‑month‑old girl noted that her left eye had looked “funny” for the last 3 months; pediatrician detected leukocoria and referred her to an ophthalmologist. The child is otherwise healthy, born 2 weeks prematurely (birth weight 6 lb 8 oz), with no systemic disease, animal exposure, or family history of childhood ocular disease. On exam she fixes and follows with the right eye but not the left; right posterior pole visible only; left eye has a white vascular lesion with large serous retinal detachment. Strong objection to occlusion of the right eye.
History Narrative: The family of a 9-month-old girl noted that her left eye has looked “funny” for the last 3 months. The girl’s pediatrician detected leukocoria and immediately referred her to an ophthalmologist for evaluation and treatment. The child is an otherwise healthy 9-month-old with a negative review of systems with the exception of the white pupil. She was born 2 weeks prematurely and had a birth weight of 6 lb., 8 oz. There is no history of systemic disease or exposure to animals, and there is no family history of childhood ocular disease. Examination reveals a normal right eye, though only the posterior pole can be seen on retinal examination. In the left eye, a white, vascular lesion is obvious with a surrounding large serous retinal detachment. The child fixes and follows well with her right eye, but does not fix or follow with her left eye. Strong objection to occlusion of the right eye is noted.
Symptom Onset: 3 months prior (onset at ~6 months of age)
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Characteristics: Unilateral leukocoria with a white vascular intraocular mass and large serous retinal detachment in the left eye; normal-appearing right eye with a small inferior retinal tumor; child fixes/follows with right eye only; onset noted at ~6 months (3 months prior to presentation).


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: white pupil (leukocoria), poor fixation/following of left eye, strong objection to occlusion of right eye
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — the child does not fix or follow with the left eye, strongly objects to occlusion of the right eye, and has vision-dependent functional impairment (reliant on right eye), risking amblyopia without treatment.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative review of systems except for the white pupil
Risk Factors: Uncorrected hyperopia (anisometropia), strabismus, obstruction of visual axis (media opacity), prematurity (born 2 weeks early)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fixes and follows (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: No fixation (count fingers or worse)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: LP (light perception)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation and following (behavioral assessment)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen (applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Right eye: fixes and follows well (normal direct/consensual) — reactive; Left eye: does not fix or follow — likely unreactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: None
Pupils – Other: leukocoria
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact in both eyes
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: large serous retinal detachment in left eye; right eye peripheral retina shows a small tumor in inferior retina.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Anisometropic amblyopia of the right eye secondary to uncorrected hyperopia. Child is 9 months old with leukocoria in the left eye found to have a large vascular intraocular tumor with serous retinal detachment consistent with retinoblastoma in the left eye; small tumor present in the inferior retina of the right eye. Right eye fixes and follows; left eye does not. Plan: urgent tumor workup and management for presumed left retinoblastoma (MRI of brain and orbits to assess extraocular/CNS spread, ultrasound B-/A-scan), genetic counseling and RB1 testing, evaluation of siblings/parents. For amblyopia management: prescribe full cycloplegic refraction (or symmetrically reduced hyperopic correction) and begin spectacle correction; consider atropine penalization if poor adaptation to spectacles; initiate occlusion therapy of the sound eye as indicated (start with 2 hours/day for mild–moderate amblyopia) and close follow-up with repeated exams under anesthesia as needed. Monitor for treatment response and adjust refractive correction and patching; consider surgical options only if media opacity or refractory anisometropia after conservative therapy.
Primary Diagnosis: Anisometropic amblyopia in the right eye due to uncorrected hyperopia
Secondary Diagnoses: Anisometropic amblyopia (right eye) due to uncorrected hyperopia; Hyperopia (refractive error)
Differential Diagnosis: Retinoblastoma; Coats’ disease; Persistent fetal vasculature (PFV); Toxocariasis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right eye


Plan
All Medications: atropine eye drops (topical atropine)
All Procedures: occlusion therapy (eye patching) spectacle correction (cycloplegic refraction with hyperopic correction) atropine penalization (atropine eye drops for cycloplegia/optical penalization) magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of head and orbits B-scan and A-scan ultrasonography examination of eyes under anesthesia ( EUA ) enucleation (pathologic examination of enucleated eye ) genetic testing/counseling (RB1 testing) bone marrow and cerebrospinal fluid evaluation (select cases) strabismus surgery (if indicated after amblyopia treatment) refractive surgery options (PRK, LASIK, clear lens extraction)
Follow-Up Instructions: Ophthalmologic follow-up examinations as needed after amblyopia treatment, with regular monitoring of refractive error and visual acuity (maintenance patching may be needed until age 7–8 years to prevent recurrence)
Patient Education: Explain leukocoria is a serious sign needing urgent evaluation; retinoblastoma is a possible cause and requires prompt imaging (MRI of brain/orbits) and specialist care. For amblyopia: correct refractive error with prescribed glasses (full cycloplegic or symmetric reduced hyperopic correction), consider atropine drops to aid adaptation, and treat with occlusion (patching) of the sound eye (often 2 hours/day for mild–moderate cases) or optical penalization if needed. Emphasize close follow-up to monitor vision, refractive changes, and adherence; genetic testing/counseling is recommended if retinoblastoma is diagnosed; seek immediate care for any new white pupil, eye misalignment, or loss of fixation.
Referrals: Immediate referral to an ophthalmologist (urgent) and genetic counseling/testing for retinoblastoma (RB1)


CASE 78 — Childhood Torticollis
===============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Failed preschool vision screening test
DURATION: 4 years
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A robust 4-year-old boy who failed his preschool vision screening; failure was confirmed by his pediatrician and he was referred for ophthalmologic evaluation. No prior ophthalmologic problems, no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other eye complaints. No family history of amblyopia or childhood eye disease. Vision testing by technician showed 20/40 OU with Allen figures; stereoacuity 60″ on Titmus fly. Motility: orthotropia at distance, 4 PD exophoria at near, full ductions/versions. Cycloplegic refraction (1% cyclopentolate): +3.00 OD, +1.00 OS. Ophthalmologist HOTV acuity: 20/50 OD, 20/30 OS.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy failed his preschool vision screening test. The failure was confirmed by his pediatrician and he was referred for ophthalmologic evaluation. He is a robust 4-year-old with no prior medical or ophthalmologic problems. Review of systems is negative; there is no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other ophthalmologic issues. He has not had a previous ophthalmologic examination and there is no family history of amblyopia or childhood eye disease.
Symptom Duration: Chronic
Characteristics: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: failed preschool vision screening test; no history of eye injury, strabismus, spectacle wear, squinting, or other ophthalmologic problems; review of systems negative; chronic left head tilt; mild facial asymmetry; possible decreased vision in right eye (amblyopia)
Prior Treatments: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — minimal impact; child is asymptomatic or minimally symptomatic and able to perform daily activities with compensatory head tilt, though may have reduced fine visual tasks with the right eye (20/50) and possible stereo limitations.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/72 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 96 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Method: HOTV acuity test
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal appearing lids and lashes
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external examination of the orbits (no proptosis, enophthalmos, or palpable masses)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, normal appearing corneas (no opacities)
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear, no cataract/NORMAL


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions (orthotropia at distance; 4Δ exophoria at near)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry.
Primary Diagnosis: Right superior oblique palsy with chronic left head tilt and mild facial asymmetry
Secondary Diagnoses: Childhood torticollis; Right superior oblique palsy; Anisometropic hyperopia (right +3.00, left +1.00); Amblyopia of the right eye; Exophoria 4Δ at near; Mild facial asymmetry; Hyperopia.
Differential Diagnosis: Right superior oblique palsy; Amblyopia due to anisometropia/refractive error; Deprivational amblyopia from media opacity (less likely)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Right
Complications: Over- or under-correction after surgery; postoperative Brown’s syndrome (limited elevation in adduction); development or persistence of amblyopia; potential need for additional surgeries; persistent diplopia or anomalous head posture.


Plan
All Medications: 1% cyclopentolate
All Procedures: vision screening cycloplegic refraction (cycloplegia with 1% cyclopentolate) HOTV acuity testing Allen figure testing Titmus fly stereo testing motility evaluation (ductions/versions) vertical/horizontal prism dissociation (fixation preference testing) vertical prism correction inferior oblique myectomy inferior oblique recession superior oblique tuck strabismus surgery (rectus muscle surgery as adjunct) occlusion (patching) for amblyopia
Follow-Up Instructions: Parents should be advised at the time of surgery of the potential for over‑ or under‑correction and need for additional surgery in the future; children should undergo periodic ophthalmologic evaluations with attention to fusion, stereopsis, and visual acuity testing to detect, prevent, and treat amblyopia.
Patient Education: Explain torticollis and its causes (muscular vs ocular), focusing on superior oblique palsy: why the head tilt occurs and how it helps vision. Describe the proposed management plan: glasses for refractive error, observation for mild/symptomatic cases, possible vertical prism if small hypertropia and symptomatic, and surgery (inferior oblique weakening or superior oblique tuck) if needed to improve head posture, fusion, or diplopia. Explain risks/benefits of surgery, including possible need for additional procedures and temporary or persistent limitations in eye movement (Brown’s syndrome). Emphasize monitoring for amblyopia in young children: need for regular visual acuity and stereo/fusion checks, and prompt treatment (patching/optical correction) if amblyopia develops. Advise parents on signs to watch for (worsening head tilt, double vision, decreased vision in one eye, eye-turn, facial asymmetry) and to return for follow-up if noted. Provide practical guidance: ensure child wears prescribed glasses consistently, encourage compliance with any patching, and schedule regular ophthalmology visits.


CASE 79 — Aniridia
==================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Anomalous left head tilt since infancy with intermittent eye misalignment and frequent eye rubbing/blinking
DURATION: 5 years
LATERALITY: bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I don't like it when my head is straight."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 5-year-old boy brought for evaluation of a longstanding anomalous left head tilt present since infancy (since he first gained head control in the first year of life). Parents report he strongly resists attempts to straighten his head. Over the past 2 months they noted his eyes sometimes do not move together. He has frequent eye rubbing and blinking. No other systemic symptoms; family history unremarkable.
History Narrative: A 5-year-old boy brought for evaluation of a long-standing anomalous left head tilt present since infancy. No neck muscle abnormality on orthopedic exam. Parents report he resists straightening his head and, over the past 2 months, occasional discordant eye movements. Family history unremarkable. Review of systems notable only for frequent eye rubbing and blinking. He has had the left head tilt since he first gained head control in the first year of life.
Symptom Onset: Since infancy (noted since he first gained head control during the first year of life)
Symptom Duration: Since infancy (about 4–5 years)
Progression: Progressive—risk of progressive corneal opacification, cataracts, and glaucoma leading to future vision loss
Characteristics: Sporadic aniridia with foveal hypoplasia, photophobia, congenital nystagmus, alternate fixation, predisposition to progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma; associated with WAGR syndrome features (developmental delay, genitourinary abnormalities) and 11p13 deletion; high risk for Wilms tumor.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Bright light / photophobia
Alleviating Factors: Occlusion (covering one eye) is tolerated and improves fixation; sunglasses or shaded contact lenses relieve photophobia.
Associated Symptoms: photophobia, frequent eye rubbing and blinking, congenital nystagmus, anomalous head tilt (torticollis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — photophobia, visual impairment from foveal hypoplasia and risk of progressive corneal opacification/cataract/glaucoma can impair vision-dependent activities; requires sunglasses/contact lenses, low-vision services, and educational/rehabilitation support.
Systemic Symptoms: frequent eye rubbing and blinking behavior; developmental delay; genitourinary abnormalities (renal mass/Wilms tumor risk)
Risk Factors: WAGR syndrome (11p13 deletion) with high risk for Wilms tumor; family history unremarkable; congenital aniridia associated with foveal hypoplasia, photophobia, risk of progressive cataracts, corneal opacification/vascularization, and glaucoma.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 92 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow (no formal Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Photophobia; conjunctiva/sclera notable for corneal opacification/vessel advancement risk (early exam shows no current conjunctival/scleral abnormality)
External Exam – Orbit: Normal external orbital structures; no proptosis or enophthalmos noted.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Absent iris (aniridia)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: normal
Extraocular Movements: Extraocular movements: full but with moderate overelevation of the right eye in adduction (suggesting right superior oblique palsy)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and pupillary examination are normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Foveal hypoplasia
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Sporadic aniridia with associated developmental delay and genitourinary abnormalities consistent with WAGR syndrome; high risk for Wilms tumor due to 11p13 deletion. Vision currently reasonable but at risk for progressive corneal opacification/vascularization, cataract, and glaucoma. Recommend regular ophthalmic surveillance including glaucoma screening, low-vision evaluation and early educational intervention; serial abdominal ultrasounds until ~3 years of age with MRI/oncology referral if renal abnormality detected.
Primary Diagnosis: Aniridia
Secondary Diagnoses: Aniridia; foveal hypoplasia; congenital nystagmus; photophobia; infantile torticollis due to congenital right superior oblique palsy; WAGR syndrome (sporadic aniridia with developmental delay and genitourinary abnormalities) with Wilms tumor risk
Differential Diagnosis: Superior oblique palsy; Brown’s syndrome; Dissociated vertical deviation; Congenital fibrosis syndrome
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Progressive corneal opacification and vascularization; progressive cataracts; glaucoma; foveal hypoplasia leading to visual loss; increased risk of Wilms’ tumor (renal malignancy)


Plan
All Procedures: comprehensive ocular motility evaluation (three-step test); cycloplegic refraction; fundus examination; abdominal ultrasound screening; magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) of abdomen/orbit/brain if indicated; biopsy of renal mass if imaging consistent with Wilms’ tumor; referral to pediatric oncologist; prescription of sunglasses or shaded contact lenses; periodic ophthalmological screening examinations; low-vision evaluation; early childhood educational intervention
Follow-Up Instructions: Periodic ophthalmological screening examinations to monitor for progressive corneal opacification, cataract, and glaucoma; abdominal ultrasounds at regular intervals until ~3 years of age for Wilms tumor surveillance (with MRI and biopsy if abnormalities found); early childhood educational intervention and low-vision evaluation.
Patient Education: Discuss aniridia including cause, risk of associated conditions (WAGR syndrome, Wilms’ tumor), and potential eye complications (photophobia, corneal opacification/vascularization, cataract, glaucoma, foveal hypoplasia leading to reduced vision and nystagmus). Advise protective measures (sunglasses or shaded contact lenses) for light sensitivity. Explain need for regular ophthalmology follow-up for glaucoma screening and monitoring of cornea and lens, and that surgery is high-risk and reserved for refractory problems. Recommend periodic abdominal ultrasounds until ~3 years of age to screen for Wilms’ tumor (with MRI/biopsy and oncology referral if abnormalities found). Emphasize early low-vision evaluation and educational/intervention services to support visual development.
Referrals: Pediatric oncology (if Wilms’ tumor confirmed) and referral for biopsy/pediatric oncologist consultation; periodic ophthalmology follow-up; low-vision evaluation and early childhood educational intervention; abdominal ultrasound surveillance with pediatric radiology (and MRI if ultrasound abnormality)


CASE 80 — Retinopathy of Prematurity
====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: “jiggling eyes” and large pupils noted during the first month of life; decreased vision/does not see as well as siblings
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "jiggling eyes" "closes his eyes in bright sunlight" "does not appear to see as well as his two older siblings"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 9-month-old boy referred for “jiggling eyes” and large pupils noted during first month of life; parents report photophobia (closes eyes in bright sunlight) and apparent reduced vision compared with siblings; born at term with prenatal care and no perinatal complications; medical history notable for hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and developmental delay.
History Narrative: A 9-month-old boy was referred for eye evaluation because of “jiggling eyes” and large pupils first noted during the first month of life. He closes his eyes in bright sunlight and appears to have poorer vision compared with his two older siblings. He was born at term with prenatal care and no perinatal complications. Medical history includes hypospadias repair 2 months earlier and developmental delay. There is no family history of ophthalmologic or medical problems; his parents and two older siblings have normal eye exams. On exam he fixates and follows but with abnormal fixation and a low-amplitude, high-frequency horizontal pendular nystagmus. There is peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization, a markedly hypoplastic iris with only a small peripheral remnant, and bilateral anterior pyramidal cataracts.
