Retinal exam shows absence of an obvious umbo or foveal pigmentation. Genetic testing demonstrated a deletion of 11p13.
Symptom Onset: first month of life
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Type I, both eyes
Characteristics: Type I, both eyes


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Prematurity, low birth weight, neonatal oxygen therapy, systemic illness/instability
Associated Symptoms: jiggling eyes; photophobia (closes eyes in bright sunlight); decreased visual behavior compared with siblings; nystagmus; abnormal fixation and following
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant visual impairment with nystagmus, foveal hypoplasia, large pupils and bilateral cataracts likely limits visual function (reduced acuity and photosensitivity) affecting daily activities, but child can fix and follow.
Systemic Symptoms: developmental delay; history of hypospadias (congenital genitourinary abnormality)
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Prematurity (low gestational age/low birthweight), supplemental oxygen therapy, neonatal intensive care interventions, systemic illness/instability in the neonate (eg, sepsis, anemia, blood transfusion), respiratory distress/ventilation, and multiple births (plus low socioeconomic/limited prenatal care as contributing factors).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 110 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 30 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: Fixes and follows (central, steady, maintained) — approximately 20/200 (uncorrected)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/200
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fix and follow (central, steady, unmaintained)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow testing
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry (Tono-Pen) under topical anesthesia


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization
External Exam – Orbit: Normocephalic, no proptosis or enophthalmos evident
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Peripheral corneal opacification and vascularization
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Severely hypoplastic iris with only a very small peripheral remnant (near‑aniridia)
Anterior Segment – Angle: Narrow/closed (synechial/obliterated)
Anterior Segment – Lens: Anterior pyramidal cataract in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: large pupils
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Sluggish
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: large pupils
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions of gaze without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: peripheral corneal opacification with vascularization, severely hypoplastic (nearly absent) iris with only a small peripheral remnant, and bilateral anterior pyramidal cataracts
Fundus Exam – Macula: Absent foveal pigmentation / no obvious umbo
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal caliber and course, no neovascularization noted
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina: avascular with neovascularization (stage 3 ROP changes)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Type I retinopathy of prematurity in both eyes (zone/stage meeting treatment threshold), severe ROP requiring treatment; plan for prompt therapy with intravitreal anti‑VEGF (bevacizumab) or laser photocoagulation, frequent follow‑up until disease regression and continued screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age for possible late reactivation; counsel regarding risk of amblyopia, strabismus, and refractive errors; coordinate care with neonatology and arrange ophthalmic follow‑up and genetic/pediatric evaluation as indicated.
Primary Diagnosis: Retinopathy of prematurity (Type I, both eyes)
Secondary Diagnoses: Retinopathy of prematurity; developmental delay; hypospadias (post-repair); nystagmus; anterior pyramidal cataracts; corneal opacification/vascularization; iris hypoplasia/aniridia; foveal hypoplasia; risk of Wilms’ tumor (11p13 deletion)
Differential Diagnosis: Aniridia (congenital PAX6-related); WAGR syndrome-associated ocular anomalies; Persistent fetal vasculature with anterior segment dysgenesis
Severity: Type I
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: retinal detachment; posterior retinal fold; cataract; subretinal hemorrhage; intraretinal hemorrhage; vitreous hemorrhage; severe myopia; amblyopia; strabismus; severe refractive error (myopia, astigmatism, anisometropia)


Plan
All Medications: bevacizumab
All Procedures: intravitreal injection of bevacizumab laser photocoagulation (peripheral retinal photocoagulation) cryotherapy
Follow-Up Instructions: Frequent ophthalmic examinations until disease regression; continuous screening up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age for those treated with bevacizumab; ongoing monitoring for amblyopia, strabismus, and refractive errors.
Patient Education: Explain retinopathy of prematurity (ROP) in simple terms: premature birth can cause abnormal blood vessel growth in the retina that can threaten vision. Describe the diagnosis and severity (Type I, both eyes) and that treatment options include laser photocoagulation or intravitreal anti-VEGF injections (bevacizumab), which aim to stop abnormal vessels and preserve vision. Emphasize the need for frequent follow-up exams until the disease has fully regressed and longer monitoring (up to 60–70 weeks postmenstrual age) after anti-VEGF due to possible late reactivation. Review possible complications and long‑term risks: retinal detachment, cataract, hemorrhage, and higher rates of severe myopia after laser; unknown long‑term systemic/ocular effects after anti‑VEGF. Advise monitoring for other vision problems common in preterm children (amblyopia, strabismus, refractive errors) and need for regular ophthalmic care, glasses, patching or surgery if indicated. Recommend coordination with the pediatrician for overall health and developmental follow‑up; if ROP is sporadic, discuss genetic counseling only if other concerns arise. Provide clear emergency signs to return immediately: sudden redness, discharge, swelling, acute changes in eye appearance or eye movements, or a sudden decline in visual behavior.
Referrals: Geneticist and pediatrician


CASE 81 — Childhood Esotropia
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Recurrent esotropia after prior strabismus surgery with left-eye amblyopia and dissociated vertical deviation in an infant with history of infantile esotropia.
DURATION: 11 weeks
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 11-week-old former 24-week premature female presented for ROP screening; last exam 1 week prior showed immature retina in zone I without plus disease. Since that exam she developed sepsis and respiratory compromise requiring IV antibiotics and re‑intubation/ventilatory support; currently intubated on 50% oxygen. Birth weight 650 g; history notable for anemia of prematurity and necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks prior. She blinks briskly to light; current fundus exam shows dilation and tortuosity of posterior pole vessels (plus disease) in zone I.
History Narrative: An 11-week‑old former 24‑week estimated gestational age premature girl, birth weight 650 g, presented for ROP screening. She is a frail-appearing infant who is intubated and receiving 50% oxygen. Since her last exam 1 week prior (which showed immature retina in zone I without plus disease), she developed sepsis and respiratory compromise requiring re‑intubation/ventilatory support; she has anemia of prematurity and had necrotizing enterocolitis 4 weeks earlier. Previously screened for ROP; blinks briskly to indirect ophthalmoscope light in either eye.
Symptom Onset: 11 weeks of age
Symptom Duration: 11 weeks
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Infantile esotropia with recurrent esotropia, left dissociated vertical deviation, bilateral inferior oblique overaction, left-eye amblyopia, status post strabismus surgery.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Sepsis, respiratory compromise requiring re‑intubation and ventilatory support, supplemental oxygen, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, extreme prematurity (low gestational age), very low birth weight
Alleviating Factors: Correction with full cycloplegic spectacles (reduction/elimination of deviation) and bifocals if fusion/stereopsis present
Associated Symptoms: sepsis, respiratory compromise requiring re-intubation/ventilatory support, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, frailty, blinking briskly to light, receiving supplemental oxygen (50%)
Prior Treatments: strabismus surgery
Systemic Symptoms: Sepsis, respiratory compromise requiring re‑intubation/ventilatory support, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, frail appearance, on 50% oxygen
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: extreme prematurity, low birth weight (650 g), prolonged ventilation/re‑intubation and supplemental oxygen, sepsis, anemia of prematurity, necrotizing enterocolitis, intraventricular hemorrhage (possible risk), prolonged intravenous nutrition


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 140 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 50 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: Fixes and follows; central, steady, maintained (CSM)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Fixes and blinks to light (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Method: Preferential looking (Teller acuity card)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tono-Pen (handheld applanation tonometry)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal appearing for age (no conjunctival injection or scleral icterus noted)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no corneal pathology noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep (no anterior chamber reaction)
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: briskly reactive to light; pupils appear normal in size and shape
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly constricts to near stimulation
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: blinks briskly to the light of the indirect ophthalmoscope with either eye
Extraocular Movements: Restriction of extraocular movements consistent with bilateral inferior oblique overaction and dissociated vertical deviation of the left eye.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal appearing optic nerve
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Dilation and tortuosity (plus disease)
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Immature retina in zone I with peripheral avascularity (leading edge of vascular development)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Infantile esotropia status post prior strabismus surgery with recurrent esotropia. Left-eye dissociated vertical deviation and bilateral inferior oblique overaction are present. Left-eye amblyopia. Recommend full cycloplegic refraction and new spectacles, recheck alignment in a few months to assess for accommodative component; consider bifocals only if fusion/stereopsis documented. If residual esotropia >10–12 PD at distance in full correction, surgical correction (options: bilateral medial rectus recession, medial rectus recession with lateral rectus resection, or bilateral lateral rectus resection based on measurements and prior surgery) is indicated. Monitor vision and alignment regularly; treat amblyopia as needed and repeat surgery if deviation recurs.
Primary Diagnosis: Infantile esotropia status post strabismus surgery, now with recurrent esotropia
Secondary Diagnoses: Infantile esotropia status post strabismus surgery; recurrent esotropia; dissociated vertical deviation OS; bilateral inferior oblique overaction; left amblyopia; retinopathy of prematurity (zone I, type I with plus disease); history of extreme prematurity, sepsis, respiratory compromise, necrotizing enterocolitis, anemia of prematurity
Differential Diagnosis: Infantile (congenital) esotropia; Accommodative esotropia; Third‑nerve (oculomotor) palsy with medial rectus overaction
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: recurrent esotropia; dissociated vertical deviation (left); bilateral inferior oblique overaction; amblyopia (left); potential recurrence of amblyopia and/or esotropia; need for repeat strabismus surgery; risks related to ROP (retinal detachment, visual impairment/blindness)


Plan
All Medications: intravenous antibiotics, supplemental oxygen, bevacizumab, spectacles (glasses), bifocals, cycloplegic refraction (cycloplegic agent)
All Procedures: strabismus surgery (prior) — unspecified procedure(s) for infantile esotropia; bilateral medial rectus recession (as possible surgical option); medial rectus recession with ipsilateral lateral rectus resection (option); bilateral lateral rectus resection (option); prescription of full cycloplegic spectacle correction; amblyopia treatment (patching/visual therapy)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow up in the next few months to recheck ocular alignment with new full cycloplegic spectacle correction; monitor vision for amblyopia and adjust treatment as needed, perform regular childhood follow-ups (intervals based on angle stability, visual acuity, and age), and consider surgery if residual esotropia >10–12 PD at distance with full correction.
Patient Education: Provide full cycloplegic spectacle correction and return for follow-up to monitor alignment and vision; if orthotropic at distance but esotropic at near and fusion/stereopsis present, consider bifocals; monitor for and treat amblyopia as needed; if residual esotropia >10–12 PD at distance with full correction, discuss strabismus surgery options (medial rectus recession ± lateral rectus surgery); schedule regular childhood follow-ups for recurrence and stereopsis monitoring.


CASE 82 — Childhood Exotropia
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Recurrent inward drift of the left eye (intermittent esotropia), noted especially when tired
DURATION: 5 years
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 5-year-old girl with crossed eyes since 2 months of age, with strabismus surgery at 8 months. Eyes were well aligned after surgery until recently when the left eye intermittently drifts inward, especially when tired. Never treated with occlusion therapy. Current glasses at least 1 year old. No other medical issues. Family history: sister with esotropia requiring surgery. Visual acuity with current correction 20/30 OD, 20/50 OS. Current spectacles +2.00 OD, +2.50 OS.
History Narrative: A 5-year-old girl with a history of crossed eyes since 2 months of age presents for an examination. She has a history of strabismus surgery at 8 months of age. Her mother reports that her eyes were well aligned following surgery until recently when her left eye has been noted to intermittently drift inward, especially when she is tired. She has never been treated with occlusion therapy. Her current glasses are at least 1 year old. Her past medical history is otherwise unremarkable. The family history is notable for a sister with esotropia that required surgical intervention.
Symptom Onset: 2 months of age
Symptom Duration: Since 2 months of age
Progression: Intermittent, worsening (increased frequency of inward drift, noted recently)
Characteristics: Intermittent exotropia — pseudodivergence excess type; intermittent outward deviation with poor fusional control, better at distance than near (pseudodivergence pattern), lifelong tendency, associated with reduced stereopsis (3000″), asymmetric OKN, may have inferior oblique overaction, V‑pattern, dissociated vertical deviation.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: tiredness
Alleviating Factors: When she is tired, the left eye drifts inward less—eyes are better aligned after prior surgery; improved alignment with spectacle correction (full hyperopic correction likely to help).
Associated Symptoms: Intermittent inward drifting of the left eye (worse when tired)
Previous Episodes: Yes — crossed eyes noted since 2 months of age; surgery at 8 months; eyes well aligned until recently when intermittent inward drift began.
Prior Treatments: Strabismus surgery at 8 months of age
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — intermittent symptoms (worse when tired) with preserved functional vision (20/30 OD, 20/50 OS) allowing most daily activities with occasional difficulty in prolonged near tasks and reduced stereopsis.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: Sister with esotropia that required surgical intervention
Risk Factors: Family history of strabismus (sister with esotropia); early-onset strabismus (onset at 2 months); prior strabismus surgery; uncorrected/undercorrected hyperopia.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/50
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen acuity with spectacles
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonopen applanation


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: White conjunctivae and sclera (no injection)
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet, deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear posterior chamber intraocular lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full versions with moderate inferior oblique overaction OU and V‑pattern (esotropia increased in downgaze to 20Δ and decreased in upgaze to 5Δ).
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal appearing optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia. The patient is a 5-year-old girl with early-onset intermittent exotropia, asymmetric OKN suggestive of early-onset etiology, moderate inferior oblique overaction bilaterally with a V-pattern, moderate left dissociated vertical deviation, reduced stereoacuity (3000″ on Titmus), hyperopia undercorrected on current glasses (+3.00 OD, +3.75 OS cycloplegic), and intermittent exotropia greater at near (20 PD) than distance (8 PD). Management options include full hyperopic spectacle correction and observation/medical therapies (occlusion or orthoptic exercises as appropriate), with surgical intervention considered if control worsens.
Primary Diagnosis: Pseudodivergence excess type intermittent exotropia
Secondary Diagnoses: Intermittent exotropia (pseudodivergence excess type), inferior oblique overaction OU, V‑pattern strabismus, dissociated vertical deviation OS, amblyopia OS (reduced VA 20/50), high hyperopia OU (cycloplegic +3.00 OD, +3.75 OS), asymmetric OKN (nasal‑to‑temporal deficit)
Differential Diagnosis: Infantile (congenital) esotropia; Partially accommodative (refractive) esotropia; Sensory esotropia; Sixth nerve palsy (acquired/paralytic)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Recurrence of exotropia (20–40%); decreased stereopsis; amblyopia; need for repeat surgery; cosmetic/psychosocial concerns


Plan
All Medications: cyclopentolate, homatropine, scopolamine, atropine
All Procedures: Strabismus surgery (prior at 8 months of age); potential future procedures discussed: bilateral lateral rectus recessions, lateral rectus recession with ipsilateral medial rectus resection, bilateral medial rectus resections.
Follow-Up Instructions: If managed medically: follow-up every 2–6 months for reevaluation. If surgery performed: healing ~6 weeks, with continued long-term follow-up (monitor for recurrence, decreasing stereopsis, and amblyopia).
Patient Education: Intermittent exotropia is a lifelong condition caused by difficulty the brain has with fusing the two eyes’ images; it may improve or be controlled but is not “cured.” Management options include updating full hyperopic eyeglass correction, passive measures (patching/overminus glasses) for young children, and active convergence exercises (e.g., pencil push-ups) once the child can cooperate. Surgery is considered if control becomes poor or the eye drifts outward much of the time; recurrence after surgery is possible. Regular follow-up is important to monitor control, stereopsis, and for development of amblyopia so treatment can be adjusted promptly.


CASE 83 — Childhood Ptosis
==========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: intermittent drifting out of the right eye for 2 years
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "the eye is now deviated outward approximately 60% of the day." "right eye closure in sunlight."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 4-year-old boy with a 2-year history of intermittent right-eye outward drifting, worse when tired or looking at distance, initially occurring 2–3 times/day but recently present ~60% of the day, with deviation up to ~50–60Δ at distance, worse in sunlight causing right eye closure, no trauma or systemic symptoms.
History Narrative: A 4-year-old boy presents with a 2-year history of intermittent drifting out of the right eye. His mother states that the deviation is worse when the child is tired or when he is looking at distant objects. His mother initially noted this deviation approximately two to three times a day; however, it recently has been getting worse. She states that the eye is now deviated outward approximately 60% of the day. There is no history of antecedent head trauma, febrile episode, or other medical problem. There is no family history of strabismus or amblyopia. Review of systems is notable for right eye closure in sunlight.
Symptom Onset: 2 years prior (onset at age 2)
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Congenital (present since birth), intermittent worsening over time
Characteristics: Congenital (present since birth), unilateral (right upper lid), associated anomalous chin‑up head posture, risk for amblyopia/anisometropia, levator dysfunction spectrum (can be mild–severe)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: tiredness; looking at distant objects; sunlight (causing right eye closure)
Alleviating Factors: covery of orthotropia on refixation or blinking
Associated Symptoms: intermittent outward deviation of the right eye (worse when tired or looking at distance), right eye closure in sunlight, anomalous chin‑up head posture
Previous Episodes: Intermittent drifting of the right eye noted for ~2 years, initially 2–3 times/day and recently ~60% of the day.
Impact on Daily Activities: An anomalous chin-up head posture to compensate for right upper‑lid ptosis, causing potential interference with development and coordination but no amblyopia; likely mild–moderate impact on activities (postural strain and cosmetic concern).
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel.
Risk Factors: Congenital ptosis (often idiopathic); family history typically absent; associated conditions increasing risk: perinatal factors (birth trauma), developmental cranial nerve/muscle anomalies, syndromic associations (Marcus Gunn jaw-winking, Horner syndrome, congenital fibrosis), neurogenic disorders (third nerve palsy), myasthenia gravis, mechanical lesions (lid hemangioma, neurofibroma), and hereditary disorders (congenital fibrosis, chronic progressive external ophthalmoplegia)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.4 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 18 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/30
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: 20/30 OU
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart, crowded HOTV equivalent — monocular testing with age-appropriate Snellen (20/30 OU)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Tonometry (Goldmann applanation)


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Normal, no evidence of ptosis
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea without signs of scarring or keratopathy
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens, no cataract


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3mm, round
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk response to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to accommodation (normal)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 3mm in diameter, round, with brisk response to light
Extraocular Movements: Full.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Unremarkable, normal anterior segment on slit-lamp examination
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Scan shows no retinal or macular abnormalities; macula flat and normal.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin‑up head posture and absence of amblyopia.
Primary Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis of the right upper lid with anomalous chin-up head posture and absence of amblyopia
Differential Diagnosis: Intermittent exotropia (pseudodivergence excess type); Congenital exotropia; Third nerve palsy; Myasthenia gravis
Laterality: Right
Complications: early and late infection; suture granuloma; exposure keratopathy; retrobulbar hemorrhage; overcorrections


Plan
All Procedures: Patch test; Cycloplegic refraction; Alternate cover testing (simultaneous prism and cover as indicated); 30-minute occlusion (patch) test; Occlusion therapy for amblyopia; Pharmacologic or optical penalization for amblyopia; Fasanella–Servat procedure; Levator resection; Frontalis suspension (using autogenous fascia lata, banked fascia lata, silicone rod, Supramid/nylon/Prolene/Gore-Tex sutures, or palmaris longus tendon); Surgical reduction of levator (levator surgery)
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow the child throughout childhood for surveillance of amblyopia and anisometropia until at least age 7–10; after ptosis surgery, expect ~1 month healing with nighttime lubrication for ~1 month (longer if Bell’s reflex absent) and monitor lid height for recurrence.
Patient Education: Congenital ptosis is a condition present from birth causing drooping of the upper eyelid; it can affect vision and head posture. Key points: - Watch for amblyopia (lazy eye) and unequal refractive error (anisometropia); regular eye exams and vision testing are important through at least age 7–10. - Treatment options: - Non-surgical: glasses if significant refractive error; patching or penalization if amblyopia develops. - Surgical: recommended if ptosis significantly blocks vision or causes chin-up head posture. Procedures depend on levator muscle function (levator resection for mild–moderate cases; frontalis suspension for severe cases with poor levator function). Discuss risks (infection, exposure keratopathy, overcorrection) and materials (autogenous fascia lata has best long-term results). - After surgery: expect about 1 month for healing, temporary incomplete eyelid closure during sleep—use lubricating drops/ointment at night initially; long-term lubrication may be needed if Bell’s phenomenon is poor. - Follow-up: regular postoperative visits to monitor lid height and vision; lifelong monitoring in childhood to detect recurrent ptosis or recurrent amblyopia. Seek prompt care for redness, sudden vision change, severe pain, or wound issues.


CASE 84 — The Apparently Blind Infant
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: droopy right upper lid
DURATION: Since birth
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "lazy"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 17-month-old boy with a right upper eyelid that has been "lazy" since birth. Parents report a persistent chin‑up head posture (~15°) when walking or watching TV and occasional occlusion of the pupil by the lid. No history of trauma, no observed ocular motility disturbance. No family history of ptosis, strabismus, or neuromuscular disease. Child otherwise healthy, developing normally; review of systems negative. Fixes and follows with both eyes without preference.
History Narrative: The parents report a droopy right upper eyelid present since birth. The 17-month-old boy holds his chin up when walking or watching TV and occasionally lets the lid block his pupil. No ocular motility disturbance has been noted. No family history of ptosis, strabismus, or hereditary muscular disorders. The child is otherwise healthy, developing normally, with a negative review of systems.
Symptom Onset: Since birth
Symptom Duration: Since birth
Progression: Stable since birth.
Characteristics: Isolated developmental visual maturation (DVM): transient visual impairment present from birth with normal development, good fixation and following, equal visual acuity in both eyes, moderate–severe congenital right upper lid ptosis (absent lid crease, MRD1 0.5 mm R / 3.5 mm L), levator function 6 mm R / 12 mm L, preferred 15° chin-up head posture, minimal anisometropia (–0.25+1.50×90 R, +0.50 L), pupils equal and reactive, full motility, no afferent pupillary defect, normal anterior and posterior segment exams.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: chin-up head posture
Associated Symptoms: droopy right upper lid; chin-up head posture (15 degrees); occasionally lets the lid block his pupil; no ocular motility disturbance; good fixation and following visual behavior; no family history of ptosis/strabismus/hereditary muscular disorders; otherwise healthy, normal development
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal — child has good fixation/following in both eyes, normal development, no functional limitation aside from occasional pupillary occlusion by lid and a 15° chin-up posture; no surgical treatment indicated.
Systemic Symptoms: Negative review of systems


Objective Exam
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: fixation and following (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: fixation and following (central, steady, maintained)
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: Central, steady, maintained (CSM) bilaterally
Visual Acuity – Method: Fix and follow


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right upper eyelid ptosis with absent lid crease; right palpebral fissure 6.5 mm, left 10 mm; MRD1 right 0.5 mm, left 3.5 mm (improves to 2 mm right with chin-up); levator function right 6 mm, left 12 mm; no lash abnormality noted.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear and unremarkable corneas
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Unremarkable
Anterior Segment – Iris: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive to light, no heterochromia
Anterior Segment – Lens: clear crystalline lenses in both eyes


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 3 mm, round, briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Briskly reactive to light (implies reactive to near)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: 3mm, round, and briskly reactive to light; no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Versions are full.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: normal-appearing optic nerves bilaterally
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal.
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: A 17-month-old boy with congenital right upper‑lid ptosis since birth. External exam: moderate–severe right ptosis with R palpebral fissure 6.5 mm vs L 10 mm; MRD1 R 0.5 mm, L 3.5 mm (improves to 2 mm R with chin‑up posture); levator function R 6 mm, L 12 mm; absent right lid crease. Pupils equal, reactive, no APD. Full versions, orthotropic. Cycloplegic refraction essentially plano with minimal anisometropia (R −0.25 +1.50 ×90, L +0.50). Normal fixation and following OU with no fixation preference. Findings (absent lid crease, increased fissure in downgaze, chin‑up posture, moderate levator function) are consistent with congenital (simple) ptosis. No evidence of Horner syndrome, third‑nerve palsy, jaw‑wink, myasthenia, or associated ocular motility or neurologic abnormalities. Vision currently equal and not amblyopic; no surgical intervention indicated at this time. Recommend periodic developmental/neurologic surveillance and routine ocular follow‑up to monitor for amblyopia or refractive changes.
Primary Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis
Differential Diagnosis: Congenital ptosis (levator dysgenesis); Horner syndrome; Third cranial nerve palsy; Marcus Gunn jaw-winking ptosis
Severity: Moderate to severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: amblyopia


Plan
All Procedures: exam under anesthesia, funduscopy, refraction, corneal diameter measurement, measurement of intraocular pressure, cycloplegic refraction, slit-lamp examination, electrophysiologic testing, neuroimaging
Follow-Up Instructions: For isolated DVM: monitor developmental and neurologic status with periodic pediatric assessments; expect vision normalization by 6–8 months—if vision does not improve by 6–8 months, obtain electrophysiologic testing and neuroimaging. If associated neurologic/developmental abnormalities, continue follow-up as vision may improve up to 1–2 years.
Patient Education: This right upper‑lid droop (congenital ptosis) has been present since birth and is most consistent with a developmental under‑development of the levator muscle. Because your child fixates and follows well in both eyes and has only minimal refractive difference, immediate surgery is not indicated. Watchful follow‑up is recommended: monitor vision and development closely, have routine pediatric and ophthalmic exams to detect any amblyopia (lazy eye), significant refractive error, or changes in eyelid function, and return sooner if you notice worsening drooping, abnormal eye movements, unequal pupil sizes, or signs of developmental delay. If vision does not remain normal by about 6–8 months of age (or later if neurologic issues are present), further testing (electrophysiology, neuroimaging) will be recommended.


CASE 85 — Optic Nerve Hypoplasia
================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: May be blind; poor eye contact and not responding to bright lights
DURATION: 3 months
LATERALITY: Bilateral
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 3-month-old girl brought by parents for concern she may be blind; parents report poor eye contact, lack of response to parental facial expressions, and apparent unresponsiveness to bright lights. Prenatal and birth histories unremarkable. No prior ophthalmologic or medical problems. Two older siblings had normal visual responses at comparable age. Development otherwise normal. Parents note she does blink to bright light; occasional OKN beat but attention poor. No spontaneous nystagmus; eyes orthotropic with full versions. Pupils 5 mm, moderately reactive, no afferent pupillary defect. Anterior segment and fundus exams normal. Parents requested VEP testing.
History Narrative: A 3-month-old girl is brought to her ophthalmologist by her parents because of concerns that she may be blind. They report that she has poor eye contact, that she does not respond to parental facial expressions, and that she does not appear to respond to bright lights. The child’s prenatal and birth histories are unremarkable. Her past ophthalmologic and medical histories are unremarkable. The child has two siblings, aged 2 years and 5 years, who visually responded much more at 3 months of age than the patient does and who are developmentally and visually normal at this time. The review of systems is otherwise negative. The child appears to be normal from a developmental standpoint, though the child has not been seen by her pediatrician in several weeks.
Symptom Onset: 3 months of age
Symptom Duration: 3 months
Progression: Stable to potentially progressive endocrine issues; visual impairment may improve with maturation or amblyopia therapy but optic nerve hypoplasia is a nonprogressive structural defect.
Characteristics: Bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia associated with septo‑optic dysplasia, growth hormone deficiency (short stature, low weight), absence of septum pellucidum, small optic nerve with double‑ring sign, potential for nystagmus and variable vision ranging from severe impairment to normal.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: poor eye contact; failure to fixate or follow; no response to facial expressions; does not respond to bright lights; occasional nystagmus on OKN; blinks to bright light; orthotropic eyes; pupils 5 mm moderately reactive; normal anterior segment and fundus
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — marked visual impairment in infancy limiting fixation and visual engagement, likely affecting daily visual interactions and requiring habilitation and medical follow-up.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 90/60 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 130 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 30 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: NLP (no fixation)
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: NLP
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: No fixation (central, steady, maintained absent)
Visual Acuity – Method: Fixation absent (no fixation or following)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: normal
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Normal
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Clear and unremarkable (normal)
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear; normal.


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: 5 mm, moderately reactive (no afferent pupillary defect)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Moderately reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Moderately reactive to light (preserved near response inferred)
Pupils – RAPD: No afferent pupillary defect
Pupils – Other: No afferent pupillary defect, no paradoxical pupillary reaction
Extraocular Movements: Full versions; orthotropic on oculocephalic testing
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segment and other anterior structures appear normal (no abnormalities on slit-lamp examination).
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia. The child also has growth hormone deficiency resulting in short stature and low weight.
Primary Diagnosis: Septo-optic dysplasia with bilateral, asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia
Secondary Diagnoses: Septo-optic dysplasia; bilateral asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia; growth hormone deficiency (pituitary hormone deficiency)
Differential Diagnosis: Delayed visual maturation; Cortical visual impairment; Bilateral optic nerve hypoplasia/structural optic nerve abnormality
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: Septo‑optic dysplasia with bilateral asymmetric optic nerve hypoplasia; growth hormone deficiency leading to short stature and low weight; risk of additional pituitary hormone deficiencies; potential amblyopia and poor vision/severe visual impairment; strabismus (esotropia) with higher recurrence after surgery; increased risk of endocrine abnormalities presenting later; need for lifelong endocrine monitoring; risk to better-seeing eye (functional monocular) requiring protective eyewear.


Plan
All Medications: pituitary hormone replacement as needed; corticosteroids (stress-dose)
Follow-Up Instructions: Ongoing evaluation of growth and endocrine parameters throughout early childhood with endocrinology follow-up; administer stress-dose corticosteroids if corticotrophin deficiency suspected during acute illness/surgery; continue amblyopia therapy and defer strabismus surgery until amblyopia maximized (typically postpone surgery until school age); prescribe polycarbonate safety spectacles to protect the better-seeing eye.
Patient Education: Explain optic nerve hypoplasia and its variable vision outcomes; reassure that visual maturation may improve over months and avoid labeling as permanently visually impaired in infancy; emphasize need for endocrinology evaluation and ongoing pituitary hormone testing and replacement as needed; discuss possible neuroimaging if indicated; advise trial of amblyopia therapy before assuming irreversible loss and that strabismus surgery is usually delayed until amblyopia treatment is maximized and often until school age; recommend protective polycarbonate eyewear for the better-seeing eye; instruct parents about lifelong growth and endocrine follow-up (including possible delayed hormone deficiencies) and stress-dose steroids if corticotrophin deficiency is present during illness or surgery; coordinate care with pediatrician, neurologist, and endocrinologist and provide clear counseling to alleviate parental concerns.
Referrals: Endocrinology (pituitary/hormone evaluation)
Emergency Instructions: Stress doses of corticosteroids should be administered in situations such as acute illness, surgery, or serious injury for patients with corticotrophin abnormalities.


CASE 86 — Ptosis
================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Enlarging right upper eyelid skin nodule with eyelid droop (ptosis)
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 55-year-old man with an approximately 8-month history of an enlarging, fairly rapidly growing skin nodule of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region, with one episode of bleeding after picking at the lesion; extensive sun-exposure history; no prior skin cancer.
History Narrative: A 55-year-old man presented for evaluation of an enlarging skin nodule of his right upper eyelid. The lesion had been present for approximately 8 months and had grown fairly rapidly. The patient recalled one episode of bleeding from the area after he had picked at the lesion. While there was no previous history of skin cancer, the patient reported an extensive sun-exposure history. His ophthalmic examination was within normal limits except for the external examination. There was a large elevated nonpigmented nodule involving the skin of the right upper eyelid near the medial canthal region. The nodule measured approximately 15 mm × 12 mm × 7 mm and showed numerous small telangiectatic vessels on its surface. The edges of the tumor had a thickened pearly appearance. There was no ulceration present. The skin underlying the lesion was freely moveable, and the tumor did not appear fixed to the underlying deep structures. There was no regional lymphadenopathy. The skin of the patient’s face showed diffuse sun-exposure changes consisting of fine wrinkles and telangiectatic vessels and scattered areas of crusting.
Symptom Onset: Approximately 8 months prior
Symptom Duration: 8 months
Progression: Enlarging over ~8 months (fairly rapid growth)
Characteristics: Droopiness of the upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion (aponeurotic ptosis)


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: taping of the lid or the use of eyelid crutches attached to spectacles
Associated Symptoms: visual field impairment
Previous Episodes: One episode of bleeding after picking at the lesion
Impact on Daily Activities: Improved superior visual field postoperatively; prior to surgery ptosis caused symptomatic visual field impairment interfering with activities (now improved)
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: fair skin; northern European ancestry; extensive sun exposure; history of previous basal cell carcinoma; family history of skin cancer


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: JOD 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/25 (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Large elevated nonpigmented nodule of right upper eyelid near medial canthus; skin freely movable, numerous surface telangiectatic vessels, thickened pearly edges, no ulceration; bilateral aponeurotic ptosis of upper eyelids (levator dehiscence)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, unremarkable corneas bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens (phakic)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light; size 3 mm (OU)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions (intact)
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: disc normals (no pallor or cupping)
Fundus Exam – Macula: WNL


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion, causing superior visual field impairment; patient underwent bilateral external levator advancement with intraoperative adjustment under local anesthesia, resulting in improved visual field without complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Ptosis of both upper eyelids due to levator aponeurosis disinsertion
Differential Diagnosis: Basal cell carcinoma; Squamous cell carcinoma; Sebaceous gland carcinoma; Malignant melanoma
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral
Complications: undercorrection; overcorrection; asymmetric or unsatisfactory lid contour; lagophthalmos with exposure symptoms


Plan
All Procedures: External levator advancement on both upper eyelids
Follow-Up Instructions: Postoperative monitoring of eyelid height and contour; report any signs of infection, wound dehiscence, increasing pain, vision changes, or exposure symptoms; routine postoperative visit for suture removal and intraoperative-adjustment assessment (typically within 1 week) with additional follow-up as needed for symmetry, under/overcorrection, or lagophthalmos.
Patient Education: Ptosis is a drooping of the upper eyelid most often caused by age-related loosening of the levator aponeurosis. If mild and not affecting vision, no treatment is needed; temporary measures include taping the lid or using an eyelid crutch on glasses. If the droop interferes with your superior peripheral vision or daily activities, surgical repair (levator advancement or Müller’s muscle procedure) can restore eyelid position. Surgery risks include under- or overcorrection, asymmetric contour, or incomplete eyelid closure leading to dryness; these are usually manageable. After surgery, you may have improved visual fields and should follow wound care instructions, attend postoperative visits for lid position adjustment, and report any persistent double vision, severe pain, redness, or vision changes promptly.


CASE 87 — Thyroid Eye Disease
=============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Drooping of her upper eyelids with interference in vision (bilateral upper eyelid ptosis)
DURATION: 3–4 years
LATERALITY: Bilateral
PATIENT QUOTES: "I often found myself manually lifting my lids to improve my visual field."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 72-year-old woman with a 3–4 year history of slowly progressive bilateral upper eyelid drooping now severe enough to interfere with vision; she often manually lifts her lids to improve her visual field. No periocular trauma or prior eyelid surgery. No history of diplopia, muscle weakness, neurologic disease, or fluctuating lid position. Previous photos showed no prior ptosis. She had bilateral cataract extractions with IOLs 4 years ago.
History Narrative: The patient is a 72-year-old woman referred for slowly progressive drooping of her upper eyelids over the past 3–4 years, now severe enough to interfere with vision; she often manually lifts her lids to improve her visual field. No prior periocular trauma or surgery. She underwent bilateral cataract extraction with intraocular lens placement 4 years earlier. No history of muscle weakness, fatigue, or neurologic disease. She denied diplopia or significant diurnal fluctuation of lid position. Inspection of old photographs did not show ptosis previously.
Symptom Onset: 3 to 4 years
Symptom Duration: 3 to 4 years
Progression: Slowly progressive over 3–4 years
Characteristics: Progressive bilateral upper eyelid retraction with proptosis, widened palpebral fissures, scleral show, eyelid retraction causing exposure symptoms, restrictive motility possible, may have increased IOP in upgaze, risk of optic neuropathy.


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Manually lifting her lids to improve her visual field; frequent artificial tears and punctal occlusion; wearing a moisture shield at bedtime.
Associated Symptoms: Drooping of the upper eyelids causing visual field obstruction; manual lifting of lids to improve vision; corneal exposure symptoms (dryness) responsive to artificial tears; no diplopia, no significant lid fluctuation, no pain.
Prior Treatments: Bilateral cataract extraction with intraocular lens implantation 4 years prior; thyroid hormone replacement therapy.
Impact on Daily Activities: Patient often manually lifts her lids to improve her visual field; ptosis interferes with her vision and daily functioning.
Systemic Symptoms: Hypothyroidism (on thyroid hormone replacement)
Risk Factors: Thyroid dysfunction (hypothyroidism/Graves disease), older age (age-related changes), prior thyroid disease/endocrinopathy


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 74 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Eyelid retraction bilaterally with widened palpebral fissures and scleral show; marked upper eyelid retraction and proptosis with deep superior sulci; absent upper eyelid creases; ptosis previously noted (aponeurotic) greater on right prior to retraction treatment.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: No conjunctival injection; sclera notable for mild scleral show from eyelid retraction
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal drainage patent (no punctal or canalicular obstruction inferred)
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis with widened palpebral fissures and scleral show; eyelid retraction (marked) with exposure signs.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Corneal exposure changes from lagophthalmos with drying; treated with frequent artificial tears, lubricating ointment, moisture shield, and punctal occlusion.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Clear, without iris abnormalities
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, and reactive (normal size)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and symmetric
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full ductions and versions; orthophoric (no motility restriction)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal macula
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy) manifesting with bilateral eyelid retraction and proptosis causing exposure symptoms. Vision 20/20 OU, orthophoric with full motility, no compressive optic neuropathy or significant diplopia. Managed conservatively with lubricants and punctal occlusion; underwent four‑lid recession after 4 months of stability with good cosmetic and symptomatic outcome. Associated hypothyroidism on thyroid hormone replacement.
Primary Diagnosis: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy) with eyelid retraction and bilateral proptosis
Secondary Diagnoses: Hypothyroidism
Differential Diagnosis: Aponeurotic ptosis; Neurogenic ptosis (eg, Horner’s syndrome, myasthenia gravis); Myogenic ptosis (eg, oculopharyngeal dystrophy, chronic progressive external ophthalmoplegia); Mechanical ptosis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Bilateral


Plan
All Medications: artificial tears; lubricating ointment (bedtime); topical intraocular pressure–lowering drops; prednisone (oral corticosteroid) 80–100 mg daily; intravenous pulsed high-dose steroids; orbital radiation therapy (alternative); thyroid hormone replacement therapy
All Procedures: frequent artificial tears; punctal occlusion; four‑lid recession (recession of all four eyelids)
Follow-Up Instructions: Maintain thyroid hormone replacement and routine endocrinology follow-up; use frequent artificial tears and punctal occlusion for corneal lubrication; return for postoperative follow-up after eyelid recessions and report any vision changes, diplopia, increased redness, pain, or worsening exposure.
Patient Education: Thyroid eye disease (Graves’ orbitopathy) can cause bulging eyes, eyelid retraction, dry or exposed corneas, double vision, and rarely optic nerve problems. Treatment depends on severity and activity: mild cases often need lubricating eye drops/ointments, eyelid measures (moisture shields at night, punctal occlusion) and IOP drops if needed; active inflammation or vision threat may require high‑dose steroids, orbital radiation, or urgent orbital decompression. Rehabilitative surgery (orbital decompression, strabismus surgery, eyelid repair) is done when inflammation is inactive and tailored to your symptoms. Report sudden vision loss, worsening double vision, severe pain, or new drooping immediately. Coordinate care with your endocrinologist to optimize thyroid hormone levels, and attend regular ophthalmology follow‑up for vision, eye pressure, and corneal health.
Referrals: Endocrinology


CASE 88 — Ectropion
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Pain around both eyes (dull ache worsened with eye movement) and gritty sensation in both eyes.
DURATION: 18 months
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "gritty"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 44-year-old woman referred for evaluation of dull aching pain around both eyes worse with eye movement, with 18 months of increasing prominence of both eyes and bilateral gritty sensation. She denied diplopia or blurred vision, had no prior ocular surgery or trauma, no known thyroid dysfunction but reported heat and cold intolerance without changes in weight, body hair, or voice.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman was referred for evaluation of dull aching pain around both eyes, worse with eye movement. Over the past 18 months she had noted increasing prominence of both eyes and a “gritty” sensation in both eyes. She denied diplopia, blurred vision, prior ocular surgery or trauma. Past medical history was unremarkable with no known thyroid dysfunction, though she reported heat and cold intolerance without recent changes in weight, body hair, or voice. No known cardiovascular or neurologic disease. Examination later showed 20/20 corrected visual acuity OU, normal color vision, normal pupillary reactions without RAPD, orthophoria with mild (–1) restriction of upgaze bilaterally, upper and lower eyelid retraction with palpebral fissures 16 mm OD and 15 mm OS, temporal flaring of upper lids, 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos, increased resistance to retropulsion without palpable orbital masses, exophthalmometry 22 mm OD and 21 mm OS, IOP 21 mm Hg OD and 20 mm Hg OS, and slit-lamp evidence of scattered inferior punctate epithelial erosions and mild bulbar conjunctival injection.
Symptom Onset: 18 months
Symptom Duration: 18 months
Progression: Progressive
Characteristics: Involutional lower eyelid laxity with medial punctal malposition causing ocular irritation (involutional ectropion)


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Eye movement
Alleviating Factors: lubrication with artificial tears or lubricating ointment
Associated Symptoms: grittiness, tearing, ocular irritation, pain around both eyes (dull ache worse with eye movement), prominence of both eyes, heat and cold intolerance, diplopia absent, blurred vision absent
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Prior Treatments: lubricating/artificial tear therapy (topical lubricants/ointment)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate limitation — daily activities mildly affected by ocular irritation and gritty sensation, requiring lubrication and postoperative improvement.
Systemic Symptoms: Heat and cold intolerance
Recent Travel: No recent travel reported
Risk Factors: Age-related eyelid laxity (involutional changes)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 118/72 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 72 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5/5
Visual Acuity – Method: Corrected visual acuity
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 21 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 20 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Upper and lower eyelid retraction bilaterally with temporal flaring of the upper lids; left lower eyelid involutional ectropion; 2 mm bilateral lagophthalmos; scleral show; no madarosis or trichiasis noted.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mild bulbar conjunctival injection bilaterally, most pronounced at the insertion of horizontal rectus muscles
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal punctal apposition compromised with poor punctal apposition to the globe due to lower eyelid ectropion (involutional); epiphora/tearing noted.
External Exam – Orbit: Normal globe position with mild proptosis (exophthalmometry 22 mm OD, 21 mm OS); increased resistance to retropulsion; no palpable orbital masses.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Scattered inferior punctate epithelial erosions bilaterally
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: deep
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic, no lens opacities noted

Pupils – Size/Shape: Both pupils reacted normally, without relative afferent pupillary defect.
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Normal
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: without relative afferent pupillary defect
