Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Both optic discs flat without pallor
Fundus Exam – Macula: normal
Fundus Exam – Vessels: normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No retinal lesions; periphery grossly within normal limits.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Involutional ectropion of the left lower eyelid causing poor punctal apposition and ocular surface irritation with inferior punctate epithelial erosions; diagnosis confirmed clinically and managed surgically (lateral tarsal strip with medial spindle) after conservative lubrication.
Primary Diagnosis: Involutional ectropion of the left lower eyelid
Differential Diagnosis: Graves’ (thyroid) orbitopathy; Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital myositis); Bilateral orbital metastases/diffuse infiltrative disease (amyloidosis, sarcoidosis)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left
Complications: dry eye with punctate epithelial erosions, corneal exposure/keratitis, tearing due to poor punctal apposition, conjunctival hypertrophy/keratinization, lagophthalmos-related exposure keratopathy


Plan
All Medications: artificial tears; lubricating ointment
All Procedures: lateral tarsal strip procedure; medial spindle procedure
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up in oculoplastics clinic in 1 week for wound check and suture removal as indicated, then at 6–8 weeks to assess eyelid position and symptom resolution; continue lubricating drops/ointment as needed; seek urgent care sooner for increasing pain, redness, discharge, vision loss, or wound dehiscence.
Patient Education: Lubricating eye drops during the day and ointment at night to protect the cornea and relieve grittiness; avoid dry or windy environments and use humidifiers as needed; protect eyes from sun with sunglasses; apply warm compresses and gentle eyelid hygiene if irritation occurs; attend follow-up appointments to monitor healing and report increased pain, vision changes, excessive tearing, or wound problems; surgical repair (lateral tarsal strip ± medial spindle) is effective for involutional ectropion if symptoms persist despite lubrication.
Referrals: Consultation with an internist or endocrinologist to evaluate thyroid status


CASE 89 — Entropion
===================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left eye irritation for one year
DURATION: 1 year
LATERALITY: Right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 70-year-old man with one year of left eye irritation. He had bilateral cataract surgery 3 years ago and no other ocular disease or trauma. Symptoms include left lower‑lid malposition with conjunctival injection and 1+ inferior superficial punctate keratitis; both lower lids have moderate horizontal laxity with delayed snapback.
History Narrative: A 70-year-old man presented with left eye irritation that had been present for the last year. He had bilateral cataract surgery 3 years previously, and there was no other history of ocular disease or trauma. Examination showed a corrected vision of 20/20 OD and 20/25 OS. The external examination showed a marked out-turning of the left lower eyelid with associated diffuse tarsal conjunctival injection. Left corneal exam showed 1+ superficial punctate keratitis inferiorly. No masses were palpated. There was mild dermatochalasis of both upper lids. Both lower lids exhibited moderate horizontal laxity with delayed snapback test. The remainder of the eye examination was within normal limits.
Symptom Onset: 1 year
Symptom Duration: 1 year
Progression: Chronic (symptoms present for 1 year)
Characteristics: Involutional entropion of the lower eyelid due to horizontal lid laxity, retractor dehiscence, overriding preseptal orbicularis, and relative enophthalmos; symptomatic with inferior conjunctival injection and 1+ inferior SPK; bilateral mild dermatochalasis; delayed snapback and moderate horizontal laxity in both lower lids.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: eyelid irritation, ocular irritation, lid edema
Alleviating Factors: Taping the lid; lubricating drops and ointment
Associated Symptoms: left eye irritation; 1+ inferior superficial punctate keratitis; conjunctival injection of tarsal conjunctiva; epiphora (possible)
Prior Treatments: Lubricating drops and ointment; taping of the lid (temporizing measures); temporizing everting sutures (Quickert‑Rathbun sutures)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — intermittent ocular irritation and inferior superficial punctate keratitis causing discomfort but vision largely preserved (20/25 OS), managed with lubricants and surgery.
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Age-related eyelid laxity (involutional changes), lower eyelid retractor dehiscence, overriding preseptal orbicularis, relative enophthalmos from orbital soft tissue atrophy, prior ocular irritation or eyelid edema (spastic component), cicatricial causes (eg, Stevens–Johnson syndrome, cicatricial pemphigoid, prior trauma/chemical/thermal burns), prior eyelid surgery (blepharoplasty)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Marked out-turning of the left lower eyelid with mild dermatochalasis of both upper lids; both lower lids show moderate horizontal laxity with delayed snapback; overriding of preseptal orbicularis on the right lower lid noted (entropion)
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Marked tarsal conjunctival injection diffusely (left lower eyelid)
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis or enophthalmos noted; orbital examination within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: 1+ SPK inferiorly
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Pseudophakia OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and equal bilaterally
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: EQUAL AND REACTIVE
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion in all directions, intact bilaterally
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Left corneal exam: 1+ inferior superficial punctate keratitis; diffuse tarsal conjunctival injection of the left lower eyelid; otherwise within normal limits.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits (no optic nerve abnormality noted)
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Involutional entropion of the right lower eyelid causing inferior corneal punctate keratitis and conjunctival injection; managed surgically with a Wies procedure resulting in satisfactory correction and no postoperative complications.
Primary Diagnosis: Involutional entropion of the right lower eyelid
Differential Diagnosis: Involutional (age-related) entropion; Spastic entropion (lid spasm from irritation/edema); Cicatricial entropion (posterior lamellar scarring/syndromes or burns)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right
Complications: None reported


Plan
All Medications: lubricating drops and ointment
All Procedures: Wies procedure of the lower eyelid
Follow-Up Instructions: The patient underwent a Wies procedure with satisfactory correction; follow-up for routine postoperative wound care and monitoring for complications. Arrange postoperative visits to assess healing, suture removal as indicated, check eyelid position and corneal surface, and provide lubricating drops/ointment as needed.
Patient Education: Lubricating eye drops and ointment to relieve irritation; temporary measures such as taping the lid or placement of everting (Quickert-Rathbun) sutures can provide short‑term relief; definitive surgical options (lateral tarsal strip, Wies procedure, or reattachment of lower lid retractors, sometimes combined) correct the entropion and are recommended for long‑term improvement; follow postoperative care instructions and return for follow‑up to monitor healing and vision.


CASE 90 — Trichiasis
====================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Severe right eye pain for 2 years, relieved by manually retracting the right lower eyelid
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
PATIENT QUOTES: "she can alleviate her pain if she manually retracts her right lower eyelid."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 75-year-old woman with a 2‑year history of progressively worsening severe right eye pain; she discovered that manually retracting her right lower eyelid alleviates the pain. Examination showed in-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing the ocular surface and moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; gentle downward traction restores the lid but the in-turning recurs with blinking.
History Narrative: A 75-year-old woman presented with progressively worsening severe right eye pain for 2 years. She reported that manually retracting her right lower eyelid alleviated the pain. Examination revealed in-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing the epibulbar surface and moderate horizontal laxity of the lower lid. With gentle downward traction the lid margin could be restored to normal position, but the in-turning recurred with blinking.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Progressive over 2 years
Characteristics: In-turning of the right lower eyelid with lashes rubbing on the epibulbar surface; moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; involutional entropion with aberrant/distichitic lashes and associated blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Blinking (recurrence of in‑turning with each blink)
Alleviating Factors: manually retracting her right lower eyelid
Associated Symptoms: Severe right eye pain relieved by manual retraction of the right lower eyelid; eyelid irritation from lashes rubbing the cornea; recurrent focal lash irritation; blepharitis-related symptoms from meibomian gland dysfunction (eyelid irritation, likely tearing and foreign-body sensation).
Previous Episodes: Recurrent misdirected lashes treated previously with epilation and office cryotherapy (recurrences within ~3 months)
Prior Treatments: Wies procedure on the lower eyelid and cryotherapy to aberrant lashes; prior office epilation and medical management with lubricating drops/ointment and lid hygiene for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant — severe right eye pain for 2 years causing need to manually retract eyelid for relief; intermittent vision/comfort impairment and need for ongoing treatments and procedures.
Risk Factors: Age-related changes (involutional): horizontal eyelid laxity, lower eyelid retractor disinsertion/dehiscence, orbicularis muscle overriding, relative enophthalmos; chronic blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction; prior chronic posterior lamellar scarring (e.g., cicatricial disease/trauma)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 130/78 mm Hg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths per minute
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/80
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J+2
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen distance acuity with correction (manifest refraction)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Right lower eyelid entropion with lashes rubbing on the globe (trichiasis/distichiasis); moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four lids.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Nontoxic, injected due to chronic irritation from trichiasis
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Normal globe position; right lower eyelid entropion with moderate horizontal laxity; no orbital mass or proptosis.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Punctate epithelial erosions of the superior cornea from lash irritation consistent with localized epithelial defect from trichiasis
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Angle: Open
Anterior Segment – Lens: 1+ nuclear sclerotic cataract (mild age‑related lens changes)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal and reactive, normal size (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric reaction to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Brisk
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact (normal)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Right lower eyelid entropion with lashes rubbing on the epibulbar surface (trichiasis/distichiasis); moderate horizontal lower lid laxity; no posterior lamellar scarring; blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four eyelids.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Trichiasis of the lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) associated with right lower eyelid involutional entropion. Concomitant blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction of all four eyelids. Lid not restricted, consistent with involutional rather than cicatricial entropion.
Primary Diagnosis: Trichiasis of the lower eyelid due to aberrant lashes (distichiasis) and lower lid entropion
Secondary Diagnoses: Blepharitis; Meibomian gland dysfunction (all four eyelids); Distichiasis
Differential Diagnosis: Involutional entropion; Spastic entropion; Cicatricial entropion
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right
Complications: loss of skin pigmentation and eyelid notching


Plan
All Medications: lubricating drops and ointment
All Procedures: Wies procedure; cryotherapy to aberrant lashes; additional in‑office cryotherapy for focal recurrence; lid hygiene program for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction
Follow-Up Instructions: Lid hygiene program for blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction; monitor for lash recurrence with additional in‑office cryotherapy as needed; routine postoperative follow‑up to assess entropion repair and focal lash regrowth.
Patient Education: Use lubricating eye drops and ointment regularly to protect the cornea and relieve irritation; practice daily lid hygiene (warm compresses and lid massage/cleaning) to treat blepharitis and meibomian gland dysfunction; understand that epilation offers temporary relief but lashes often recur; discuss definitive options (cryotherapy, electrolysis/laser for focal lashes, or surgical repair such as entropion repair and wedge excision or lash-follicle removal) and that repeat treatments may be necessary.


CASE 91 — Dacryocystitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: chronic irritation of the left eye worsening over the last 2 years
DURATION: 2 years
LATERALITY: right
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 64-year-old woman with a 2‑year history of chronic left eye irritation that had been worsening, with tearing, pain, redness, and swelling in the medial canthal area consistent with dacryocystitis; symptoms were due to nasolacrimal duct obstruction (right side diagnosed) related to prior nasal surgery, ultimately responsive to oral antibiotics and requiring dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tubing.
History Narrative: A 64-year-old woman presented with complaints of chronic irritation of the left eye that had been worsening over the last 2 years. There was no previous ocular history.
Symptom Onset: 2 years
Symptom Duration: 2 years
Progression: Resolved after treatment
Characteristics: infection of the lacrimal sac due to nasolacrimal duct obstruction causing tearing, pain, redness, and swelling in the medial canthal area


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Nasal surgery–related nasolacrimal duct obstruction (previous nasal surgery)
Alleviating Factors: Warm compresses; oral antibiotics; subsequent dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tubing
Associated Symptoms: tearing, pain, redness, swelling in the medial canthal area, chronic irritation, epiphora
Previous Episodes: Recurrent episodes over the past 2 years
Prior Treatments: previous nasal surgery; oral antibiotics; dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — chronic tearing, irritation, pain and recurrent infections impaired comfort and required medical/surgical treatment but patient retained most daily function.
Risk Factors: Previous nasal surgery leading to nasolacrimal duct disruption; chronic nasolacrimal duct obstruction.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 132/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 16 mm Hg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Diffuse thickening of the lid margin with fine telangiectatic vessels; entropion of the left lower eyelid easily returned to normal position; row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to the gray line (distichiasis) contacting the globe; scattered debris including collarettes on lashes of all four eyelids.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Inferior bulbar conjunctiva: rose bengal staining in areas associated with abnormal lashes; mild punctate corneal changes inferiorly.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Nasal lacrimal duct obstruction of right side with prior dacryocystitis; resolved inflammation after antibiotics and subsequent DCR with silicone tubing in place, good resolution of epiphora.
External Exam – Orbit: No proptosis; orbit without pulsation or globe displacement.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Mild inferior punctate corneal epithelial staining/changes.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal.
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerosis consistent with age-related cataract (no significant cortical or posterior subcapsular changes noted)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: PERRL (brisk)
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: PERRL and equal, no afferent pupillary defect
Extraocular Movements: Full and intact without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The tarsal conjunctiva showed mild erythema without cicatricial changes; inferior fornix deep without symblepharon. Eversion revealed a row of fine nonpigmented lashes posterior to the gray line contacting the globe. Mild inferior punctate corneal staining and inferior bulbar conjunctival rose bengal staining in areas touched by abnormal lashes. Diffuse lid margin thickening with fine telangiectatic vessels and scattered debris/collarettes on all four eyelid lashes.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Dacryocystitis of the right lacrimal system secondary to nasolacrimal duct disruption from prior nasal surgery, presenting with acute infectious inflammation that responded to oral antibiotics. Resolved acute inflammation followed by dacryocystorhinostomy with silicone tube placement, resulting in good resolution of epiphora; ongoing follow-up with otolaryngology and oncology for underlying midline disease.
Primary Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis
Secondary Diagnoses: disrupted nasolacrimal duct from prior nasal surgery; entropion of the left lower eyelid; distichiasis; blepharitis/meibomian gland dysfunction; dry eye (tear film insufficiency)
Differential Diagnosis: Dacryocystitis secondary to nasolacrimal duct obstruction; Acute bacterial dacryocystitis with lacrimal sac abscess; Chronic dacryocystitis from canalicular or punctal stenosis
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: right
Complications: recurrence of infection/epiphora; chronic dacryocystitis; lacrimal sac abscess/pyocele; need for surgical intervention (DCR)


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics antibiotic ophthalmic drops silicone tubing
All Procedures: oral antibiotics; dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) with placement of silicone tubing
Follow-Up Instructions: Continue ENT and oncology follow-up; monitor surgical site and lacrimal function after DCR; routine ophthalmic follow-up for epiphora resolution and tube care.
Patient Education: Apply warm compresses frequently to the inflamed area; complete the prescribed oral (or IV, if indicated) antibiotics as directed. Expect that most patients ultimately require dacryocystorhinostomy (DCR) after infection resolves to prevent recurrence; surgery is usually deferred until the acute infection is controlled but may be performed sooner if infection persists. Ophthalmic antibiotic drops are of limited value. Follow-up with ENT and ophthalmology for postoperative care and ongoing management; report increasing pain, fever, spreading redness, or failure to improve promptly.
Referrals: ENT and oncology follow-up


CASE 92 — Idiopathic Orbital Inflammation
=========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially
DURATION: 48 hours
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have constant tearing from my right eye since the surgeries, and recently I've noticed intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid with tenderness and mucous discharge."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old woman with constant right-sided epiphora since prior midfacial surgeries (including nasal septum removal and frontoethmoidectomies) who presented with tearing and medial right lower eyelid pain. More recently developed intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid with tenderness, mucous discharge, and whitish mucoid discharge expressed from the punctum; had been treated with topical antibiotic previously. Nasolacrimal irrigation showed distal nasolacrimal duct obstruction with patency of the proximal system. Conjunctiva mildly injected; no palpable lacrimal sac mass.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old woman was referred for evaluation of tearing and pain of the right lower eyelid medially. Her past medical history was significant for lethal midline granuloma (midfacial necrotizing lesion) for which she had undergone multiple procedures, including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies. She reported constant epiphora of the right eye since the time of her surgeries and had more recently noted intermittent redness of the right lower eyelid, associated with tenderness and mucous discharge from the eye. She had been previously treated with an unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop. Examination showed erythema of the right lower lid medially with associated tenderness to palpation. No definite masses were palpated in the lacrimal sac region. There was a whitish mucoid discharge expressed from the punctum upon palpation of the lacrimal sac, and the conjunctiva was mildly injected. The puncta of the right upper and lower eyelid were present and appeared patent. There was a well-healed surgical incision over the nose with residual distortion of the nasal architecture. The remainder of the ophthalmic examination was completely normal. Nasolacrimal irrigation was performed and demonstrated complete obstruction of the distal nasal lacrimal system. The upper system was patent as demonstrated by the reflux of irrigating solution through the upper and lower puncta. Inspection of the inner nose showed a large nasal defect with an absent septum.
Symptom Onset: acute onset
Symptom Duration: intermittent (constant since surgeries) — chronic since prior midfacial surgeries; epiphora present continuously since surgeries, recent intermittent redness/tenderness (acute on chronic)
Progression: Resolved with treatment
Characteristics: Idiopathic orbital inflammation with associated scleritis and secondary choroidal effusion


Subjective (cont.)
Alleviating Factors: Systemic corticosteroid therapy (IV methylprednisolone then oral prednisone)
Associated Symptoms: tearing (epiphora), pain of right lower eyelid medially, intermittent redness of right lower eyelid, tenderness, mucous/whitish mucoid discharge, mild conjunctival injection
Previous Episodes: Recurrent episodes of dacryocystitis/epiphora since prior nasal surgeries (chronic, intermittent redness and tenderness)
Prior Treatments: multiple procedures including removal of the nasal septum and frontoethmoidectomies; previously treated with an unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop
Impact on Daily Activities: Mild — able to perform usual activities after steroid treatment; temporary pain and vision changes initially limited function but resolved with therapy.
Systemic Symptoms: Afebrile; no systemic signs of infection; no evidence of collagen‑vascular disease; otherwise systemically well.
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms.
Risk Factors: Prior midfacial surgery and nasal/ethmoid surgery with resultant anatomic disruption (including absent nasal septum) leading to chronic nasolacrimal obstruction; prior infections/inflammation from underlying midline granulomatous disease (lethal midline granuloma) and chronic nasolacrimal stasis.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5/8
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Erythema and mild edema of the right lower eyelid medially; puncta present and appeared patent; whitish mucoid discharge from punctum; tenderness to palpation.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Mildly injected conjunctiva on the right; otherwise normal sclera.
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Right lower lid erythema and tenderness medially; whitish mucoid discharge from punctum; upper and lower puncta present and patent; reflux through puncta on irrigation; distal nasolacrimal duct obstruction on irrigation
External Exam – Orbit: Mild right lower eyelid erythema and tenderness medially with mild conjunctival injection; no palpable lacrimal sac mass; remainder of ophthalmic exam normal
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea, no epithelial defect, no stromal opacities or edema
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Quiet, deep anterior chamber without cells or flare
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Nuclear sclerotic changes consistent with age-related cataract (mild)


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, and reactive to light (no anisocoria)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Brisk and symmetric to light
Pupils – RAPD: Present
Pupils – Other: relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD) that resolved
Extraocular Movements: Full motility without restriction (orthotropic)
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Mild conjunctival injection of the right eye; otherwise slit-lamp exam within normal limits (no corneal ulceration, anterior chamber quiet, no hypopyon).
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Idiopathic orbital inflammation with associated scleritis and secondary choroidal effusion; prompt marked clinical response to high‑dose intravenous steroids with resolution of pain, improvement in vision and afferent pupillary defect, MRI negative for occult neoplasm, systemic workup for collagen‑vascular disease and lymphoma negative; continued oral prednisone with slow taper and clinical remission.
Primary Diagnosis: Idiopathic orbital inflammation
Secondary Diagnoses: scleritis; secondary choroidal effusion; prior lethal midline granuloma (midfacial necrotizing lesion) with nasal septum removal and frontoethmoidectomies; nasolacrimal duct obstruction leading to chronic epiphora and recurrent dacryocystitis
Differential Diagnosis: Infection (dacryocystitis/orbital cellulitis); Autoimmune orbital disease (sarcoidosis, rheumatoid arthritis, Wegener granulomatosis); Thyroid-related orbitopathy
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: methylprednisolone; prednisone; unknown ophthalmic antibiotic drop
All Procedures: nasolacrimal irrigation; dacryocystography (discussed as test); dacryoscintigraphy (discussed as test); passage of Bowman probe (mentioned as diagnostic maneuver); intravenous methylprednisolone administration; conversion to oral prednisone; orbital biopsy (stated as indicated if no improvement)
Follow-Up Instructions: Referred for systemic evaluation for collagen-vascular disease or lymphoma; slow taper of oral steroid therapy with outpatient follow-up and repeat MRI to exclude underlying neoplasm.
Patient Education: Explain idiopathic orbital inflammation (an inflammatory condition of the tissues around the eye), its typical sudden onset with pain and swelling, and common causes to rule out (infection, autoimmune disease, thyroid eye disease, or tumor). Reassure that rapid improvement with high‑dose corticosteroids is expected and is a hallmark of this diagnosis. Advise the patient about the steroid treatment plan: need for high‑dose IV steroids initially with transition to oral prednisone and a slow taper to prevent recurrence and reduce side effects. Discuss common steroid side effects (increased blood sugar, mood changes, insomnia, weight gain, fluid retention, elevated blood pressure, increased infection risk, and possible bone thinning with long courses) and the importance of monitoring (blood pressure, blood glucose, and bone health as appropriate). Instruct to report any new symptoms promptly (worsening vision, increased eye pain, fever, cough, or systemic symptoms). Explain the rationale for further testing and follow‑up: systemic evaluation for autoimmune disease or lymphoma, and follow‑up imaging (MRI) to exclude occult neoplasm if indicated. Advise avoiding abrupt steroid discontinuation and to follow prescribed taper; provide written instructions for dosing and taper schedule. Encourage routine ophthalmology follow‑up to monitor vision, inflammation, and steroid side effects.
Referrals: Systemic evaluation for collagen-vascular disease or lymphoma.


CASE 93 — Orbital Cellulitis
============================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision
DURATION: 2 days
LATERALITY: left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 63-year-old man with a 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision. No prior ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury. No history of sinus disease, diabetes, rheumatoid or collagen vascular disease. Past medical history significant for poorly controlled hypertension.
History Narrative: A 63-year-old man with poorly controlled hypertension presented with a 2-day history of left eye pain and decreased vision. He had no prior ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury, no history of sinus disease, diabetes mellitus, rheumatoid or collagen vascular disease, malignancy, immunosuppressive therapy, or recent trauma. Review of systems revealed no systemic symptoms suggestive of thyroid disease.
Symptom Onset: 2 days
Symptom Duration: 2 days
Progression: Improved after surgical drainage and IV antibiotics; complete resolution on follow-up.
Characteristics: Acute onset left orbital inflammation with eye pain, redness, chemosis, proptosis, decreased vision, painful ophthalmoplegia, periorbital edema/erythema, 3 mm proptosis, 2+ RAPD OS, choroidal thickening, diffuse anterior orbital inflammation with scleral thickening and subperiosteal orbital abscess associated with ethmoid sinusitis.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: eye pain, decreased vision, fever not reported, periorbital edema and erythema, conjunctival injection, chemosis, proptosis, limited extraocular motility with pain on movement, relative afferent pupillary defect, choroidal thickening
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes of ophthalmic disease, surgery, or injury.
Impact on Daily Activities: Significant impairment — marked periorbital edema, severe pain, decreased vision OS (20/400), limited extraocular motility, and hospitalization/surgery causing inability to perform normal activities until recovery.
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported (afebrile; no history of sinus disease, diabetes, rheumatoid or collagen vascular disease).
Recent Travel: No recent travel
Risk Factors: Poorly controlled hypertension; ethmoid sinusitis (associated); recent eyelid/eyelid trauma absent; no diabetes mellitus; no immunosuppression; age 63 (older adult)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 150/90 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 88 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/400
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 22 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: marked periorbital edema and erythema
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Marked left periorbital edema and erythema with 3 mm left globe proptosis; increased resistance to retropulsion of the left globe; significant motility deficit in all directions of gaze with associated pain.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear, no corneal abnormalities detected
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Deep and quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: normal


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal, round, reactive to light (left with 2+ RAPD)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect of the left pupil
Pupils – RAPD: 2+ left
Pupils – Other: 2+ relative afferent pupillary defect OS
Extraocular Movements: Significant motility deficit in all directions of gaze with associated pain
Slit Lamp Exam Description: 3+ left conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 63-year-old man with acute left orbital cellulitis complicated by subperiosteal orbital abscess and associated ethmoid sinusitis. Presented with 2 days of left eye pain, decreased vision to 20/400 OS, a 2+ left RAPD, marked periorbital edema/erythema, 3 mm proptosis, resistance to retropulsion, painful global motility limitation, 3+ conjunctival injection with diffuse chemosis, and diffuse choroidal thickening. Orbital MRI with gadolinium showed diffuse anterior orbital inflammation, posterior scleral thickening, and choroidal thickening without discrete neoplasm. Failed to improve on prior therapy; taken to OR for drainage of large subperiosteal orbital abscess and ethmoidectomy. Cultures negative. Treated with IV ceftriaxone and clindamycin with marked clinical improvement, transitioned to oral antibiotics after 48 hours and discharged; subsequent follow-up showed complete resolution of periorbital swelling and motility restriction.
Primary Diagnosis: Orbital cellulitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Poorly controlled hypertension; Ethmoid sinusitis; Subperiosteal orbital abscess
Differential Diagnosis: Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital pseudotumor); Preseptal (periorbital) cellulitis; Thyroid‑related (thyroid eye disease); Orbital abscess secondary to sinusitis
Severity: Severe
Laterality: Left
Complications: subperiosteal orbital abscess


Plan
All Medications: ceftriaxone; clindamycin; gadolinium (contrast for MRI)
All Procedures: Drainage of large subperiosteal orbital abscess; ethmoidectomy; preoperative blood cultures; intraoperative cultures of abscess material; intravenous ceftriaxone and clindamycin administration; conversion to oral antibiotics on discharge.
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up examination to confirm resolution of periorbital swelling and motility restriction; outpatient oral antibiotics after 48 hours IV then discharge; routine clinical follow-up visits to monitor recovery.
Patient Education: Orbital cellulitis is a serious infection behind the eyelid that can threaten vision and requires prompt treatment. You had an infection with an orbital abscess related to sinus disease; treatment includes hospitalization, intravenous antibiotics, and sometimes surgery to drain the abscess and treat the infected sinuses. Signs that require immediate return to the hospital include worsening vision, increasing eye pain, fever, new drooping or inability to move the eye, or spreading redness/swelling. After discharge, finish the entire course of prescribed antibiotics, keep follow-up appointments (including ENT and ophthalmology), avoid nose-blowing or activities that increase sinus pressure until cleared, use prescribed pain medications and cool compresses for comfort, and report any new fever, change in vision, or recurrence of swelling promptly.
Referrals: ENT surgeon; infectious disease consult (pediatric infectious disease)


CASE 94 — Dacryoadenitis
========================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever for 4 days
DURATION: 4 days
LATERALITY: Right
PATIENT QUOTES: "somewhat lethargic and continued to complain of pain around the right eye."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 7-year-old boy with 4 days of right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever; initially diagnosed with preseptal cellulitis and started on oral antibiotics, with progression over 2 days to worsening right upper lid edema causing complete ptosis, lethargy, persistent periorbital pain, low-grade fever (100°F), conjunctival injection, decreased right eye motility, mild proptosis, and palpable preauricular and submandibular lymphadenopathy; no definite decreased vision.
History Narrative: A 7-year-old boy was referred for evaluation and treatment of right-sided eyelid swelling, pain, and fever that began 4 days earlier. He had no definite complaint of decreased vision. He was initially diagnosed with preseptal cellulitis and started on oral antibiotics. Two days later his right upper lid edema progressed to complete ptosis despite antibiotics, and he was referred to a tertiary care center. On arrival he was somewhat lethargic, continuing to complain of right eye pain, and had a temperature of 100°F. Exam showed marked periorbital edema and erythema with complete right eyelid ptosis, enlarged preauricular and submandibular lymph nodes, diffuse conjunctival injection, decreased motility of the right globe in all directions, mild proptosis obscured by lid edema, normal pupillary reactions without relative afferent pupillary defect, quiet anterior segments, and a dilated fundus exam without significant abnormalities.
Symptom Onset: 4 days prior to presentation
Symptom Duration: 4 days
Progression: Progression of the right upper lid edema despite oral antibiotics over 2 days, leading to complete ptosis.
Characteristics: Inflammation and rapid enlargement of the lacrimal gland with eyelid erythema, edema (temporal upper eyelid), tenderness, possible conjunctival injection, regional lymphadenopathy, often unilateral, may cause S-shaped eyelid deformity and tearing; may be infectious (eg, EBV, bacterial), idiopathic, or neoplastic.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: Eyelid swelling, eye pain, fever, lethargy, ptosis (complete), periorbital edema and erythema, conjunctival injection, decreased ocular motility in all gazes, mild proptosis, regional lymphadenopathy, tearing (epiphora)
Previous Episodes: None
Prior Treatments: Oral antibiotics (initiated for presumed preseptal cellulitis)
Impact on Daily Activities: Moderate — significant eyelid edema, pain, ptosis, decreased ocular motility and lethargy limiting normal activities and school attendance for days; self-limited with expected recovery.
Systemic Symptoms: Fever of 100 °F; lethargy.
Risk Factors: Recent/acute Epstein–Barr virus (EBV) infection; prior mumps (historical); bacterial infections (Staphylococcus aureus, Streptococcus species, Neisseria gonorrhoeae); immunization status (lack of HiB/mumps vaccination historically increases related infections); adjacent sinusitis or local periocular infection allowing spread; systemic immunosuppression (inferred as general risk for infections).


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Temperature: 100 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 18 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/25
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+20/25
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart, uncorrected (measured with eyelid gently lifted)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 18 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Marked periorbital edema and erythema of the right upper eyelid with complete ptosis; “S-shaped” deformity of the upper eyelid; right upper lid fullness in superotemporal region over lacrimal gland; no eyelash abnormality noted.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: diffusely injected
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Enlarged, tender right lacrimal gland with erythema and edema of the temporal upper eyelid (acute dacryoadenitis); S-shaped upper eyelid deformity noted on clinical photograph.
External Exam – Orbit: Mild proptosis OD with decreased motility of the right globe in all directions of gaze; marked right periorbital edema and erythema with complete right upper eyelid ptosis; palpable preauricular and submandibular lymphadenopathy.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear cornea — no anterior segment inflammation noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Quiet
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: reacted normally
Pupils – Reaction to Light: reactive to light
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: No RAPD detected
Pupils – Other: No relative afferent pupillary defect (RAPD)
Extraocular Movements: Decreased motility of the right globe in all directions of gaze
Slit Lamp Exam Description: Anterior segments were quiet on slit-lamp examination.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits
Fundus Exam – Macula: No significant abnormalities
Fundus Exam – Vessels: Vessels normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: No peripheral retinal lesions seen; peripheral fundus within normal limits (no abnormalities noted)


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: Acute dacryoadenitis of the right lacrimal gland, clinically consistent with an acute inflammatory enlargement with eyelid erythema, edema, tenderness, "S-shaped" upper lid deformity and regional lymphadenopathy. Serologic testing positive for anti-EBV viral capsid antigen antibodies with negative anti-EBV nuclear antigen antibodies supports recent Epstein–Barr virus infection as the presumed etiology. No evidence of orbital involvement on clinical exam; management with anti-inflammatory therapy (topical or oral corticosteroids) and observation recommended; disease is typically self-limited.
Primary Diagnosis: Acute dacryoadenitis
Secondary Diagnoses: Epstein–Barr virus (acute EBV infection)
Differential Diagnosis: Acute dacryoadenitis (infectious—EBV or bacterial); Idiopathic orbital inflammation (orbital pseudotumor) involving lacrimal gland; Lacrimal gland tumor (primary epithelial or metastatic)
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Right


Plan
All Medications: oral antibiotics (unspecified), oxacillin (example penicillinase-resistant penicillin), topical or oral corticosteroid medication
Follow-Up Instructions: Follow-up for resolution of symptoms.
Patient Education: Acute dacryoadenitis is inflammation of the lacrimal (tear) gland, often causing pain, swelling and fullness at the outer upper eyelid. In many cases—especially when viral (e.g., EBV)—it improves on its own over days to weeks. What to expect and do: - Rest, avoid rubbing the eye, and apply cool compresses to the affected eyelid for comfort. - Take prescribed medications exactly as directed: antibiotics only if a bacterial cause is suspected; corticosteroids may be used if inflammation is significant and after your doctor’s evaluation. - Watch for warning signs that require urgent care: worsening pain, increasing swelling, fever, new decrease in vision, trouble moving the eye, or any pus/discharge. - Keep follow-up appointments so the doctor can confirm the inflammation is resolving and rule out other causes. - Most cases resolve without surgery; if symptoms persist or recur, further testing (blood tests or imaging) may be recommended.


CASE 95 — Orbital Tumors of Childhood
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid
DURATION: 1 week
LATERALITY: left
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 12-year-old boy with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid, associated redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of the left upper lid producing an “S-shaped” deformity; manual eyelid elevation revealed marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva with enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland, moderate tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland, and mild regional lymph node enlargement. No other systemic disease; ocular motility and remainder of eye exam normal without intraocular inflammation.
History Narrative: A 12-year-old boy presented with a 1-week history of swelling of the left upper eyelid. The boy was otherwise healthy without known chronic medical disease. On examination, there was redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of the left upper lid that created an “S-shaped” deformity of the eyelid. Manual elevation of the left upper eyelid revealed marked injection and chemosis of the bulbar conjunctiva laterally with enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland. There was moderate tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland. Mild enlargement of the regional lymph nodes was also noted. The remainder of the ocular examination, including motility, was normal. There was no evidence of intraocular inflammation.
Symptom Onset: 1 week
Symptom Duration: 1 week
Progression: Resolved
Characteristics: Most common primary benign orbital tumors in children: dermoids, epidermoids, and cystic lesions; also common are vascular tumors (capillary hemangiomas) and neural tumors (optic nerve gliomas, neurofibromas); most common primary orbital malignant tumor is rhabdomyosarcoma; secondary orbital invasion can occur from neuroblastoma, Ewing’s sarcoma, osteosarcoma.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: swelling of the left upper eyelid; redness; fullness of temporal left upper lid causing S-shaped deformity; conjunctival injection and chemosis laterally; enlargement and erythema of palpebral lacrimal gland; moderate tenderness over enlarged lacrimal gland; mild regional lymph node enlargement
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Contacts with Similar Symptoms: No known contacts with similar symptoms
Risk Factors: Congenital inclusion of surface ectoderm (embryologic sequestration at suture lines), superficial orbital location near bony sutures; pediatric age (childhood)


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 36.8 °C
Vitals – Respirations: 18 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen — 20/20 (measured by Snellen chart, age-appropriate testing)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Not recorded


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: redness and fullness of the temporal aspect of the left upper lid with an “S-shaped” deformity; enlargement, erythema, and tenderness of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland; mild regional lymphadenopathy
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Injection and chemosis of the bulbar conjunctiva laterally; otherwise no scleral abnormality noted
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Enlarged, erythematous, tender palpebral lacrimal gland with associated conjunctival injection and chemosis (left)
External Exam – Orbit: Left upper eyelid temporal fullness with S-shaped deformity; redness and fullness over lacrimal gland region with enlargement and erythema of palpebral lacrimal lobe, conjunctival injection and chemosis laterally; moderate tenderness over enlarged lacrimal gland; mild regional lymphadenopathy; otherwise normal motility and no intraocular inflammation.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: No inflammation; anterior chamber normal
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear natural lens


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Equal and reactive, normal size
Pupils – Reaction to Light: brisk and equal to light in both eyes
Pupils – Reaction to Near: normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light and accommodation in both eyes
Extraocular Movements: Full and unrestricted in all directions of gaze.
Slit Lamp Exam Description: No intraocular inflammation; otherwise normal anterior segment on slit-lamp exam.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Normal


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 12-year-old boy with 1-week history of left upper eyelid swelling. Examination notable for redness and temporal fullness of the left upper lid with an S-shaped deformity, marked injection and chemosis of the lateral bulbar conjunctiva on manual eyelid elevation, enlargement and erythema of the palpebral lobe of the lacrimal gland, moderate tenderness over the enlarged lacrimal gland, and mild regional lymphadenopathy. Ocular motility and intraocular exam otherwise normal. Imaging may show lacrimal gland enlargement or mass without bony erosion or soft-tissue invasion. Clinical presentation and imaging consistent with an orbital dermoid cyst of the right orbit. Surgical excision via lid crease incision performed with complete removal; no postoperative sequelae.
Primary Diagnosis: Dermoid cyst of the right orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Lacrimal gland inflammation (dacryoadenitis); Dermoid/epidermoid cyst; Orbital cellulitis/abscess; Lacrimal gland tumor (e.g., pleomorphic adenoma, lymphoma)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: right
Complications: None reported


Plan
All Procedures: Surgical excision of orbital dermoid cyst via lid crease incision (complete excision).
Follow-Up Instructions: Routine postoperative follow-up for wound check and suture removal; monitor for recurrence, infection, or inflammatory reaction; return sooner if increasing pain, redness, swelling, discharge, fever, or vision changes.
Patient Education: A dermoid cyst is a benign, slow‑growing lump that often occurs near the eye and is made of tissue trapped under the skin. It can enlarge or rupture over time, causing inflammation. There is no medication that makes it go away. Surgical removal is recommended to prevent future irritation, inflammation, or cosmetic problems. Surgery is typically done through a small eyelid incision for a concealed scar; timing may be delayed in very young infants to reduce anesthesia risk. Risks of surgery include bleeding, infection, scarring, and rarely damage to nearby tissues. After complete excision the prognosis is excellent, with low risk of recurrence. If you notice increasing size, redness, pain, drainage, or changes in vision, seek prompt medical care.


CASE 96 — Orbital Tumors in Adults
==================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Mass/visible swelling over the right eye present since shortly after birth with slow enlargement
DURATION: Progressive since shortly after birth (approximately 8 months)
LATERALITY: left
PATIENT QUOTES: "N/A"
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 8-month-old boy with a congenital mass over the right eye noted shortly after birth, slowly enlarging over time without color change or size change with crying/Valsalva; otherwise healthy, full-term birth, no other symptoms. Exam: central fixation, following in each eye, moderately firm 20×10×5 mm fixed superotemporal right eyelid mass with intact overlying skin, no measurable proptosis, normal retropulsion, no other palpable masses.
History Narrative: An 8-month-old healthy boy presented for evaluation of a mass over the right eye. The mass had been present since shortly after birth and had slowly enlarged. The child was born full-term after an uncomplicated pregnancy and was otherwise healthy with no known medical problems. Parents reported no change in color or size of the mass with crying or Valsalva. There were no other masses or systemic symptoms noted.
Symptom Onset: Shortly after birth
Symptom Duration: Present since shortly after birth (approximately 8 months)
Progression: Progressive proptosis
Characteristics: Dark-red, encapsulated, well-circumscribed retrobulbar tumor causing progressive proptosis and compressing posterior globe.


Subjective (cont.)
Aggravating Factors: Valsalva/crying had no effect
Associated Symptoms: painless progressive proptosis
Previous Episodes: No previous episodes mentioned
Impact on Daily Activities: No impact on daily activities
Systemic Symptoms: None
Risk Factors: None identified; patient otherwise healthy with no known risk factors.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 110/70 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 120 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 37.0°C
Vitals – Respirations: 20 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/60
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/40
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: N5/6
Visual Acuity – Method: Snellen chart (age‑appropriate fixation preferential — Teller acuity cards)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)

External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis of the left eye (retrobulbar, well‑circumscribed mass causing posterior globe displacement)
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Clear.

Pupils – RAPD: None detected

Fundus Exam – Vessels: Normal
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Within normal limits

Primary Diagnosis: Cavernous hemangioma of the left orbit
Differential Diagnosis: Dermoid/epidermoid cyst; Capillary hemangioma; Lymphangioma; Rhabdomyosarcoma
Severity: Moderate
Laterality: Left

Follow-Up Instructions: The patient did well postoperatively; monitor postoperative recovery, follow refractive error (stabilized at –2.00 D) and resolve choroidal striae with periodic ophthalmic follow-up.


CASE 97 — Benign Tumors of the Eyelid
=====================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: increasing painless prominence of the left eye
DURATION: many years
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "increasing painless prominence of the left eye without associated diplopia."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: 44-year-old man referred for evaluation of progressive, painless prominence (proptosis) of the left eye over many months/years, without diplopia; noted increasing prominence with two changes in glasses prescription in the past year.
History Narrative: A 44-year-old man was referred for evaluation of proptosis of the left eye. The patient had no previous medical history. He reported increasing painless prominence of the left eye without associated diplopia. His glasses prescription had been changed twice in the past year. Examination showed visual acuity of 20/20 OU with a manifest refraction of –2.25 D OD and +1.00 D OS. Motility and pupil examination were within normal limits. The palpebral fissure was widened on the left side, and Hertel exophthalmometry disclosed 4 mm relative proptosis OS. There was increased resistance of the left globe to retropulsion OS. No bony defects or definite masses were palpated. There was no regional lymphadenopathy and no bruits were auscultated over the orbits. The anterior segment was quiet by slit-lamp examination; the bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity. Dilated examination of the left fundus revealed choroidal striae of the posterior pole. The optic nerve was normal, and no hemorrhage or exudate was present. The right fundus was normal.
Symptom Duration: increasing over many years
Progression: Progressive over many years
Characteristics: Very slow growth over many years; lack of skin ulceration, scaling, madarosis, or telangiectasia; painless; typically well-circumscribed; arising from eyelid skin or adnexal structures.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: increasing painless prominence of the left eye, decreased myopia (change in glasses), no diplopia
Previous Episodes: None
Impact on Daily Activities: Minimal to none
Systemic Symptoms: No systemic symptoms reported


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 128/76 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 78 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6 °F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Method: manifest refraction
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 14 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Goldmann applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: Palpebral fissure widened on the left; upper eyelid lesion excised with care to avoid eyelid margin distortion; no madarosis; no skin ulceration or telangiectasia.
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: bulbar conjunctiva unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity
External Exam – Lacrimal System: No epiphora; lacrimal system without abnormality.
External Exam – Orbit: Proptosis OS 4 mm; increased resistance to retropulsion OS; widened palpebral fissure OS; no bony defects palpated; no regional lymphadenopathy; no bruits over the orbits.
Anterior Segment – Cornea: quiet by slit-lamp examination; bulbar conjunctiva unremarkable — no corneal abnormality noted
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: quiet
Anterior Segment – Iris: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Lens: Clear lenses OU


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Within normal limits
Pupils – Reaction to Light: Within normal limits
Pupils – Reaction to Near: Normal
Pupils – RAPD: None detected
Pupils – Other: within normal limits
Extraocular Movements: Within normal limits
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The anterior segment was quiet by slit-lamp examination; the bulbar conjunctiva was unremarkable without evidence of abnormal vascularity.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: The optic nerve was normal
Fundus Exam – Macula: choroidal striae of the posterior pole
Fundus Exam – Periphery: Peripheral retina flat and unremarkable without hemorrhage or exudate.


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 44-year-old man with left orbital mass presenting as progressive, painless left proptosis (4 mm relative), increased palpebral fissure, decreased myopic refraction OS, resistance to retropulsion, choroidal striae on fundus exam, and otherwise normal vision and motility. Orbital CT showed a well-circumscribed retrobulbar lesion without bony change and only marginal contrast enhancement, consistent with a benign intradermal nevus/cavernous hemangioma–type lesion. Given clinical and radiographic features suggesting a benign eyelid/orbital tumor, observation versus surgical excision is appropriate. Excisional biopsy of the upper eyelid lesion was performed; histopathology confirmed intradermal nevus. No further treatment required; patient doing well without recurrence.
Primary Diagnosis: Benign eyelid tumor, most likely intradermal nevus
Differential Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus; seborrheic keratosis; squamous papilloma; hidrocystoma
Severity: Mild
Laterality: Left


Plan
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy of the upper eyelid lesion (office procedure under local anesthesia) with primary repair or allowed to heal by secondary intention as indicated and specimen sent for histopathologic examination
Follow-Up Instructions: The patient has done well without evidence of recurrence.
Patient Education: Benign eyelid tumor (intradermal nevus): slow‑growing, noncancerous lesion. Options include observation or simple surgical removal under local anesthetic in the office. Procedure risks: bleeding, infection, scarring, eyelid margin distortion, or slight change in eyelid position; care is taken to minimize these. Excised tissue is sent for pathology to confirm the diagnosis. No medical treatment is needed. If observed, return for follow‑up if the lesion changes in size, color, becomes painful, ulcerates, or causes eyelid irritation or vision changes.


CASE 98 — Malignant Tumors of the Eyelid
========================================
Subjective
CHIEF COMPLAINT: Left upper eyelid lesion, slowly enlarging over several years (worse over the last several months)
DURATION: several years (slowly enlarging for the last several months)
LATERALITY: Left
PATIENT QUOTES: "I have had this left upper eyelid lesion for several years and it has been slowly enlarging for the last several months."
HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS: A 32-year-old man with a left upper eyelid lesion present for several years that had been slowly enlarging over the last several months, without redness or bleeding; otherwise healthy with no prior skin disease or skin cancer.
History Narrative: A 32-year-old man presented for evaluation of a left upper eyelid lesion that had been present for several years and that had been slowly enlarging for the last several months. He reported no associated redness or bleeding from the lesion and there was no history of previous skin disease or skin cancer. He was in good health and took no medications. His ophthalmic examination was within normal limits with the exception of the external exam. There was a 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped skin lesion involving the preseptal eyelid skin of the left upper lid. The tumor was amelanotic, and the overlying skin was intact without ulceration or telangiectasia. There was no regional lymphadenopathy and no other skin lesions were present on the head and neck region.
Symptom Onset: Several years, with slow enlargement over the last several months
Symptom Duration: several years (with slow enlargement for the last several months)
Progression: Slowly enlarging over several months after years of very slow growth
Characteristics: 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth, dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion with intact overlying skin, no ulceration, telangiectasia, madarosis, or regional lymphadenopathy; slowly enlarging over several years.


Subjective (cont.)
Associated Symptoms: No associated redness or bleeding.
Previous Episodes: No prior episodes reported
Impact on Daily Activities: None
Systemic Symptoms: None reported
Risk Factors: Sun exposure (sun-exposed skin) / UV radiation; history of skin lesions not present but location on sun-exposed eyelid; fair risk inferred from lesion on lower/medial canthus—chronic UV exposure.


Objective Exam
Vitals – Blood Pressure: 120/78 mmHg
Vitals – Pulse: 76 bpm
Vitals – Temperature: 98.6°F
Vitals – Respirations: 16 breaths/min
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Right: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Uncorrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Corrected Left: 20/20
Visual Acuity – Near Acuity: J1+
Visual Acuity – Method: 20/20 (Snellen)
Intraocular Pressure – Right: 16 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Left: 15 mmHg
Intraocular Pressure – Method: Applanation tonometry


Objective Exam (cont.)
External Exam – Lids/Lashes: 10 mm × 8 mm × 5 mm smooth dome-shaped, amelanotic skin lesion of the left upper (preseptal) eyelid; overlying skin intact without ulceration, telangiectasia, or madarosis; no regional lymphadenopathy
External Exam – Conjunctiva/Sclera: within normal limits
External Exam – Lacrimal System: Normal
External Exam – Orbit: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Cornea: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Anterior Chamber: Within normal limits
Anterior Segment – Iris: Normal
Anterior Segment – Lens: Phakic


Objective Exam (cont. 2)
Pupils – Size/Shape: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light (normal)
Pupils – Reaction to Light: briskly reactive to light
Pupils – RAPD: Absent
Pupils – Other: PERRL and reactive to light
Extraocular Movements: Full range of motion OU without restriction
Slit Lamp Exam Description: The ophthalmic examination was within normal limits with the exception of the external exam.
Fundus Exam – Optic Nerve: Within normal limits


Assessment
Assessment Narrative: 32-year-old man with a several-year history of a slowly enlarging, smooth, amelanotic dome-shaped lesion of the left upper eyelid preseptal skin (approximately 10 × 8 × 5 mm). Clinical features favored a benign nevus but given growth the lesion was excised; intraoperative frozen section demonstrated nodular basal cell carcinoma with clear margins. Full-thickness skin graft repair of the defect performed; patient recovering well with satisfactory functional and cosmetic outcome and counseled regarding increased risk for new skin malignancies and dermatologic follow-up.
Primary Diagnosis: Lesion of the right upper eyelid (medial canthal region); probable basal cell carcinoma
Differential Diagnosis: Intradermal nevus (amelanotic nevus); Seborrheic keratosis; Papilloma (squamous papilloma)
Severity: Mild
Laterality: left


Plan
All Procedures: Excisional biopsy with intraoperative frozen section analysis and full-thickness skin graft repair; cryotherapy to margins (advocated as adjunct)
Follow-Up Instructions: He has been educated about his increased risk for new skin malignancies and is under the care of a dermatologist for continued surveillance.
Patient Education: Educated about his increased risk for development of new skin malignancies and advised to undergo continued dermatologic surveillance.
Referrals: Dermatology for continued surveillance and oncologist if sebaceous carcinoma or melanoma suspected (complete metastatic evaluation)
